<strong>aside</strong>: a comment somewhat off the subject, but thrown in for interest, color, or perhaps comic relief.

<strong>aspect</strong>: the manner in which action is looked at in contrast to tense, voice, mood, number, and person.

Linear action has translations that include <em>was/were/is/are working</em>; <em>used to work</em>; <em>would work</em>; <em>kept working</em>. In past time, Latin and Greek use the imperfect for linear action in past time. English uses auxiliaries plus present participle: <em>was/were working.</em>

Inchoative/ingressive/inceptive: the beginning of an action; covered by the *imperfect tense in Latin but by the *aorist tense in Greek.

Iterative: “The grandfather clock chimes every hour.” “It will chime every hour.” “It chimed every hour.”

Cyclical: “The cam turns at a thousand RPM’s.” “It turned at a thousand RPM’s.” “It will turn at a thousand RPM’s.”

Progressive: is working, was working, will be working

Characteristic action: “She works at Walmart” (present). She would work at Walmart during the day and go to college in the evening” (past). “She will work at Walmart, not K-Mart” (future). English simple past often covers characteristic action in past time, especially if linear indicators are present: “She <em>often</em> worked at Walmart in the daytime and went to college in the evening.”


Punctiliar action is also called simple action, aoristic action, factitive action, snapshot action. “I say to you, you will get out till you have paid the last farthing/cent/quadrans” (Mt <strong><sup>5</sup></strong>:26, present). “I said that you would not get out” (past). “I will say this again tomorrow” (future).

Ingressive action is the beginning of an action. In Greek, ingressive action was covered by the aorist tense; in Latin it was covered by the imperfect tense. Inchoative/inceptive/ ingressive action is both punctiliar and linear in that its inception is a point but its continuation is linear; thus Greek’s use of the aorist (punctiliar) and Latin’s use of the imperfect (linear) to cover it.


Conative action is attempted action: “On account of which work of these are you stoning me (trying to stone/getting ready to stone)?” (Jn <strong><sup>10</sup></strong>:32, present); “were you trying to stone me”; will you be trying to stone me?” The Latin and Greek imperfect can cover the conative idea.


Perfect is action completed before some point in time:


Strictly speaking, Hebrew verbs have only aspect. The perfect and imperfect “tenses” are really completed or uncompleted action. That is why there is no present tense form in the finite verb.

(in a set with *person, *number, *tense, *mood, and *voice as possible features of verb forms) 	(in a set with *linear, *punctiliar/*factitive/simple, *perfect)

<strong>aspiration</strong>: adding an “h” sound to the front of a vowel or end of a consonant. The initial <em>p</em> and <em>t</em> in English words have a puff of air after them; when preceded, for example, by <em>s</em>, however, the aspiration is lost: <em>pit</em> <em>vs</em>. <em>spit</em>; <em>pat</em> <em>vs</em>. <em>spat</em>; <em>pend</em> <em>vs</em>. <em>spend</em>; <em>pot</em> <em>vs</em>. <em>spot</em>; <em>putter</em> <em>vs</em>. <em>sputter</em>; <em>tar</em> <em>vs</em>. <em>star</em>; <em>ted</em> <em>vs</em>. <em>stead</em>; <em>till</em> <em>vs</em>. <em>still</em>; <em>top</em> <em>vs</em>. <em>stop</em>; <em>tub</em> <em>vs</em>. <em>stub</em>.

The Greek <em>rho</em> originally had an <em>h</em> sound preceding it. English derivatives pronounce the <em>rh</em> simply as <em>h</em>: rhododendron, Rhoda.

<strong>assimilate/assimilation</strong>: a kind of euphony that changes the theoretical sound—often created by inflection—into another that is the same as or phonologically related to the subsequent one: syndicate, but sym- before lip letters (*labials) as in symphysis; syl- before l’s as in syllogism; cum becomes con-, com- before labials, col- before “l” words (collate), cor- before “r” words (corregenda). In Greek we have gamma changing value to *velar *nasal (-ng-) before velars (gogguzo/gonguzo); nu changing to ng represented by gamma in compounds (egkasivizo/engkaivizo/to inaugurate)

(in a set with *elision, *contraction)

<strong>assonance/assonant</strong>: having the same vowel sound as is Disney’s <em>Huey</em>, <em>Dewey</em>, and <em>Louie</em>.

<strong>asterisk</strong>: a symbol (*) for drawing attention to some special feature in the written situation—a footnote, an omission, an irregularity; also more recently called *star.

<strong>aspirate</strong>: having a puff of air associated with it, as with initial <em>p</em>’s, <em>t</em>’s, and <em>c/k</em>’s in English. Also a designation used in older Greek grammars for the fricatives <em>phi</em>, <em>chi</em>, <em>theta</em>. Initial <em>rho </em>had a rough breathing on it; thus the transliteration <em>rh</em> in English derivatives. In Latin <em>ph</em> was probably an aspirate rather than a *fricative.

<strong>asyndeton</strong> a pattern of *syntax that does not connect grammatical items together with conjunctions as would normally be done: “That car is large, expensive, different; I am not impressed.” (in a set with *hypotaxis, *parataxis)

<strong>asyntactic</strong>: not following recognizable syntax.

<strong>atony</strong>: without accent

<strong>attainable wish</strong>: a wish that refers to the future. In classical Greek, it was covered by the optative mood preceded by ei gar or eithe C & SH 102)

<strong>Attic reduplication</strong>: a kind of reduplication found in Greek verbs like the change from ago to egagon (to lead), in going from first to third principle part.

<strong>attraction of case: </strong>With relative pronouns in Greek, attraction of case means that the case of the antecedent is used instead of the case implied by usage within the clause; this happens especially if the clause function would have been accusative. Latin follows a similar pattern. (in a set with *attraction of gender)

<strong>attraction of</strong> <strong>gender: </strong>In Greek, attraction of gender means that the subject *relative pronoun takes the gender of the upcoming *predicate nominative rather than the *gender of the *antecedent.

(in a set with *attraction of case)

<strong>attributive adjective: </strong>an adjective positioned next to the noun it modifies instead of being on the other side of an expressed or implied *linking verb: “he is a tall man” <em>vs</em>. “that man is tall.”

In addition to adjectives, with personal possessive adjectives English makes a vocabulary distinction between *attributive and *predicate adjectives. <em>My, our, your, her, </em>and <em>their </em>stand immediately ahead of the noun they modify. <em>Mine, ours, yours, hers, theirs</em> stand only on the other side of linking verbs. “<em>My</em> plate is on the table”; but “the plate on the table is <em>mine</em>.” There is no predicate-position form for <em>its.</em>

In Greek there are *first attributive adjectives, which appear between article and noun, as well as *second attributive adjectives, which appear after the repeated article: article noun article adjective.

(in a set with *predicate adjective, *first attributive position, *second attributive position)

<strong>attributive noun</strong>: (in a set with *appelative noun)

<strong>attributive adjective</strong>

<strong>audiolingual</strong>: a type of language learning that is derived from the oral nature of language, hence, an emphasis on speaking and listening.

<strong>augment</strong>: In Greek, augment adjusted the front of roots in *secondary tenses of the *indicative mood as part of the sign for these tenses. (1) *Temporal augment applied to words beginning with vowels was basically a matter of lengthening the vowel: alpha to eta, epsilon to ei- or ai-, iota to long iota, omicron to omega, upsilon to long upsilon. Omega and eta, of course, could not lengthen further. Diphthongs followed the same pattern as single vowels: ai- eta w/ subscript, ei to eta w/ subscript, <em>etc.</em> New Testament verbs starting with eu- usually did not lengthen the eu- to eeu- in the secondary tenses. New Testament Greek often omitted the augment for pluperfects. Note: whereas temporal augment was a matter of lengthening; *contraction was a matter of combining, which also created a long vowel sound of some kind.

(2) *Syllabic augment added an epsilon to words starting with a consonant. Additionally, initial <em>rho</em> became <em>err</em>-, including the augment, that is, by doubling the <em>rho</em> before adding the <em>epislon</em>.

(in a set with *syllabic augment, *temporal augment, *tense formative, *tense sign)

<strong>auricular confession</strong>: in the Roman Catholic Church, the practice of confessing sins to a priest as an intercessor to God on the sinner’s behalf. It is a confession into the “ear” of the priest, especially since the confessional booth somewhat separates the confessor from the priest visually (Lat. <em>auris</em>).

(in a set with *priest, *prophet, *intercessor)

<strong>authentic</strong>: actually written by the one it claims to have been written by

<strong>autodafé</strong>: the public announcement of a sentence that was imposed by the Inquisition Webster

<strong>autograph</strong>: (1) a person’s signature, the name personally written; (2) an author’s first-hand ancient writing in contrast to subsequent copies of it made by others.

<strong>autography</strong>: writing something in a person’s own handwriting

<strong>auxiliary verb</strong>: also called *helping verb, one that accompanies the verb root in a verbal phrase: “<em>am</em> going,” “<em>are</em> going,” “<em>is</em> going,” “<em>has been</em> going,” “<em>have been</em> going,” “<em>had been</em> going,” “<em>will have been</em> going,” “<em>should</em> go,” “<em>should be</em> going,” “<em>were</em> going,” “<em>was</em> going,” “<em>will/shall</em> go,” “<em>will be</em> going,” “<em>would</em> go,” “<em>may</em> go,” “<em>might</em> go,” “<em>can</em> go,” “<em>could</em> go,” “<em>kept</em> going,” “used to go,” “tried to go,” Consequently, auxiliary verbs create phrase verbs, progressive verbs, and other *aspects in verbs. A feature of the English language that gives it greater precision of verbal expression than many languages have. The use of auxiliaries contrasts with using inflected forms on the verb itself.

<strong>axiom</strong>: an obviously true principle, especially a statement of such. (in a set with *adage, *apothegm, *proverb)

<strong>ayin</strong>: the sixteenth letter of the Hebrew alphabet: ע; transliterated ‘

<strong>backhand</strong>: handwriting that slants to the left, a characteristic more often associated with lefthanded writers.

<strong>backslash</strong> (in a set with *slash)

<strong>backronym</strong>: an acronym whose letters came first and then what they stood for was figured out later: JOY for Just Old Youth, or for Jesus first, Others second, Yourself last.

<strong>balanced construction</strong>: English uses a comma between short parallel phrases/clauses; a long-time principle of rhetoric that maintains balance in oral or written presentation. Greek used ho men . . . ho de to mark a set of evenly weighted clauses; likewise ou monon . . . alla kai. Latin has non solum . . . set etiam.

<strong>blessing</strong>: a statement of well-wishing. In newer versions, the Greek word translated “blessed” has been translated “happy” (<em>makarios</em>) as in the beatitudes (cp. Latin <em>beatus</em>; <em>beatitudo</em> ??) of Mt <strong><sup>5</sup></strong>:3-12 (cp. Lk <strong><sup>6</sup></strong>:2-23). Such a rendering loses the interpersonal element in the original Hebrew word picture (<em>baruch</em>), which stresses the action of one person upon other. <em>Happy</em> describes simply the condition of a person in a favorable condition, a condition that could issue from something besides a favorable attitude pronounced by one person pronounced on another.

The full *citation has three levels:<strong> 4</strong>:5:1, <em>i.e</em>., book, and paragraph. Individual documents within the Bible as a whole.

<strong>bookends</strong>: a pattern of presentation that uses the same imagery or statement at the end of the piece as at the beginning in order to give a sense of unity and completeness.

<strong>bork</strong>: to attack for political gain

<strong>bound construction</strong>: a set of words that cannot be rearranged, taken apart, or given inserts without interfering with its meaningfulness. Examples are prepositional phrases, article-noun relations, English adjective-noun connections.

<strong>bound form</strong>: an element that never stands alone. The Hebrew article and inseparable prepositions are a case in point as well as English suffixes like –<em>ness, -ity, -ism</em>, <em>etc.</em>

<strong>boustrophedon</strong>: a writing that runs the text alternately in both directions

<strong>brachiology</strong>: conciseness in speaking

<strong>break</strong>: what a voice does when it changes suddenly or slips into crying; the experience a young man has when his voice is changing

<strong>breathe</strong>: to speak in *voiceless exhalation

<strong>breathing marks</strong>: On all Greek words that begin with a vowel, one of two “breathing marks” is added to indicate proper pronunciation. The “smooth breathing” corresponds to the English <em>h</em> in <em>honor</em>; the “rough breathing” corresponds to the English <em>h</em> in <em>home</em>. As such, they represented respectively a glottal stop and a glottal *fricative. Therefore, all Greek words—like all Hebrew and English words—begin with a consonant. Additionally, all words beginning with <em>rho</em> have a rough breathing mark.

Breathing marks are written ahead of capitals, over the second member of a *proper diphthong (even if the first vowel is capitalized), before acute or grave accents, and under a circumflex. If an *improper diphthong happens to be capitalized (Hadees), the iota subscript comes up on the line after the capital but the breathing mark appears before the capitalized vowel (and also before any accompanying acute or grave accent and under a circumflex since accents also have to be put ahead of the capital). In this rather infrequent case, the iota subscript is treated as if it did not exist.

<strong>bulletin</strong>: a brief message such as would be placed on a bulletin board, an announcement. A general warning placed through police communication channels; an APB.

<strong>buzz word</strong>: a new expression that “catches on” as a way of saying something that comes up often in conversation. “Ask your doctor if xxx is ‘right for you.’” (in a set with *buzz word, *catch phrase, *colloquialism, *curse word, *formal term, *informal term, *obsolete *provincialism, *regionalism, *slang, *street talk, *vulgarism)

<strong>by-laws</strong>: an agreed-upon set of rules of operation by an organization. In a religious context, by-laws differ from a *creed by avoiding doctrinal matters—at least as much as possible—and by sticking purely to matters of procedure. (in a set with *statement of faith, *scripture, *creed)

<strong>by-word</strong>: a proverbial expression; an often-used word; an *epithet; an object of notoriety

<strong>Byzantine text</strong>: a late text form of the Greek New Testament; also called the *majority text (in a set with *majority text, *neutral text,*western text)

<strong>cacophony</strong>: harsh, dissonant words in a literary work

<strong>call letters</strong>: the capital letters used to designate a particular radio or television station. (in a set with *handle)

<strong>calligraphy</strong>: beautiful, ornate writing [<Gr kalos + grapho]

<strong>canon: </strong>the authoritative body of literature that serves as the measure of orthodoxy for a particular religion. In Christianity, the New Testament canon has traditionally been limited to (1) writings by apostles or those who served as extensions of the apostolic office—in contrast to those written later and later anonymous writings (apostolicity). (2) writings that were universally received—in contrast to those that appeared in one corner of the Mediterranean (catholicity), and (3) writings that harmonize with the previous two principles (orthodoxy).

(in a set with<strong> *</strong>deutero-canonical)
