<strong>cuneiform</strong>: having a small wedge-shaped characters as used for writing ancient Akkadian, Assyrian, Babylonian, Persian, or Sumerian texts.

<strong>curated document</strong>: one that has been heavily edited and revised in route to its present form

<strong>curse formula</strong>: a stylized expression that pronounces a curse on someone for doing something, as an inscription on a tomb that pronounces a curse on grave robbers for disturbing the remains of the one buried there. forbidden

<strong>curse word/curse/cuss</strong>: (in a set with *oath, *swear; *archaism, *buzz word, *catch phrase, *colloquialism, *informal term, *provincialism, *regionalism, *slang, *vulgarism)

<strong>cursive</strong>: a flowing form of writing that joins letters together in contrast to putting them down separately by printing; an easier form of writing that enables the scribe to keep the writing utensil on the page instead of picking it up after each letter entry

(in a set with *block lettering, *printing, *writing)

<strong>custom of usage: </strong>Two languages may share certain language features, but they do not necessarily use them in the same situations. An example may be Latin’s usage of the imperfect tense in different circumstances from where English does. English uses the simple past if there are accompanying adverbs to indicate linear action: always, usually, daily, <em>etc.</em> Latin’s omission of possessive adjectives in cases where possession is clear from other facts in the sentence. English very much wants to say his, their, her, our, <em>etc.</em> English uses a futuristic present in if-clauses of the future; Latin and Greek use the future tense in such situations. Greek has relative clauses, but they are not used as frequently as in English because Greek likes participles.

<strong>cut</strong>: a *slam, an *insult, a *slur, an overt negative evaluative comment to a person; differs from *innuendo and insinuation by being direct rather than implied and thus inferred.

<strong>dactyl</strong>: in poetry a metrical foot consisting of one accented syllable followed by two unaccented syllables, one long syllable followed by two short ones

<strong>dactylic hexameter</strong>: Wh 289

<strong>dactylology</strong>: the use of the fingers to communicate with the deaf using a *manual alphabet

(in a set with *signing, *sign language))

<strong>dagesh forte</strong>: a dot in the center non-guttural ???? Hebrew letters that indicates doubling.

<strong>dagesh lene</strong>: a dot put in the center of six Hebrew letters (<em>beth, gimel, dalet, kaph, phe, tav)</em> that shifts them from fricatives to corresponding voiceless stops. In modern pronunciation, <em>gimel</em> and <em>dalet</em> are always pronounced as stops regardless of the written form.

<strong>daily</strong>: n. short for daily *newspaper, one that comes every day, every day but one, or every weekday but not on weekends

<strong>dalet/daleth</strong>: the fourth letter of the Hebrew alphabet: ד

<strong>dangling participle</strong>: an introductory participle that does not modify the subject of the main clause: “Looking at the rest of the schedule, there are four home games left and two away.” A similar lack of proper connection exists when a preposition-gerund combination begins the sentence: “By driving the nail into a stud, it was strong enough to hold the heavy picture.” The actor in the *gerund should also be the actor in the main verb: “By driving the nail into a stud, I kept the heavy picture from pulling it out.”

<strong>dative absolute</strong>: one possible use of the dative case for an absolute construction in the New Testament is  ________.

<strong>dative case</strong>: Dative case most basically indicates relationship. In that role there is *dative of possession, *indirect object, dative case after certain verbs implying relationship or attitude (Gr & Lat), dative nouns with certain verbs implying relationship. There is also *dative of degree of difference, *dative of reference, *dative of separation (Lat).

In Greek, the dative case appears after certain kinds of prepositions, especially those that imply location (note *locative case). The case also appears after certain Greek and Latin verbs that imply relationship.

Latin has appropinquo, to draw near to/approach near; credo, to believe; ignosco, to pardon/forgive; impero, to command; invideo, “to envy”; nosceo, to harm; nubo, to marry; parco, to spare, pareo, to obey; persuadeo, to persuade; placeo, to please; servio, to serve; studeo, to study; as well as compounds that may govern the dative: adsum, to support; obsequor, to obey; praesto, to surpass.

<strong>dative of agent</strong>: the case used on a noun for agency with gerundive (fut. passive ptc.)

<strong>dative of degree of difference</strong>: “He was older by six years.” Greek puts “six years” in the dative case with no accompanying preposition. (Latin follows the same pattern, using the ablative case.) Degree of difference calls for no special construction in English. Instead it is covered by an adverb-adjective combination or by using by plus the measurement: “He was six years older”; “older by six years.” (in a set with *degree of difference, *ablative of degree of difference)

<strong>dative of interest</strong>: same as *dative of reference WH326

<strong>dative of possession</strong>: appears in linking-verb Greek and Latin sentences to indicate the “owner”: “There is a lid to the box.” (wh 349#6) Acts<strong> 4</strong>:32 says, “No one was saying that anything of-the-things being to-him was his-own.”

<strong>dative of purpose</strong>: Latin uses dative on nouns that can indicate purpose: WH 53: Vitae discimus (“We learn for life”). WH330

<strong>dative of reference</strong>/<strong>interest</strong>:  (Wheelock p. 270). In Greek an expression like “Ananias by” (Ananias onamati, Acts <strong><sup>5</sup></strong>:1).)

<strong>dative of relationship</strong>: 	Dative case appears on nouns connected to adjectives that imply the manner of relationship introduced by English “to”: “He was hostile to his father [father = Greek and Latin dative case with no accompanying preposition].” The dative case on nouns is used also in place of accusative in Latin and Greek verbs that imply relationship: to follow (Gr), trust (L), persuade (L), serve (L), <em>etc.</em> More specifically the usage is dative or personal relationship.

<strong>dative of separation: </strong> Wh 307#13 306#23 242 Latin

<strong>Decalog(ue), The</strong>: a designation for the ten commandments found in Jewish law (Ex <strong><sup>20</sup></strong>:1-17; Deut <strong><sup>6</sup></strong>:6-21). [Gr deca + logos]

<strong>decipher</strong>: to figure out a *cipher; to determine the meaning of a message written in code, to figure out what is unclear, as with someone else’s sloppy handwriting, an enigmatic *notation, or partially destroyed *inscription

<strong>declamation: </strong>the delivery of a memorized piece written by another; an vehement presentation marked by strong feeling; a tirade

(in a set with *oration, *speech, *reading)

<strong>declaration</strong>: a formal announcement delivered in writing or orally; a statement of goods taken along when entering another country

<strong>declarative sentence</strong>: a sentence that is simply stating something in contrast to asking, exclaiming, or commanding it; hence, it is punctuated with a period and does not use the *imperative mood: “He is going home.”  (in a set with *exclamatory sentence, * interrogative sentence, *imperative sentence)

<strong>declension</strong>: n. (1) the type of *inflection used on nouns and adjectives  (2) a set of endings for specific nouns and adjectives that indicated number, gender, and case

English does not decline its nouns except for singular and plural and for “possessive,” but English pronouns do have different forms for subjective, objective, and possessive usages.


Greek has three main declensions with six subdivisions in the first declension, two subdivisions in the second, and several subdivisions in the third. Latin has five declensions with three subdivisions in the second (populus, magister/liber, concilium); five subdivisions in the third; and two subdivisions in the fourth. In general, Hebrew does not decline its nouns and adjectives for case. It does decline for number (sg/pl) and gender (mas/fem).

There is a general correlation between declensions and grammatical gender. Greek first-declension nouns are feminine in four subsets (-<em>ee, -ā, a/a</em>) and masculine in the last two subsets (-<em>ees, -ās</em>). Second declension noun in –os are mostly masculine (except for  ) and –on nouns are all neuter. The third declension, also called the consonant declension, has nouns that are masculine and feminine sharing declension patterns and neuter nouns that use a set with the same genitive and dative forms as the masculines and feminines.


First-declension nouns in Latin are feminine except for <em>agricola</em> (farmer), <em>auriga</em> (charioteer), <em>conviva</em> (dinner-guest), <em>incola</em> (inhabitant), <em>nauta</em> (sailor), <em>pirata</em> (pirate), <em>poeta</em> (poet). Second-declension nouns in –<em>us</em> are masculine (except for <em>abyssus,</em> feminine) or neuter words in –<em>um</em>. Third declension uses the same ending set for masculine and feminine, and has neuters that are like them except in the nominative/vocative and accusative. Fourth declension has mostly masculines together with some feminines that use the same ending set; there are a few neuters that are like the first subdivision except for nominative/vocative and accusative. Fifth declension nouns are all feminine except for the word <em>dies</em> (day).

(in a set with *case [nouns/adjectives], *conjugation [verbs]; *declinable nouns, *indeclinable nouns, *inflection [n/adj, v]; *dictionary form)

<strong>declinable nouns</strong>: Most nouns have a set of case forms in Greek and Latin, but a few have only one form: nihil, satis (L) (in a set with *indeclinable nouns or *indeclinable adjectives)

<strong>decrypt/decryption</strong>: to *decode, *decipher a *cryptogram. To change from a special code to an understandable message; to figure out the meaning of what is obscure or unreadable

<strong>deed</strong>: n. a statement of ownership particularly landed property????? (in a set with *title, *writ)

<strong>defective spelling</strong>: the shorter form of a Hebrew word that does not have a doubled last consonant with inserted vowel letter between the duplicates (in a set with *plene spelling)

<strong>defective verb</strong>: a verb that does not have all its principal parts or lacks some forms within a conjugation set. The Latin inquit appears mostly in third singular present and less frequently in first or second singular, but does not have a full conjugation; also <em>memini</em>,<em> meminisse</em>; (in a set with *composite verb, *deponent verb, *regular verb, *suppletive verb)

<strong>definiendum</strong>: the word or expression being defined (Lat neut gerundive < <em>definire</em>)

(in a set with *definiens)

<strong>definiens</strong>: the word(s) used to define another word (Lat nom/mas/sg/pres/act/ptc < <em>definire</em>)

<strong>definite article</strong>: In English, <em>the</em> is the definite article; it is pronounced “thee” before vowel sounds and “thuh” before consonant sounds. The pattern is an oral one; consequently, the variant pronunciation of <em>the</em> is relative to sound, not writing. For example, people say <em>thuh</em> before <em>eu-/u</em> words that have a <em>y</em> sound pronounced on the front: <em>thu</em> eulogy<em>, the euphemism/eulogy/union, etc., </em><strong>(insert panel</strong>: <em>eucane, eucakyptol, eucalyptus, eucharist, euchire, euchromatin, eudemonism, eugenic, eugenol, euglena, euhermerism, eukaryote, eunuch, eunonymous, eupatrid, eupepsia, euphonium, euphony, euphorbia, euporia, euphotic, euphuism, euplastic, euphold, eupnea, eureka, euripus, europium, eurybathic, euryhaline, euryterid, eurythermal, eurythmics, eurythmy, eurytopic, eustachian tube, eustasy, eustele, eutectic, euthanasia, euthenics, eutherian, eutrophic; upsilon, user</em><strong>).</strong>

<em>Thuh</em> occurs before a few <em>o-</em> words with a <em>w</em> sound on the front. People say, “He is not <em>thuh</em> one”; “she gave it <em>thuh </em>once-over.”

On the other hand, people say <em>thee honor </em>because the <em>h</em> represents a glottal stop (as it does in <em>heir, honest,</em> <em>hour, </em>and <em>humble </em>for some speakers). (All other h words have <em>thuh</em>: <em>hassel, hearth, hit, hook, hut, hydrant</em>.) The <em>h </em>on the special words has the same value as the *glottal stop that is actually on the front of all English words written with a first vowel; they really begin with a glottal stop, the sound represented by the <em>h</em> of <em>honor, etc.</em> There is just no written symbol used. Thus, <em>apple</em> is (with the <em>h</em> of <em>honor</em>) really <em>happle</em>, <em>egg</em> is <em>hegg</em>, <em>it</em> is <em>hit, owe </em>is <em>howe, under </em>is<em> hunder</em>; all <em>eu-u-</em> words, though, that pronounce a <em>y</em> on the front replace the <em>h</em> glottal stop with that <em>y</em>. (All English as well as Greek, Latin, and Hebrew, words begin with a consonant in point of sound.)

One refinement in English definite article use is whether two definite nouns have an article with each one or one article ahead of both. The difference is that two articles enter the nouns separately into the sentence and give them equal attention. One article ahead of both enters them into the sentence as a combination.

Latin has no articles at all, but <em>ille</em> sometimes approaches the value of a definite article, especially in identifications like “Magna Ille Alexander,” Alexander the Great.

Greek’s articles do not distinguish between definite and indefinite, but between quality and identity. English article usage calls for definiteness in many places where Greek calls for identity; so, <em>the</em> frequently translates <em>ho/hee/to</em>. But, strictly speaking, the two ideas are not the same. English also can use the *indefinite article to indicate quality, especially with a *noun typical of its class; so the Greek anarthrous noun can often be translated with a/an. The main thing to remember in translating Greek articles is that <em>the </em>does not necessarily equal the Greek article and <em>a/an</em> does not necessarily equal the Greek anarthrous usage. Ex. Jn <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1 does not mean, “In a beginning was the word and the word was with the God and the word was a God.”

(in a set with *indefinite article)

<strong>degrees of comparison</strong>: an expression that applies to adjectives and adverbs in English, Latin, and Greek. The first degree is called the *positive degree, the second degree is the *comparative degree, the third degree is called *superlative degree. Adjective comparison:<em> quick/quick</em><em><u>er</u></em><em>/quick</em><em><u>est</u></em> or <em>dignified/</em><em><u>more</u></em><em> dignified/</em><em><u>most</u></em><em> dignified</em>; adverb comparison: <em>quickly, </em><em><u>more</u></em><em> quickly, </em><em><u>most</u></em><em> quickly.	</em>

Hebrew does not have distinctive forms for comparative adjectives and adverbs. Instead, the idiom is as follows: “<em>First noun of the comparison</em> is <em>adjective</em> + <em>from</em> (<em>min</em>) second noun of the comparison: “He is tall from his brother.” (in a set with *positive, *comparative, *superlative)

<strong>degree</strong> <strong>of difference</strong>: the amount of difference between two things in regard to time, space, weight, <em>etc</em>., most of the time involving a *comparative degree adjective or adverb. English covers the idea by putting the measurement ahead of the adjective as a kind of *adverbial noun (“six inches taller”; “four minutes faster”; two ounces lighter”) or by using by plus the measurement (“taller by six inches”; “faster by four minutes”; “lighter by two ounces”).

Greek puts the measure in the dative case ().

Latin puts the measurement in the ablative case (). (in a set with *ablative of degree of difference, *dative of degree of difference)

Hebrew has no comparative- or superlative-degree adjectives. Consequently, it has no construction for degree of difference and must rely on some kind of *circumlocution to express the idea.

<strong>deictic</strong>: pointing out directly, demonstrating

<strong><u>dele</u></strong>: remove from the text in contrast to *stet, “let it stand.”

<strong>deliberative questions</strong>: questions that imply uncertainty: “What are we going to do?”

<strong>deliberative subjunctive</strong>: the use of the subjunctive mood in Latin and Greek for questions that imply uncertainty. Same as *dubitative subjunctive. (in a set with *deliberative question)

<strong>delta</strong>: fourth letter of the Greek alphabet: δ/Δ

<strong>demonstratives</strong>: pronouns or adjectives that point out

<strong>demonstrative adjective</strong>: an adjective that points out: this book, that car. In English, Latin, and Greek, demonstrative adjectives are the same as demonstrative pronouns, the differences being whether there is an accompanying noun.

The Greek demonstrative adjectives are <em>houtos</em> (this), <em>ode</em> (this), <em>ekeinos</em> (that). Demonstrative adjectives in Greek always take predicate position, i.e., not between article and noun but before article or immediately after noun.

Latin’s demonstrative adjectives are <em>hic/haec/hoc</em> (this), <em>iste/ista/istud</em> (that as of second person), <em>ille/illa/illud</em> (that as of third person), <em>is/ea/</em>id (that), and <em>idem/iadem/idem</em> (the same). They all are capable also of standing alone as *demonstrative pronouns (or substantive adjective, if a person prefers so to call them).

<strong>demonstrative pronouns</strong>: pronouns that point out: “this” and “that.” The English demonstratives this/these are near demonstratives and that/those are distant demonstratives.

In contrast to English’s two demonstratives, Latin has three, each one corresponding to the persons in verbs: hic/haec/hoc for this near me/us, ille/illa/illud for that distant to both speaker and hearer, and iste/ista/istud for that associated with the hearer/s. In addition, Latin has two other demonstratives that cover third-person references. <em>Is/ea/id</em> is a less emphatic demonstrative than ille. Is/ea/id can stand alone as a third-person pronoun parallel to <em>ego/nos</em> and <em>tu/vos</em> (you), and it can modify nouns as a demonstrative adjective. The suffix –dem can be added to <em>is/ea/id</em> to make a word that means the same (one/thing). Some adjustments in spelling are involved for ease of pronunciation. Final <em>m</em> on <em>is/ea/id</em> shifts to <em>n</em>, a dental nasal before a dental stop (<em>eu</em><em><u>n</u></em><em>dem</em>, <em>etc.</em>). The theoretical nominative masculine singular <em>i</em><em><u>s</u></em><em>dem</em> becomes <em>īdem</em>, losing sibilant <em>s</em> before a dental and showing *compensatory lengthening on the <em>i</em>.

It may be a matter of taste whether lone demonstratives are called pronouns or substantive adjectives. The difference is whether they are thought of as being primarily alone and then used as adjectives or vice versa.

(in a set with *demonstrative adjective; *near demonstrative, *distant demonstrative, *aforementioned demonstrative, *to-be-said demonstrative pronoun)

<strong>demotic</strong>: an offshoot of *hieroglyphics, a simplified form of hieroglyphics, as something more usable by the common person; also modern colloquial Greek (Gr <em>deemos>deemotees> deemotikos, </em>people)

(in a set with *hieratic, *hieroglyphics)

<strong>denominative</strong>: formed from a noun as <em>muscular</em> from <em>muscle </em>(n > adj) or <em>memorize</em> from <em>memory</em> (n. > verb).

<strong>denotation/denote</strong>: the straightforward, factual meaning of a term; perhaps its derivational meaning or word picture; the original meaning from which specializations may have developed

(in a set with *connotation)

<strong>dentals</strong>: sounds that are made with the teeth: <em>t, d, th, dh, n</em>: (in a set with *labials, *labio-dental, *velars/*palatals, *liquids, and *glottals)

<strong>dependent: </strong>an element that can stand on its own to express a complete thought; some grammatical feature that does not need another element in order to stand. (in a set with *independent)

<strong>dependent clause</strong>: Also called *subordinate clause. A dependent clause is a one that does not stand by itself because it is not perceived to express a complete thought.

<strong>dependent verb</strong>: the verb in a *dependent clause of a *complex sentence; a verb whose clause cannot stand by itself.

(n a set with independent verb; *leading verb, *main verb)

<strong>deponent verb</strong>: a Greek or Latin verb that lacks an active voice form. Consequently, its (*middle voice or) *passive voice form is translated into English as active voice. English does not have deponent verbs


Latin deponents include <em>adhortor, adloquor, adsequor/assequor, aggredior, apricor, deponents include admiror, arbitror, colloquor, commoror, complector, confiteor, conor, consector, consequor, convivor, egredior, emorior, experior, fateor, for, fruor, hortor, iaculor, imitor, infitior, ingredior, intueor, loquor, meditor, mentior, mereor, miror, moderor, molior, morior, nascor, nitor, obsequor, opinor, osculor, patior, peregrinor, persequor, piscor, polliceor, precor, proficiscor, profiteor, profor, queror, regredior, rusticor, sector, sequor, suspicor, testor, tueor, utor, vagor, vereor.</em>

Deponents in Greek and Latin are not necessarily deponent in all parts. Some are active or middle depending on the principal part: <em>erchomai-eleusomai-eelthon, gnoosko-gnoosomai, isthioo-phagoo, kaioo, lambanoo-leepsomain, piptoo-pesoumain, </em>

(in a set with *first deponent, *second deponent, *semi-deponent)

(in a set with *composite verb, *defective verb, *regular verb, *suppletive verb)

<strong>derivation</strong>: another term for *etymology, the descent that a present term reflects. Derivation can explain the difference in pronunciation between spellings that look just alike. <em>Mētro-</em> and mētro- have long e and short e respectively because the former comes from a Greek word with eta (<em>mētra</em>, uterus) and the latter comes from another Greek word with short e (<em>metron</em>). Similarly, ped- is pronounced with long e in words having to do with child (<em>pēdiatrician</em>) because it comes from the Greek word for child (<em>paid</em>-) , whose ai over time went from ai, to ae, to ē. <em>Pĕd- </em>comes from the Latin root ped-, which has a short e (pedal).

Derivation also explains why some odd consonant combinations appear in words that are no longer pronounced with all those sounds in them, especially words that have weird consonant combinations on the front: pt-, pn-, kt-, thch-, -thph-, rrh /rh, gn-, gh, w(rung).

Derivation maintains spelling patterns from feeder languages in the receiver language. <em>Abdomen</em> changes the e to i when additions occur (cp. nomen <nominis, <em>etc.</em>). <em>Dexter </em>(right) drops the last e and becomes <em>dextrad</em> when suffixes are added. Two consonants normally indicate a short previous vowel because that is the way Latin spelling worked.

Derivation explains why words that do not look like they are related are nevertheless related. <em>Agent</em> (leader) and <em>actor</em> (doer) are related in that they come from different principal parts of the same Latin verb: ago, agere, egi, actus.

Derivation explains pronunciation patterns that create “silent letters.” <em>S</em><em><u>c</u></em><em>ience</em> and related words have lost the k sound that was in the Latin verb <em>scio</em>. English has developed a pattern of pronunciation that makes the letter c say s ahead of e, i, or y. So <em>resus</em><em><u>c</u></em><em>itation</em>, <em>science</em>, and the like end up losing the k sound altogether; two esses sound the same as one ess. For additional examples, see under *silent letters and *spelling.

Derivation lies behind the less usual ways of forming English plurals. –on becomes –a (protozoon > protozoa, Gr.), -um becomes -a (datum > data, L. & Gr.), -is becomes –ēs (crisis > crises, L. & Gr.), -us becomes –i (alumnus > alumni, L.), -a becomes –ae (amoeba > amoebae, L.), -ma becomes –-mata (stigma > stigmata, Gr.), -ex becomes –ices (cortex > cortices), -ix becomes -ices (appendix > appendices), -ax becomes –aces (thorax > thoraces, Gr.), -nx becomes ngēs (menix > meninges; phalanx > phalanges, Gr.), # becomes –en (ox > oxen).

Derivation shows what letters of a root surprisingly may be kept when additions are made to the word. The general rule is that is the vowel on the end of a root was part of the root—and therefore invariant, that vowel stays when suffixes are added or compounds are made: tachyphagia, tracheotomy, audiology,

All Greek prepositions ending in a vowel—with the exception of <em>amphi</em>, <em>peri</em>, and <em>pro</em>—threw away that vowel when prefixed to a root beginning with a vowel. That same pattern—with exceptions—obtains in English compounds involving those prepositions.

<strong>derivative</strong>:  a word descending from an earlier word in another language; a *paronym. Words taken from Latin and Greek often lose their original n/g/c ending: victori-a < victory;    Sometimes they are brought over without change from the dictionary form:

<strong>descriptive clause</strong>: alternate term for *nonrestrictive clause

<strong>descriptive genitive</strong>: the use of the Latin or Greek genitive case to tie a descriptive noun to another noun—corresponding to an “of” phrase in English: “a man of honor,” meaning “an honorable man.” WH333#16

<strong>descriptive grammar</strong> a grammar that simply shows how a language works without attempting to set standards for its usage (in a set with *prescriptive grammar)

<strong>descriptive linguistics</strong>: the complete analysis of a language at a particular stage of its development in contrast to *historical linguistics and *comparative linguistics. Historical linguistics looks at the preceding (and succeeding aspects) of a language’s development as a way of elucidating features of the language. Comparative linguistics draws correlations between the target language and related contemporary languages.

(in a set with *comparative linguistics, *historical linguistics)

<strong>descriptor</strong>: a term or phrase that describes, or modifies.

<strong>determiners</strong>:  words that limit or restrict the number of those in a class: *definite articles, *demonstratives, *possessive adjectives, and words like <em>any</em>, <em>all</em>, <em>both</em> ahead of the nouns they delimit. Almost always, English omits <em>the</em> when other determiners accompany the noun. Greek keeps the article with demonstratives (<em>houtos, hode, ekeivos</em>), always putting them in *predicate position.

<strong>deutero-canonical</strong>: having a secondary place of honor in the authoritative literature of a religion. In Christianity there are some books of the New Testament itself that some scholars consider *deutero-canonical because they have decided on literary, historical, or doctrinal grounds that they are not like what they consider the “received” literature within the canon.

(in a set with *canonical)

<strong>deutero-Pauline</strong>: secondarily Pauline; i.e., written by someone besides Paul, but following the sentiments of the apostle. They may be said to be “in the Pauline school” or in the Pauline tradition.

<strong>deverbative</strong>: a word formed from a verb in the same language rather than in terms of its ancestral origins: <em>runner</em>, commandment, adoration, carriage. (in a set with *denominative)

<strong>devotion</strong>: a short, light presentation intended to encourage the hearers’ “devotion” to God and to spiritual things

<strong>diacritical mark</strong>: a mark used within a word to mark certain phonetic features, in contrast to *punctuation marks to show relationship between words. As such, the category includes the accent mark (fiancé), diëresis (the double dot [¨]), an umlaut (¨), the *cidella (as under the <em>c</em> of <em>façade</em>), the grave accent (à al mode), or the *circumflex (bête noire).

<strong>diagonal</strong>: another term for <em>slash</em>. a line rising to the right (/) used between entries for different parallel realities:  “I/We must take care of the problem.” Similar in value to <em>and/or</em>. The idea contrasts from appositional <em>or</em>, which gives alternate names for the same thing; it means “another way of saying,” “to put it another way.”

<strong>	example </strong>(in a set with *backslash)

<strong>diagramming</strong>: a technique of analyzing sentence structure so as to picture the relationship of subjects to verbs to direct objects to indirect objects, the relationship of subordinate parts to independent parts, the relationship of modifiers to phrase heads, the relationship of adverbial clauses to main verbs. The process requires the student to determine consciously these kinds of syntactical features. It facilitates the analytical method of interpretation. It does not so much teach the things involved as require concentration on them. After all, a person has to know the relationships to choose how to picture them. <strong>Append a diagram of Hebrews 1:1-4</strong>

<strong>dialect</strong>: an adaptation of a general language in a certain locale or in a certain community of speakers. The kinds of things that occur are things like in African-American English, where there is a tendency to drop the third singular final “s” on present tense indicative active verbs, not pronouncing final consonants, developing new meanings for words, and new words for things.

<strong>dialog/dialogue</strong>: a discussion back and forth between two or more people about some issue; contrasts with *monologue. (in a set with *monologue, *soliloquy)

<strong>Diatessaron</strong>: an early Christian work that combined the four gospels into one narrative of the life of Jesus Christ. Any work of that nature.

<strong>diatribe</strong>: an abusive denunciation of someone or something; a *rant

<strong>dictaphone</strong>: the trade name for an instrument that records *dictation that can later be retrieved in manageable segments by a typist or stenographer.

<strong>dictate</strong>: to word out a communication for someone who writes down what is said.

<strong>dictation</strong>: a wording out of a message to another person who is responsible for taking it down and putting it into a form that can be sent to the addressee.

<strong>dictation theory of inspiration</strong>: a view of divine-human co-operation in the production of scripture that pictures the writer as taking down God’s words *verbatim. The idea surely caricatures the belief that scripture <em>is</em> the word of God in contrast to <em>containing</em> the word of God or <em>becoming</em> the word of God. The actual view of verbal inspiration is that the wording freely chosen by the original human author is protected from error. The Ten Commandments written by the finger of God might indeed qualify as an example of dictation theory.

(in a set with *dynamic inspiration, *infallibility, *plenary inspiration, *verbal inspiration; *inerrancy)

<strong>diction</strong>:  the choice of words in speaking; distinctness, or clarity, of pronunciation in speaking or singing. It deals with avoiding the choice of words with the wrong connotation for the circumstance, with the wrong word out of a set that are similar—like effect/affect. (in a set with *elocution, *enunciation, *pronunciation, *saying, *slur)

<strong>dictionary</strong>: an alphabetical listing of words in a language (in a set with *lexicon)

<strong>dictionary form</strong>: the customary form of a word’s entry into a lexicon, dictionary, encyclopedia, or glossary, which is organized by alphabetical sequence.

With Latin and Greek verbs, the dictionary form is first person, singular, present, indicative active. For English verbs, the entry is the infinitive form (without <em>to</em>). For Hebrew and Aramaic verbs, it is the third person masculine singular of the Qal stem. Lexicographers list

Greek contract verbs in their uncontracted form so the reader will know for sure what kind of contract verb it is. Impersonal verbs, as they are called, appear in their third-person singular form, because they are “impersonal” in that infinitives are their subjects. That infinitive subject gets covered over by typically translating the construction with the expletive <em>it</em>: “<em>It </em>is necessary <em>to obey</em> in everything”: note ἀδυνατεῖ, ἀνήκει, δεῖ, διαφέρει, δοκέω, ἔξιστιν, ἐπιτρέπει, ἔστιν, καθῆκεν, μέλει, πρέπει, συμφέρει in Greek.

For English, Greek, and Latin nouns it is the nominative singular—followed by the genitive singular. These two case forms are always enough to show which declension they are in.

For Greek and Latin adjectives it is the nominative masculine singular form, followed in order by feminine and neuter forms (or perhaps only the variable terminations for those genders). Dictionaries for Greek, Latin, and English may also give the forms for the comparative and superlative degrees of an adjective, sometimes separately, sometimes put along with the positive degree of that word. In Greek, some adjectives have one form for masculine and feminine and another for neuter: such as alpha-privative adjectives with first-second declension endings; third-declension adjectives

Adverbs, interjections, prepositions usually one form. In cases where a preposition, e.g., has a different form before vowels, its standardized form is entered alphabetically.

The dictionary form is basically the simplest form of the word.

<strong>dictionary of slang</strong>: a resource that organizes together the informal terms and expressions in every-day use that are not normally included in a regular dictionary, as in a <em>Dictionary of American Slang</em>.

<strong>didactic literature</strong>: content intended to teach, particularly religious teaching found in the New Testament.

<strong>dieresis</strong>: two side-by-side dots placed above a second vowel that immediately follows another vowel (¨) to show that the two vowels are pronounced separately instead of together as in a diphthong or vowel glide: coöperate, gastroïntestinal, naïve, psychoanalysis. In familiar words like “cooperate,” the dieresis is usually omitted.

Greek examples include the word periïsteemi,

The same symbol is used in phonetic spelling to indicate the “ah” sound for an <em>a</em> (ä).

The symbol is also used as an umlaut (¨) in spelling German words with *rounded vowels. An alternate way of indicating this feature of pronunciation is to put an <em>e</em> after the other vowel.

(in a set with *punctuation marks)

<strong>digamma</strong>: same as *stigma, the Greek symbol for 6.

<strong>digest</strong>: a shortened and perhaps simplified form of a longer writing. It is a process that can involve removing of sections not as essential the presentation without altering the remaining material.

<strong>diglossia</strong>: a situation where two levels of a language are used for different functions in a society: high and low German ???, formal and familiar French, standard and informal/conversational English

<strong>diglot</strong>: a book with more than one version, or language, of a text often in parallel columns [Gr] (in a set with *polyglot):

<strong>digraph</strong>: two letters for one consonant sound: ch, (dh), ph, gh, rh, sh, th, zh. The term could be used to include writing the same consonant twice without any difference from writing it once: hiss, buzz. (Often using two of the same consonants together is done to indicate a short vowel ahead of the double letter. Two letters for one vowel sound is usually called a *diphthong in English. Note: in linguistic notation, placing an <em>h</em> after another consonant often indicates a *fricative. (in a set with *double consonant, *vowel glide, *spelling)

<strong>digression</strong>: a leaving of the subject at hand to discuss a perhaps related matter but in such a way as to focus on it for its own sake. Then the speaker returns to the original subject.

<strong>diminutive form</strong>: a form that means smaller than what the original term meant. Latin added an –l- + case endings to a previous root for this purpose: ocellus for oculus (L) wh 306, misellus for miser, the <em>r</em> being assimilated Wh 306, anulus < anus “ring” wh 218

Greek has iota-diminutives: <em>amnos/amnion</em> (<em>lamb</em>),

In English –ette often has that value: major, majorette (in a set with *iota-diminutive) WH306#6 333#13 322 #7#12

<strong>diphthong</strong>: In English spelling, the term covers two kinds of things: (1) two vowel letters that form one sound (writing) and (2) two vowels that cover two sounds where there is a glide from one vowel to another (sounding). The former is like the vowel letters in <em>feat</em>, <strong>insert panel </strong><em>feet, foot, taunt, bought, goal, gr</em>ie<em>ve, groove. suit. </em>The latter is like the vowel glide in <em>real. </em><strong><em>Insert </em></strong>

<strong><em>panel</em></strong><em>, bail, boil, bout, </em>Sometimes a diphthong in point of sound is represented by only one letter in point of writing: <em>I, butte, made. </em>

In Greek, the written diphthongs represent vowel glides to high front (i) and high back (<em>u</em>). Consequently, all its diphthongs end in iota or upsilon, and the list of diphthongs can be generated simply by starting at each of the other vowels and gliding to iota or to upsilon.

(in a set with *digraph, *vowel glide, *monophthong; see also **pronunciation)

<strong>disputation</strong>: (1) argument about whether something is true; (2) a formal, perhaps learned discussion in which the two sides give organized presentations for the view being defended; a debate

<strong>disquisition</strong>: a formal discourse on a subject of importance

<strong>disuade</strong>: opposite of <em>persuade</em>; to discourage someone from a certain course of action

<strong>distych</strong>: an ancient hinged writing tablet with two panels

<strong>direct discourse</strong>: a quotation that gives the purported words of the original speaker. In Greek of the New Testament the conjunction hoti appeared before the quoted material even though in classical Greek hoti was used to introduce *indirect discourse.

<strong>direct object</strong>: answers the “what” question after a verb: “The bat hit [what?],” <em>i.e</em>., “The bat hit [the ball].” Direct objects are a kind of *verbal adjunct that appear after action verbs whether finite, infinitive, participial, or gerund. Hebrew uses –eth as a direct object sign. Latin and Greek use the accusative case to do so. English has certain placements for direct objects to indicate that role int he sentence.

<strong>direct question</strong>: a question that purports to quote the very words *verbatim that a questioner has used. Contrasts with *indirect question, which reproduces only the idea in the original quotation.

(in a set with *question; * informational question)

<strong>directory</strong>: an alphabetical listing of individuals, companies, <em>etc.</em>, compiled along with relevant information for each one.

<strong>discourse</strong>: an oral presentation in public that includes *speeches and *addresses as well as *sermons, which take place in religious settings. In the New Testament, the gospels and Acts provide narratives with a significant number of discourse interludes, which can be loosely classified as sermons, speeches, or informal addresses, depending on preference in terms.

From a grammatical standpoint, discourse pertains to a construction that deals with another person’s words. There is *direct discourse, which gives the purported words of the speaker, and indirect discourse, which gives idea the speaker expressed.

In Latin the verb idea in indirect discourse is covered by an infinitive.

In English it is covered by a finite verb.

In Greek it was usually covered by a finite verb in koine while classical Greek could use finite verbs, infinitives, or participles, depending on the leading verb. Indirect discourse occurs after verbs of speaking, mental action, or the senses: say/retort/exclaim/indicate/show that; think/suppose/believe/imagine that; feel/feel/sense/see that.

In Greek, “indirect discourse” labels what can cover indirect statement as well as indirect question because the same formula applies to both. In Latin, however, indirect statement covers the verb with an infinitive, but in indirect question is covered by the subjunctive with the clause introduced by an interrogative word or <em>–ne</em> added to the first word of the clause.

(in a set with *sermon, *speech, *address<strong>)</strong>

<strong>disjunctive accent: Hebrew disjunctive</strong> accents space, separate, or show slight pauses between words. Such accents contrast with *conjunctive accents, which indicate continuation and unity (phrasing) between words. (in a set with *conjunctive accent)

<strong>dissertation</strong>: a formal writing that closely examines some topic; such a writing that results from original research and is usually required for earning a doctoral degree.

<strong>distant demonstrative</strong>: a demonstrative that points to something/someone thought of as distant from the speaker: “that/those.” Latin had two, what could be called, distant demonstratives, one associated with the hearer (<em>iste/ista/istud</em>) and another for distant to both speaker and hearer (<em>ille/illa/illud</em>). (in a set with *near demonstrative)

<strong>distant future</strong>: a verb form that indicates action to take place after a good while.

(in a set with *near future, or *immediate future; *temporary future, *permanent future)

<strong>distant past</strong>: a verb construction that indicates action that occurred a good while ago.

(in a set with *near future, or *immediate past)

<strong>distinctive: </strong>related in sound yet signifying difference in meaning: hi<em>t</em> <em> vs.</em> hi<em>d</em> (vocalization); <em>d</em>ine <em>vs</em>. <em>n</em>ine (nazalization); <em>p</em>at <em>vs</em>. <em>f</em>at (fricativization)

<strong>distribution</strong>: placement in a sentence; one of the factors in defining *parts of speech.

(in a set with *inflection for defining “parts of speech”, alternative to *case form)

<strong>distributive</strong>: in contrast to collective. Considering the members of a set individually: <em>each/every</em> vs <em>all</em> as in “<em>every</em> good boy does fine”; “<em>each</em> good boy does fine”; or <em> vs.</em> “<em>all</em> good boys do fine.” <em>Every </em>is distributive singular in that it looks at the members of a set one by one; so <em>every</em> has *concord with a singular verb.

Greek and Latin use the same word for distributive and collective, the singular for distributive and the plural for collective: <em>pas/pantes</em> (Gr); <em>omnis/omnes</em> (Lat). Greek also has <em>hekastos</em> (“each”) for distributive. All three words can be pronouns or adjectives.

<strong>disyllable/disyllabic</strong>: a word having two syllables

<strong>disyllabic enclitic</strong>: a two-syllabic enclitic. Greek examples are <em>eisin, estin, esmen, eimi, este</em>? and all the non-interrogative forms of <em>tines</em> < <em>tis/ti</em>

<strong>dithyramb</strong>: a case of wildly enthusiastic speech-making. A Greek troupe’s frenzied hymn and dance used in the worship of Dionysius.

<strong>Ditto</strong>: (1) a double vertical mark above the line of writing to indicate repetition of the item directly above it in a list ("). On typewriters the ditto was the same key and shape as double quotation marks, which in turn were the same before and after a quotation. With the advent of computers and their extra possibilities and “smart quotes,” the ditto can be supplied from a specialty list.

(2) “<em>Ditto”/dittos</em>” has become a *short for “I agree with you.” Cp. “roger that.”

<strong>dittography</strong>: a case of writing something twice. In ancient manuscript copying, a word or set of words might have been put down twice where they originally appeared once (say, “verily, verily” instead of just “verily”). In today’s circumstance, it happens when a word like <em>the</em> is typed twice on a computer and a red saw-toothed line shows up under the second entry.

(in a set with *haplography, *homoioteleuton, *metathesis as types of *scribal errors)

<strong>ditty</strong>: a short, light-hearted, simple song or verse

<strong>doctrine/doctrinal</strong>: the general teaching on a important subject, especially religious or political. <em>A</em> <em>doctrine</em> refers to one specific belief within a whole “body of doctrine.” As a general reference, <em>doctrine</em> is something conceptual in contrast to *history, *exhortation, <em>etc.</em>

(in a set with *indoctrinate, *didactic literature)

<strong>document</strong>: a written piece serving as a formal source of information

<strong>documentary</strong>: a presentation that reviews an important even or major issue

<strong>dogma</strong>: an authoritative principle dealing with morality or belief associated with a particular religious community or system of thought

<strong>dot</strong>: the term for a *period when it is used in an e-mail address or web site

<strong>double accusative</strong>: Some a verbs can carry two objects, one of the person and one of the thing

<strong>double consonant</strong>: a single letter for two sounds at once the English <em>-x</em> (<em>ks</em>), the Greek <em>zeta</em>, <em>xi</em>, and <em>psi</em> (note Acts <strong><sup>5</sup></strong>:5, <em>exepsuxen</em>—three in one word) ; the Hebrew <em>tsade</em>.

<strong>double meaning</strong>: (1) the usage of one word with two meanings simultaneously. Perhaps Joseph did not want to make Mary a public example because he thought her story was conceivable. On a 19° day, when my wife was glad that the sun was shining bright, I exclaimed, “Cool!” Jokes often play on such usage—especially “groaners”/*Tom Swiftys.

Other, more serious types of double meaning have arisen. (2) Fourfold interpretation of supposed that divine revelation in scripture carried more than the literal, straightforward meaning. Hidden to the uninitiated eye lay figurative, spiritual, and analogical meanings<strong>check this out</strong>. Philo of Alexandria was an early proponent of this view of divine revelation, a notion about multiple meanings that continued down into the time of the Reformation. In fact, one triumph of the reformation was discarding this fanciful notion about language. The rejection of this false principle is capsulized in Calvin’s famous dictum: “The meaning of scripture is one.”

A vestige of the multiple-meanings notion remains in the concept of (c) *double reference prophesy. It is an interpretation device that seeks to justify apparent New Testament misapplication of Old Testament predictions. In general, the answer lies elsewhere in class prediction (which is particularized to a given case), original application is a type of a similar case that better embodies the crucial element involved, <em>etc</em>. The use of 2 Sam<strong> 7</strong>:14 in Heb<strong> 1</strong>:5 is a case in point (class prediction—elaborate).

<strong>double negative</strong>: “I’m <em>not</em> doing <em>nothing</em>.” In English, grammarians sometimes claim that double negatives cancel each other out. That is not really what happens, though, when such a proscribed construct is actually used. It just isn’t considered standard usage. For fun, there are even titles that spoof this issue: “Don ‘t Nobody Bring Me No Bad News.”

Certain kinds of “double negatives” are allowed, as in certain kinds of *litotes where <em>not</em> combines with a negative prefix: <em>not infrequently</em>. Though, strictly speaking, it is a double negative, people commonly use the expression “I <em>can’t</em> help <em>but</em> think” rather than “I can’t help thinking.” Unaccepted double negatives include expressions like “<em>can’t hardly.</em>”

In Latin and Greek doubling—or even tripling—negatives stresses the negativity. <em>Ou mee</em> occurs regularly in Greek with this value.

<strong>double signal</strong>: two entries for the same item in a segment of syntax. Examples include a nominative pronoun plus a person-and-number verb termination (“<em>s</em>he work<em>s</em>), a prepositional prefix plus the same preposition placed independently (<em>in</em>truded <em>into</em> the proceedings). Double signals often give emphasis.

(in a set with *redundancy)

<strong>double-space</strong>: having two strokes of the space bar on a typewriter or computer. The spacing used after a *colon, *exclamation mark, *period, or *question mark (usually).  (2) an empty line between lines of type; typed on every second line

(in a set with *single space, *space, *triple space, *space-and-a-half)

<strong>double subject</strong>: Saying the subject of a sentence two different times: “The times, they are a-changin’.” (in a set with *nominative pendens)

<strong>double talk</strong>: talking with intent to obscure, to keep the hearer from understanding correctly, talking in a way that does not make sensible communication

<strong>double negative</strong>: two negatives used together, a practice shunned in formal English writing and speaking. In Greek and Latin, double (and even triple) negatives pile up to emphasize the negation rather than to cancel each other out.

<strong>doxology</strong>: a short Christian hymn of praise to God often sung in response to an offertory. Aside from a musical setting, doxology is used as a descriptor for certain eloquent passages in praise to God: Rom <strong><sup>11</sup></strong>:33-34; <strong><sup>16</sup></strong>:25-27; (2 Cor <strong><sup>9</sup></strong>:15); Eph <strong><sup>3</sup></strong>:20-21; Jude 24-25

<strong>drift</strong>: the general line of thought

<strong>dual forms: </strong>some languages have distinctive noun, adjective, pronoun, and verb forms for two of something or someone. Hebrew uses the termination –<em>ayim</em> for both masculine and feminine duals. Classical Greek had duals forms for verbs that were used in poetry.

(in a set with *singular, *plural):

<strong>dubitative subjunctive</strong>: same as *deliberative subjunctive; the use of the subjunctive mood in Latin and Greek for questions that imply uncertainty, inexactness, or tension created by such as circumstance (in a set with *deliberative questions)

<strong>duplicate</strong>: in two copies, an expression that was used when typewriters were in general use and the typist made a carbon copy at the same time as the original  (in a set with *duplicate)

<strong>dynamic inspiration</strong>: a view of divine assistance in the speaking of human prophets where God gives heightened abilities in contrast to dictated words, implanted ideas, planted experiences, or granted offices (2 Sam <strong><sup>7</sup></strong>:3-17).

(in a set with *dictation theory of inspiration, *verbal inspiration, *plenary inspiration, *inerrancy)

<strong>echolalia</strong>: the involuntary repetition of words just heard

<strong>eclectic text</strong>: a text like the Nash papyrus that evidently chooses variant New Testament Greek readings and conflates them into its ongoing reading

<strong>eclogue</strong>: a pastoral poem in the form of a dialog between shepherds

<strong>economy of expression</strong>: efficiency in expression, getting a lot said in few words (in a set with *terse)

<strong>ecstatic utterance</strong>: speaking that takes place in an altered state of consciousness, hence, not necessarily having *linguisticality and not necessarily understood or remembered even by the speaker. cp. the utterances of the priestess at the Delphic oracle and the utterances of an aged woman in the Sibylline oracles. Sometimes equated with “tongue speaking in the New Testament (in a set with *glossolalia, *speaking in tongues, *xenoglossia)

<strong>edict: </strong>

<strong>edit</strong>: to correct, size down, or otherwise improve an original manuscript (in a set with *amend, *emend)

<strong>editorial</strong>

<strong>editorial we</strong>: the use of <em>we</em> for <em>I</em> to avoid taking undue credit or appearing proud.

<strong>elaboration</strong>: giving more information, “fleshing out” the picture. Elaboration can be done by giving examples, reading a poem or scripture, telling a story that embodies the point, fill in the accompanying feature of the situation, filling in more details of the topic, showing how it connects to other things or differs from them.

<strong>elative</strong>: not having a specific other referent in view (*comparative degree adjective) or not having specific other individuals in a group/class (*superlative degree adjective)

<strong>elative adjectives</strong>: elative comparative (“rather __”)

Elative superlative (“<em>very</em> __”); English also uses expressions like “<em>good, good</em> man”; “<em>good “n” deep</em> hole”; “it is <em>powerful</em> cold”; “it is a <em>big-time</em> cold”; “it was <em>mega</em> cold”; “it was super cold”; and other *slang expressions.

<strong>elegiac couplet</strong> wh 301

<strong>elision/elide</strong>: elision drops out a sound for ease of pronunciation, <em>etc.</em> In Latin and Greek dentals cannot stand before sibilants. One way to solve the “problem” in the process of inflection, <em>etc.</em>, is to elide one or the other sound. A similar thing happens in English when seemingly awkward consonant combinations appear on the front of words that derive from Greek: pneumonia. It happens on the ends of words like paradigm; when the <em>–os</em> or –<em><u>ma</u></em> was lost, the<em> g</em> could no longer be pronounced with the preceding syllable; so it has been elided and the <em><u>g</u></em> becomes a *silent letter. The pronunciation becomes paradigm. The “contraction” of <em>do not</em> to d<em>on’t</em>, <em>etc.</em> is another kind of elision. Dropping consonant sounds on the end of French words is another illustration.

Greek words whose vowel ending bumps up against a vowel beginning on the next word, may elide the first vowel: <em>tout’ estin</em>.

(in a set with *aphesis, *contraction, *crasis; *ellipsis; *assimilation, *euphony)

<strong>ellipsis/elliptic</strong>: an omission perhaps from a quotation marked in writing by three double-spaced periods or asterisks ( . . . ). In repetitive wording, the omission may be marked with a comma. In Latin poetry, prepositions were often omitted to help conform to the demands of meter.

<strong>Example *</strong><em>Elision</em> is usually applied to an omission within a word; <em>ellipsis</em> is applied to omissions within a group of words.

Ellipses happen in the New Testament Greek text. In Luke’s genealogical list for the Messiah, he omits the noun before “<em>of</em> ___” (<strong><sup>3</sup></strong>:23-38). The direction of the genealogy runs backwards in time; so <em>son</em> goes ahead of the next proper name. Other contexts may call for supplying <em>father</em>, if the sequence is running forward, or <em>brother</em>, if the context is contemporaneous. The word for <em>to be</em> is often omitted in proverbs or *aphorisms, and other cases where <em>to be</em> does not have a time reference *linking verb).

(in a set with *elision, *elliptical, *assimilation, *euphony)

<strong>elliptical clause/sentence</strong>: a clause that omits words that would normally appear in a full sentence as sometimes happens with *sayings in order to give them greater “punch”: “What a bummer!” “Well begun, half done”; “Penny wise, pound foolish.” gregg23

<strong>elocution</strong>: use of language in a form well-suited to public speaking

<strong>eloquence</strong>: elegant, powerful, persuasive discourse

<strong>elucidate: </strong>to cast light on some statement by another person or in a text of scripture (in a set with *ambiguity)

<strong>emend/emendation</strong>: an emendation amounts to a correction of an obvious error in a writing, especially an ancient one (in a set with *amend, *edit)

<strong>emphasis</strong>: Stress sets up contrast with parallel alternatives. Emphasis is achieved (1) by saying something louder or more deliberately, (2) by gestures that accompany the speaking, (3) by underlining it in writing; (4) by putting it in a prominent position like the front, the end, or an otherwise unusual place in a sentence; (5) by assigning two elements to it as in *hendiadys or double signal (as kind of redundancy), (6) by making the word bigger as by adding a prefixed preposition (Latin and Greek compounds) or lengthening the word from, say, ou (not) to ouchi (surely not) in Greek. (7) Adding other words creates emphasis: “I <em>certainly</em> am.”

Latin and Greek have verb endings that indicate person and number; so pronouns in the nominative create a (8) double signal, thus, stressing the subject in a way akin to underling it in writing.

Emphasis has been done also (9) by using an unusual form: in classical Greek, after a past-tense main verb of fearing, an optative verb covered the action in the fear clause; to emphasize the object of fear, however, the subjunctive could be used—as it was in clauses after primary tenses in the leading verb. The same optative-to-subjunctive shift could occur in purpose clauses after leading verbs in a secondary tense. C&S192 (10) Hebrew used an infinitive absolute plus the same root in finite form to say “certainly do”: Is <strong><sup>6</sup></strong>:9-10. General communication may use (11) exaggeration for emphasis: “He’s as big as a barn.”

At the word level, accent marks indicate the emphasis, stress, accent a syllable with the word has. Stress and emphasis indicate emphasis within a sentence or paragraph.

Emphasis, however expressed, most of the time, sets up contrast with parallel alternatives; simply indicating something without stress does not have parallel alternatives consciously in mind.

Exaggeration is a form of emphasis of content.

(in a set with *accent, *secondary accent, *stress, *accent, *double signal, *intense)

<strong>emphatic form</strong>: a word that by its meaning shows emphasis. In English verb forms, using <em>do </em>as an auxiliary verb creates an emphatic form in the present and past tenses: I <em>do</em> like classical music”; “I <em>did</em> finish my homework.” Emphatic future has to rely on stressing the auxiliary verb <em>will</em>, which is a second way in English to create emphasis: “I will do my homework; just let me watch the game first.”

The emphasis effect may also be achieved by making the words bigger (#6 under *emphasis). Latin example include <em>exoro</em> < <em>oro</em>, <em>namque</em> < <em>nam;</em>

<strong>emphatic negative</strong>: <em>ou mee </em>in Greek for either questions or statements

<strong>enchiridion</strong>: a handbook on some subject

<strong>enclitics</strong>: particularly common in Greek—as well as in Latin, words pronounced with a preceding word; as such, enclitics do not bear their own accent if they can avoid it. In Greek, the enclitic threw away its accent if it could; if it could not throw it away, it threw it back—as an acute on the *ultima of the preceding word.  If it could not throw it back, it kept the accent.

In Latin the suffixed -<em>ne</em> on the first word of a sentence makes a question out of the sentence. Latin also has the intensive enclitic <em>–ce</em> Wh 310

English enclitics include situations like “Go, get <em>’em</em>.”

(in a set with *proclitics)

<strong>encode</strong>: to write a message using special symbols that are not known by outsiders

<strong>encomium</strong>: a tribute, a formal expression of praise, eulogy.WH320

<strong>encrypt</strong>: to put a message into a secret code so the information is not so likely to fall into the wrong hands

<strong>encyclical</strong>: a writing intended for circulation. In the New Testament, Paul sent Galatians to the churches of Galatia (<strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:2). Colossians was evidently intended for such use (Col <strong><sup>4</sup></strong>:16) as well as a Letter to the Laodecians (Col <strong><sup>4</sup></strong>:16), which may be the same as our Ephesians. The General Epistles were encyclicals in Asia Minor (James <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1; 1 Pet <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1; 2 Pet <strong><sup>3</sup></strong>:1; Jude 17). Revelation was to circulate among the seven churches of Asia (Rev <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:4). Even Romans was circulated among household churches within Rome (<strong><sup>16</sup></strong>:5, 14, 15).

In more modern usage, papal letters to the hierarchy of the Catholic church have been called encyclicals. (in a set with *personal letter)

<strong>encyclopedia</strong>: usually a multi-volume work alphabetically arranged on major topics (in a set with *dictionary)

<strong>endearment term</strong>: same as *affectionate term.

<strong>ending</strong>: a termination on a word, such as one that signals singular or plural on nouns and verbs, or person on verbs. A *morpheme found in this location. (*in a set with *zero ending)

<strong>endnote</strong>: serves the same purposes as a *footnote but is located at the end of the article or chapter. The practice replaces footnotes because of the greater ease of arrangement.

(in a set with *footnote, *marginal note, *marginalia, *sidebar, *sidenote)

<strong>endorse</strong>: to apply one’s signature to a check; to recommend a candidate for office

<strong>enigma/enigmatic</strong>: an unclear presentation, often intentionally so, that challenges the hearer to figure out the mystery; a *riddle. Something that cannot be figured out because crucial information has been lost.

<strong>enthymeme</strong> (sp)

<strong>entitle/entitled</strong>: having a *title, or *heading as on an essay or other written piece

<strong>enunciate/enunciation</strong>: Enunciation stresses clarity of *pronouncing a sound within a word; hence, it deals with phonemes. Enunciate contrast with *slur.  Pronunciation stresses saying the sound of the whole word; hence, it deals with part of what is meant by *morphemes. Diction stresses the choice of words. Elocution stresses the expressing phraseology—sets of words; hence, it deals with tagmemes. <strong>check this out</strong>  As a result, enunciation, pronunciation, and elocution have to do with the sounds, while saying can include the meaning involved—as with diction. (in a set with *articulation, *pronunciation, *elocution, *diction, *saying, *slur)

<strong>epenthesis</strong>: the insertion of a sound in the middle of a word but without a view to altering meaning, as it is with *infix. In some short verbs, Greek inserts a corresponding nasal ahead of a final root consonant in order to build up the basic root—shown in the aorist—into a form for the first principal part. Some of these verbs also double the aorist’s initial root consonant with an iota between them: <em>elabon < la</em><em><u>m</u></em><em>banoo, elthon < la</em><em><u>n</u></em><em>thanoo, elipon < li</em><em><u>m</u></em><em>panoo, elachon < la</em><em><u>g</u></em><em>chanoo, emathon < ma</em><em><u>n</u></em><em>thanoo, emneesa < mi</em><em><u>m</u></em><em>neeiskoo,  epleesa < pi</em><em><u>m</u></em><em>pleemi, enepreesa < empi</em><em><u>m</u></em><em>preemi, epreestheen < pi</em><em><u>m</u></em><em>pramai, eputhon < pu</em><em><u>n</u></em><em>thanoo, ethigon < thi</em><em><u>g</u></em><em>ganoo, </em>

In addition to this set, there are other instances of similar alterations that could be called <em>epentheses</em>. The <em>o/e</em>- variable is a set of connecting vowels that joins root and personal endings in the present, imperfect, and future tenses. <em>Hud</em><em><u>oo</u></em><em>r</em> is the nom sg that has no vowel between delta and rho in the rest of the declension. Greek does not like the <em>rho-sigma</em> sequence that the nom sg would theoretically carry. It looks like an <em>omega</em> was inserted to solve the impossible pronunciation <em>hydr</em>.

The variant form of the English indefinite article <em>a/an</em> affords many examples of a thing similar to epenthesis in English even though it is between words rather than within them. <em>An- </em>occurs before vowels and <em>a-</em> before consonants (except *glottal stops and a few glottal fricatives). Since language is always a matter of sound before it is a matter of writing, the <em>n</em> appears relative to sound, not writing. English says <em>a </em>when a spoken <em>y</em> consonant sound actually precedes the written <em>eu-/u-</em>:<em> euphemism/eulogy/union, etc., </em><strong>(insert panel</strong>: <em>eucane, eucakyptol, eucalyptus, eucharist, euchire, euchromatin, eudemonism, eugenic, eugenol, euglena, euhermerism, eukaryote, eunuch, eunonymous, eupatrid, eupepsia, euphonium, euphony, euphorbia, euporia, euphotic, euphuism, euplastic, euphold, eupnea, eureka, euripus, europium, eurybathic, euryhaline, euryterid, eurythermal, eurythmics, eurythmy, eurytopic, eustachian tube, eustasy, eustele, eutectic, euthanasia, euthenics, eutherian, eutrophic</em>.

Also note <em>u- </em>words with with <em>y</em> pronounced on the front: (<strong>insert panel</strong>) <em>ubiety ubiquitous, ufology, ukase, ukuklele, unanimity, una voce, uniaxial,unicameral, unicorn, unicellular,uniform, unify, unilateral, unilinear, unilingual, unilocular, uninucleate, union, unipolar, unique, unisex, unison, unitard, unite, universal, univocal, uraes, uranium, urea, unredinium, ureter, urethane, urethra, uric, urinal, urochord, uropod, uroscopy, use, usurp, usury, utensil, utilitarian, utopia, utricle, uvarovite, uvea, uxorious.  </em>

Likewise people say <em>an honor </em>because the <em>h</em> represents a glottal stop (as it does in <em>heir, honest,</em> <em>hour</em>—and <em>humble </em>for some speakers). There is some variation ahead of the glottal fricative <em>h</em>: <em>anhydrous</em>, but <em>a hassle, a hearth, a hit, a hook, a hut, a hydrant</em>.

A similar issue arises with a few <em>o</em> words that are actually pronounced with the consonant <em>w</em> sound on the front. People say, “There is not <em>a</em> one that is righteous”; “this is <em>a </em>once-in-a-lifetime opportunity.”

The pattern obtains with the *<em>a</em>- privative in Greek. It is plane <em>a-</em> before consonants including glottal fricatives, that is, rough breathing marks: <em>aorist</em> (<em>a- + horizoo</em>). Before smooth breathing, the prefix is <em>an-</em>:  English derivatives generally preserve this pattern: <em>agenesis, amoral, atheist, </em>

Latin does something similar to Greek by building up words that differ between third and first principal part. Words like <em>vi</em><em><u>n</u></em><em>co</em> show <em>vici</em> in the perfect active stem; it is a question whether the nasal is added to the first root or dropped from the third (cp. Gr <em><u>la</u></em><em>m</em><em><u>b</u></em><em>anoo/e</em><em><u>lab</u></em><em>on</em>). Cp. <em>cer</em><em><u>n</u></em><em>o/crevi</em>, <em>conti</em><em><u>n</u></em><em>go/contigi, contu</em><em><u>n</u></em><em>do/contudi</em>, <em>corru</em><em><u>m</u></em><em>po/corrupi</em>, <em>desi</em><em><u>n</u></em><em>o/desivi</em>, <em>po</em><em><u>n</u></em><em>o/posui</em>, <em>profu</em><em><u>n</u></em><em>do/profudi</em>, <em>reli</em><em><u>n</u></em><em>quo/reliqui</em>, <em>sper</em><em><u>n</u></em><em>o/sprevi</em>, <em>tango/tetigi</em>,

(in a set with *affix, *infix, *suffix; *morpheme, *epenthesis, *excrescence; *allomorph)

<strong>ephemeral paper</strong>: a publication that is not intended for long-term relevance or use; something that, being subject to ongoing time, is apt to be supplanted by further developments.

<strong>ephemeron</strong>: an example of printed material that has temporary interest

<strong>epigram</strong>: a short, concise, witty poem or paradoxical statement

<strong>epigraph: </strong>another term for *inscription. A short introductory statement of a topic

(in a set with *inscription; epigram)

<strong>epigraphy</strong>: the study of *inscriptions, the writing on utensils, pottery, and the like [<Gr. <em>epi</em> + <em>grapho</em>, to write]:

<strong>epilogue</strong>: a brief statement to the audience after a play or to the readership at the end of a book. Epilogue and *prologue deal more with the historical setting (prologue) and consequent occurrences (epilogue) than with conceptual and ideological matters <em>per se</em>.

(in a set with *prologue, *preface, *introduction)

<strong>epistle</strong>: a more formal writing than letter. Epistle connotes a letter-form that emphasizes explanatory, narrative, <em>etc.</em>, content. Inasmuch as letters and epistles are “sent,” they are called “missives.” In the New Testament, <em>epistles</em> designate the writings Romans through Jude in contrast to the gospels, Acts, and the Apocalypse/Revelation.

(in a set with *letter, *missive)

<strong>episode</strong>: one installment in a series of segments that make up a continuing, usually fictional, story as with soap operas or series of shows with the same actors doing similar things as in crime scene investigations.

(in a set with *prequel, *sequel)

<strong>epistolary</strong>: the adjective for <em>epistle</em>; having to do with an epistle

<strong>epistolary aorist</strong>: the use of a past tense for the present in anticipation of the reader’s point in time. New Testament examples probably include Acts <strong><sup>23</sup></strong>:30; Phil<strong> 2</strong>:28; Col<strong> 4</strong>:8.

<strong>epitaph</strong>: an *inscription on a tombstone; a short piece about a deceased person intended for long-term expression

<strong>epitasis</strong>: the middle section of a play that leads to the catastrophe

<strong>epithalamium</strong>: a poem of praise for a groom and his bride on the occasion of their marriage

<strong>epithet</strong>: a term, often derogatory, for describing a person, possibly used in place of the person‘s name. (in a set with *name calling, *by-word)

<strong>eponymy</strong>: the derivation of the name of something from an individual, as <em>Bayer </em>aspirin, <em>Marriott</em> hotel,

<strong>eponym</strong>: someone whose name is supposedly the person a city or place was named from: Edom, Moab, Israel, Dan.

<strong>epopee</strong>: a lyric, epic poem as a literary genre

<strong>epos</strong>: a group of poems that address the same important theme

<strong>epsilon</strong>: fifth letter of the Greek alphabet: ε/Ε Short <em>e</em>, paired with <em>eta</em>, the long <em>e</em>.

<strong>equal sign</strong>: a mathematical symbol equation (=)

<strong>equative verb</strong>: a verb that indicates being rather than action; another term for *copulative verb, *linking verb, *state-of-being verb, *to be verb; cp. *fientive verb and stative verb. (in a set with *active verb, *middle-voice verb, *passive verb)

<strong>eristic</strong>: having the character of a disputation, argumentation, polemics; a polemical writing or argument

<strong>errata</strong>: errors; a notice placed variously that identifies typos or printing mistakes discovered in the body of the work

<strong>esoteric</strong>: suitable for use by “insiders,” those prepared to understand its import; beyond the reach of normal persons (in a set with *exoteric)

<strong>essay</strong>: a *theme, a written explanatory treatment of some topic

<strong>eta</strong>: seventh letter of the Greek alphabet: η/Η. Long “e,” paired with epsilon, short “e.”

<strong>ethogram</strong>: a catalog that summarizes pictorially the behavior of animals

<strong>etiology</strong>  the backstory for some term, idea, or custom; their origin

<strong>etymology</strong>: the study of the origin of words; also called *derivation

<strong>etymon</strong>: the earlier form of a word or *morpheme whether in the same language, an earlier language, or another contemporary language. (in a set with *loan word)

<strong>eulogy</strong>: a speech given at a funeral or memorial service, praising the accomplishments, <em>etc.</em>, of the deceased.

<strong>euphemism</strong>: a polite or respectful way of saying something otherwise unpleasant. Euphemisms often deal with death, bodily elimination, the place names for such processes. Death is called “sleep.” A place for defecation or urination may be called a restroom, men’s room, water closet, someone’s facilities. “Covering the feet” evidently has that meaning in the Old Testament.( ).

In the discussion about the elements in the Lord’s supper, it has been proposed that “fruit of the vine” is a euphemism for wine. The comment is in response to the idea that “fruit of the vine” is so worded because it was not intended to be fermented wine. <em>Wine</em>, however, is not likely to be considered a word or thing that someone would seek a euphemism for.

(in a set with *crude)

<strong>euphony/euphonic</strong>: “well” + “sound”; a way of making something seemingly easier to pronounce. English speakers, for example, routinely say “dipthong” or “diptheria” instead of <em>di</em><em><u>ph</u></em><em>thong</em> or <em>di</em><em><u>ph</u></em><em>theria</em>. The adjustment is from sequential fricatives to stop plus fricative. Greek makes the opposite change: when a stop-fricative sequence is created by inflection, word building, or word sequence, Greek shifts the stop to a fricative to produces a fricative-fricative sequence. English changed the Greek root pne- to “neumonia” rather than pneumonia. The same happens to <em>kt</em>- and other “odd” consonant combinations. Some of these are considered “standard”; others are considered _____

The practice in English of voicing through intervocalic consonants as in “ladder” for “latter”; “udder” for “utter.”

Adjusting voicing in clusters: <em>rex</em> < <em>regs</em> to <em>recs</em> to rēx, “king” (Latin).

Sequencing words like Donna Michelle rather than Michelle Donna makes it easier to say.

Latin: irregularities in the irregular verb fero: <em>ferris</em> for <em>feriris</em> (2nd pl pass ind pres) <strong>check other places. </strong>

Various kinds of *assimilation, or modifications that make sounds “alike,” (1) during compounding: col-late (< con + late); syl-labus (<syn + ), sym-bol (syn + bol); efficio (< ex + facio). It happens also (2) in creating *cognates: scrib-o becomes scrip-tor when the agent ending –tor is suffixed to the root scrib. There are examples across (3) *principal parts: scrib-o to scrip-si, when the *voiceless <em>s</em> is added to the root after voiced b to help create the perfect active; likewise <em>a</em><em><u>g</u></em><em>-o</em> becomes <em>a</em><em><u>c</u></em><em>tus</em> in the fourth principal part. (4) <em>Sequencing of words</em> creates the same result: <em>haf to</em> for <em>have to</em>.

Greek, like Latin, in general does not tolerate dental-sibilant sequences.  <em>Sy</em><em><u>n</u></em> (with) + <em>zao</em> (to live) becomes <em>syzao</em> (to live with) by *eliding the <em>nu</em>.

Latin <em>pot-sum</em> > <em>possum</em>; <em>per</em> becomes <em>pel</em>- when prefixed to roots beginning with<em> l</em>.

Hebrew examples

English does not write the oral differences in nearly so many circumstances as does Greek especially and to a lesser extent Latin. For that reason, an English-speaking student of Greek senses that Greek presents an extremely large number of changes across principal parts, inflection, word-building, and word sequencing. Most of these “irregularities” are no more irregular than what occurs in English, but they seem more so because they are written. The English habit of voicing through voiceless consonants is one of those unwritten practices that create difficulties for Spanish speakers, for example, because their first language does not do this unexpected thing; consequently, they will say <em>sāys </em>instead of<em> sez.</em>

So, when undesirable sound combinations come about in *inflection, principal parts, *word-building, or word sequencing, various changes relieve the “difficulty,” or foster ease of pronunciation, efficiency of expression, or sound more pleasant to the ear. (1) The first letter is omitted. The Latin <em>scribien</em><em><u>t</u></em><em>s</em> becomes <em>scribiens</em> (nom mas sg present act participle); elsewhere in the declension the <em>nt</em> participle morpheme shows up fully. In Greek, the <em>tau</em> in <em>thap</em><em><u>t</u></em><em>o</em> falls out in the future (<em>thapso</em>) and first aorist (<em>ethapsa</em>). Likewise with <em>sodzo</em>. Greek words in a sequence—one ending with a vowel and the next beginning with a vowel—may *elide the first vowel: <em>apo archees kata oikon</em> becomes <em>ap archees kat oikon</em>; <em>dia autou</em> becomes <em>di’ autou</em>, <em>kata oikon</em> becomes <em>kat’oikon</em>, <em>oude an</em> becomes <em>oud an</em>.

(2) The second letter is discarded: Latin shows <em>amor</em> for the theoretical <em>amors</em> of the third declension nom mas sg. Greek *elides the sigma from the first aorist sign after roots ending in lambda (*liquid aorists): <em>angello</em> > <em>eengeila</em>.

(3) Both letters may disappear. Greek shows <em>agōn</em> for theoretical <em>agon</em><em><u>ts</u></em> in the nom mas sg pres act ptc (The elided <em>t</em> exposes the <em>s</em> to the <em>n</em> so that the <em>s</em> is also lost; the previous <em>o</em> lengthens to *compensate for the double loss). In Latin also, dental-sibilant sequences are not allowed, but <em>n</em> is not included in the dental-sibilant category (<em>agens</em>).

(4) A <em>vowel</em> may be interfixed to separate the “offending” letters: example. A <em>consonant</em> is frequently interfixed to separate vowel-plus-vowel sequences. The English indefinite article varies from <em>a </em>to <em>an</em>, depending on whether the next word starts with a consonant or vowel.  The same applies to the negating prefix <em><u>a</u></em>(<em>n</em>), which produces <em><u>a</u></em><em>theist</em> and then <em><u>an</u></em><em>ion</em>. Greek adds <em>k</em> to <em>ou</em> ahead of words beginning with vowels (<em>ouk echousin</em>) and within a word adds a <em>k</em> to the <em><u>ou</u></em><em> + </em><em><u>eti</u></em> combination to make <em>ou</em><em><u>k</u></em><em>eti</em>.  (5) There may be a shift to a more acceptable consonant combination. The Greek <em>ouk</em> becomes <em>ouch</em> before words that start with a fricative. <em>Nu</em> becomes <em>mu</em> before *labials. Voiced consonants become unvoiced before unvoiced consonants and vice versa. Non-nasals become nasals before *nasals. (6) *Metathesis may take place to undo the undesirable combination.
