In English dialect, ask becomes ax (aks): “I’m not gonna ax you agin.” Hebrew does this with the hithpael stem when a sibilant begins the root that hith comes up against.

In most of these examples the influence is backwards onto the preceding sound.

(in a set with *elision, *ellipsis, *elliptical, *elision, *assimilation, *metathesis)

<strong>evangelistic sermon</strong>: a sermon whose purpose is to urge audience members to accept the Christian “good news” about salvation through Christ; *outreach sermons

<strong>exact rhyme: </strong>repeats the end sounds exactly: crime, thyme. (get other examples) (in a set with *eye rhyme, *perfect rhyme, *near rhyme,<em> etc</em>.)

<strong>exaggeration</strong>: saying something in a more extreme way than the situation really is; a device sometimes used deliberately for emphasis instead of deception. A common characteristic of humor.

(in a set with *overstatement, *understatement)

<strong>exception</strong>: what does not follow the standard pattern. In language there are innumerable exceptions to the very great number of patterns. They occur in the sound value a certain letter or digraph may have (especially in English!), how a verb is carried through its principal parts, how a verb is conjugated through its person and number, how adjectives and nouns decline through their n/g/c, how adjectives move through their degrees,  <strong>and the like</strong>

<strong>excerpt</strong>: a portion lifted from a larger writing or speech

<strong>exclamation</strong>: another term for *interjection. Latin interjections include <em>vae</em> ()<em>, opi</em> (ouch)!<em>, heu,</em> alas)

<strong>exclamation mark</strong>: a mark (!) used for added emphasis. It usually appears after *interjections.

<strong>exclamatory sentence</strong>: a sentence that expresses strong feeling in contrast to just saying it, asking about it, or commanding it: In a huff he said, “I’m going home!” (in a set with *declarative sentence, *imperative sentence, *interrogative sentence)

<strong>excrescence</strong>: a superfluous addition in the sense that it does not add meaning. An example is grammatical “gender” when added as a feature of words for sexless things.

(in a set with *epenthesis)

<strong>excursus</strong>: a separate essay perhaps tangential to the topic of a book but added in the back to flesh out a matter of related interest in a fashion that would interrupt the body of the work as a digression.

<strong>exegesis/exegetical</strong>: careful explanation that strives to get accurately the author’s meaning out of the text (in a set with *exposition/expository)

<strong>exegete</strong>: one who does *exegesis

<strong>exegetical sermon</strong>: a sermon that ties closely to a text and endeavors to cover the same ground as that portion of scripture includes. (in a set with *evangelistic sermon, *expository sermon, *outreach sermon, *textual sermon, *topical sermon, *theme sermon)

<strong>exhortation</strong>: A sentence that says, “<em>Let’s . .</em> . or <em>Let us</em> . . . .” Latin and Greek use first person plural subjunctive to express exhortation. In Latin it is part of the *jussive among independent subjunctive usages, a usage that also applies to the first singular and to the third person: “Let him do his own work”; “let them do their own work.” In such cases let does not mean “allow.” (The Latin second person jussive is rare except in poetry, the *imperative mood fulfilling that role in normal cases.) As a result, Latin does not distinguish hortatory subjunctive because it is part of the jussive, a larger division of subjunctive usage.

Greek separately identifies *hortatory subjunction because the jussive idea for third (or second) person in Latin is replaced by third-person imperative mood in Greek. (Latin has no third-person imperative.)

The idea is called *cohortative in Hebrew.

<strong>exoteric</strong>: suitable for public use in contrast to what would be appropriate for insiders; contrasts with *esoteric

<strong>expletive</strong>: a word with no meaning in itself. It is used (1) to signal the fact that the subject comes later. Two common English expletives are <em>it</em> and <em>there</em>, one from pronoun origin and the other from adverb origin. “For full credit, <em>it</em> is necessary to complete the assignment” (<em>to complete</em> is the subject); “There are monsters under my bed” (<em>monsters</em> is subject). (2) Expletives can fill out the structure of a sentence: “<em>It</em> is raining outside.” (3) a synonym for *curse word.

<strong>explosive</strong>: a lengthened form of *plosive, a linguistic term for an “explosive” sound like the <em>ch</em> in <em><u>ch</u></em><em>uck</em>.

<strong>exposé: </strong>the unveiling of some topic, especially if it needs to be discredited

<strong>exposition/expository: </strong>explanation that stresses bringing out the idea for a listener’s benefit

(in a set with *exegetical/exegesis)

<strong>expository sermon</strong>: a kind of sermon that takes significant elements of a scripture portion and forms them into a coherent sequence without necessarily trying to equal the full range of subject matter the text contains.

(in a set with *evangelistic sermon, *exegetical sermon, *outreach sermon,*textual sermon, *topical sermon, *theme sermon)

<strong>express</strong>: to put ideas into words

<strong>expurgate</strong>: to purge objectionable material from a publication or telecast; to “bleep” certain vulgar, obscene, or simply false items or words.

<strong>extemporary</strong>: presented orally without organized notes or extensive immediate preparation (in a set with *ad lib, *off-the-cuff)

<strong>extent of space</strong>: Classical Greek used the accusative case on distance terms without an accompanying preposition. Hex stadia edramen”; “He ran six stades.”

<strong>extent of time</strong>: In Greek and Latin, extent of time is covered by the accusative case on the time word with no accompanying preposition: “He worked <em>(for</em>)<em> six hours</em>.” The idea can be diagrammed as an arrow: . English may or may not use <em>for</em> with the time word.

(in a set with *time, *time when, *time during/time within which)

<strong>extinction</strong>: the process of passing out of existence as in the case of a language that no longer has native speakers

<strong>eye rhyme</strong>: what on a page looks like it would rhyme, but doesn’t: <em>home/come; tomb/comb.</em> The phenomenon can occur in English because the same sound may be spelled in more than one way, and the same spelling can indicate more than one sound.  (get different examples)

<strong>fable</strong>: a story with a moral point to it. It does not have to be as true to life like Jesus’ *parables, and so uses plants and animals anthropomorphically in narrative form to make a proverbial point. Jotham’s Fable of the Bramble Bush (Jud <strong><sup>9</sup></strong>:8-15) is an example.

(in a set with *parable/parabolic, *story, *morality play)

<strong>facetiae</strong>: “factitious” writings; humorous, lighthearted sayings

<strong>factitive</strong>: (1) a type of verb that can govern a direct object plus objective complement as in “They chose him governor.” (2) a descriptor for *aoristic action, or simple action, action that is simply referred to as a fact in any tense without linear, perfect, conative, or ingressive implication: “I worked at the store,” “I see the results already,” <em> vs.</em> “I used to work,” “I was working,” “I tried to work,” “I began to work,” “I work at the store,” “ I am working,” “I have worked all night,” “I have the work done,” I will be working.” Factitive action is punctiliar or snapshot rather than motion picture.

(in a set with *aspect, *aktionsart; *verb; *tense)

<strong>familiar address</strong>: a manner of speaking as to equals, using first names; contrast with *polite address. (in a set with *honorifics)

<strong>farce</strong>: a play with an improbable plot and exaggerated character with actions designed to be funny; like the skits by The Three Stooges. An absurd, slap-stick, ludicrous, ridiculous play.

<strong>fascicle</strong> (also <strong>fascicule</strong>): a work published in sections as available and then often published complete as one volume when all the parts have been prepared

<strong>fear clause: </strong>a kind of *object clause that appears after a leading verb of fear. In classical Greek it called for a special formula: me + subjunctive after *primary tenses; and me + optative after secondary tenses. Using the subjunctive after secondary tense stressed the object of the fear. Latin had the same construction.

<strong>feminine ending</strong>: a declension ending on nouns or adjectives that indicates whether the word is grammatically feminine instead of *masculine or *neuter.

(in a set with *masculine ending, *neuter ending)

<strong>feminine rhyme</strong>: a rhyme pattern that does not accent the last syllable <em>mother/brother.</em>

<strong>festschrift</strong>: a collection of essays in honor of a scholar, written by colleagues, by well-known persons of the same discipline, or by former students.

<strong>fiction</strong>: a *genre of writing in story form that does not purport of tell about actual persons and events. (in a set with *non-fiction)

<strong>fientive verb</strong>: verbs found in Greek, Latin, and Hebrew that correspond to an English linking verb plus predicate adjective: Latin <em>salver</em>, to be safe; <em>sapere</em>, to be wise; <em>valeo</em>, to be strong; Greek <em>chalepaino</em>, to be angry; <em>heetaomai</em>, to be inferior; Hebrew <em>katon</em>, to be small. Also sometimes included in *stative verbs.

(in a set with *active verb, *middle-voice verb, *passive verb; *stative verb; *copulative verb, *equative verb, *linking verb, *state of being verb, *to be verb)

<strong>figurative</strong>: having to do with the usage of a word or phrase where the thing meant is referred to as something else or in other than a straightforward manner. Contrasts with *literal.

(in a set with *literal; *figure of speech)

<strong>figure of speech</strong>: any of a number of ways of saying something in other than a straightforward fashion; a *trope. Some kind of comparison between the thing intended and the word/words used is often involved.

Allegory/allegorical: allegorizing the comparison

Anaphora

chiasmus

*Hendiadys

irony

Metaphor: calls one thing something else

Metonymy: calls the whole by a part (“metonymy of the whole”) or calls the part by the whole (“metonymy of the part”)

*Parable of tares and of sower close to allegories

Simile uses <em>like</em> or <em>as</em> in comparing one thing to another.

zeugma

Euphemism: a polite way of referring to something distasteful, something in poor taste; or personal, private, sensitive

(in a set with *euphemism, *hendiadys, *irony, *metaphor, *metonymy, *mixed metaphor, *simile, *zeugma)

<strong>filibuster</strong>: prolonged speechmaking intended to delay the legislative process at least until such time as the side doing the filibuster has driven away enough of the opposition to gain a victory in the voting (in a set with *talkathon)

<strong>filler words</strong>: words used to fill time during speaking so the speaker can thinks of what to say next: <em>er, uh, you-know, like</em>.

<strong>fine print</strong>: details of a contract or written agreement that are tucked away in an ambiguous place—or print size—so the signator will tend not to notice the objectionable clause in the document.

<strong>finite verb</strong>: a type of verb that has person; hence, limited to a certain grammatical person Contrasts with infinitives, participles, and gerunds (and Latin gerundives) as possible kinds of verb forms

(in a set with *verbals;

<strong>first aorist</strong>: In Greek, first aorist verbs use sigma-alpha as part of the tense sign for aorist active and middle voice—the verb’s third principal part. (in a set with *second aorist)

<strong>first aorist passive</strong>: In Greek, first aorist passive verbs used theta-eta as part of the sign for the aorist passive/future passive tenses—the sixth principal part. (in a set with *second aorist passive)

<strong>first Asclepiadean </strong>wh 290 a type of poetic meter

<strong>first attributive position</strong>: in Greek the placement of an adjective between article and its noun--in contrast to *second attributive—after a noun’s repeated article. In addition to adjectives, first attributive modifiers can be genitive nouns or prepositional phrases—in short, anything that can have an adjectival value except a relative clause.

(in a set with *attributive adjective, *predicated adjective, *second attributive position)

<strong>First Great Commandment, The</strong>: the “shema” of Deut <strong><sup>6</sup></strong>:4-5 (cp. Mt<strong> 22</strong>:34-40): The first great commandment is to love God without restraint.

<strong>first deponent</strong>: In Greek, a verb that lacks the active voice, but has middle and passive voice. A first deponent will not have a fourth principal part because only the perfect active is built on that root. So, the remaining five principal parts are represented, but they are entered in the lexicon with a middle-voice ending in the first three parts. (in a set with *deponent, *second deponent, *semi-deponent).

<strong>first perfect</strong>: In Greek, first perfect verbs uses k(a) as part of the sign for the perfect active voice—fourth principal part. (in a set with *second perfect).

<strong>first person</strong>: the one/ones speaking, in contrast to the one/ones spoken to or the one/ones spoken about

(in a set with *second person, *third person)

<strong>flapped </strong><strong><em>r</em></strong>: (in a set with *trilled <em>r</em>)

<strong>font</strong>

<strong>foot:</strong> in poetry a set of syllables grouped by a pattern of accented-unaccented syllables, long-short syllables; a metrical foot

<strong>footnote</strong>: an entry at the “foot” of a page that elaborates (content footnote) or gives reference(s) for the claim made in the text above (in a set with *endnote, *marginal note, *marginalia, *sidebar, *sidenote)

<strong>foreword</strong>: usually the first entry, often not written by the author, that prepares the reader for what is to be said in the book. It often explains the historical factors that prepared the way for the writing and highlights the writer’s experience and qualifications for the undertaking.

(in a set with *introduction, *chapter, *part, *epilogue, *appendix, *index)

<strong>form</strong>: (1) form in language contrasts with function and meaning. (2) <em>A</em> form is a particular possibility a word may have that identifies it by number, tense, and the like.

(in a set with formant, *formative)

<strong>form letter</strong>: a letter that is the same for every addressee with only those items changed that are peculiar to a particular recipient.

<strong>formal equivalence</strong>: a type of translation that endeavors to provide word-for-word correlation with the original language.

<strong>formal term</strong>: a term used in academic or elite circumstances (in a set with *archaism, buzz word, *informal term, oath, *obsolete term, *provincialism, *regionalism, *slang, *technical term, *vulgaris)

<strong>formant</strong>: an element added to a root in inflected languages to indicate *tense, *person, *mood, *number, *voice, *aspect; *gender, *case. (in a set with *morpheme; *form, *formative)

<strong>formative</strong>: an element added to roots to make connection with additions to the word: connecting vowels, tense signs; signs for number, gender, and case. *Form looks at the whole word with all its combined elements of meaning and usage. *Formant is a morpheme that contributes one added element of meaning to the *root, such as a *tense formant. *Formative can be a splicing mechanism that ties root and formant together, as a *connecting vowel. A morpheme is any meaning unit including the root itself and those units that change the part of speech.

(in a set with *morpheme; *form, *formant)

<strong>formulaic</strong>: having a set wording as on ancient bills of sale, funerary curses, repeated verbal religious rituals, <em>etc.</em>

<strong>four-letter word</strong>: a way of referring to a curse word since many such words have four letters.

<strong>fragment sentence</strong>: a sentence that does not have all the parts needed for expressing a complete thought. Fragment sentences happens mostly in written communication because the written medium is not one in which some people habitually operate; and it is slower to put down, which sets a person up for omitting something important to the thought. See also *sentence fragment.

<strong>frame of reference</strong>: the intended range of topic. An example of issue is the use of Rom <strong><sup>5</sup></strong>:12-21 to argue that there was no such thing as death in the animal kingdom before Adam and Eve sinned; by implication animals did not eat other animals; there were no carnivores, only herbivores. The apostle, however, is talking about humankind and sin reigning in death even over them that had not sinned like Adam did (<strong><sup>5</sup></strong>:14). “Over-interpreting” it to apply more widely loses apostolic authority for that further claim. One might think accordingly that animals will be resurrected because the text goes on to say that by man (the Man) came will come eternal life (<strong><sup>5</sup></strong>:21).

<strong>free verse</strong>: so-called because it is “free” of the typical forms associated with poetry. Instead of rhyme scheme and meter, free verse may strive only for heightened imagery, *balanced construction, or *elliptical expression. (in a set with *poetry, *prose)

<strong>frequentative form</strong>: Latin added –<em>t</em>- to a root to indicate frequently doing the action indicated by the root WH 292, 307). In Greek, –<em>izo</em> verbs originally had a frequentative, or repetitive, value.

<strong>free-standing</strong>: considered a separate word and so written in contrast to existing only as an affix or *combining form in other words. The word <em>all</em> in contrast to –<em>tion</em>.

<strong>fricative</strong>: a sound made by restricting the air flow at variouis pronunciation points: <em>f/ph</em>, <em>v</em> (<em>bh</em>, *bilabial *voiced); <em>th</em> (thin), <em>dh</em> (then); <em>ch</em> (Bach), <em>gh</em> (voiced *palatal/*velar); <em>h</em> (honor; Greek smooth breathing [’], Hebrew <em>aleph</em>).

<strong>function word</strong>: preposition, conjunction, and article Goetchius, 25)

<strong>funerary inscription</strong>: an inscription placed at a burial sight

<strong>fused verbs</strong>: another term for *merged verb or *phrasal verb.????

<strong>fused sentence</strong>: another term for *run-on sentence

(in a set with *comma)

<strong>fusillade</strong>: n. a rapid barrage of insults

<strong>future tense</strong>: (a) English <em>will</em> (<em>shall</em> for emphatic), (b) “<em>going to</em> __ this job.” The older will-shall distinction no longer holds: <em>shall</em> for first person and <em>will</em> for second and third person, their being reversed for emphasis. Most of the time in spoken English, emphatic future stresses the auxiliary word, but a pattern has been developing in written English of underlining the auxiliary or using shall for emphasis.

(in a set with *imperatival future, *near future, *distant future, *futuristic present)

<strong>future perfect</strong>: a tense that indicates action completed before some future point in time: <em>will have done</em> it, <em>will have</em> it <em>done</em>. The latter option indicates a *stative idea. A tense formed by combining <em>will have</em> with the past participle (the third principal part of the English verb). Greek and Latin have equivalent forms.

<strong>futuristic present</strong>: In if-clauses and other introductory adverbial clauses, English can use the present tense with future force: “If he goes to college, he will be more likely to succeed.” “Wherever he works, he will need to learn to get out of bed in the morning.” New Testament examples include Mt <strong><sup>7</sup></strong>:41, 42, 52; Jn <strong><sup>14</sup></strong>:18.

<strong>galley proofs</strong>: the pages of a book as they come from type set or computer-generated copy. Any typographical mistakes found by the author or other proof readers are noted and corrected before final publication.

(in a set with *galley sheets, *proof reader))

<strong>galley sheets</strong>: another expression for *galley proofs.

<strong>gamma</strong>: third letter of the Greek alphabet: γ/Γ

<strong>gazette</strong>: a newspaper or official journal

<strong>gazetteer</strong> a geographic index or dictionary Webster)

<strong>gender: </strong>Gender is a grammatical category that could in most cases as easily be called <em>x, y,</em> or <em>z</em>. In grammar issues, gender is not *sex reference. While languages that have grammatical gender usually do use masculine for male referents and feminine for female referents, sexless items may be masculine, feminine, or neuter. In form, English has sex-reference only, and then only in pronouns and possessive adjectives of the third person singular. With pronouns there are *masculine, *feminine, *neuter, and *common. Gender distinction exists in third-person singular reference (<em>he/she/it</em>), but not in first or second person or in third-person plural. Outside third singular, pronouns and possessive adjectives are “common” (<em>I/me/my</em>; <em>we/us/our</em>; <em>you/your</em>; <em>they/them/their</em>). Third person personal pronouns are limited to <em>he/she/it</em>. The same is true of *reflexive and *intensive pronouns: <em>himself, herself, itself.</em>

In popular usage outside the realm of grammar, the word <em>gender</em> has come to have sex reference as in social-cultural contexts. That leaves <em>sex</em> for biological contexts.

In grammatical gender, the rule in Greek, Latin, and traditional English has been that masculine takes preference in ambiguous, mixed, or indifferent situations. With the abandonment of grammatical gender in favor of sex-reference only, modern English usage of third singular pronouns has posed an issue of late. Current practice has started using the third plural common possessive <em>their</em> (and even pronouns <em>they</em> and <em>them</em>) for singular ambiguous, mixed, and indifferent situations. So, comments are made like “this one,” “their,” “a person.” These are considered less cumbersome than the “he or she,” “he/she,” “s/he” phraseology or alternating “he” and “she” for indifferent references in an ongoing text.

Latin and Greek customarily used neuter for ideas or general reference: <em>Nobiscum versari iam diutius non potes; </em><em><u>id</u></em><em> patiar</em>”; “You are no longer able to stay with us now; I will not permit it.”

Masculine gender

Feminine gender

Neuter gender does not exist in Hebrew.

Common gender is a feature of many English pronoun types and of the first person endings on Hebrew verbs, which distinguish masculine and feminine gender in the other persons.

(in a set with *number and *case for *“locating” a *noun or *adjective)

(in a set with *masculine, *feminine, *neuter, *common)

<strong>genealogy/genealogical lists</strong>: In the Old Testament, the historical connection of persons to the past is maintained by genealogical lists; they keep track of inheritance issues and authority/responsibility lines.  The two genealogies in the New Testament trace the origins of Jesus. Matthew’s list presumably traces his genealogy through Joseph, while Luke’s trace his genealogy through Mary, replacing Mary’s name with Joseph’s in the immediately previous generation. These lists converge in two different sons of David/Solomon.

<strong>generalization</strong>: a statement that speaks of a category while meaning less than the category. It is an instance of the difference between form of statement and meaning of the statement. “American are money-hungry” probably indicates the speaker’s belief that a sizeable portion of American people have that characteristic.

<strong>generative grammar</strong>: in the case of previously unwritten language, linguists attempt to analyze the language systematically, discover its rules of operation, and delineate them in organized fashion. In so doing, the linguist is “generating” a grammar that can enable a new speaker to communicate in the language.

<strong>genitive absolute: </strong>Greek used the genitive case for the subject and participle of an *absolute construction; hence, it is called a genitive absolute. Classical Greek often widened the condition for usage by specifying that the subject of the dependent element not appear as the subject of the main clause rather than the subject not appear at all in the main sentence.

<strong>genitive case: </strong>Genitive case equals the *possessive case in English terminology. Most often genitive case ties one noun to another, the nature of the case determining the exact way the genitive-noun *referent relates to the *phrase head: “to the carrying away <em>of-Babylon.</em>” The carrying away was <em>to</em> Babylon, but the Greek merely ties <em>Babylon</em> to the preceding noun (Mt <strong><sup>11</sup></strong>:1, 12). Consequently, the hearer must be more actively involved by needing to figure out that real connection. Greek and Latin genitive case is handled in English with a possessive case (’s or s’) or an “of” prepositional phrase.

The genitive case can express the part of the whole: “he conquered <em>part of</em> the city.” See *descriptive genitive, *genitive of apposition, *genitive of material, *genitive of possession, *objective genitive, *partitive genitive (genitive of the whole), *genitive of value, *subjective genitive. In Greek the genitive case appears after certain prepositions, especially those implying separation. Many genitive usages in Greek and Latin correspond to “of” expressions in English.

<strong>genitive of apposition</strong>: same as appositive genitive; the genitive case renames the noun it is attached to: “the city of Cincinnati.” In most places where English uses “of,” Greek and Latin use the genitive case, and Hebrew has a *construct form on the phrase head.

<strong>genitive of indefinite value</strong>: WH354#10

<strong>genitive of measure</strong>: Greek uses the genitive case on measurement words to indicate amount. Acts<strong> 4</strong>:22: “The man on whom this sign of healing occurred was of over forty years [<em>etoon pleioon tessarakonta</em>)?  “Did you sell the land <em>for so much</em>? (Acts <strong><sup>5</sup></strong>:8, “of-so-much”). Latin: <em>Arma sunt </em><em><u>parvi pretii</u></em> (“The weapons are <em>of little value</em>” WH 231#2); akin to *genitive of value.

<strong>genitive of value wh 216</strong>

<strong>genre/literary genre</strong>: a category of literature like *history, *prophecy, *wisdom literature, *gospel, *apocalyptic, *acts, *novel, *poetry, *didactic.

<strong>gentilic</strong>: a termination that indicates someone as being a resident of or in the nation of; often translated in scripture from Hebrew –<em>i </em>as –<em>ite</em>; cp. biblical and modern terms <em>Bangladeshi, Canaani, Irani, Iraqi, Israeli, Kuwaiti, Pakistani, Uzbekistani, Waorani, Yemeni.</em> Other endings of similar import are (Mexic)<em>an</em>, (Egypt)<em>ian</em>, (New York)<em>er</em>, (Scots)<em>man</em>, (Chin)<em>ese</em>.

<strong>geocentric viewpoint</strong>: events viewed from the standpoint of the earth rather than of heaven of God or from an omniscient perspective. The beginning of Genesis starts with an omniscient viewpoint (1:1, “heavens and the earth,” universal reference), moves to a geocentric viewpoint (<strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:2-<strong><sup>2</sup></strong>:3), narrows to an anthropocentric viewpoint (<strong><sup>2</sup></strong>:4ff.). Thence it narrows further to Seth’s descendants, to Noah’s family, to Seth, to Abraham, to Isaac (<em> vs.</em> Ishmael), to Jacob (<em> vs.</em> Esau). The result is a document that provides the setting that zeroes in on place of National Israel in the scheme of things. (in a set with *anthropocentric viewpoint, *omniscient point)

<strong>gerund</strong>: In English, a gerund is a verbal noun with an –<em>ing</em> termination: “<em>Giving</em> is more blessed than <em>receiving</em>.”

In Latin,

(in a set with *finite verbs, *infinitives, *participles)

<strong>gerundive</strong>: in Latin, another name for the future passive participle, a passive verbal adjective often uised to express obligation or necessity

<strong>geschichte</strong>: German for history (sp)

<strong>gesticulate</strong>: to use bodily motions to indicate or reinforce ideas expressed while speaking

<strong>gesture</strong>: visual indicator of some message: (1) a sign or signal: bowing, curtsying, holding one’s hand up (as if to answer), holding one’s hand out with the palm facing toward someone (meaning stop), nodding, pointing with the chin, rolling the eyes, rub one’s stomach, shaking the head, *shrugging, snapping the fingers, squinting the eyes, sticking out the tongue, thumbing the nose, winking,<em> etc</em>.; (2) an act of kindness by which a person communicates goodwill with perhaps the implication of willingness for further efforts toward a meeting of minds or change of procedure.


Biblical gestures in the first sense include (1) shaking the dust off your feet at someone ();

(2) putting one’s hand under another man’s thigh accompanying a legal agreement ();

(3) spitting in someone’s face as a gesture of contempt (Num <strong><sup>12</sup></strong>:14; Job <strong><sup>17</sup></strong>:6; <strong><sup>30</sup></strong>:10; Is <strong><sup>50</sup></strong>:4);

(4) removing a shoe and spitting in the face of someone who does not want to fulfill the Levirate marriage responsibility (Deut <strong><sup>25</sup></strong>:5-10; Ruth <strong><sup>4</sup></strong>:7-9);

(5) sitting in sackcloth and ashes as a gesture of repentance ();

(6) rending one’s garments as a gesture of abhorrence ();

(7) stretching forth the hand to signal attention to what a person is about to say to a group of people ();

(8) prostrating oneself before another as a sign of total submission and the complete abandonment of personal dignity, in the style of Muslim prostration in worship.

(9) “wagging the head,” (moving the top of the head back and forth?) or shaking the head (moving the front of the face from side to side as in the current American gesture?); <em>kinoutes</em> (Gr) means moving in general; so unclear): Mt<strong> 27</strong>:39 = Mk <strong><sup>15</sup></strong>:29; cp. Job <strong><sup>16</sup></strong>:4; Ps <strong><sup>22</sup></strong>:7; <strong><sup>109</sup></strong>:25; Lam <strong><sup>2</sup></strong>:15; Zeph <strong><sup>2</sup></strong>:15; ).

(10) hissing: Jer <strong><sup>19</sup></strong>:8; Zeph <strong><sup>2</sup></strong>:15.

(11) the laying on of hands in connection with appointment to a duty or office; the bestowal of a blessing, the transmission of supernatural gifts, the bestowal of healing

(12) anointing with olive oil in connection with appointment to a duty or office: the appointment of a prophet (), a king ()

(13) using third person for second person as a sign of respect when speaking to a dignitary (Num <strong><sup>27</sup></strong>:15-17)

(in a set with *kinesics, *anointing with oil, *laying on of hands)

<strong>ghost writer</strong>: the person who actually wrote the book even though the name of another person appears as official author. A practice sometimes followed when the subject of the article or book is not particularly skilled in written communication even though his or her life or ideas provide the subject matter for the writing.

Similar to the phraseology “by __________ <em>with</em> _____,” where the second person actually put the article or book together on the basis of information supplied by the first through interviews.

<strong>gibberish</strong>:

<strong>gimel</strong>: the third letter of the Hebrew alphabet: ג

<strong>given name</strong>: the “first” name, which distinguishes one person from others within a related group (in a set with *surname)

<strong>glossary</strong>: a collection of terms belonging to a field of study or thought, usually placed after the body of a book.

<strong>glossolalia</strong>: a “speaking in tongues,” which some Christians equate with ecstatic utterance but others with xenoglossia, as descriptions of the New Testament phenomenon.  (in a set with *ecstatic utterance, *xenoglossia, “speaking in tongues):

<strong>glottals</strong> (in as set with *labials, *dentals, liquids, and *velars)

<strong>glyph</strong>: a carved symbol for a meaning or sound, usually incised/engraved rather than raised; a figure like an arrow or a human on a sign that gives information

<strong>glyptics</strong>: the art of engraving on precious stones

<strong>glyptograph</strong>: an engraving on precious stones

<strong>go-between</strong>: an *intermediary, as a prophet or priest in divine-human relations

<strong>gonzo</strong>: highly unconventional, subjective, exaggerated style in journalism; similar to *yellow journalism

<strong>gospel</strong>: as a literary unit, <em>gospel</em> refers to canonical, uncanonical, and pseudepigraphical (1)accounts of the life of Christ. Over time, a gospel came to be thought of also as (2) a literary form. It was a specialized biography that focused on the years of his ministry and stressed the final week of that ministry. Prior to the beginning of Christ’s ministry, the canonical gospels tell only about events surrounding his birth (Matthew and Luke) and a set of incidents when he was twelve at his dedication in the temple.

Uncanonical and pseudepigraphical gospels tried to fill in the gaps from those earlier years. <em>Gospel</em> thus became a type of literary *genre in post-apostolic times in which to indulge the imaginations of writers and put their own ideas into the mouth of Jesus.

In *lectionaries, gospel refers to (3) a reading from the gospels in contrast to one taken from the epistles.

(in a set with *biography, * autobiography):

<strong>govern/government</strong> is in a set with *concord to form the manner of connection between parts of an expression. Governance refers to what one word requires another to be like. That can happen (1) between a preposition and its object as in <strong>English</strong>, where prepositions require their objects to be in the objective case. <strong>Greek</strong> prepositions require their objects to be in the genitive case (especially if the preposition implies separation), dative case (especially if the preposition implies location), or accusative (especially if the preposition implies destination). <strong>Latin</strong> prepositions call for objects in the ablative case (especially if the preposition implies separation or location) or the accusative case (especially if the preposition implies destination).

Governance also refers to (2) what a verb requires its object to be. After most English, Latin, and Greek verbs the accusative case appears. In some instances where Latin and Greek verbs imply relationship, the “object” is in the dative case, a type of *dative of relationship. In some Latin verbs that imply separation, the “objects” appear in the ablative case. In some instances where the verb affects only some of the object, the genitive case appears. Comparable to “eat of the fruit of the tree.” The Hebrew equivalent of this last construction uses min + maqef. (in a set with *concord)

<strong>grace</strong>: prayer at a meal

<strong>grammar</strong>: (1) collectively, the devices that show the relationship between parts of a sentence.

(2) A book, or text, that describes the workings of a language. An instructional grammar is designed to introduce students to the language; so it sequences aspects of the language for learning purposes. Vocabulary sets and exercise become part of the grammar. Larger items like subjunctive mood will probably get spread out across different chapters. By contrast, a descriptive grammar, or reference grammar, presents in one place everything the author wants to say about each feature of the language.

<strong>grammarian</strong>: one who deals with grammar

<strong>grammatical</strong>: having to do with the syntax and perhaps the inflectional forms of a language (in a set with *syntax, *inflection; * declension, *conjugation)

<strong>grammatical gender</strong>: the gender assigned somewhat arbitrarily to nouns in inflectional languages—and to verbs sometimes. Since adjectives in such languages must agree in gender with their nouns, adjectives also have gender, but not in themselves.

English grammatical gender is almost non-existent except for an occasional usage of <em>she</em> for a ship and the like. <em>She </em>is typically used in Iceland for the volcanoes that are there, and may be so called when brought into English. Masculine and feminine genders usually correspond with male and female sex, but neuter things and abstractions are assigned various genders. Latin, for example, often class abstractions as feminine. Other classifications are correlated with certain nominalizing *morphemes: <em>-al, -e, -ar</em> (neuter), <em>-or, -tor</em> (masculine). Greek is similar in this regard.

*Common gender means that a form lacks gender distinction in an otherewise inflectional set—like English first and second person pronouns and possessive adjectives and third person plural pronouns and possessive adjectives: <em>I, me, my, mine; we, us, our, ours; you, your, yours; they, them, their, theirs; </em>but <em>he, his; she, her, hers; it, its.</em> Hebrew, which uses gender in its second- and third-person verbs, has common gender in first person.

Hebrew has only masculine and feminine (and common) grammatical gender while Greek and Latin have also neuter. As noted above, English has virtually no grammatical gender and so, for the most part, has common nouns.

Grammatical gender gets involved in the translation process when pronouns whose Greek or Latin *antecedents are a different gender from what is appropriate in English. On such case of interest pertains to the Holy Spirit. If a person first concludes for the personality of the Spirit, then <em>He</em> is the appropriate pronoun reference in English even though Greek uses a neuter pronoun is to match the antecedent. Acts <strong><sup>8</sup></strong>:16 refers to the Spirit (neuter) of <strong><sup>8</sup></strong>:15, but the ASV of 1901 says “<em>it</em> fell on none of them,” a matter corrected in the NASB: “<em>He</em> fell on none of them.” Examples of neuter pronouns for the Holy Spirit include Jn <strong><sup>14</sup></strong>:17; <strong><sup>15</sup></strong>:26.

(in a set with *gender; *masculine, *feminine, *neuter, *common; *translation; *capitalization)

<strong>graph</strong>: a letter of the alphabet, the spelling of a word,

<strong>grapheme</strong>: a letter of the *alphabet; all the alternative forms (letters, *digraphs, or *consonant clusters) for representing the same sound/*phoneme. English includes the following:


Consonants

<em>c1</em> [generally before <em>a, o, u</em> as in <em><u>c</u></em><em>oat</em>], <em>k </em>[leukocytopenia]<em>, ch</em> [school], <em>ck </em>[black] for the phoneme <em>/k/</em>

<em>f, ph, gh </em>[enough] for the *phoneme <em>/f/</em>

<em>	g1</em> [generally before <em>a, o, u</em> as in <em><u>g</u></em><em>oat</em>], gg [] for the phoneme <em>/g/</em>

<em>g1</em> [regime], <em>s </em>[measure and with most medial –<em>si- </em>words like <em>deri</em><em><u>s</u></em><em>ion</em>], ss1 [fission] for the phoneme /<em>zh</em>/

<em>h1 </em>[honor]<em>, # </em>[zero symbol, i.e., no letter, but a *glottal stop pronounced as in <em>apple</em>] for the phoneme<em>/א/</em>

<em>j, g1</em> [generally before <em>e, i, y</em> as in <em><u>g</u></em><em>in</em><em><u>g</u></em><em>er</em>] for the phoneme <em>/j/</em>

<em>ch1</em> [church], <em>tch</em> [batch] for the plosive phoneme <em>/ch/</em>

<em>s1 </em>[silent]<em>, ss1 </em>[missing], <em>sc </em>[scythe and all others <em>sc </em>followed by<em> e, i, </em>or <em>y</em>] for the phoneme <em>/s/</em>

<em>sh1, sch </em>[bu]<em>, ss1 </em>[mission]<em>, ti </em>[fiction]<em>, si </em>[]<em>, ci</em> for the phoneme <em>/sh/</em>

<em>t, d1 </em>[ripped]<em>, th </em>[thyme] for the phoneme <em>/t/</em>

<em>	w, # </em>[zero symbol, i.e., no written letter as on _<em>one</em>] for the phoneme <em>/w/</em>

x, cks [racks], ks [] , cs [sacs] for the phoneme <em>/ks/</em>

<em>	y, #</em> [zero symbol, i.e., no written letter as often on the front of words with <em>u-</em> or <em>eu-</em> as in _<em>union</em> and _<em>euphoria</em>] for the phoneme <em>/y/</em>

<em>z, s1</em> [rids and most cases where preceded by a vowel or *voiced consonant] for the phoneme <em>/z/</em>

To this list theoretically could be added most cases of single and doubled consonants: <em>b/bb, c/cc, d/dd, f/ff, g/gg, l/ll, m/mm, n/nn, p/pp, r/rr, s/ss, t/tt, v/vv, z/zz.</em> The qualification, however, is that several of these consonants as singles have other values besides the one they share with their doubles.

(in a set with *pronunciation, *spelling)

<strong>grave accent</strong>: an accent mark that slants up to the left (`). In English spelling the grave accent appears on a few words and phrases of French origin, though often not actually written except in formal settings: à la carte, à la mode, cause célèbre, crèche, pièce de résistance, pied-à-terre, vis-à-vis, voilà.

In Greek spelling, the grave accent represents a fall tone and appears only on the last syllable. (in a set with *acute accent, *circumflex accent; *diacritical marks)

<strong>greater Asclepiad</strong>: kind of meter Wh 337

<strong>greeting</strong>: (a) an address to someone when they meet.

(b) the opening address to the recipient(s) of a letter. Paul, James, Peter, John, Jude begin all their letters in this way: Luke includes his greeting to Theophilus in his introductions to the gospel (<strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:30 and Acts (<strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1): Rom <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1-7; 1 Cor <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1-9; 2 Cor <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1-2; Gal <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1-5; Eph<strong> 1</strong>:1-2; Phil <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1-2; Col <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1-2; 1 Th <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1; 2 Th <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1-2; 1 Tim <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1-2; 2 Tim <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1-2; Tit <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1-4; Phil 1-3; James <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1; 1 Pet <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1-2; 2 Pet <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1-2; 1 Jn <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1-4; 2 Jn 1-3; 3 Jn 1; Jude 1-2; Rev <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:4-7.

(in a set with *introduction, *salutation)

<strong>Grimm’s Law</strong>: a pattern of consonant change from Indo-European to Germanic correspondents. In general, the alterations stay within the same pronunciation points: labial, dentals, and velars. The changes mean that <em>p, t, k</em> became <em>f, th, h</em> (voiceless stops became voiceless fricative); <em>b, d, g</em> became <em>p, t, k</em> (voiced stops lost voicing); and <em>bh,dh, gh</em> became <em>b, d, g. </em>

(voiced fricatives became voiced stops)

<strong>“groaner”</strong>: a bad joke (but not *risqué), a *Tom Swifty, often taking advantage of the multiple meanings of a word (in a set with *Tom Swifty, *double meaning,)

<strong>guideword</strong>: the words at the top of a dictionary page that show the first and last word entered alphabetically on that set of pages; guidewords make it faster and easier to see whether the desired word falls on those pages

<strong>gutturals</strong>: Hebrew <em>aleph, he, heth, ayin</em>

<strong>hackneyed</strong>: an trite, overused expression that marks the speaker as uneducated

<strong>Haggadah</strong>: the part of the Talmud that deals with historical material in contrast to legal concerns

<strong>Hagiographa</strong>: “holy writings”; one of the three basic divisions of Old Testament literature: “The Writings” along with “the Law” and “the Prophets.” Same as the Hebrew term <em>Chetubim</em>.

(in a set with *Torah, *Nebiim )

<strong>Hagiography</strong>: literature, often idealizing, about the lives of saints

<strong>handbook</strong>: a *manual brief summary of subject matter associated with a special field of operation (in a set with *manual)

<strong>handle</strong>: the name or expression used by Cbers to identify themselves.

(in a set with *call letters)

<strong>hapax legomenon/hapax legomena</strong>: once-used word within some category, whether in all the literature of that language, in a certain work, in a certain writer, in a given time frame, and the like. [Gr once + said]

<strong>haplography</strong>: an omission, especially of repeated elements, a kind of copyist error

(in a set with homoioteleuton, dittography, metathesis as types of scribal errors)

<strong>haplology</strong>: the dropping of a repeated element in a word. An examples is <em>cephalgia</em> as a short for <em>cephalalgia</em>, the more original word being comprised of <em>cephal</em>- (“head”) and –<em>algia, a </em>suffix for pain. Latin has <em>nutrix from nutritrix</em>, (“nurse”).

<strong>harangue</strong>:

<strong><u>he</u></strong>: the fifth letter of the Hebrew alphabet

<strong><u>he</u></strong><strong> directional/directive</strong>: the old Hebrew accusative ending –<em>ah</em> appended to an appropriate noun can signal direction toward, as with <em>mitsraim</em><em><u>ah</u></em> for “toward Egypt.”

<strong><u>he</u></strong><strong> interrogative</strong>: prefixed to the front of the first word of the sentence to show that a questiion is being asked.

<strong><u>He</u></strong><strong> locale</strong>: in Hebrew language study, an older designation for direction. (*He directive)

<strong>heading</strong> (In a set with *title)

<strong>headline</strong>: a summarizing set of words over an article in a newspaper, magazine, or other *ephemeral paper

<strong>heavy endings</strong>: in Hebrew, the pronominal endings for second and third person plural verbs and possession

<strong>Hebraism</strong>: a distinctive manner of expression found only in Hebrew, but translated word-for-word into Greek or English. (in a set with *Hellenism, *Latinism, *Aramaism, *Anglicism) Dana & Mantey, Manual Grammar, pp. 14-15.

<strong>hellenism</strong> (in a set with *Hebraism, *Aramaism, *Latinism, *Anglicism)

<strong>helping verb</strong>: another term for *auxiliary verb, separate words that combine with the verb in contrast to inflectional changes to the verb itself

<strong>hendecasyllabic</strong>: having eleven syllables

<strong>hendiadys</strong>: a figure of speech in which appear two nouns, two entries, two *word pictures for the same thing.  It is kind of “acceptable” redundancy. So, two nouns joined by <em>and</em> say what could be said by an adjective-noun combination. NT examples.

Hendiadys raises the well-known question in Gen<strong> 1</strong>:26 as to what the difference is between the imago dei and the <em>similitudo dei</em>. Whereas it may be possible to imagine some plausible distinction between the two phrases, they may be nothing more than two words pictures for the same general idea of likeness to God.

<strong>hermeneutics</strong>: a more formal word for interpretation that includes the discipline of *interpretation as such as well as the process of integration that carries all the way up to *worldview. (in a set with *authorial intent, *interpretation)

<strong>het/heth</strong>: the eighth letter of the Hebrew alphabet: ח

<strong>hexameter</strong>: a line of verse containing six metrical feet

(in a set with *trimeter, *tetrameter, pentameter)

<strong>hieratic</strong>: an offshoot of hieroglyphics (in a set with *demotic)

<strong>hieroglyph</strong>: a pictograph; a pictorial symbol that represents meaning or sounds [Gr hieros, holy + glyphein, to carve]

<strong>High Priestly Prayer, The</strong>: The extended prayer of Jesus recorded in Jn <strong><sup>17</sup></strong>.

<strong>hint</strong>: a kind of implication that deliberately requires the listener to infer some further point. Hint differs from *innuendo and *insinuation by having a neutral connotation; it differs from *slam and *cut by being indirect.

<strong>historical linguistics</strong>: Historical linguistics looks at the preceding (and succeeding stages of a language’s development as a way perhaps of elucidating seemingly aberrant features of the language. *Comparative linguistics shows the similarities between contemporary related languages. *Descriptive linguistics focuses on a certain stage of a language without concerning itself with historical backgrounds or other contemporary related languages.

(in a set with *comparative linguistics, *descriptive linguistics)

<strong>historical present</strong>: a present tense verb used for relating a past event. Also called *vivid narration: “Last week I was walking along the railroad tracks, and this six-foot-long black snake crawls across the gravel about five yards ahead of me, and starts over one of the rails just before a freight train comes barreling down on us.”

<strong>history</strong> (in a set with *storie, *saga, *epic, *historie, *novel, *novelle, *geschichte)

<strong>homily: </strong>a sermon, a presentation in a religious setting (in a set with *sermon, *sermon types ,  ,  ,  )

<strong>homiletics</strong>: the discipline of sermon preparation.

<strong>homoioteleuton</strong>: a kind of copying error that omits the material between a recurrent element in a segment of text. (in a set with *metathesis, *haplography, *dittography as types of *scribal errors)

<strong>homolegoumena</strong>: books of the Bible that everyone accepted as canonical in the early years of the church

<strong>homograph</strong>: a word spelled like another word but with different pronunciation and different meaning, a two-pronunciation word: <em>abuse</em>, <em>aged</em>, <em>attribute</em>, <em>bases</em>, <em>bass</em>, <em>blessed</em>, <em>boule,bow1, 2, 3/bowed/bower</em>, <em>buffet</em>, <em>business</em>, <em>close </em>(v, adj)<em>, closer </em>(n, adj), <em>concrete</em>, <em>content</em>, <em>contrary</em>, <em>crooked</em>, <em>desert</em>, <em>divers, do</em>, <em>does</em>, <em>document</em>, <em>dogged/doggedly</em>, <em>dove</em>, <em>drawer</em>, <em>excess, excuse</em>, <em>expose</em>, <em>fine</em>, <em>fragment, glower</em>, <em>grease, house </em>(n, v), <em>implement</em>, <em>infinite</em>, <em>insurance</em>, <em>invalid</em>, <em>lead</em>, <em>learned</em>, <em>legged, live,</em> <em>lives, minute</em>, <em>moment</em>, <em>moped</em>, <em>mouse </em>(-s or –z), <em>mouth</em>, <em>mow/mowed/mower</em>, <em>number1, nun (Hebrew letter), offence</em>, <em>overage</em>, <em>pan, peaked</em>, <em>perfected, </em>(<em>Polish/polish</em>), <em>read</em>, <em>refuse, regress, route, row, separate, sewer, shower, (sine/sine), sow, striped, supplement, tender, tear, tier/tier, tow, tower, use, wicked, wind, windy, wound. </em>Note also <em>decent/descent</em>.

A large group of words ending in –<em>ate</em> can have a verb—always in –āte—and a corresponding adjective or agent-noun in –ăte. <strong>[insert panel-also under pronunciation]</strong> <em>advocate</em>, <em>aggregate</em>, <em>animate, approximate</em>, <em>articulate</em>, <em>associate</em>, <em>consummate</em>, <em>coordinate, correlate</em>, <em>delegate, deliberate</em>, <em>deviate</em>, <em>document</em>, <em>duplicate</em>, <em>elaborate</em>, expatriate, <em>graduate</em>, <em>initiate</em>, <em>intimate</em>, <em>moderate</em>, <em>officiate, palp, pan1, 2, 3, personate, precipitate</em>, <em>predicate</em>, <em>profligate</em>,<em> regenerate,</em> <em>ruminate</em>, <em>separate</em>, <em>streaked</em>, <em>subordinate, syndicate</em>.

The –<em>ate</em> with adjectives and nouns may have a long <em>a</em> if there is no related verb. <strong>[insert panel-also under pronunciation]</strong>

Many two- or three-syllable words can be verbs or nouns, spelled the same way but often accented on the first syllable as nouns or adjectives and on the second syllable for verbs: <strong>[insert panel-pronunciation]</strong> <em>absent</em>, <em>addict</em>, <em>address</em>, <em>appropriate, attribute</em>, <em>cement</em>, <em>collect</em>, <em>combat</em>, <em>combine</em>, <em>commune</em>, <em>compact</em>, <em>console</em>, <em>construct</em>, <em>consummate, content</em>, <em>contest</em>, <em>converse</em>, <em>convert</em>, <em>convict</em>, <em>defect, desert</em>, <em>digest</em>, <em>entrance</em>, <em>envelope</em>, <em>impact</em>, <em>import</em>, <em>incline</em>, <em>increase</em>, <em>intimate</em>, <em>liver, object</em>, <em>perfect</em>, <em>pervert, predicate, present</em>, <em>proceed, produce</em>,<em> progress, project</em>, <em>rebel</em>, <em>recall</em>, <em>record</em>, <em>refuse</em>, <em>regress, reject</em>, <em>reprint, retread, separate</em>, (<em>shit</em>), <em>subject</em>, <em>suspect, transfer</em>,

(in a set with *homonym, *homophone)

<strong>homonym</strong>: a word sounding like another word with the same spelling but different meaning

<em>aircraft, alert, beat</em>, <em>bid</em>, <em>bison</em>, <em>bit/bit</em>, <em>bound, burst</em>, <em>clad</em>, <em>cost</em>, <em>craft</em>, <em>cut</em>, <em>deer</em>, <em>drove</em>, <em>elk, felt, fish</em>, <em>flounder</em>, <em>founder,</em> <em>fry</em>, <em>ground</em>, <em>hit/hit</em>, <em>hurt</em>, <em>knit</em>, <em>let</em>, <em>lit/lit</em>, <em>live</em>, <em>luster, miss</em>, <em>mouse, muff, muffle, mug, mule, mull, mum, mummy, mush, nag, nap1, 2, 3, 4, nave, net, nit, nix, nog, noodle, novel, nun, pant, parr </em>(sg & pl),<em> peck, pen, perch, peer, pet, put, quit</em>, <em>rid</em>, <em>rifle, rose</em>, <em>saw, set</em>, <em>shed</em>, <em>sheep</em>, [shit], <em>sounder,</em> <em>spit</em>, <em>split</em>, <em>spread</em>, <em>stern</em>, <em>stole, stranger</em>, <em>swallow, sweat, tales, tote, trailer</em>, <em>wet</em>, <em>wilt, you/ your </em>(sg $ pl)<em>, . </em>

Depending on how broadly <em>homonym</em> is defined, there is a large number of places in Latin and Greek where the same form within a word covers more than one place in the inflection. Latin and Greek both use the same form for nominative, vocative, and accusative on neuter words. In Latin the nominative plural and genitive and dative singular are the same in the first declension (-<em>ae</em>). Second-declension –us nouns have the same ending for nominative plural and genitive singular (-<em>i</em>). The same pattern applies to fourth declension masculines with the accusative plural also the same -<em>ūs</em>. Fifth declension nominative singular and plural are alike with the accusative plural also the same (-<em>ēs</em>). Third declension masculines and feminines use the same ending for nominative and accusative plural (–<em>ēs</em>). Except for the singular of second declension –<em>us</em> nouns, which use –<em>e</em> for vocative, vocatives for nouns in any gender are the same as the nominative, -<em>ius</em> nouns and -<em>mi</em> excepted (-i).  Dative and ablative plurals are always the same. Third conjugation first singular verbs are the same in future indicative and present subjunctive (<em>faciam</em>, <em>e.g.</em>). Third declension adjectives are the same for masculine and feminine.

Greek has about the same array of common forms within inflections.

With a broad definition of <em>homonym</em>, English homonyms are much more frequent because of the paucity of distinctive inflectional forms to distinguish case, gender, number, tense, voice,<em> etc</em>., in nouns, adjectives, and verbs. English’s high number of feeder languages creates the same end product beginning at entirely different starting points.

(in a set with *homonym, *homophone)

<strong>homophone</strong>: a word that has the same sound as another with different spelling and different meanings
