waist/waste, wait/weight, waive/wave, waiver/waver, walk/wok, wand/wanned, want/wont, war/wore, ward/warred, ware/wear, warn/worn, waive/wave, wart/wort, watt/wot), wattle/waddle, waver/waiver, way/weigh, ward/warred, ware/wear, wares/weirs?, weak/week, weave/we’ve, we’d/weed/we’ld, we’ll/weal, which/witch, whoa/woe, who’s/whose, wind1/wined, winds/winze, wood/would, yack/yak, y’all.yawl, yawn/yon, yawned/yond, yoke/yolk, yore/your/you’re, you’ll/yule, </em>

(in a set with *homograph, *homonym)

<strong>honorific</strong>: a title standing alone or put ahead of a person’s name to indicate respect, especially in reference to a social superior. These may be stand-alone terms like Your Honor, Reverend, Doctor; or titles of public office (Mayor Johnson, Senator Riggs, President Lincoln; or to the *titular system of countries that have such a system (Lord Mills, Queen Mary)

<strong>hortatory</strong>: the adjective for exhort/exhortation in contrast to narration or explanation

<strong>hybrid verb</strong>:  a verb whose principal parts come from different roots. Also called *suppletive verb.

<strong>hymn</strong> (in a set with *ode, *paean): a song of praise or gratitude to God

<strong>hype</strong>: exaggerated praise or claims of greatness, importance, and the like. Hype can show up in advertisements, which often exaggerate need and the advertised solution to need.

<strong>hyphen/hyphenation</strong>: a one-space horizontal line used (1) to join two words into one whole. It is used to join words, for instance, that together make a unit of modification attached to the next word: a superlative-degree adjective. In this example, superlative modifies degree and the two in turn taken together modify the next term. To say “a community-church building” is not the same thing as to say “a community church-building.” A hyphen helps the eye distinguish, say, a noun (stick-up) from a phrase (stick up the bank). Consistency is hard to discern in the hyphenation pattern for two words, hyphenated words, and joined words. It is really a matter of time where the left-to-right stages of the pattern has developed to.

It may make a difference in hyphenation where the word set appears: “That is a big-time win for us” (attributive adjective) <em> vs.</em> “That win was big time for us” (predicate adjective).

Hyphens are not used to join adverbs to adjectives that in turn modify nouns: “a quickly written note.” Such a set of words usually stand out well enough not to confuse a reader. Sometimes an adjective-noun combination makes such an obvious combination that a reader’s eye connects the two words naturally without using a hyphen: a second declension noun, a First Temple sarcophagus.

(2) Hyphens also mark the break in a word that is too long to fit on the line of writing.

(3) In telephone numbers, a hyphen separates the exchange from the specific number: 539-5031.

(4) A similar usage applies to lengthened area codes: 66502-2314.

(5) Finally, a hyphen appears as part of the standard spelling of words made up of two parts, especially if not using the hyphen would create a diphthong or unpronounceable letter combination: <em>co-operation, pick-up</em> [truck], <em>co-ordinating, semi-colon, salpingo-oophorectomy.</em> Since custom drives usage, words that were formerly hyphenated are now considered one word to the extent that the hyphen is considered no longer needed: <em>cooperate</em>. Writing conventions sometimes take a while to catch up with the spoken realities. For instance, <em>rain check</em> has the same accent and pitch pattern as <em>homework, rainbow, railroad,</em> <em>etc.</em>, but dictionaries still list it as two words. About all a writer can do is consult a dictionary to see how far convention has carried a particular word.

(in a set with *dash, *dieresis).

<strong>hypotactic/hypotaxis</strong>: the pattern of subordinating clauses and phrases to other clauses and phrases rather than making them grammatically parallel: “She stayed and helped with the clean-up”; <em> vs.</em> “She stayed to help with the clean-up.” NT ex. Acts 5:19 (in a set with *paratactic; note also *asyndeton)

<strong>iambic pentameter</strong> (note other forms of poetic *meter)

<strong>iambic trimeter wh 343</strong>

<strong>iambic dimeter wh 217</strong>

<strong>ibidem</strong>: a Latin term that means “in the same place” as the previous reference. (abbreviated ibid. in *footnote/* and endnote references

<strong>identical rhyme</strong>: the full repeat of the word: home, home (get other examples) (in a set with *exact rhyme, *eye rhyme, *imperfect rhyme, *rime riche, *suspended rhyme)

<strong>idiom</strong>: an expression that carries a meaning not easily associated with the normal meaning of its words and a meaning that would not convey its idea if translated by word picture into another language. English ex. “ I don’t give a hoot.” The meaning of an idiom lies at the level of the whole expression instead of at the level of its combined constituent parts.

<strong>if-clause</strong>: the dependent part of any *conditional sentence: “<em>If he crosses the bridge, </em>he breaks the treaty.” Same as *protasis.

<strong>illative</strong>: a continuation of an idea without exactly involving the precise meaning of the connecting word: inerrancy is the word of God written and therefore inerrant in the autographs. It is one thing to say that because scripture is the word of God written, it will have no mistakes in it; it is another thing to say that in keeping with the fact that scripture is the word of God written, it is inerrant. One causes (causal), the other correlates (illative); <em>therefore </em>can be used both ways<em>.</em>

<strong>illiterate</strong><em>: </em>unable to read (in a set with *literate)

<strong>immediate future</strong>: a verb construction for action in the *near future. Same as *near future. (in a set with *distant future).

<strong>immediate past</strong>: a verb construction for recent action. (in a set with *distant past). Same as *near past.

<strong>immediate prohibition</strong>: Hebrew distinguishes immediate prohibition from *permanent prohibition by using lo + imperfect (rather than al + shortened imperfect). (in a set with *permanent prohibition):

<strong>imperatival future</strong>: the future tense used to express a command. In English such a usage often has an intensive value: “Yes, you will.” Particularly under the influence of Hebrew idiom, the future indicative in New Testament Greek may carry an imperatival force: Acts<strong> 1</strong>:8.

In Latin the same possibility existed: “you shall not steal.” (L, ex. Wh 319, 320)

<strong>imperatival infinitive</strong>: an infinitive that has the value of a command:

<strong>imperatival participle</strong>: In Greek, a participle that has the value of a command. The Great Commission says, strictly, “Having gone, make-disciples.” An aorist participle depending on an imperative ends up also being imperatival. See possible imperatival <strong>participles in Romas .</strong>

<strong>imperative mood</strong>: The imperative mood gives a command, advice, request, or entreaty.

Additionally, there are some cases of idiomatic usages: Jesus says, “Destroy this temple, and in three days I will rebuild it” (). Paul says, “Be angry, and do not sin” ().

The idea of “permissive imperative,” identified in some New Testament Greek grammars, may misunderstand the “let” idea as “allow” in English. “Let” doesn’t mean that in English idiom either.

In Greek the imperative second plural always has the same form as the corresponding indicative.

(in a set with *indicative, *subjunctive, *optative, and *infinitive moods; *commands)

<strong>imperative sentence</strong>: a sentence that gives a command—uses the imperative mood—in contrast to simply declaring it, asking about it, or emotionally saying it: “It’s late, John; go on home.” (in a set with *declarative sentence, *exclamatory sentence, *interrogative sentence)

<strong>imperfect rhyme</strong>: Only some of the sounds match: cat, cot; hop, cup. Get other examples.  Also called *near rhyme. (in a set with *eye rhyme, *perfect rhyme)

<strong>imperfect tense</strong>: In Latin and Greek, the imperfect tense is a past tense that looks at action as being in motion, as motion picture <em> vs.</em> snap shot, linear <em> vs.</em> *punctiliar, *progressive <em> vs.</em> *factitive. Linear can be continuous, iterative, *characteristic, *progressive, *cyclical, *tendential/*conative.

The imperfect tense can be translated <em>was working </em>(<em>progressive action</em>), <em>would work </em>(characteristic action), <em>tried to work</em> (*conative/*tendential action), <em>used to work</em> (action no longer occurring).

In Latin, the imperfect tense also covers *inceptive/*inchoative/*ingressive action in past time, an aspect associated with the *aorist tense in Greek.

Since Hebrew does not have tense, strictly speaking, but completed <em> vs.</em> uncompleted action, its “imperfect” tense corresponds more closely with the future tense: <em>will work</em>, <em>will be working</em>, <em>etc.</em>

(in a set with *present tense, *past tense, *aorist tense, *future tense, *perfect tense, *past perfect tense/*pluperfect tense, *future perfect tense)

<strong>impersonal verb</strong>: a verb that regularly is used only in the third person singular because it typically uses an infinitive as its subjects and is typically translated into English with the expletive it: Greek <em>dei</em> (“it is necessary”); <em>chree</em> (“it ought”); Latin: <em>licet</em> (“it is permitted”).

<strong>improper diphthong</strong>: a Greek diphthong comprised of a long vowel plus iota. Such an iota does not affect pronunciation, a fact shown by subscripting it under the long vowel. If, however, the long vowel gets capitalized, the iota comes back up on the line after it and any accents or breathing marks are written ahead of the capital: Hadeees.

<strong>imply/implicatio</strong>n<strong>: </strong>an unsaid idea that follows from what has actually been said. A speaker implies; the hearer infers. (in a set with infer/inference, innuendo)

<strong>inceptive action</strong>: an aspect of verbal action that refers to the start of an action. Same as *inchoative and *ingressive.   approaching something wh 312 = ingressive?

<strong>inchoative action</strong>: an aspect of verbs that refers to the beginning of an action; also called *ingressive or *inceptive action.

(in a set with *continuous action, *cyclical action, *iterative action, *linear action, *progressive action)

<strong>inclusive language</strong>: the attempt to avoid terminology that uses grammatical gender in situations that are not specifically male or female. The practice sets aside the pattern descending from Hebrew and the classical languages of masculine taking preference in mixed or ambiguous situations. In part, the perceived difficulty is a function of English’s loss of grammatical gender; so all that is left for pronoun usage is sex reference. To remedy the situation, writers and speakers resort to saying “he or she” or even alternating <em>he</em> and <em>she</em> in the same segment of writing. The first procedure seems awkward; the second creates ambiguity and grates on expectancy. Returning to the use of the indefinite pronoun <em>one</em> resurrects the stilted connotation that word has picked up. “A person” may work better. At least in deference to feminist sensitivities, some kind of restructuring or *circumlocution seems preferable to the first three.

Inclusive language has been brought into speech about the Judaeo-Christian God. Although there are a few cases where he is pictured as fulfilling a role normally associated with female, He is revealed to us, and talked about, as male despite the obvious point that God, being spirit, is neither male nor female. That matter is in stark contrast to, and surely deliberately different from, the conceptualizations of deity/deities in surrounding Near Eastern cultures. It is clear, then, that the reason for usage is to indicate something besides sexuality. That reason must have to do with projecting greater power and final authority. The issue comes down, then, to agreeing or disagreeing with the fundamental idea that there is absolute equality between men and women as to roles, a viewpoint that scripture denies in favor of division of labor and complementary roles. That point is different from equality of worth—unless a person is prepared to claim that being higher in authority means greater in worth.

At any rate, in the translation of scritpture, it is the translator’s role to translate what the text says rather than to make it say what the translator prefers to have it say. (in a set with *politically correct)

<strong>incorporative language</strong>: a language that compounds sentence parts into one unit. (in a set with *inflectional language, *isolating language, *agglutinative language)

<strong>indeclinable: </strong>In inflected languages, a noun or adjective without case forms like the other words do: Latin examples include centum (adj., hundred),nihil (n. nothing), satis (n., enough), necesse (adj., necessary). English nouns and adjectives are not declinable except for the apostrophe “s” for possessive case. Since that general feature exists in the language, speakers get tangled up in the use of pronouns, which are still declinable for the most part. As a result, people misuse pronouns in places like the compound object of prepositions: “ between you and I” instead of “between you and me.” As evidence that declension is continuing to disappear from English, note the current practice of avoiding whom lest the phraseology sound pedantic: “Who are you talking to?” instead of “To whom are you talking?”

In the New Testament, Greek brings most of the Hebrew names over as indeclinable nouns. The Greek infinitive also can be called an indeclinable noun; if its case is important to identify, the neuter singular article may accompany it.

Greek has the indeclinable adjective perix (Acts<strong> 5</strong>:16).

Latin has the indeclinable adjective <em>quot</em> (how many).

<strong>indefinite article</strong>: In English, <em>a/an</em> is used before consonants and vowels respectively to indicate one of something where there is no specific example in mind. The <em>a</em> form of the indefinite article is pronounced “uh,” not <em><u>ā</u></em>, and is treated much like a *proclitic, pronounced with a light accent and attached to the next word.

Latin does not have articles, although <em>unus</em> sometimes approaches that value.

Greek article vs no-article does not distinguish between definite and indefinite, but between quality and identity. However, <em>heis/mia/hen</em> seems to approach the value of an indefinite article in some New Testament cases: Mt <strong><sup>9</sup></strong>:18; <strong><sup>12</sup></strong>:11; <strong><sup>25</sup></strong>:60; Acts <strong><sup>28</sup></strong>:

(in a set with *article, *definite article, *determiner)

<strong>indefinite pronoun</strong>: someone, something; anyone, anything; “a person”; one; “you” used advisedly; “they” with indefinite reference: “Well, that’s what they say.”

<strong>indefinite relative pronoun</strong>: whoever, whomever, whosever [whosoever], whatever [whatsoever]

<strong>indentation</strong>: a spacing to the right in the first line of a paragraph, traditionally a half-inch. English customarily calls for six spaces in writing/typing/printing.

<strong>independent</strong>: said of clauses that carry the main thought of the sentence, a clause to which other clauses grammatically attach. Same as insubordinate. (in a set with *dependent)

<strong>independent clause</strong>: a clause that can standing alone because it is conceived of as being complete—as making sense without further explanation. Also called an *insubordinate clause.

<strong>independent verb</strong>: *same as main verb or *leading verb in a *complex sentence; the verb in a clause that can stand by itself .

(in a set with *main verb, *leading verb ; *dependent verb)

<strong>independent nominative</strong>: same as parenthetic nominative, where the nominative noun does not serve as subject of any verb.

<strong>index</strong>: a unit in the back of a book that lists items within the work as a whole: scripture references cited, *excerpts or quotations from ancient authors cited (as NT quotations of OT texts), *allusions and verbal parallels to the Old Testament found in the New, manuscripts consulted as in the original text of the Bible, abbreviations used, pages where various specific subjects are covered,<em> etc</em>.

<strong>indicative mood</strong>: the *mood of a verb that simply “indicates” action in contrast to commanding action (*imperative mood) or “suggesting” action. *contingent action (*subjunctive mood), or—as in Greek—uncertain action (*optative mood). The last mood is missing in English and Latin. So its values get absorbed into the subjunctive in Latin or covered by *circumlocution. Likewise, the subjunctive has virtually disappeared from English, its usages being absorbed into the indicative.

(in a set with *imperative mood, *subjunctive mood, *optative mood, *infinitive mood)

<strong>indictment</strong>

<strong>indirect /imperative/command</strong>: The dative-case noun or pronoun in the main clause is the same referent as the subject of the subjunctive verb in the dependent cluase. Copnsequently, English can translate the construction with an infinitive phrase: <strong>example </strong>Several Latin verbs can be followed by *jussive clauses in the subjunction: <em>hortor, impero, moneo, oro, persuado, peto, quaero, rogo</em> (as well sometimes as <em>malo, nolo, volo</em>).  WH ch. 36 314#123 334#20 (in a set with *indirect discourse, *indirect question, *indirect statement)

<strong>indirect discourse</strong>: a dependent construction that gives the idea in a speaker’s original wording in contrast to *direct discourse, which gives the purported words that were used to say something. Depending on how the term is used, “indirect discourse” can make a statement or ask a question (*direct question).  Not every verb can introduce indirect discourse. It follows verbs of speaking (say, tell), mental action (hope, think, believe, suppose), and the senses (feel, see, hear, sense).

English usually uses a finite verb, but it can use an infinitive if the subject in the indirect discourse part is the same as that of the main verb. English uses that to introduce indirect discourse, but that is often omitted: “My son said ( ) he was going to take a nap.”

Since Latin uses an infinitive construction for *indirect statement, it needs to be categorized separately from *indirect question within indirect discourse; it is, strictly speaking, a phrase in that language.

Classical Greek likewise could use participles and infinitives in addition to *finite verbs, depending on the identity of the leading verb; those cases should be classified under phrases as well. New Testament Greek usually used a finite verb with hoti as the introductory conjunction. 	(in a set with *indirect statement, *indirect question; *direct statement, *direct question, *quotation)

<strong>indirect object</strong>: the person or thing to whom or for whom the verb operates on the *direct object. Consequently, there can be no indirect object without a direct object. If <em>to</em> appears where no direct object exists, it does not indicate an indirect object: “I went <em>to</em> town”; I was bored <em>to</em> tears”; “I was important <em>to</em> him”; “I raised the bid <em>to</em> $10”; “I wanted <em>to</em> go.”

The indirect object in English is indicated by the preposition <em>to</em> if it comes after the direct object. If it precedes direct object, <em>to</em> does not appear: “she gave <em>the poor dog</em> a bone”; or “she gave a bone <em>to the poor dog</em>.”

In Latin and Greek, the dative case without a preposition indicates the indirect object. Hebrew uses the *inseparable preposition ﬥ prefixed to a noun or a pronominal suffix to show indirect object: he directive.

(in a set with *direct object)

<strong>indirect question</strong>: a dependent grammatical structure that conveys the idea that was in a direct question rather than the very words originally used. The leading verb will mean <em>ask</em>, <em>question</em>, <em>wonder</em>, <em>tell</em>. It can be introduced by interrogatives (how, when, who/what/which, why), by whether/if, and the like. To introduce the dependent construction, Greek uses introductory interrogatives and ei (if/whether). Latin likewise uses interrogatives (<em>cur, quam, quando, qui/quae/quod, quis/quid, ubi, unde), uter, utrum . . . an</em>), -<em>ne</em> (on the first word of the dependent clause). Latin uses subjunctive mood on the verb inside the indirect question.

(in a set with *indirect statement, *indirect imperative/command)

<strong>indirect reflexive</strong>: WH315#170

<strong>indoctrinate/indoctrination</strong>: to teach someone, usually with the negative implication that the teaching is done in such a way as not to allow the student to know or understand and evaluate alternative viewpoints (in a set with *doctrine)

<strong>ineffable:</strong> Not able to be uttered because of its great dignity; applied to the *tetragrammaton, the four-consonant personal name for the Hebrew God. Based on the commandment not to take the Lord’s name in vain, a Jewish tradition developed of not saying the name at all. Since the Hebrew alphabet had consonants only, the original vocalization was lost. The New Testament follows the *Septuagint in not challenging this custom, using Lord (<em>kyrios</em>) where the personal name of God would appear. That choice of substitution coincides with the Hebrew practice of saying “my Lord” (<em>adonai</em>) instead of God’s proper name when the tetragrammaton appears in the Old Testament text. See also *Jehovah.

<strong>inerrancy</strong>: a term used in Christian circles for the belief that scripture does not err relative to the intended content of scripture as such. As such, it is not meant to include a reader’s misinterpretation, scribes’ transmissional errors, mistakes of form or grammar, substitution of synonyms (spelling, diction, <em>etc.</em>).

(in a set with *dictation theory of inspiration, *dynamic inspiration, *infallibility, *plenary inspiration, verbal inspiration)

<strong>infallible</strong>: a view of the nature of scripture originally synonymous with *inerrant. With the rise of the “inerrancy debate in the late twentieth century, infallible shifted toward meaning “able to accomplish that whereunto it was sent” (cp. Is <strong><sup>55</sup></strong>:10-11). That more functional emphasis left open the question of whether there might be errors of content in the Bible that in fact everyone has to use—transmitted, conflated, and the like. it would not, in this view, have to be accurate in certain kinds of information—science, history, geography—that lay outside the purpose of divine revelation: human-divine behavioral relationship. Such a view has been criticized for putting the nature of scripture beyond confirmation, beyond the realm of verifiability and falsifiability.

<strong>infallibility</strong>:  Infallibility pictures inspiration as an operation relative the purpose of revelation, which entails God’s word not returning to him void, but fulfilling the purpose for which he sent it (Is <strong><sup>55</sup></strong>:10-11). Matters of science, history, geography could conceivably lie outside this purpose and therefore not be subject to the *inerrancy principle. <em>*Plenary inspiration</em> and <em>*inerrancy</em> are terms invoked to contrast with this looser definition.

(in a set with *dictation theory of inspiration, *dynamic inspiration, *plenary inspiration, *verbal inspiration; *inerrancy)

<strong>infer/inference</strong>: the drawing of a further idea out of what someone has said. The hearer infers; the speaker *implies. (in a set with *imply/implication, *innuendo)

<strong>infinitives</strong>: Infinitives are “infinite” in that they are not limited by person. They are not *inflected except for voice and tense. In verb *paradigms, they parallel *moods and *participles. Infinitives can fill *noun slots, adjective slots, or adverb slots. If infinitives have subjects, the subjects are in *accusative case: “Dad wants <em>me</em> to play taps on Memorial Day.” Infinitives can have all the *verbal adjuncts used with *finite verbs: adverbial modifiers (to go <em>soon</em>; to go <em>to town</em>), *direct objects (“Dad wants me to play <em>taps</em>”), *indirect objects (“He went to give <em>the cows</em> some hay”), *objective complement (“They tried to make Jesus <em>king</em>”), and *retained object (“They wanted Jesus to be made <em>king</em>”).

English infinitives usually have <em>to</em> as their accompanying sign. Certain constructions, however, omit it: “Let me <em>do</em> it”; “she may <em>go</em> home”; “Could/would/should they <em>come</em> over tomorrow?”

Participles also are infinite in that they do not have person, but they differ from infinitives in having number-gender-case form in Greek and Latin and thereby attach to nouns as modifiers.

(in a set with *complementary infinitive, *split infinitive, *imperatival infinitive, *finite verb, *participle, *gerund)

<strong>infix</strong>: a change in the middle of words that changes the meaning. English infixes can make some nouns plural, change the tense of verbs, or create cognate words. (Entered here by sound rather than spelling since some words like <em>break/broke </em>have or pick up a non-syllabic –<em>e</em> or other feature in spelling.)

Noun plurals: <em>foot/feet</em>; <em>goose/geese</em>; <em>man/men</em>; <em>mouse/mice</em>; <em>woman/women</em>; <em>this/these</em> (omitted here are –<em>is</em> words that become plural by –<em>es</em>; the –<em>is</em> is an ending rather than part of the root)

Verb tenses: <em>abide/abode</em>, <em>bear/bore</em>(<em>bare</em>); <em>beget/begot(begat), begin/began</em>; <em>behold/beheld, bid/(bade</em>), <em>bind/bound</em>; <em>bite/bit</em>; <em>blow/blew</em>, <em>break/broke</em>; <em>breed/bred</em>; <em>break/broke</em>, <em>breath/breathe, </em>(<em>bring/brang</em>), <em>choose/chose</em>; <em>cleave/clove</em> (or <em>cleaved/cleft</em>; formerly <em>clave</em> as in KJV Gen. 34:3), <em>come/came</em>; <em>dig/dug</em>; <em>dive/dove</em>; <em>draw/drew</em>; <em>drink/drank/drunk</em>; <em>drive/drove</em>; <em>fall/fell</em>; <em>feed/fed</em>, <em>freeze/froze</em>; <em>fight/fought</em>; <em>find/found</em>; <em>forbid/forbad//forbade, forget/forgot</em>; <em>forgive/forgave</em>; <em>forsake/forsook, freeze/froze</em>, <em>get/got</em>(<em>gat</em>); <em>grind/ground</em>, <em>give/gave</em>; <em>grow/grew</em>; <em>hang/hung</em>; <em>heave/hove</em>; <em>hide/hid</em>; <em>hold/held</em>; <em>know/knew</em>; <em>lead/led</em>; <em>leave/left</em>, <em>light/lit</em>; <em>may/might</em> (<em>maghd>might</em>); <em>meet/met</em>; <em>misread/misread, mistake/mistook, rēad/read</em>/; <em>ride/rode</em>; <em>ring/rang/rung</em>; <em>rise/rose</em>; <em>run/ran</em>, (<em>see/sawI, seek/sought</em> (<<em>seeghd>seekt>sought</em>); <em>shake/shook</em>, <em>shine/shone</em>, <em>shoot/shot</em>, <em>shrink/shrank/shrunk</em>; <em>shrive/shrove, sing/sang/sung</em>; <em>sink/sank/sunk</em>; <em>sit/sat</em>; <em>slide/slid</em>, <em>smite/smote,</em> <em>sneak/snuck</em>; <em>speak/spoke</em>(<em>spake</em>); <em>speed/sped</em>; <em>spin/spun</em>; <em>spit/spat</em>; <em>spring/spran/sprung</em>; <em>stamp/stomp</em>; <em>stand/stood</em>; <em>steal/stole</em>; <em>stick/stuck</em>; <em>sting/stung</em>; <em>stink/stank/stunk</em>; <em>stride/strode, strike/struck</em>; <em>string/strung</em>; <em>strive/strove, swear/swore</em>(<em>sware</em>); <em>swim/swam</em>/(<em>swum = swimmed</em>); <em>swing/swung</em>; <em>take/took</em>; <em>teach/taught</em> (<<em>teeghd > teacht</em>); <em>tear/tore</em>; <em>tooth/teeth, throw/threw</em>; <em>tread/trod</em>; <em>understand/understood</em>; <em>wake/woke,</em> <em>wear/wore</em>; <em>weave/wove</em>; <em>win/won</em>; <em>wind/wound</em>; wit/wot, <em>wring/wrung</em>; <em>write/wrote</em>.

Sometimes infixes combine with suffixes for tense change: <em>deal/dealt, feel/felt, flee/fled, hear/heard, mean/meant, sell/sold</em>, <em>tell/told, weave/woven</em>. Latin does the same thing going from first to third principal part: <em>venio/vēni,</em>

Cognate words: <em>abide/abode; bind/bond; blew/blown; breathe/breath; deep/depth, died/dead; feed/fed, give/gift (<gived); heat/hot; lift/loft, melt/molten, ride/road; shine/sheen, sing/song, sip/sup/soup?; shoot/shot, sit/seat; stamp/stump; strike/stroke; strip/stripe; sup/soup; thief/theft wind/wīnd</em>

Greek often creates cognate words and principal parts by shift <em>e</em> to <em>o</em>: <em>legoo/logos, pempoo/pepompha, </em>

(in a set with *affix, *epenthesis, *prefix, *suffix)

<strong>inflected languages</strong>: languages that use endings on nouns to indicated their sentence functions, on adjectives to show what they modify, and on verbs to indicate *aspect, *mood, *number, *person, *tense, and perhaps *gender.

<strong>inflection</strong>: Inflection carries a word through its possible forms. It includes what can occur in *declension for *nouns and *adjectives and *conjugation for *verbs. (in a set with *distribution for defining *parts of speech; *indeclinable, *declinable, *declension, *conjugation)

<strong>inflectional language</strong>: a language that indicates the relationship between communication parts by the use of *affixes on roots. (in a set with *inflectional language, *agglutinative language)

<strong>informal term</strong>: an every-day term used by people who does not function in the technical field involved. (in a set with archaism, buzz word, catch phrase, curse word, *formal term, *oath, *obsolete term, *provincialism, *regionalism, *slang, *street talk, *vulgarism)

<strong>informational question</strong>: a form of question that asks for information without implying either an anticipated positive or negative response; a straightforward question, nothing implied, but one that does anticipate response in contrast to *rhetorical question.

(in a set with *question; *rhetorical question; *question anticipating negative response, *question anticipating positive response)

<strong>ingressive action</strong>: the start of an action; also called *inceptive and *inchoative (in a set with *ingressive aorist, *inchoative, *incorporative language, *ingressive imperfect)

<strong>ingressive aorist</strong>: the aorist tense was typically used to cover past-tense ingressive action in Greek. Acts 3:8 seems to have an *ingressive imperfect, however (cp. Latin).

<strong>ingressive imperfect</strong>: Latin could use the imperfect tense to cover the beginning of an action in past time. Acts 3:8 may be a Greek example of using the imperfect rather than the usual aorist for this idea.

<strong>initial</strong>: v. to put ones *initials on something to indicate approval of its contents

<strong>initials</strong>: the combination of the first letters of a person’s name.

(in a set with *abbreviation, *acronym)

<strong>innuendo</strong>: an indirect *put-down, *insinuation, subtle derogatory remark; *hint

<strong>instrument: </strong>also means; refers to the “tool” used for accomplishing something: “he cut down the cherry tree with a hatchet.” Latin uses the ablative case on the noun involved with no preceding preposition. Greek uses the dative case for this concept—also without a preceding preposition. New Testament Greek often adds <em><u>en</u></em> (“in”) ahead the dative-case noun for the instrument.

<strong>instrumental dative</strong>: the dative case in Latin and Greek used to indicate the means (<em> vs.</em> personal agency) by which something is accomplished. In New Testament Greek, <em>en</em> often precedes the noun.

<strong>instrumental case</strong>: in the eight-case analysis of Greek, the instrumental dative is separated out as a case function by itself.

<strong>inscription/inscribe</strong>: a writing on some object like a coin, sarcophagus, pottery, or a wall.

<strong>inseparable prefixes</strong>: prefixes that exist only in combination with roots; they are not free-standing. Greek has <em>a</em>(<em>n</em>)-, a prefix meaning “not”; <em>archi</em>-, ; <em>dus</em>-, ; <em>nee</em>-, ;

<strong>inseparable preposition</strong>: In Hebrew certain prepositions that do not stand alone, but are prefixed to their “object”: <em>le</em>- (to), <em>be</em>- (in), <em>ke</em>- (as/like), (and <em>me</em> < <em>min</em> [from]).

<strong>inseparable suffixes</strong>: Latin used several additions on the ends of words, additions that did not appear free-standing and were not part of the suffix system on nouns, adjectives, verbs, and cognate-noun creators: -<em>ne</em> (attached to the first word of a clause to indicate that a question is being asked if there is no interrogative that would otherwise so indicate), -<em>ve</em> (attached to the second element in a list to mean “or”), -que (attached to a word in a list that meant “and”), -<em>dem</em> (added to the regular third-person personal pronoun to make an indefinite pronoun -<em>is</em> to is<em>dem</em>),

Older English had the directional suffix –<em>ward</em> as in us<em>ward</em> for “toward us. Modern English still uses after<em>ward</em>, to<em>ward</em>, heaven<em>ward</em>, home<em>ward</em>; lee<em>ward</em>; south<em>ward</em>, north<em>ward</em>, east<em>ward</em>, west<em>ward</em>; in<em>ward</em>, out<em>ward</em>; up<em>ward</em>, down<em>ward</em>; on<em>ward</em>, way<em>ward</em>; for<em>ward</em>, front<em>ward</em>, back<em>ward</em>; un<em>toward</em>. Another directional suffix is –<em>ad</em>: <em>caudad</em>, toward the tail, <em>cephalad</em>, toward the head, <em>dextrad, </em>toward the right, <em>sinistrad</em>, toward the left

(in a set with *separable suffixes, *suffixes)

<strong>inside address</strong>: The date, name, and address of the recipient are repeated from the envelope on the letter itself to avoid confusion if the letter gets separated from its envelope.

(in a set with *salutation, *body, *complimentary close, *signature, *post script as parts of a letter)

<strong>insinuation/insinuate</strong>: a negative comment not directly said, an expression that expects the hearer to infer the intended negative connotation. Insinuation and *innuendo differ from *cut, *slam, and *insult by being indirect. It differs from *hint* by being negative.

<strong>instructional grammar</strong>: a grammar designed to introduce a student to a language. Its layout involves exercises, vocabularies, units sequenced to inform on a graduated basis. (in a set with *manual grammar, *reference grammar, *descriptive grammar)

<strong>instrumental dative</strong>: In Greek the dative case without accompanying preposition can be used on a noun to indicate the thing someone used to accomplish a result. In places, the New Testament uses en ahead of such a noun.

<strong>insubordinate clause: </strong>a clause that can stand alone because it is considered to express a complete thought. Also called an *independent clause. Contrasts with *subordinate clause, or *dependent clause.

<strong>insult</strong>: a direct negative comment to a person that either “stretches the truth” or intentionally misrepresents his character. A *cut, *a slam. An insult differs from *innuendo and *insinuation by being direct. It differs from hint by being negative.

<strong>intense/intensive</strong>: Prepositional prefixes in Greek and Latin words were sometimes simply intensifiers. <em>Eathioo </em>in Greek means “to eat”; katesthioo means “to hog it down, to gluttonize.” The Piel? (active) and Pual? (passive) stems in Hebrew are intensive stems. There are intensifiers that can be added to Greek and Latin verb roots in order to intensify the meaning of the root.

(in a set with *emphasis)

<strong>intensive pronoun</strong>: In English, -<em>self</em> pronouns restate a noun. Historically intensive pronouns were placed immediately after the associated noun. The modern habit is to make it *postpositive at the end of the clause if the intensive restates the subject: “John himself did it” <em> vs.</em> John did it himself.”

To create intensive pronouns, English uses the –<em>self</em>/<em>-selves </em>suffix on <em>my</em>-, <em>our</em>-, <em>your</em>-, <em>him</em>-, <em>her</em>-, <em>it</em>-, <em>them</em><strong>-</strong> (but not on I/me, we/us, you, he/his/she/its, or they/their). The –<em>self</em> *morpheme does not combine with *nominative/*subjective forms of pronouns. More complicated is that fact that in first and second persons, –<em>self</em>/<strong>-</strong><em>selves </em>is added to the possessive pronoun form, but in the third person –<em>self-selves</em> is added to the objective/accusative form of the pronoun rather than the possessive. As a result, it is considered ungrammatical to say <em>meself</em>, or <em>hisself</em>, <em>theirselves</em>; and <em>itself</em> is not spelled <em>it</em><em><u>s</u></em><em>self</em>, which would be a possessive form attached to –<em>self</em>.


singular		plural

1	myself	ourselves

2	yourself	yourselves

3	himself/herself/itself	themselves

In Greek, <em>autos</em> serves as the intensive pronoun for all persons if it is in *predicate position, that is, if it stands before the article or after the noun. If <em>autos</em> stands in *attributive position (between article and noun or after the article repeated), it means “same.” Standing alone outside the *nominative, it is a *personal pronoun for the *third person. If it does appear alone in the nominative, it emphasizes the subject expressed in the ending on the verb; in doing so, it is comparable to underlining the pronoun in written English: “He crossed the bridge.”

In Latin, <em>ipse/ipsa/ipsud</em> are the nominative forms of the intensive pronoun for all persons.

<strong>intercede/intercession</strong>: to be the “go between” on someone else’s behalf. *Intercessory prayer is on behalf of someone else.

<strong>intercessor</strong>: a go-between, especially from man and God. A priest intercedes for men to God; a prophet is a *spokesman for God to men.

(in a set with *intermediary, *priest, *prophet; *auricular confession)

<strong>intercessory prayer</strong>: prayer on behalf of someone else. In liturgical church, a *priest’s role as intercessor is signaled by his facing away from the congregation—in the same direction as the congregation—when offering the pastoral prayer.

<strong>interfix</strong>: to add a sound between undesirable sound sequences to make them pronounceable.

<strong>interject</strong>: to insert something into a speech or perhaps previously prepared text being now delivered orally as at a scholarly conference.

<strong>interjection</strong>: a word or phrase, not part of the syntax of the sentence, that expresses emotion: <em>alas</em>! <em>boy</em>! <em>hot dog</em>! <em>ho hum!</em> <em>hurrah</em>! <em>man</em>! <em>ole</em>! <em>ouch</em>! <em>pah</em>! <em>pash</em>! (short for <em>passion</em>), <em>pshaw! oyez oyez</em>! <em>rah</em>! <em>ugh</em>!, <em>wahoo, whee,</em>  Some words said to animals offer further examples: <em>click click</em> (to a horse), <em>git</em> (to a dog), <em>hey </em>(to a cow),<em> scat</em> (to a cat), <em>shoo</em> (to a chicken), <em>sooey</em> (to a pig).

Latin: <em>hui</em> (boy, whee)

Greek: idou (behold),

<strong>intermediary</strong>: a *go-between, an *intercessor like a *priest or *prophet in divine-human relations.

In some societies, important business is carried through an intermediary as a sign of respect rather than transacted face to face, the latter being consider too forward, too presumptuous, too “in your face.” The practice is seen in Gen <strong><sup>32</sup></strong>, when Jacob returns to Canaan to meet his brother Esau. Similarly, at the behest of Nathan the prophet, Solomon’s mother approaches David about having Solomon succeed him as king of Israel (1 Kg <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>). In the New Testament, James and John evidently have their mother approach Jesus about having them sit on the right hand and on the left in His kingdom. Since it was really James and John who were making the request, Mk <strong><sup>10</sup></strong>:35-41 can present it as their approach to Jesus while Mt <strong><sup>20</sup></strong>:20-24 reports it as their mother approaching Jesus with the request.

<strong>interpolate/interpolation</strong>: A copyist might correct his work by interpolating a previous omission (*haplography). A *copist, *editor, or *redactor may add an explanatory note, one entered into the text line rather than in a footnote or in the margin. The concern is that a marginal note, taken by a later copyist as a previous omission, was added into the regular line of writing.

Gen <strong><sup>35</sup></strong>:6; Josh <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:10, 11, 23 illustrate the kinds of things that might constitute interpolations.

<strong>interpretation</strong>: ideally, coming to understand the author’s meaning; as a term it is often used to include a reader’s inferential application beyond *authorial intent.

<strong>interrogative</strong>: having to do with asking a question. Asking a *question is done in a number of ways. (1) There are *interrogative words, words that by their very meaning ask a question: <em>why, who, where, what when, how</em> (on the front of a sentence); Latin: <em>cur</em>/why, <em>ubi</em>/where; Greek <em>tis/ti</em> (with acute on first syllable = who/what/why). (2) a suffix on the end of the first word: <em>-ne</em> on the first word of a Latin *informational question whose word order is identical to the wording of a statement. (3) rearrangement of words in English: “Are you coming?” as a transform of “You are coming.” (4) distinctive pitch pattern: raised pitch at the end of an English sentence makes a question even with the same wording as in a statement: “You are coming?” (5) helping words in the English verb form: “Did you look?” <em> vs.</em> “You looked.” (6) Hebrew uses an interrogative ha- prefixed to the first word of an informational question, whether direct or indirect question. (7) punctuation in a written language: English (?); Greek (;). 	In English, Latin, and Greek, interrogative pronouns, interrogative adjectives, and adverbs may indicate questions.

So, there are interrogative pronouns (“<em>Who</em> stole my book?”), interrogative adjectives (“<em>Whose</em> book did he steal?”), or interrogative adverbs (“<em>Where</em> did he stash the stolen book?”), “<em>When</em> did he steal the book?” “<em>Why</em> did he steal the book?” “<em>How</em> did he steal the book?”).

In all these languages indicators for interrogation tend to come first in their clauses.

(in a set with *direct question, *indirect question, *informational question, *rhetorical question, *question anticipating a negative response, *question anticipating a positive response).

<strong>interrogative adjective</strong>: question-asking adjectives that modify nouns: <em>which, what </em>in initial position: “What (book) are you reading?” “Which (book) are you reading?” The interrogative adjectives are adaptations of interrogative pronouns, which are among the question words that stand alone.

<strong>interrogative adverb</strong>: <em>when, where, how </em>in initial position as a question in English.

<strong>interrogative conjunction</strong>: Latin has <em>an</em> Wh 232

<strong>interrogative pronoun</strong>: <em>who/whom/whose, what</em> in initial position

Gr

Lat

<strong>interrogative sentence</strong>: a sentence that asks a question in contrast to simply saying something, commanding something, or emotionally expressing something: “When will you be going home?”

(in a set with *informational question; *question anticipating positive response, &question anticipating negative response; *declarative sentence, *exclamatory sentence, *imperative sentence)

<strong>interrogative words</strong>: words that always ask a question. Certain English words always ask questions if they appear first in the clause: <em>Who/whom/whose, what; when, while, where; which.</em>

Greek interrogative words include <em>tis/ti</em> with acute accent on the first syllable; it is usually first in clause, but not necessarily (Jn <strong><sup>21</sup></strong>:12),

Latin interrogatives include <em>cur</em> (why),

<strong>interrogatory</strong>: another term for *interrogative

<strong>intervocalic</strong>: between vowels. English tends to vocalize intervocalic voiceless *consonants.

<strong>intervocalic sigma</strong>: Greek developed a practice of eliding intervocalic sigmas when inflection placed sigma after a short vowel and before another vowel of any length; exposed vowels then contracted according to the same rules of contraction. The phenomenon shows up in the genitive plural of the second declension; the ending was originally –asoon, which became -oon circumflexed: -asoon > oon (gephurasoon > gephuroon);

wherever –<em>sai</em> occurs as the second singular middle/passive: -esai > eei (luesai > lueei)

wherever –<em>so </em>occurs as the second singular middle/passive: eso > ou (lueso > luou)

(note also *contraction)

<strong>intonation</strong>: the use of pitch to distinguish questions from statements rather than depending on some syntactical transform or additional wording to do so: “You are happy?” <em> vs.</em> “Are you happy?” (2) akin to *modulation

<strong>intransitive verb</strong>: a verb that does not have a direct object in a given sentence. Intransitiveness has to be figured on a per-sentence basis because a given verb may be used transitively or intransitively depending on the sentence, in “He worked yesterday” <em> vs.</em> “He worked the clock.” Consequently, a dictionary can give only general information on this aspect of a verb’s usage.

A verb like <em>drink</em> may change meaning in the different usage. Intransitively, <em>drink</em> means drink alcohol; transitively can be used for drinking anything.

(in a set with *transitive verb; *linking verb/*equative verb/*to be verb/*copulative verb)

<strong>introduction</strong>: a standard element in longer writings, such as a book, that states the purpose of writing, the method of research that underlies it, the scope of the presentation, and any distinctive viewpoints taken or notable structure of presentation, the manner of presentation for optimum benefit to the reader. In ancient literature, there are New Testament examples at the beginning of Luke’s gospel (Lk<strong> 1</strong>:1-4) and his Acts of the Apostles (<strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1-2ff).

(in a set with *acknowledgments, table of contents, *epilogue, *greeting, *preface, *prologue)

<strong>invective</strong>: a denunciation, an abusive discourse

<strong>inveigle</strong>: to win over by coaxing

<strong>inverted pyramid</strong>: a structure of writing in newspaper articles that starts with the main idea and works down to more detailed matters as the piece proceeds.

<strong>inverted sentence</strong>: a sentence that places the subject after the verb: “Here are two examples of my writing.” “My objections to the proposal are <em>these.</em>”

<strong>invocation</strong>: a prayer at the beginning of a public gathering, especially one of a religious nature

<strong>iota</strong>: ninth letter of the Greek alphabet¨: ι/Ι

<strong>iota-diminutive</strong>: in Greek terminology iota was sometimes added to a root to create a word to indicate something in smaller form: biblos > biblion, biblaridion, teknion < teknon, paidon (< pais), chorion < chera,  (the added iota gets the accent)

<strong>iota subscript</strong>: The Greek iota is written under a long vowel since it does not in that case affect pronunciation—as in the dative singular of the first two declensions or as the result of contraction in contract verbs and elsewhere. The iota “subscript” is written on the line after the main vowel if all capitals are used or if the long vowel is capitalized in c and lc; subscripts are not written under capitals (Aidees).

<strong>ipsissima verba</strong>: the exact words, the very words of another; the words themselves [Lat]

<strong>irony</strong>: a figure of speech that says the opposite of what is meant and does so in a way that makes the real meaning clear. “He’s a great man,” said in a way that denies that he is great. The opposite, intended meaning is indicated by an opposing context, by tone of voice, by a contradictory facial expression or some other accompanying gesture.

Paul evidently meant his words ironically in 2 Cor <strong><sup>12</sup></strong>:13: “How were you inferior to the other churches except that I was not a burden to you? Forgive me this wrong!”

<strong>irregular</strong>: an case of not following expected pattern, whether for making a plural, for moving through the principal parts of a verb, for moving through the degrees of an adjective or adverb, for the formation of *cognate words, or even for moving through the declension of a specific noun or verb. Latin uses a different dative and ablative plural for <em>dea</em>, goddess. By pattern it should be <em>deis</em> in those spots; but since <em>deus,</em> god, would be the same thing in those places, Latin reaches over into third declension to get substitute endings <em>de</em>–<em>a-bus</em> to supply a form that is distinguishable from the masculine word <em>deis</em>. The nominative masculine plural of <em>deus</em> should be <em>dei</em>, but as time went on <em>di </em>replaced it.

(in a set with *regular) (*defective verb, , *deponent verb, *impersonal verb, *irregular verb, *suppletive verb).

<strong>ironic</strong>: characterized by *irony. Ironic can describes incongruities, adverse effects, surprise consequences.

<strong>irregular adjectives</strong>: In Latin, there is a group of nine first-and-second declension adjectives that among themselves are alike but differ from the other adjectives in this group by having –<em>ius</em> in the genitive singular of all three genders and –<em>i</em> in the dative singular of all three genders. Additionally, they may show –<em>ud</em> in the neuter nominative/accusative singular.

<strong>irregular nouns</strong>:

<strong>irregular verbs</strong>: a verb whose conjugation is unusual in some way. In many languages, the verb “to be,” for example, is irregular. That may result from the fact that the forms of this verb are so often used. In English, there are <em>am, is,</em> and <em>are</em> in the present tense and <em>was, were</em> in the past. Besides, with singular <em>you</em> belongs <em>were</em> even though everywhere else <em>were</em> is a plural form.

In Latin there <em>volo</em> (vis, vult, <em>etc</em>) does not move through its person-and-number format in the normal way. Some words exist only in the third person, sometimes called *impersonal verbs: <em>inquit? </em>(Lat); <em>deoo? chree</em> (Gr)

(in a set with *regular verb; *composite verb, *defective verb, *deponent verb, *suppletive verb)

<strong>irregular comparison of adjectives</strong>: Irregular comparison of adjectives means that the roots of the three *“degrees” of the adjective do not come from the same root; they are like the English “good, better, best.”

<strong>irregular comparison of adverbs</strong>: the roots of the three *“degrees” of the adverb do not come from the same root. Latin Greek In Latin and Greek, since adverbs of manner are usually built on corresponding adjectives, the irregularity of adverb comparison parallels the irregularity of the corresponding adjective comparison.

<strong>isolating language</strong>: a language that does not use inflection to indicate the association of language parts, using instead *distribution/*word order, pitch pattern, <em>etc.</em>, for that purpose. Contrasts with *agglutinative language, which uses separable affixes to indicate relationship, and *inflectional language, which uses terminations to convey connections, and *incorporative language, which combines sentence elements into one compounded form. (See Dana & Mantey, A Manual Grammar of the Greek New Testament, p. 5.

<strong>itacism</strong>: the tendency for other Greek vowels to move in the direction of short iota, starting in the fourth or fifth century A. D.

<strong>italic/italics, italic script</strong>: a type style that is more slanted than the normal type (in a set with *underlined and *bold-faced)

<strong>iterate</strong>: to repeat

<strong>iterative action</strong>: action that occurs repeatedly. The type of action originally indicated by -<em>Izo</em> verbs in Greek.

<strong>itization (sp)</strong>

<strong>jargon</strong>: every-day special words and phrases associated with a certain subject or field of activity. Jargon has more of a down-to-earth ring to it than *technical terminology. (in a set with *technical terms)

<strong>jeer </strong>

<strong>Jehovah</strong>: an artificial personal name for the Hebrew God. It is formed from the consonants of His ineffable name and the vowel points from adonai (“my Lord”), which in the standardized Hebrew Old Testament text the Masoretes put on the four consonants of the tetragrammaton as a reminder not to say the divine personal name itself.

<strong>jingle</strong>: a saying that is short, light-hearted, memorable, perhaps musical, and used frequently in advertising (in a set with *catch phrase)

<strong>jot</strong>: the smallest Hebrew letter, the <em>yod/yodh</em>, called an <em>iota </em>(Mt <strong><sup>5</sup></strong>:18), the closest Greek equivalent letter: י

<strong>journal</strong>: (1) a scholarly *periodical published usually each quarter.

(2) a *diary

(3) a record of travel

<strong>jussive</strong>: equivalent to a command form but for first and third persons. Latin uses the independent subjunctive for this idea. Greek has a third person imperative that is equivalent in value and is translated “let” just like the Latin jussive.

<strong>jussive noun clause</strong>: a Latin construction that indirectly reports a command or entreaty. It is comprised of a leading verb that implies urging to action, a pronoun object and ut/ne + subjunctive: Imperavit eis ut hoc facerent (“He commanded them to do this).” English covers the idea with an infinitive verb and no re-expression of the subject since it is the same as the leading verb. WH 253 (Cp. *object clause in Greek.)

<strong>kana</strong>: Japanese syllabic writing used alongside kanji for noting inflection, particles, function word WB

<strong>kaph</strong>: the eleventh letter of the Hebrew alphabet: כ with ך  as its terminal form

<strong>kappa</strong>:  the tenth letter of the Greek alphabet: κ/Κ

<strong>kenning</strong>: a figuratively, compound expression used in place of a noun or name. A <em>storm of swords</em> is a kenning for <em>battle.</em>

<strong>key</strong>: the answers to a test

<strong>kinesics</strong>: movements and gestures that are used to convey meaning—to reinforce or elaborate accompanying verbal communication: raised eyebrows, rolling the eyes, a shrug, a wink, snap of the fingers.

<strong>kappa</strong>: tenth letter of the Greek alphabet

<strong>kethibh</strong>: what is “written” in the Hebrew text of the Old Testament in contrast to what is to be “read.” (in a set with *qere):

<strong>koph</strong>: the nineteenth letter of the Hebrew alphabet, also <em>qoph</em>: <strong>ק</strong>

<strong>koppa</strong>: Greek symbol for ninety: κ

<strong>labio-dental</strong>: sound made by the lips and teeth in combination (usually the top teeth and the bottom lip): <em>f/ph, v. </em>(l<em>ab</em>-, cp. l<em>ip</em>)

<strong>labials</strong>: sounds that are made with the lips: <em>p, b, f/ph, v, m, </em>along with bilabial fricative, bilabial trill

(in a set with *dentals, *velars, liquids, and *glottals)

<strong>lallation</strong>: the substitution of the phoneme <em>/l/</em> for <em>/r/</em>, a speech shift associated with orientalσ speaking English

<strong>lambda</strong>: eleventh letter of the Greek alphabet:  <strong>λ/Λ</strong>

<strong>lamed/lamedh</strong>: the twelfth letter of the Hebrew alphabet: <strong>ל</strong>

<strong>lament</strong>: an expression of grief; a * lamentation

<strong>lamentation</strong>: a writing that is sorrowful about current or anticipated circumstances; a *lament

<strong>language</strong>: a conventional sound-symbol system for transferring information from one mind to another. Written language is subsequent to spoken language inasmuch as it merely indicates by symbols the speech-sounds that already exist. Sign language uses gestures in place of sounds to convey thought.

Human language has a number of basic features. It is hierarchical, meaning that it has systems within systems—inflection of word forms within clauses, within sentences, within paragraphs, <em>etc.</em> Language is conventional, i.e., it is arbitrary as to what sounds have what *referents; it does not need to be inherently the way it is, and it is not in-born (innate); it is learned rather than inherited.  It is subject to change, or drift; it is virtually impossible to keep a language from changing its features over time. Language is productive in that it develops new utterances and features. Language is displaceable in that it does not need to be in the presence of what it describes. Language has specialization; it is culture specific. It has all the features that are necessary for covering what needs to be covered within its culture surrounding. Finally, language is interchangeable. The same one person can both send and receive messages.

As a result of the characteristics, it is not appropriate to speak of one language as “better” than another. Every language, in fact, takes care of everything its culture needs to talk about. Some languages do have more developed, or replete, verb systems, for example. English has an especially elaborate verb system that by form can distinguish more tenses and aspects than most language can. Its vocabulary is also quite extensive. Lastly, as a matter of practical fact, English has a highly developed terminology for all the technical and scientific things that have come into usage in the modern world.

Furthermore, it is not appropriate to blur the distinction between human language and animal communication systems. With rare exceptions, its particulars are innate. They may be iconographic, as the dancing of bees to tell where honey is. They are one-level rather than hierarchical, and the like.

<strong>Language drift/linguistic drift</strong>: Over time, various influences cause a language to change in vocabulary, inflection, grammar, and sound. English has a rather extreme example in meaning drift on the word let, all the way from “prevent” to “allow”! In the King James Version (1611) of Rom <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:13, Paul says that he wanted to come to the Romans many times, but he “was let” hitherto; that is, he was prevented previously. The word <em>bad</em> has come to mean very good” in the sense that someone is difficult to outdo. Same as *linguistic drift. (in a set with *meaning)

<strong>last word</strong>: the practice of trying to say the last thing in a discussion or argument to create the impression that in so doing a person has won the day.

<strong>lateral</strong>: having to do with a sound made by pass air along one or both sides of the tongue, as in the lateral <em>s </em>or <em>l</em>

<strong>lateral “s”</strong>: the sound for a bilateral “l” put in place of the expected <em>ess</em> sound. The tip of the tongue is not cupped downward with air moving only through that restricted path. Instead, it is cut off at the alveovelar ridge and has voiceless air moving around it, as with the first <em>l</em> in the double <em>l</em>’s (Lluwellyn sp?) of Welch names.

<strong>Latinism</strong>: (in a set with Hebraism, Aramaism, Hellenism, Anglicism) See Dana & Mantey Manual Grammar, p. 15; A. T. Robertson, Grammar of the Greek NT, 108-11.

<strong>laying on of hands</strong>: (1) a *gesture for appointment to an office or duty. New Testament examples include the appointment of the seven to serve tables (Acts <strong><sup>6</sup></strong>:1-6). The formal setting aside of Paul and Barnabas by the church in Antioch for the first missionary journey of Paul (Acts <strong><sup>13</sup></strong>:1-3). The appointment (and endowment?) of Timothy for ministry with Paul by “the presbytery” and Paul (1 Tim <strong><sup>4</sup></strong>:14; 2 Tim <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:6). In the Old Testament there was the appointment of the Levites before the Lord (Num <strong><sup>8</sup></strong>:10) and the appointment of Joshua as Moses’ successor (Num <strong><sup>27</sup></strong>:15-23; Deut <strong><sup>34</sup></strong>:9),

(2) a gesture accompanying the transmission of supernatural gifts (Acts <strong><sup>8</sup></strong>:17-19).

(3) a gesture associated with supernatural healing (<strong>????)</strong>

<strong>leading verb</strong>: same as independent clause or *main verb of a sentence, but tends to be used when talking about a subsequent *subordinate clause. (in a set with *independent verb, *main verb)

<strong>leaf/leaves</strong>: the sheet of material that make up a book/*codex (in a set with *pages)

<strong>lection: </strong>what is to be read in contrast to what is on the page. A reading, especially one that is part of an arranged series or readings used to cover the Bible in an ecclesiastical year.

<strong>lectionary</strong>: a book comprised or religious texts arranged to correspond to events in the religious year.

<strong>lecture</strong>: a formal presentation by a notable speaker or one offered by a professor in formal education as part of a college course-for-credit; contrasts with recitation course, practicum, colloquium, reading course, directed study .

<strong>lectureship</strong>: a series of college-level presentations outside the regular curriculum and usually open to the college or university generally.

<strong>legend</strong>: (1) kind of person in the past; a type of record of some significant event not able to be verified; in a class with folklore. Often a label used for someone particularly outstanding and well known as in the expression “He was a legend in his own time.” Differs from *myth in that the latter does not intend to be taken as partaking in real history.

(2) a written collection of symbols used in an accompanying document

<strong>lene</strong> (in a set with *dagesh lene, *dagesh forte)

<strong>lesser Asclepiad</strong>: type of poetic meter wh 342

<strong>letter</strong>: an alphabetic symbol that represents a sound, set of related sounds, or *allomorphs in a language. The different values of the letter get sorted out by circumstance or are simply known by the speakers of the language.

(2) <em>Letter</em> can also be a relatively short communication usually without an official implication. Less formal than a *missive or an *epistle. The difference between a letter of the alphabet and a letter to communicate is signaled in Latin by using the singular on <em>littera </em>for the former and the plural for the latter.

(in a set with *character, *grapheme; *consonant, *vowel; *digraph, double consonant; *diphthong, *spriant letter, vowel glide)

(in a set with *epistle, *missive)
