<strong>lexeme</strong>: the basic form of a word; its dictionary form on which other forms make adjustments. <em>Sing </em>is the lexeme for<em> sing, sang, sung.</em>

<strong>lexical meaning</strong>: the meaning given in a dictionary in contrast to *structural meaning, meaning based on alterations of the word (*form), *distribution/word order/position, and *intonation.

<strong>lexicology</strong>: the study of the components of a language

<strong>lexicography</strong>: the work of gathering together the words in a language and giving their various meanings in written form usually in alphabetical order

<strong>lexicon</strong>: an alphabetical arrangement of the words in a language; a term used more for ancient languages in contrast to a <em>dictionary</em> of modern languages <em>Lexicon</em> may imply a greater degree of presentation and examples of usage than what a dictionary might contain. Lexicons and dictionaries deal with words whereas an *encyclopedia deals with topics.

(in a set with *bilingual dictionary, *dictionary, *encyclopedia)

<strong>lexis</strong>: the full set of complete words contained in a language

<strong>library</strong>: a collection of books and other writings

<strong>libretto</strong>: the words of a musical work, especially those of an extended work like Handel’s <em>Messiah</em> or of a dramatic musical work like an opera.

(in a set with *lyrics, *lyricist, *writer, *author; *composer)

<strong>lie</strong>: to intentionally say what is not true; to *prevaricate

<strong>limerick</strong>: a whimsical anapest poem with five lines and a rhyme scheme <em>aaba</em>.

<strong>line</strong>: (1) a piece of writing that goes all the way across the page; a *stich. (2) a statement that gives a standardized representation of things, which may not be the speaker’s real conviction, but is nevertheless said in order to mislead the hearer; the party line. (3) a pick-up statement or presentation that is designed to gain another person’s trust or even romantic involvement; a pick-up line. So, <em>line</em> often implies that there is something wrong motive.

(in a set with *spin, *party line)

<strong>lines: </strong>the lines of text that comprise a written work, especially those that make up a poem. (in a set with *stichoi)

<strong>lingo</strong>: an informal, lighthearted term for the way of talking about a subject (cp. *jargon) or a way of talking within a group of speakers (insider talk).

<strong>lingua franca</strong>: the language that serves a wide area where many local languages serve as first languages. In the modern world, English has become the nearest thing to a universal language. Greek and Latin held that role for centuries around the Mediterranean area. Earlier, Aramaic served that purpose for a number of centuries in the ancient Middle East.

<strong>lingual</strong>: a sound whose production involves the use of the tongue as with <em>t, d, th,dh; j, s, sh,zh; l, r;</em> <em>c/k, g, gh,</em> (Ba)<em>ch</em>; in contrast to <em>p, b, f/ph, v</em>, <em>h</em>(onor), <em>h</em>(at); that is, *dentals, *sibilants, *liquids, and *velars, but not *labials and *glottals.

<strong>linguist</strong>: one who specializes in language analysis

<strong>linguistic atlas</strong>: a map of the areas where the various languages are spoken

<strong>linguistic drift</strong>: same as *language drift, the pattern of change that occur unofficially over time

<strong>linguisticality</strong>: having the character and characteristics of actual human language in contrast to disorganized utterance, *ecstatic utterance (as when in a trance), *noise, *glossolalia

<strong>linguistics</strong>: the study of language as such in contrast to the study of a language. Formerly called *philology.

<strong>lining out</strong>: a pattern of delivery sometimes used in teaching the words to a song or poem. The leader says a line and the audience repeats it. (in a set with *antiphonal)

<strong>linking verb</strong>: a verb that indicates being rather than action; another term for *copulative verb, *equative verb, *state-of-being verb, *to-be verb; cp. also *fientive verbs, non-action verbs use adjectives (or nominative-case nouns/pronouns) after them instead of adverbs:

<em>	to be </em>(good),

<em>become</em> (good),

<em>get</em> (good at what he does),

<em>seem</em> (good), <em>feel</em> (good, not “well” unless <em>feel</em> means act of feeling),

<em>look</em> (good, unless <em>look</em> means act of looking ),

<em>sound</em> (good), <em>taste</em> (good, unless <em>taste</em> means ability to taste),

<em>smell</em> (good, unless <em>smell means ability</em> to smell).

<em>stands </em>(complete)

Linking verbs are also created by passive transforms of sentences with direct objects plus objectives complements, where the former direct object becomes the subject of the equivalent passive sentence: “[<em>Joseph</em>]<em> found Mary pregnant</em>” appears in Matthew 1:18 in passive form: “Mary was-found pregnant,” the construction for <em>pregnant</em> being in the nominative case. The O. C. can be either a noun, adjective, or participle; when transformed it becomes nominative becomes the equivalent of “Mary was pregnant.” The phenomenon occurs in English, Latin, and Greek.

<strong>liquid future</strong>: a kind of Greek verb that ends its root with lambda or rho. Sigma, the sign for future tense, does not easily follow those sounds; so an epsilon is interfixed between the root and the tense sign. Then the intervocalic sigma rule comes into play; so the sigma drops out and the epsilon contracts with the o/e variable of the ending according to regular contraction rules.

<strong>liquids</strong>: sounds that may be thought of as between vowels and consonants: r and l.

<strong>liquid aorist</strong>: A kind of Greek verb whose root ends in a lambda or rho. Sigma alpha (-sa), the sign for first aorist, does not easily follow those sounds—sigma not at all; so the sigma drops out and the rest of the conjugation remains the same as regular first aorists. (in a set with *aorist tense, *first aorist, *second aorist)

<strong>liquid verb</strong>: In Greek, a verb whose root ends in lambda or rho.

<strong>lisp</strong>: a sound that substitutes the voiceless “th” for the ess sound: “I mith my big brother.”

<strong>litany</strong>: a liturgical prayer, that is, a prayer used in public worship in which the leader recites a series of petitions to God followed by fixed responses by the congregation

<strong>literal</strong>: a rather imprecise term for things like word picture or strictly taken in contrast to figurative interpretation (in a set with *figurative)

<strong>literally</strong>: in a straightforward way

<strong>literary</strong>: having to do with literature, with written works in contrast to oral communication.

<strong>literate</strong>: able to read

<strong>literati</strong>: literary intellectuals literary intelligentsia

<strong>literatim</strong>: letter for letter, similar to word for word, but applicable to a kind of copying that would be done by someone who does not know the meaning of the things being copied or someone who thinks of his efforts aside from understanding what he is reading. A way of reproducing by hand a previous work with such exactness that no editorial or redactional amending or emending is considered appropriate. (in a set with *verbatim)

<strong>literature</strong>: a body of written material, perhaps from a certain author, a given time frame, a certain geographical area, or on a given topic. Writing that lies above the level of a particular writing. It is a kind of thing more than a thing, and implies a quality of treatment worthy of attention.

<strong>litotes</strong>: a construction that denies the negative as a way of affirming the position: Tarsus was “no mean city” (Acts<strong> 21</strong>:39). <em>Litotes</em> amounts to the same thing as the *obverse.

(in a set with *metaphor, *metonymy, *simile, *zeugma)

<strong>loan word</strong>: a word introduced from another language because perhaps the new language lacks a term with that precise meaning or connotation: aloha, luau.

<strong>locate</strong>: identify the place of a form in a word’s inflection set, in its *paradigm

Declension inflection (for nouns and adjectives)

Conjugation inflection (for verbs)

<strong>locative case</strong>: the form of a word that indicates place where something occurs. In Greek there is ahead of such nouns a preposition (usually *governing the *dative) that implies location (in contrast to separation or destination).

In Latin, the ablative case fulfills this same role with an accompanying preposition. With the names of cities, towns, and small islands, however, along with the three words <em>domus</em>, <em>rus</em>, and <em>humus</em>, location is indicated without a preposition. In these latter situations, the locative case-form replicates the genitive on place-nouns of the first and second declension; elsewhere it is the same as the ablative.

<strong>locus classicus  </strong>a prime text for studying an important issue. Plato’s Gorgias is a source for asking whether desire goodness more than we seek our own advantage. Imprimis, newsletter of Hillsdale College, June 2023. A general work dedicated to surveying such matters is Great Ideas: A Syntopicon, Britannica, 1952.

<strong>locution</strong>: a person-specific manner of speaking or *phraseology.

<strong>logion</strong>: a supposed saying of Jesus from outside the canonical gospels but is found in early apocryphal writings. Pl. logia.

<strong>logo</strong>: a symbol that stands for a company or business. (in a set with *symbol, *trademark),

<strong>logogram/logograph</strong>: a symbol for a word, something written that does not use letters to indicate its sound (<em>&</em>, <em>numbers </em>for their words in a line of writing)

<strong>logogriph</strong>: a word puzzle or a code enmeshed in an otherwise innocuous missive.

(in a set with *anagram)

<strong>logomachy</strong>: an argument that turns out to be a dispute over terms instead of referents.

<strong>Lord’s Prayer, The</strong>: also called *The Model Prayer (Mt <strong><sup>6</sup></strong>:9-13) and *Pater Noster, the Latin name.

<strong>logorrhea</strong>: verbosity, excessive use of words

<strong>longueur</strong>: a tedious, protracted passage in a work of performing ar or literature.

<strong>lower case: </strong>the smaller size of letters in contrast to capitals (in a set with *capitals, *upper case; *minuscules, *majuscules)

<strong>lucid</strong>: easily understood relative to language expression; clear-headed relative to a person’s thinking

<strong>lullaby</strong>: a cradlesong, a song for babies or young children

<strong>LXX</strong>: the Roman numerals for seventy used as a shorthand reference to the Septuagint, the standard Greek translation of the Hebrew Old Testament in use at the time of Christ. Seventy translators were credited with the work.

<strong>lyric</strong>: a kind of expression especially in poetry that stresses the subjective, the affective, the psychological, how a person fells about the matter addressed.

<strong>lyricist</strong>: one who writes the words for a musical piece

(in a set with *lyric, *lyrics, *writer, *libretto, *author)

<strong>lyrics</strong>: the words to a song (in a set with *lyric, *lyricist, *writer, *libretto)

<strong>macron</strong>: a short, horizontal mark placed above a vowel in phonetic spelling to show that it is long rather than short or otherwise pronounced.

<strong>magazine</strong> (in a set with *periodical)

<strong>maiden name</strong>: a woman’s last name before she marries and adopts her husband’s last name. When her full name is given later, she is said to be “*nee (maiden name).” <em>Nee </em>equals French for “born.” (in a set with *name, *nee, *cognomen, *first name, *last name, *middle name, *surname)

<strong>main clause</strong>: In a *complex sentence or *compound-complex sentence, the main clause is the one that could stand by itself and be considered a complete thought. It is the *independent clause, or the *insubordinate clause, on which the other clause(s) depend(s): “If he crosses the bridge, <em>he breaks the treaty</em>.” “If he does not cross the bridge, and if he has no weapons, <em>he does not break the treaty</em>.”

<strong>main verb</strong>: the finite verb in the *main clause of a complex or compound-complex sentence.

<strong>majestic plural</strong>: a Hebrew usage of the plural to indicate great degree. The idea has been associated with the word for <em>God, </em>( אלהים ), a plural form (-<em>im</em>) nevertheless used with a singular verb for the one God of Israel.

<strong>magic square</strong>: see Lat III T & Q’s folder in the back

<strong>majority text</strong>: the text form of the New Testament that has the greatest number of manuscript witnesses. They are largely *minuscules from dates later than the *uncials.

(in a set with *Textus Receptus, *Byzantine text, *neutral text, , *uncials, *western text)

<strong>majuscule</strong>: a *capital letter; and *upper case letter (in a set with *minuscule)

<strong>malaprop/malapropism</strong>: a misused word that sounds similar to the correct one

<strong>mandate</strong>: a requirement issued by someone with authority to do so (in a set with *edict, *proclamation)

<strong>manner</strong>: the “way” in which something is done

Adverbs are used for this concept: “he worked <em>quickly</em>.”

Prepositional phrases are likewise used: “he worked <em>in earnest</em>.”

Participles can cover the idea of manner.

A noun in the Latin ablative (with or without the preposition <em>cum</em>) can mean manner; in Greek a dative-case noun does the same thing. In the New Testament <em>en</em> often precedes the dative noun).

<strong>manual</strong>: a small *handbook that tells how to do something, operate something, or use something (in a set with *handbook)

<strong>manual grammar</strong>: a grammar that is constructed in a such a way that it may be used as a reference resource on a given language. In so doing it assembles all the material on each aspect into one place in its layout, in contrast to a sequencing of material that serves to introduce the student to the language. (in a set with *descriptive grammar, *reference grammar, * instructional grammar)

<strong>manuscript sermon</strong>: a sermon read that is read rather than given spontaneously (in a set with *spontaneous delivery)

<strong>Masoretic text:</strong> the standardized text of the Hebrew Old Testament prepared cir.

<strong>mass noun</strong>: compare *collective noun

<strong>manual alphabet</strong>: an alphabet signaled by manipulation of the fingers, as used in deaf communication (in a set with *alphabet, *dactylology, *sign language, *signing)

<strong>manuscript</strong>: used especially for ancient writings done by hand; more recently applied to the original copy of a work submitted to a publisher (in a set with *scroll, *codex)

<strong>marginal note</strong>: an explanatory note that enlarges on something in the text that could be unfamiliar to the typical reader (in a set with *endnote, *footnote, *marginalia, *side bar, *side note)

<strong>marginalia</strong>: notations made in the “margin” of a writing to clarify or expand something in the text	(in a set with *endnote, footnote, *marginal note, *sidebar, *sidenote)

<strong>masculine ending</strong>: (in a set with *feminine ending, *neuter ending)

<strong>maxim</strong>: an *apothegm; a short, witty, instructive *saying

<strong>means</strong>: The instrument that an agent uses to accomplish something. While agency is personal, means is impersonal. Both concepts, however, can be covered in the New Testament by Greek constructions like dia + genitive: Acts<strong> 5</strong>:12. Same as *instrument. (in a set with *instrumental dative)

<strong>meaning</strong>:  Society (<em> vs.</em> nature or birth) determines the general meaning of words in a language’s vocabulary. Meaning indicates what by convention a word refers to; so its meaning is determined by usage.

Within this framework, in a given case, precise meaning of a word or passage is determined most exactly by the author’s intent rather than by the reader’s input. Precise meaning is determined by authorial intent rather than by word picture, by derivation, by general usage, by its meaning with other topics, or as used in some other circumstance. That meaning is retrieved (interpretation/hermeneutics) by giving attention to contextual considerations—by what fits with the topic as treated by this author (law of harmony, nature of the case).

The basic meaning of a word is its *denotation; its feel or implication from accompanying factors is its *connotation. The *word picture is its meaning based on derivation.

Meaning development, or proliferation, involves several factors that arise from usage. In scientific *nomenclature, (1) deliberate effort has systematically “coined” two-term *descriptors for plants and animals. The *terminology comes from Latin vocabulary and consists of a *noun followed by an adjective. The term plays on some distinctive feature of the living thing; so it is not an exhaustive description, but one designed simply for identification purposes.

(2) A description for one thing gets applied to other things that have a similar feature of the original usage but do not have its main feature. “Weigh in” comes from the boxing and wrestling scene, where participants are asked to weigh officially to verify that they belong to the correct weight class before they can engage in the bout. The situation involves getting started, special persons qualified to take part, “expressing themselves” by the weighing-in process, and the actual weighing. The last, main feature gets dropped when someone is asked to “weigh in” on the discussion of some topic. The person is asked to get involved; the person is someone whose viewpoint is apt to be helpful; but there is no weighing.

“Rain check” went from being a note that allowed a fan to attend a later rematch of an outdoor sport that was rained out. Now, a shopper gets a rain check for a sold-out sale item; the shopper can return later and buy the item for the sale price even after it is no longer on sale. Rain has nothing to do with it.

(3) “Specializing” takes a general expression and reducing its application to one possibility or reduces the . It has become a technical term. A grocery store can sell thousands of items every day, but today coffee is “on sale.” The expression has narrowed to an item that is being sold at a price lower than usual; lower than normal is a new specific that has been added within the general idea of selling. <strong>Find example</strong>

(4) Words and expressions, especially less-used words, can “sharpen” their meaning by people getting in the habit of using them for some recurring topic. “Right for you” becomes something of a set phrase for personalized application—correctness in light of individual person’s differences..

(5) The overuse of a word like <em>awesome</em> tends to dull its meaning over time so that it does not have the punch in may have had originally.

(6) Spreading from one application to something related. The Greek preposition <em>para</em> first of all meant “beside.” It drifted from meaning beside (parallel) to “near” (paralegal), then to near on the other side—“beyond” (paranormal).

(7) Several *prepositions illustrate the process of going from concrete to abstract. “She went into the living room”; “she went into politics.” <em>Pro </em>can apply to space, then to time, then to logical sequence.

The reason multiple meanings can exist for the same word is that each meaning is sorted out by context. That is the reason the advice to young readers works: read the whole sentence and the meaning of an unfamiliar word may become clear. A word like <em>run</em>, for example, can have enough multiple meaning that it takes two columns in a standard dictionary to outline its usages.

The similar meaning of different words (synonyms) involves getting the words sorted out by what subjects they apply to: small, wee, tiny, little, infinitesimal, microscopic, short, <em>etc.</em>, are not exactly interchangeable. <em>Summation</em> and <em>summary</em> may differ in that the former is used in courtrooms for a lawyer’s closing arguments; <em>summary </em>does not sound right in that circumstance<em>.</em> Some words are more formal while their equivalents are informal, whimsical, slang, archaic, regional, provincial, vulgar.


Whereas in English there is seldom any thought about the meaning of names—excepts perhaps nicknames, ancient Hebrew as well as Greek held the meaning of names closer to the surface. Instead of their being for identification only, they were descriptive as well, at least in their origination.

(in a set with *referent, *reader response theory, *interpretation, *hermeneutics, *semantics, *word picture)

<strong>media</strong>: the typical public sources of information like television, radio, and newspapers.

<strong>medial form</strong>: the distinctive form of a letter that is used on the front or in the middle of a word. Hebrew examples in square script are kaph, mem, nun, pe, tasde. The Greek sigma had a distinctive terminal form. (in a set with *terminal form)

<strong>meditation</strong> n. a short, light piece intended to make the audience thoughtful about some serious matter.

<strong>mem</strong>: the thirteenth letter of the Hebrew alphabet: מ  with ם as its terminal form

<strong>meme</strong>: a unit of cultural information, belief, or idea that is often repeated; it “catches on” as people assume its truth because they hear it often. An example may be the off-expressed idea that ancient Israel was chauvinistic or misogynist because it was patriarchal. The wise men did not go to the Bethlehem stable despite the recurrent pictures on Christmas cards—a *conflation of birth-narrative items for completeness sake (?).

<strong>memo</strong>: short for *memorandum

<strong>memorandum</strong>: a short reminder often distributed within an office, a company, or government agency

<strong>merged verb</strong>: a main verb plus a subsequent *particle that can be in immediate succession or separated (split): “I have to take out the trash”; or “I have to take the trash out.”

(in a set with *fused verb, *phrasal verb)

<strong>message</strong>: a short communication however transmitted

<strong>metaphor</strong>: a figure of speech that directly calls one thing something else because the two are similar in some way. Jesus called Peter “Satan” in Mt <strong><sup>16</sup></strong>:23. He calls Herod Antipas “that fox” in (Lk <strong><sup>13</sup></strong>:32). Paul calls the high priest a “whited wall” in Acts (Acts<strong> 23</strong>:3). Jesus calls conversion a new “birth” (Jn<strong> 3</strong>:3; cp.<strong> 1</strong>:13). Paul calls our body the <em>temple</em> of the Holy Spirit () and a <em>vessel</em> (). Paul speaks of his “thorn in the flesh” (2 Cor <strong><sup>12</sup></strong>:7). Jesus calls the new life in him a new <em>birth</em> (Jn <strong><sup>3</sup></strong>:3ff.). Jerusalem is called a “widow” (Lam <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1).

(in a set with *name calling, *epithet; *figure of speech, *metonymy, *simile, *zeugma)

<strong>metaphrase</strong>: to deliberately change the wording of a text as in paraphrasing, recasting poetry into prose; even to translate into another wording as from formal to slang.

<strong>metaphrast</strong>: one who deliberately alters the wording of a text

<strong>metathesis</strong>: a type of scribal error that switches the order of (letters or) words in the text being copied.

In a linguistic application, metathesis also occurs deliberately in the hithpael stem of Hebrew verbs whose roots begin with a sibilant after the <em>hith</em>- prefix. Occasionally an English name is actually pronounced with reversals of what the spelling would call for: Fiensy, pronounced Finesy. Little children sometimes do a reversal in pronouncing words new to them: aminal, elphanants, merember, basketti,

(in a set with *haplography, *homoioteleuton, *dittography)

<strong>meter</strong>: the pattern of accented and unaccented syllables and the number of such standard patterns in poetry. (in a set with *rhyme scheme; iambic pentameter, <em>etc.</em>)

<strong>metonym</strong>: a word used in a *metonymy

<strong>metonymy</strong>: a figure of speech in which a part of the whole is put for the hole (*metonymy of the whole) or vice versa (*metonymy of the part); the part becomes a symbol of the whole

<strong>mezuzah/mezuza</strong>: a small Hebrew copy of Deut <strong><sup>6</sup></strong>:4-9 and <strong><sup>11</sup></strong>:13-21. The first of these texts by itself is also called the <em>shema, </em>the Hebrew word for its first word: “hear.”

(in a set with *shema)

<strong>-mi verbs</strong>: Greek verbs that have –mi as their dictionary form rather than –oo. They differ from each other only in forms that come from the first principal part. During the koine period several of the former –<em>mi</em> verbs were regularizing into –oo verbs. (in a set with *–oo verbs, *contract verbs)

<strong>middle voice</strong>: a voice form in Greek where the subject of the verb acts on itself (direct reflexive) or the subject acts through the verb on an object in such at way that the effect redounds to itself (indirect reflexive).

(in a set with *active voce, *passive voice)

<strong>middle-voice verb</strong>: an action verb in the middle voice (in a set with *active verb, *passive verb; *fientive verb; *stative verb; *copulative verb, *linking verb, *state-of-being verb, *to be verb)

<strong>Midrash</strong>: Hebrew commentary material written from A. D. 400-1200 on the Old Testament text containing “take-offs” on characters and events.

<strong>minimal pairs</strong> two words that are just alike except for some feature. Collecting such cases help linguists figure out how a language makes plurals, changes person and number, alters tense, and the like.

<strong>minus sign</strong>: a mathematical symbol for subtraction (-)

<strong>minuscules</strong>: cp. *lower case: small Greek letters or the kinds of manuscripts written in such letters. (in a set with *uncials)

<strong>minutes</strong>: the written record of meeting

<strong>misconstruction</strong>: a grammatical construction that does not follow the rules of usage between words or within phrases, clauses, and sentences.

<strong>mislabel</strong>: to call something by the wrong word or name, especially to do so deliberately

<strong>misnomer</strong>: a wrong or unsuitable word or name for something (or someone); noun equivalent of *<em>mislabel</em>.

<strong>misplaced modifier</strong>: a modifier whose location in the sentence connects with the wrong word: (in a set with *modify, *squinting modifier)

<strong>misplaced “only”</strong>: Ideally the word “only” should immediately precede the verb or adjective it modifies, but English idiom has developed a habit of separating it from the adjective it modifies: “The football team has only allowed two sacks and committed six turnovers all season.” The same pattern can happen with “just”:  “They just committed eight turnovers all season.”

<strong>mispronounce</strong>: to pronounce a word incorrectly—in an uninformed manner: ablā´tive rather than áblative, dătive rather than dātive, <em>etc</em>.; “Let’s get <em>goin’</em>.”

<strong>misread</strong>: to mispronounce a word in reading out loud, as when saying <em>read</em> (<em>rēd</em>, present tense) for read (<em>rĕd</em>, past tense), the verb <em>lead</em> (<em>lēd</em>)for the noun <em>lead</em> (<em>lĕd</em>), <em>brought</em> for <em>bought</em>, <em>etc.</em>

<strong>missal</strong>: a book containing all the prayers and responses of the mass for the whole liturgical year in the roman Catholic Church; a prayer book

<strong>misspoke</strong>: originally meaning “spoke out of turn,” “did not say it the way I meant it,” “did not say it the way I should have,” “gave erroneous information.” By extension the term has come to be a *euphemism for “lied.”

<strong>mixed condition</strong>: a conditional sentence, particularly in the New Testament, that does not fully conform to a regular *conditional sentence format. In Greek, it can happen especially in present particular conditions by putting any verb form in the *apodosis, and by putting any future form in the *apodosis of a *vivid future condition: Acts<strong> 5</strong>:29).

<strong>mixed metaphor</strong>: an expression that contains two metaphors to the same thing: “mixed row road to hoe.”

<strong>mode</strong>: an older designation for “mood” of verbs

<strong>Model Prayer, The</strong>: also called *The Lord’s Prayer (Mt <strong><sup>6</sup></strong>:9-13) and *Pater Noster, its Latin name from the opening two words “Our father.”

<strong>modal</strong>: pertaining to mode, or mood, as a feature of verbs

<strong>model verb</strong>: a verb that serves to illustrate a certain pattern of complete conjugation: <em>luoo</em> in Greek, <em>qatal</em> in Hebrew; <em>laudo/moneo/ago/audio/capio</em> in Latin.

<strong>modify</strong>: to make the meaning of another word more specific by adding the meaning of another word to it; to add meaning to the bare term by connecting another word to it. *Adjectives modify *nouns; *adverbs modify *verbs, adjectives, other adverbs, and *prepositions: <em>big</em> man, ran <em>quickly</em>, a <em>very</em> happy mother, <em>way</em> beyond the fence. *Prepositional phrases can be adjective or adverbial modifiers: “The horse pulled the sleigh <em>over the river</em> and <em>through the woods</em>” [adverbial, modifying <em>pulled</em>]”; “The little brown church <em>in the vale.</em>”

*Clauses can also be adjectival and adverbial. Relative clauses are (usually) adjectival—clauses with <em>who</em>, <em>which</em>, or <em>that</em>: “The young man who mowed our lawn . . . .” Adverbial clauses are dependent clauses that connect with the main verb: “When I saw the signal, I ran as fast as I could” [the introductory clause connects to <em>ran</em>].

<strong>Modifiers in a series</strong>

<strong>	Two modifiers for one phrase head. </strong>If a modifier precedes two phrase heads, it is most natural to connect it with both: “The tall man and woman live up the street.” The likelihood is that a reader/listener will suppose that both people are tall. If that is not the case, the writer needs to place second the noun it does modify and put the modifier immediately before it: “The woman and tall man up the street.” Another option is to say, “The tall man and the woman up the street.”

This same pattern applies to <em>the</em> ahead of more than one noun in a series. If two nouns are being thought of separately, <em>the</em> appears twice: “The man and the woman.” If they are being thought of as a set, <em>the</em> appears once—ahead of first noun only: “The man and the woman.”

Since Latin has no indefinite or definite articles; the placement of adjectives is even trickier. Adjectives usually follow nouns. If there are two nouns ahead of the modifier, the question becomes the same as above in the English examples: does it modify both nouns or just the last one? Even if the adjective follows the first noun, it may still be meant to connect with the second one as well.

Modifiers of words used as words are singular in Latin, Greek, and English: “<em>This </em><em><u>foolish ones</u></em> is translated <em>fools</em>.”

(in a set with *agreement, *misplaced modifier, *punctuation, *serial comma, * serial modifiers, *squinting modifier, *double meaning)

<strong>modulation</strong>: the inflection of the voice as to pitch, timber, volume while speaking; in contrast to speaking in a monotone (in a set with *monotone)

<strong>moniker</strong>: *nickname

<strong>monoglot</strong>: a person that knows only one language

<strong>monogram</strong>: a person’s initials, or first letters of first, middle, and last name; especially so-called when the letters are put on a public document, pennant, and the like.

(in a set with *abbreviation, *initials)

<strong>Monograph</strong>: a scholarly writing of book length on a specific, limited subject

<strong>monolingual</strong>: able to speak one language only. (*bilingual, *trilingual)

<strong>monolog(ue)</strong>: a presentation by one speaker <em> vs.</em> a discussion between two or more people; part of a play where one actor gives a more lengthy segment. A segment spoken by one person between musical segments of a cantata. Normally associated with telling about persons and events rather than about scholarly, conceptual matters

(in a set with *dialogue)

<strong>monophthong</strong>: (1) one vowel sound represented by two vowel letters as <em>ea</em> in <em>beat</em>. (2) a vowel sound that does not glide from one vowel to another, as in <em>met.</em> A characteristic of Italian vowels, which makes it particularly suitable for operatic singing.???????

(in a set with *diphthong; *vowel glide)

<strong>monosyllabic/monosyllable</strong>: having one syllable: Greek: <em>pous</em> (foot), ek (out of); Latin: <em>ars</em> (art), ab (away from); Hebrew:

<strong>monotone</strong>: speaking at the same pitch, speed, timber, and volume

(in a set with *modulation)

<strong>mood</strong>: also “mode,” in earlier usage; the manner in which an action is meant

Indicative mood simply indicates action.

Imperative mood commands action, including request, advice, and entreaty [English, Greek, Latin, Hebrew]

Subjunctive mood “suggests” action [Greek, Latin, English rarely]. Subjunctive is used in *contingency clauses, where the actions is dependent on some other action.

Optative mood “supposes/wishes” action [Greek]

Infinitive: [older English]

*contingent action (*subjunctive mood), or—as in Greek—uncertain action (*optative mood). The optative mood is missing in English and Latin and reduced in New Testament Greek. So its values get absorbed into the subjunctive in Latin or covered by *circumlocution. Likewise, the subjunctive has virtually disappeared from English, many of its usages being absorbed into the indicative.

The various moods are indicated in inflectional languages by signs. Greek signals subjunctive mood by lengthened connecting vowels—lengthened o/e variable. The optative sign is an <em>i</em>-diphthong in the connecting vowel position.

Latin signals the subjunctive by switching connecting vowels of the four classes of verbs. Instead of <em>–ā-, -ē -, -ĕ-,</em> and <em>–ī-</em> in the first, second, third, and fourth conjugations respectively, there is –<em>e-, -ea, -a-,</em> and –<em>ia</em>- (we fear a l<em>ia</em>r/let’s eat caviar).

(in a set with *person, *number, *tense, *voice, and *aspect as possible features of verb forms)

<strong>mood sign</strong>: In Greek the sign for the subjunctive mood is the lengthened connecting vowel—the o/e variable lengthened to omega/eta. The Greek sign for optative mood is an iota diphthong in the connecting vowel position, either –oi, -ai, or -ei.

In Latin the sign for the present-tense subjunctive mood is switched connecting vowels. In the layout according to customary mood numbering, –are, -ēre, ere, -ire verbs have their vowels switched according to the jingle “We fear a liar” or “Let’s eat caviar.” The basic idea is to use an a vowel in the connecting-vowel position in place of the vowel that the last three conjugations have there. Since the first conjugation has an a vowel in that position already, it uses an e vowel.

<strong>morality play</strong>:

<strong>morph</strong>: an allomorph

<strong>morpheme</strong>: the smallest unit of meaning at the word or sub-word level. Morphemes are used to create one part of speech from another in a set of *cognate words; to indicate singular and plural, person, tense, aspect, voice gender in verbs; case, number, gender in nouns.

(in a set with *tagmeme, *phoneme)

(in a set with *suffix, *prefix,* infix, *affix, *word roots, and the like)

<strong>mophemics</strong>: the specialty that deal with *morphemes, entailing identification, description, and classification of meaning units per language

<strong>mot</strong>: a witticism, a witty or insightful remark

<strong>morphology</strong>: the study of the indivisible units of meaning that indicate inflection, derivation, and word formation

<strong>morphophonemics</strong>: the study of the pronunciation changes caused by neighboring sounds during inflection, derivation, or grammatical relations

<strong>mother tongue</strong>: one’s *native language, or first language; “the language wherein we were born” (Acts <strong><sup>2</sup></strong>)

<strong>motto</strong>: a saying that highlights a concept that group members consider their guide for operation.

<strong>movable</strong>: a letter, usually a consonant on the end of a word, that is added to separate vowels: the <em>a/an</em> variant for the English indefinite article, the <em>a/an </em>variant on the Greek negating prefix as in <em><u>an</u></em><em>ion, </em><em><u>a</u></em><em>theist</em>.

The <em>n</em>-movable in Greek appeared after all <em>–si</em> endings if the next word started with a vowel or if there was punctuation. It appeared after third singular endings in plain<em> e</em> (rather than <em>–ei</em>). It was used also after <em>esti</em>(<em>n</em>), <em>etc.</em> The New Testament affords innumerable examples of using the <em>nu</em> movable before consonants as well. So the rule is breaking down from the pattern in classical Greek. Other examples of movables in Greek are <em>houtoos</em> (<em> vs.</em> <em>houtoo</em>) before vowels, <em>ouk</em> (<em> vs.</em> <em>ou</em>) before non-fricative consonants (<em>ouch</em> appeared before fricatives) and οὐξ before vowels, <em>achris</em> (<em> vs.</em> <em>achri</em>) before vowels. <strong>musical</strong>: a play or movie that has dialogue interspersed with musical passages.

<strong>mute: </strong>synonym for a *stop; the letters <em>p, b; t, d; c/k, g</em>(<em>uh</em>).

<strong>myth</strong>: a fictitious or largely unhistorical narrative that pictures an ideology or attempts to explain some natural phenomenon that is improperly understood or difficult to describe clearly. It historicizes or gives eventness to recurrent principles at work in man’s interaction with his environment

<strong>name</strong>: The noun that someone or something is called to distinguish it from others.

(in a set with *alias, *appellation, *attributive name, *cognomen, *first name, *given name, *last name, *maiden name, *middle name, *nee, *nickname, *nomen, *proper name, *surname)

<strong>name calling</strong>: Calling someone something derogatory as when Jesus called Peter <em>Satan</em> in Mt <strong><sup>16</sup></strong>:23. He calls Herod “that old fox” in Lk <strong><sup>13</sup></strong>:32. Paul calls the high priest a “whited wall,” and then apologizes (Acts <strong><sup>23</sup></strong>:3). (in a set with *epithet)

<strong>name dropping</strong>: using the name of a well-known or important in such a way as to imply personal relationship and therefore the importance of the speaker.

<strong>namesake</strong>: the person someone is named after or the person named after the other person; someone who has the same name.

<strong>narrative</strong>:

<strong>nasal</strong>: a sound made by moving air through the nose instead of the mouth. Different kinds of nasal sounds are created by closing off the air at different pronunciation points in this process. A labio-nasal (<em>m</em>) cuts off air at the lips; a dental-nasal (<em>n</em>) cuts it off behind the teeth; a velar nasal (<em>ng</em>) cuts it off at the back of the soft pallet.

<strong>nasalize</strong>: to say a sound, particularly a vowel, in a way that runs the air through the nasal cavity instead of the oral cavity.

<strong>native language</strong>: same as *mother tongue, one’s *first language

<strong>near future</strong>: a form of the verb that indicates something fairly soon to happen. Same at *immediate future.

<strong>near demonstrative</strong>:  a demonstrative that points out something/someone thought of as near the speaker: “this/these.” Classical Greek had two near demonstratives, which distinguished “aforesaid” (houtos) from “to be said” (hode). The distinction is not maintained in the New Testament. (in a set with *distant demonstrative)

<strong>near past</strong>: a verb construction that indicates that the action took place recently. Contrasts with *distant past. Same as *immediate past.

<strong>necessity</strong>: In classical Greek, necessity could be expressed by an impersonal verbal (C & S 178). In Latin it could be expressed by gerundive (future passive ptc); “Plus novisti quid faciendum sit” (You have learned more what must be done wh 224#4AS).

<strong>negation</strong>: indicating that something is not so. Greek A-class conditional clauses may be used alone as a kind of *aposiopesis to indicate negation: Mk <strong><sup>8</sup></strong>:2; Heb<strong> 3</strong>:11; <strong><sup>4</sup></strong>:3, 5; cp. <strong><sup>3</sup></strong>:18.

<strong>negatives: </strong>words or word-elements that deny an assumed reality; in western languages there is a strong association of initial <em>n</em> with negation: German <em>nein</em>, Russian <em>nyet;</em> English <em>no, not, un- </em>(untrue), <em>in-/im-/il/ir- </em>(invalid/impersonal/illogical/irreverent), <em>non-</em> (nondenominational), <em>never</em> (<em> vs.</em> <em>ever</em>), <em>none</em> (<em> vs.</em> <em>one</em>), <em>nobody</em> (<em> vs.</em> <em>somebody</em>), <em>nor</em> (<em> vs.</em> <em>or</em>), <em>neither</em> (<em> vs.</em> <em>either</em>), <em>nothing</em> (<em> vs.</em> <em>thing</em>), <em>no one </em>(<em> vs.</em> <em>someone</em>), <em>nil, null, no-no, nah</em>, and the word <em>negate</em>, <em>etc.</em>

Some nuances between these words can be subtle. <em>Non</em>- can mean having no reference to (non-denominational, having no reference to denominationalism); <em>un</em>- means not (undenominational, not denominated).

Latin prominently uses the <em>n</em> sound, especially an initial <em>n</em> sound, for negation: <em>nolo</em> (<em>ne</em> + <em>volo</em>), <em>non, nonne, numquam</em> (<em> vs.</em> <em>umquam</em>), <em>neuter</em> (<em> vs.</em> <em>uter</em>), <em>nullus</em> (<em> vs.</em> <em>ullus</em>), <em>nescio</em> (not to know <em> vs.</em> <em>scio</em>), <em>nemo</em> (no one), <em>nisi</em> (if not <em> vs. si</em>, if), <em>neque/nec</em> . . . <em>neque/nec</em> (neither . . . nor), <em>nonnumquam</em> (never), <em>nego</em> (to deny), <em>nihil</em> (nothing), <em>nihilum</em> (nothing), <em>nimis/nimium</em> (excessively, exceedingly), <em>nisi</em> (if not) <em> vs.</em> <em>si</em> (if); <em>nondum</em> (not yet <em> vs.</em> <em>dum</em> while), <em>necesse</em> (unnecessary), <em>num</em> (introduces indirect questions that anticipate a negative response), <em>ne</em> (negative with subjunctives in dependent clauses), <em>neve</em> (nor, used for continuing <em>ut</em> or <em>ne</em> + subjunctive).

Latin tends to negate the direct object in places where English negates the verb: <em>Nullam pacem habere poteramus</em> (“We were able to have no peace”); <em>quoniam tyrannus copias ducit, nullam pacem habebimus</em> (“Since the tyrant is leading the troops, we will have no peace.” Note a similar thing in Greek when Paul writes to the Galatians: “Christ will profit you nothing” instead of “Christ won’t profit you any” (<strong><sup>5</sup></strong>:2).

Greek has two negatives and their cognates: <em>ou</em> and <em>mee</em>: <em>ouchi; ouketi/meeketi</em>; oὔ; <em>oude/meede, oudeis/meedeis, oudepote</em>/<em>meedepote</em>, <em>oupww/meepoo</em>, <em>oute/meete; ou mee equals emphatic negative</em> (certainly not). As a general pattern in New Testament Greek, <em>ou</em> and its cognates go with the indicative mood;  and its cognates go with other verb forms.

Hebrew has two negatives: לא, אל

<strong>neoplastic ????</strong>

<strong>neologism/neologize/neology</strong>: a new word, expression, or new meaning for an old one. To create a new word; same as *coin a term. Several words were coined for <em>Lord of the Rings</em>: <em>halfling, hobbit, ork,,</em>  (in a set with *nonce, *coin a word)

<strong>neuter</strong>: In English, gender corresponds almost completely with sex reference; so neuter refers to sexless things <em> vs.</em> person or animals. In Greek and Latin, neuter is used for some sexless things, but masculine and feminine are also used for them; it is therefore a grammatical category. Greek may be said sometimes to use neuter for *common, as in the case of *<em>brephos</em> (baby), <em>paidion</em> (little child), <em>teknon</em> and <em>teknion</em> (child and little child). This practice could also be viewed as applying to those of such age that gender is irrelevant.

In older English idiom, feminine gender (she) was occasionally applied to ships, countries, cities; but the practice has almost completely fallen out of usage.

(in a set with *feminine, *masculine; *gender)

<strong>neutral text</strong>: (in a set with *majority text, *western text, *Byzantine text)

<strong>newsletter</strong>: a regular communique from an organization that is sent out ot its supports or those interested in its work. It tell “news” about events instead of dealing with ideas as such. A kind of *ephemeral paper.

<strong>newsmonger</strong>: a person fixed on telling other people what is happening as a way of feeling important; a gossip.

<strong>newspaper</strong>: designed primarily to help the readership keep up on events

(in a set with *magazine, a *daily, a *weekly; *periodical)

<strong>newspeak</strong>; a deliberately unclear way of speak as a way to manipulate and mislead speak.

(in a set with *double talk)

<strong>nickname</strong>: a familiar, unofficial name given by others in popular use as a replacement of the person’s real, or given, name; a *moniker. It may be a term based on some noticeable trait or it may be a short like the letters of a first and middle name—like J.T. 	Ex. Joseph, called Barnabas by the apostles (Acts<strong> 4</strong>:36). WH333#11

<strong>night letter</strong>: a telegram sent at night for a lower cost to be received by the next morning.

<strong>Neviim</strong>: Hebrew word for “prophets” and a designation for one of the three basic divisions of Old Testament literature. (in a set with *Chetubim and *Torah)

<strong>nod</strong>: to make a gesture meaning “yes” by moving the head up and down

(in a set with *gesture)

<strong>noise</strong>: sounds that do not have linguisticality

<strong>nom de guerre</strong>: a fictitious name, a pseudonym

<strong>nom de plume</strong>: French for “*pen name,” the *alias that a writer writes under to hide his identity for some reason or to help set some air of humor or seriousness that would not be created by the use of his real name. Fearing that her words would not be taken as seriously as they should, a woman in previous times might use a pen name or mere initials to sign her work. A writer may fear that his message would be dismissed as insignificant because of who he is known or thought to be. (in a set with *aka, *alias, *pen name, *pseudonym)

<strong>nomen</strong>: the middle name in the Latin three-name system (in a set with *praenomen, *cognomen)

<strong>nomenclator</strong>: a person involved in developing the terminology for a discipline

<strong>nomenclature</strong>: a set of special, often systematic terminology associated with a particular discipline or area of interest.

<strong>nominal</strong>: (1) having to do with proper names; (2) having to do with nouns (in a set with *verbal, *prepositional, adverbial, *adjectival)

<strong>nominative absolute: </strong>English has a nominative absolute that corresponds to the Greek genitive absolute and the Latin ablative absolute: “that being said, I still do not think he will succeed.” As with all absolute construction, the subject of the dependent element cannot appear in the main clause or at least as subject of the main clause.

As an expression, *nominative absolute can be used to cover for certain Greek *hanging constructions (*pendens) that do not replace the pendens with a subsequent pronoun. (Lk <strong><sup>21</sup></strong>:6 D & M 70)

<strong>nominative case</strong>: same as subjective case in English grammatical terminology. Nominative case serves (1) as the subject of a finite verb: “I love chocolate.”

(2) for *predicate nominative in a linking-verb sentence: “She is my <em>mother</em>.”

(3) on a *word used as a word (*nominative of appellation; Jn <strong><sup>13</sup></strong>:13): He wrote <em>I</em> on the chalkboard.”

(4) as the noun, adjective, or participle after a passive-verb transform of an active-voice sentence that had a direct object plus *objective complement: “They elected him president” transformed to “He was elected president by them.” This last instance is really an extension of the predicate noun idea since it amounts to saying, “He became president” (Note Lk<strong> 2</strong>:21; <strong><sup>19</sup></strong>:2)

(5) New Testament Greek shows some examples of *independent nominative, or *parenthetic nominative, a “hanging” noun in the nominative that is not the subject of any verb (Lk<strong> 21</strong>:6; Mk <strong><sup>8</sup></strong>:2; Eph <strong><sup>4</sup></strong>:15, <em>etc.</em> D&M70).

Finally, (6) the *nominative of exclamation is a noun with no verb and carries an exclamatory intent (Rom <strong><sup>7</sup></strong>:24; Mk <strong><sup>3</sup></strong>:4; Rom <strong><sup>11</sup></strong>:33).

(in a set with *nominative of appellation, *parenthetic nominative, *subject)

<strong>nominative pendens</strong>: a “hanging” construction in the nominative case. It consists of a lead-in that is then replaced with a pronoun to sum it up before the speaker continues with the thought. Akin to *“double subject”: “The dead—they also are fortunate.” (in a set with *accusative pendens, *double subject, *independent nominative)

<strong>nomothetic</strong>: having to do with the writing laws

<strong>nonce</strong>: a term created for a special occasion; a kind of *neologism

<strong>non-distinctive</strong>: a *phonemic difference that does no change the meaning; the kinds of variants that exist in regionalism of in the systematic mispronunciations that speakers make in a second language

<strong>non-fiction</strong>: a genre of writing in story form that recounts actual events.

(in a se with *fiction)

<strong>non-literate</strong>: pertaining to a society that does not have a written form of the language

<strong>non-restrictive</strong>: not limited to one item within a class. In English punctuation, non-restrictive clauses and participial phrases are set off with commas, but non-restrictive adjectives and prepositional phrases are not marked in this way. Also called nonessential to the grammatical structure within which they appear.  Non-restrictive clauses are also called *descriptive clauses.

<strong>examples of non-restrictive phrases and adjectives</strong>:

“Do not be <em>unequally </em>yoked together with unbelievers” (2 Cor <strong><sup>6</sup></strong>:14). The point is not that it is okay to be equally yoked together with them. Rather, Paul is concerned about believers being connected with unbelievers, which is an unequal situation that may put them in compromising situations.

Not being greedy of “filthy lucre” does not mean it is okay to be greedy of clean lucre. (Tit<strong> 1</strong>:7, 11; 1 Tim<strong> 3</strong>:3, 8; 1 Pet <strong><sup>5</sup></strong>:2). The non-restrictive element in the English translation is meant to emphasize rather than contrast the “filthiness” of greediness.

“You, being evil, know how to give good gifts to your children” (Mt <strong><sup>7</sup></strong>:11). Jesus does not mean to say that “the ones of you who are evil” know how to give good gifts to you children”; others do not. “Being evil” characterizes everyone in his audience, and so is not restricted to some.

(in a set with *restrictive)

<strong>non-verbal</strong>: communication other than through words; hence, gestures as well as unintentional, unconscious cues people give that show what they are thinking or how the feel.

(in a set with *body language)

<strong>normalize</strong>: to alter peculiar or unusual features of a language in a way that conforms them to standard patterns of inflection, pronunciation. To standardize; in the case of English usage of words originating elsewhere, to anglicize. Using <em>amoebas</em> for the plural of <em>amoeba</em>, <em>hippopotoamuses</em> for <em>hippopotomi</em>.

<strong>normative grammar</strong>: an approach to grammar that calls for following conventions already established; contrasts with descriptive grammar, which merely tells how people actually use their language aside from any consideration of what is proper or improper.

<strong>notary</strong>: a public official authorized to witness the legality of public records

<strong>notice</strong>: an announcement; a formal or even legal warning

<strong>noun</strong>: a part of speech that can stand before <em>the</em>; a word that can be made plural by adding s/(z) or other some other suffix or infix indicating plurality; it can occupy *subject, *direct object, *indirect object, and *object of the preposition slots.

(in a set with *construct noun [Heb], *abstract noun, *appelative noun, *attributive noun, *collective noun, *common noun, *predicate noun, *proper noun, *noun typical of its class)

<strong>noun clause: </strong>A clause that fills a noun slot: subject, direct object, indirect object, object of a preposition. Examples are the *indirect discourse section of a sentence (but Latin uses an infinitive phrase), *indirect question, *object clauses of different sorts. In older English usage seldom occurring now, a relative clause could fill a noun slot: “Whom the gods love dies young.” (in a set with *adjectival clause, *adverbial clause *jussive noun clause [L]):

<strong>noun typical of its class</strong>: usage of a noun in the singular in such a way that it stands for all the members of the group it is in. In English, a definite article was used with such a noun until more recently. In more modern usage, an indefinite article customarily appears: “the artist should have a thorough understanding of human anatomy”; <em> vs.</em> “an artist should have a thorough understanding of human anatomy.”  In Greek, the article appears with a noun typical of its class.

<strong>noun slots</strong>: subject, direct object, indirect object, object of a preposition

<strong>nu</strong>: thirteenth letter of the Greek alphabet: ν/Ν. Nu was a movable letter after third singulars in plain –e, after all words ending in -si, after esti(n), eisi(v). In the New Testament the movable nu was always attached, regardless of circumstances, to third singulars of the imperfect and second aorist indicatives. The movable was inserted if there was upcoming punctuation or if the next word began with a vowel.

<strong>nuance</strong>: a subtle adjustment of a word’s meaning to adapt it to the demands of a new subject. cp. *connotation)

<strong>number</strong> (grammatical number)

Used on nouns, verbs and adjectives in Greek, Latin and English. Only *demonstrative adjectives (<em>this, that</em>) in English distinguish singular and plural by form.

Plural

Dual: in classical Greek, especially in poetry, there were distinctive dual forms ? in the verbs and nouns???

In Greek, Latin, and English, *word used as a word are considered singular: “<em><u>This</u></em><em> foolish ones </em>describes the monkeys.” The same goes for *hendiadys: “The <em>God and Father</em> of our Lord Jesus Christ <em>is</em>” (1 Th <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:3).

(in a set with *gender and *case for *“locating” a *noun or *adjective)

(in a set with *person, *tense, *mood, *voice, *aspect [*Aktionzart] as possible verb forms)

<strong>numbers</strong>: Greek, Latin, and Hebrew have words for numbers, just as English does. In addition, Greek and Hebrew used letters of the alphabet for numbers. When so used in Greek, the letters had a horizontal line above them or a short slanted line upward to the right after the letter. Using the same symbol to the lower left multiplied the number by one thousand. They could be added together to form numbers between the tens i(a´). note sampi, san, koppa, digamma stigma in the scheme.

Cardinal numbers: “There was one horse in the corral.”

Ordinal numbers are adjectives: “Blackie was the first horse to get in the corral.”

In sets, English can say “two by two” or “two at a time.” Greek used kata + a number for this idea: for example, kata duo = two by two: Mk <strong><sup>14</sup></strong>:19; Jn <strong><sup>8</sup></strong>:9; 21:15; Acts <strong><sup>21</sup></strong>:19; Rom <strong><sup>12</sup></strong>:5; 1 Cor <strong><sup>14</sup></strong>:31; Eph <strong><sup>5</sup></strong>:33; Rev <strong><sup>4</sup></strong>:8. Greek could also put numbers side by side for this purpose: For example, duo duo = two by two: Mk <strong><sup>6</sup></strong>:7.

(in a set with *arabic numbers, *approximate numbers, *round numbers; *ordinal numbers, *cardinal numbers; *Roman numerals)

<strong>nun</strong>: the fourteenth letter of the Hebrew alphabet: נ with ן as its terminal form

<strong>oath</strong>: (1) a commitment not to lie in a legal proceeding; (2) coarse, impolite language expressed in anger (in a set with *archaism, *buzz word, *coarse talk, *colloquialism, *curse/cuss, *swear; *archaism, *buzz word, *catch phrase, *colloquialism, *form term, *informal term, &obsolete term, *provincialism, *regionalism, *slang, *vulgarism)

<strong>obelisk/obelize</strong>: a reference mark shaped like a dagger or cross

<strong>obelus</strong>: a mark like a dash or division sign (– or ÷) used in ancient manuscripts to indicate that a passage may be spurious (not original), incorrect, or uncertain in some way.

<strong>obfuscate/abfuscation</strong>: intentionally to hide the meaning rather than unveil it; to make difficult to understand by using highly complex grammatical structures, by unexplained technical vocabulary, by confused or meaningless expressions and inappropriate grammatical connections.

(in a set with *ambiguity)

<strong>obituary</strong> a brief article announcing the death of a person and giving an outline of her life, relatives, major events, and achievements.

<strong>object</strong>: a word grammatically governed by another word. A *direct object is governed by the verb and so is put in the objective, or accusative, *case.

An *indirect object likewise comes off of a verb that also governs a direct object; in English indirect objects are objective case after the preposition <em>to</em> if placed after the direct object or in the objective case without<em> to</em> if preceding the direct object. In Latin and Greek, the dative case without a preposition indicates the indirect object. Hebrew uses the *inseparable preposition ﬥ prefixed to a noun or a pronominal suffix to show indirect object.

The *object of a preposition is governed by the preposition. English always uses objective case after all prepositions. Greek has prepositions that govern the genitive, the dative, and the accusative cases, depending on the preposition. Latin prepositions govern the accusative or ablative case, depending on the preposition.

<strong>object clause: </strong>one of several kinds of clauses that serve as replacements for a direct object; so they have a substantive value: indirect question, indirect statement (in some Greek indirect-discourse constructions), and non-interrogative “how” clauses after verbs of striving, effecting, caring for (“He is planning <em>how to persuade them</em>” C & S p. 166).

<strong>objective case</strong>: the English grammatical term for *accusative case, the case used in 
English for *direct objects, *indirect objects, and *objects of prepositions.  Greek and Latin use *dative case for indirect objects; Greek and Latin use objective case after some prepositions, especially those that imply destination.

(in a set with *subjective case, *possessive case)

<strong>objective complement: </strong>Some active-voice verbs can govern (a) two objects (*nouns, *substantives): one of the person, the other of the thing: “they elected him <em>president</em>.” That second noun “complements” the *direct object <em>him</em>. The relationship of direct object and objective complement is the same as the relationship between a subject and a *predicate nominative, the same as the relationship between a noun and its appositive, and the same as the relationship between the subject and predicate noun in the passive transform of a sentence that had an objective complement. “They elected him <em>governor</em>”; “He was <em>governor</em>”; “He, the <em>king</em>, led forth in battle”; “<em>He</em> was elected <em>governor</em>.” In each case, the more narrow-reference substantive is the class; the other is the particular—like a circle within a circle. 	Verbs typically involved in objective-complement constructions are <em>call, choose, consider, deem, elect, find, name, think</em>.

Objective complement can happen also with (b) adjectives. Passive transforms of objective-complement adjectives occur in Acts <strong><sup>5</sup></strong>:39, “And you-be-found (to be) God<strong>-</strong>opposing<strong>-</strong>(ones).”

Mt <strong><sup>25</sup></strong>:38:“When did we see you a strange-(one) and naked?”

An objective complement can be a (c) participle: Mt<strong> 25</strong>:37, 39; “Whenever he saw anyone poorly <em>clothed</em> , . . .” (WH Latin grammar). They found the prison <em>locked</em>; they found the guards <em>standing</em> (Acts <strong><sup>5</sup></strong>:23).

The English examples above have parallels in Greek and Latin as well. Acts <strong><sup>5</sup></strong>:31 says, “This one God raised up (as) leader and savior.” Acts <strong><sup>13</sup></strong>:5; 1 Jn <strong><sup>4</sup></strong>:14;

Sometimes Greek adds <em>hoos</em> ahead of the objective complement in a fashion similar to the English <em>as</em> in the translation of Acts <strong><sup>5</sup></strong>:23 above: “They regard him as [<em>hoos</em>] a prophet” (Mt <strong><sup>14</sup></strong>:5). Another, equivalent format uses <em>eis</em> to introduce the complement: “They regarded him unto [being] a prophet” (Mt<strong> 21</strong>:46). What of <em>einai</em>?

An adjective objective complement occurs in <sup>1</sup>Tim <strong><sup>6</sup></strong>:1:

A participial objective complement occurs in Mt <strong><sup>24</sup></strong>:30:

<strong>objective genitive</strong>: In Greek and Latin, objective genitive involves a noun tied to an action noun by the genitive case. The genitive-case noun refers to the object of the action that is in the other noun: “the love of God,” meaning “love <em>for</em> God,” as in “the love that I have <em>for</em> God.” Objective genitive contrasts with *subjective genitive: “the love of God,” meaning “love <em>by</em> God,” as in “the love that God has for me.” Note the situation in Rom <strong><sup>8</sup></strong>:35, (37), 39. English can use an “of” construction for either idea.

(in a set with *subjective genitive)

<strong>oblique cases</strong>: as a set, the cases outside the *nominative

<strong>obloquy</strong>: abusive language address at a person in order to disgrace him; calumny

<strong>obscenity</strong>: a vulgar expression, a curse word, a swear word, an oath; a “*four-letter word”; often intended to show disrespect, hurt someone’s feelings; words often used in times of stress, fear, and anger. On the eve of the crucifixion, Peter’s reaction the second time he was accused of being one of Jesus’ followers (Mt <strong><sup>27</sup></strong>:74).

<strong>obsolete term</strong>: a term no longer used or a meaning no longer used with that word. People may understand the meaning of an <em>obsolete</em> word, but <em>archaic</em> tends to refer to a meaning no longer in memory.

(in a set with *archaism, buzz word, *catch phrase, *colloquialism, *curse word, *formal term, *informal term, *oath, *provincialism, *regionalism, *slang, *technical term, *vulgarism)

<strong>ode</strong>: a poem of praise, a rhyming tribute (in a set with* hymn, *paean, *psalm)

<strong>octosyllable</strong>: a line of verse containing eight *syllables

<strong>off script</strong>: speaking other than the official position taken by the group the speaker represents; speaking in a way, instead, that reflects the speakers own convictions. Contrasts with *on script.

<strong>off-color</strong>: terms, expressions, or jokes with sexual inuendo.

<strong>“off-the-cuff”: </strong>without forethought, preparation, or careful weighing of pertinent information; cp. *ad lib, *extemporary;

<strong>omega</strong>: twenty-fourth letter of the Greek alphabet: ω/Ω. Long “o,” paired with omicron, short “o.” [o + mega, big “o”]

<strong>omega verbs</strong>: Greek verbs that have –oo on the end of their dictionary forms rather than –<em>mi</em>. They differ from each other only in forms that come from the first principal part. (in a set with *–mi verbs, *contract verbs)

<strong>omicron</strong>: fifteenth letter of the Greek alphabet: ο/Ο. Short “o,” paired with omega, the long “o.” [o + micron, small “o”]

<strong>omniscient viewpoint</strong>: an account told from above, or outside, the events rather than through the eyes of a character in the episode. (in a set with *anthropocentric viewpoint, *geocentric viewpoint, *omniscient viewpoint()

<strong>on script</strong>: speaking in a way that represents the groups standard claim (standard “line”) rather than the position the speaker personally believes. Contrasts with *”off script. (in a set with *spin, *line)

<strong>oneiromancy</strong>: the interpretation of dreams, especially as an aid to prediction

<strong>onomastics</strong>: the study of the origin and form of proper names or specialized terms in a certain field together with the patterns of formation through which such items pass in the process of word building

<strong>onomatopoeia</strong>: the formation of words that sound like what they mean. English has<em> buzz, clunk, hiss</em>, <em>boom</em>, <em>crash, murmur, splash, ululate </em>(lament loudly),<em> zip</em>. All the “words” for animal sounds: <em>arf</em>, <em>baa</em>, <em>caw, cheep</em>, <em>cluck</em>, <em>meow</em>, <em>moo</em>, <em>neigh</em>, <em>oink, peep, quack, ruff, woof, yap, yip</em>; these are “words” in that they are stylized sounds that do not exactly reproduce the sound the animal makes, and they are subject to *inflection for number, case, tense, person, and the like/

Greek has some examples of anonmatopoeia as well: <em>gogguzoo </em>(grumble),<em> thalassa/thaattal</em> (sounds vaguely like the waves of the “sea”), <em>ptuoo</em> (to “spit”).

<strong><u>op. cit</u></strong><strong>.</strong>: abbreviation for “opus cited”; that is, a work referred to earlier in the text or previous footnotes.

<strong>opposition</strong>: the contrast in a language between two phonemest that create meaning differences or systematic ways of changing parts of speech: English <em>read/read, lead/led, </em>Greek uses <em>o/e</em> to distinguish verb and noun roots and principal parts of verbs: <em><u>gen</u></em><em>naoo/</em><em><u>gon</u></em><em>eus </em>(to beget/a parent), <em><u>leg</u></em><em>oo/</em><em><u>log</u></em><em>os</em> (to speak/word), <em>leipoo/loipos, menoo/hupo</em><em><u>mon</u></em><em>ee</em> (to remain/perseverance [“remain under”]), <em>speiroo/spora</em> (sow/seed), <em>speudoo/spoudee</em> (hasten/zeal), <em><u>tem</u></em><em>noo/</em><em><u>tem</u></em><em>os</em> (o cut/incision), <em>trepoo/tropos</em> (turn/a turn), <em>tremoo/tromos (tremble/a trembling), trephoo/trophee</em> (nourish/nourishment), <em>pheroo/phortion</em> (to bear/burden); <em>rempoo/pepompha</em> (1st/3rd principal parsts of “send”), <em>peitho/pepoitha/epepoitheiv</em> (1st <em> vs.</em> <sup>3</sup>rd and <sup>4</sup>th principal parts, “persuade),

<strong>optative mood</strong>: a mood in Greek verbs used for expressing a wish and other types of more vague and uncertain action. It often replaced the subjunctive in subordinate clauses after *leading verbs in past tenses. The mood is tending to fall out by the time of New Testament Greek, appearing about 120 times. In Latin the function of the optative is taken over by the subjunctive. English also lacks the mood as a verb form, but covers its ideas by *circumlocution or the subjunctive mood.

<strong>oracle</strong>: a person through whom God gives answers, commands, or predicts; the revelation itself, especially an enigmatic and therefore mysterious directive or prediction. Its ambiguity makes it easier to equate with a subsequent event that it purports to envision. An example like the famous oracle of Apollo at Delphi.

<strong>parable</strong>: The use of the word varies in the New Testament. Most familiarly a parable is a story with a point, particularly in the *synoptic gospels. It is generally true-to-life rather than fanciful, like a *fable. It is a story form with usually one moral lesson, and unlike an *allegory does not have a point-by-point correspondence between the lesson of the story. The word picture in <em>parable</em> (<em>parabolee</em>) is “to throw alongside.” Hence, a comparison.

Another word translated “parable” occurs in John’s gospel (), but it is a more general term that draws attention to a comparison for the sake of interest and clarification. Word picture

In older versions of the Old Testament, <em>parable</em> translates the Hebrew word () in Balaam’s “parables” ( Num <strong><sup>23</sup></strong>:7-10, 18-24; <strong><sup>24</sup></strong>:3-9, 15-24)

Jotham’s “parable” of the bramble bush (Jud <strong><sup>9</sup></strong>:8-15) qualifies as a *fable because of its unreal character.
