<strong>paradigm</strong>: the layout of a word’s pattern of inflection

<strong>paradox</strong>: a contradiction of words but not of realities.

<strong>paragraph</strong>: a sub-unit of written communication consisting of more than one sentence and ideally containing a *topic sentence, elaboration, and finalizing comment. (in a set with verses, chapters, parts,* books, volumes); in a set with *words, *sentences)

<strong>parajournalism</strong>: a kind of journalism that mixes in exaggeration and fictional elements (in a set with *pesher?)

<strong>parallel</strong>: a sign indicating material presented elsewhere in a note or reference

<strong>parallelism</strong>: (1) the primary characteristic of Semitic poetry in which relatively brief statements are arranged in succession. These successive entries may say the same thing a second way (*synonymous parallelism), in a contrasting way (*antithetical parallelism), the same thing in an extended way (*synthetic parallelism).

(2) The use of parallel syntactical structures for the purposes of balance and elegance.

<strong>paralogism</strong>: a form of argument that conforms to the form of proper reason but in fact leads to an illogical conclusion. An example would be the informal fallacy of the undistributed middle.

Elephants love peanuts;

this animal loves peanuts;

therefore, this animal is an elephant.

<strong>paraph</strong>: a flourish over or under a person’s signature

<strong>paraphrase</strong>: to express something in wording that does not correspond word for word with the original quotation or translation, a re-expression that strives to maintain content but in a way the present speaker or writer would word it “from scratch.” It is like translating a sentence or paragraph at a time rather than a word at a time.

<strong>paraphrasis</strong>: noun for *paraphrase

<strong>pariphrastic</strong>: adjective for *paraphrase.

<strong>parapraxis</strong>:  small error, slip of the tongue, that supposedly reveals a subconscious motives Webster)

<strong>paraprosdokian</strong>:

<strong>paratactic/prataxis</strong>: The use of parallel, independent constructions in contrast to subordinating one idea to another: “She stayed and helped with the clean-up” <em> vs.</em> “she stayed to help with the clean-up.” Of course, the two sentences do not make the exact same statement, but the habit of choosing the “and” format (which says less) does not make the conversation ambiguous; it just requires the listener to be more active in bringing what he knows from his own experience and adding it to what the speaker has actually said.

Parataxis also applied to parallel parts in compound words, to parallel clauses or phrases laid side by side with or without *co-ordinate conjunctions or connectors between them.

(in a set with *hypotactic; *asyndeton)

<strong>parent language</strong>: the language from which another language has descended, as Spanish and the other Romance languages have come down from Latin.

<strong>parenthesis</strong>: two vertical arcs ( ) used basically for separating its contents from the rest of the surrounding material.

In writing, the two arcs are placed (a) before and after an insertion within a line of writing, syntactically disconnected from that setting, and adding related information not directly in the flow of thought. Implies as greater disjunction from surrounding material than a double *dash does.

(b) Separates the area code from the rest of a phone number.

(c) Groups subunits in a mathematical expression.

(d) Marks off enumerated entries as in this list of parenthesis usages.


?? in the formation of words, the practice of compounding words by putting previously existing words side by side with little or no alteration.

<strong>parenthetic nominative</strong>: another term for *independent nominative, one that does not serve as subject of the upcoming verb; one not syntactically part of the sentence structure.

<strong>parenthetical clause</strong>: WH309#96

<strong>parenthetical expression</strong>: elements in a sentence that are not necessary for expressing the basic meaning and completing the required structures of a sentence. Examples are items like appositives that are set off in commas, items that are indicated between dashes, or information put inside parentheses.

<strong>parisyllable/parisyllabic</strong>: having the same number of syllables in the nominative and genitive singular. The phenomenon occurs in Latin third declension nouns like <em>auris/auris, civis/civis, hostis/hostis, nubes/nubis, </em>.

<strong>parlance</strong>: the terminology, wording, or way of saying something in a distinct circle of language users or in a certain frame of reference, as in <em>legal parlance</em> or <em>Christian parlance</em>

<strong>parody</strong>: a humorous reproduction of another person’s literary work; if not intentional, it is so poorly done as to seem like a mockery of the original work.

<strong>paronomasia</strong>: *word play, *pun, *Tom Swifty

<strong>paronym</strong>: a word that is derived from the same root as another word

<strong>paronomous</strong>: related to another word by being derived from the same root

<strong>paroxytone</strong>: having an acute accent on the *penult, the next-to-last syllable.

<strong>parse</strong>: to locate a form in its *paradigm; to identify the formal features of a term or set of words; to identify the role of a term in a sentence—to analyze syntax

<strong>parsing guide</strong> a tool for locating words in their paradigms. It may be organized according to an important text like the Hebrew/Aramaic Old Testament or the Greek New Testament. Note the parsing guides for OT and NT.

<strong>part</strong>: In larger works, a part is a division down from the whole work or from a volume within a multi-volume set. (in a set with verse, chapter, paragraph, book, volume).

<strong>part of speech</strong>

Part of speech is not determined by meaning, but by inflection and distribution in a given sentence of a given language. Consequently, a noun is not particularly the name of a person, place, thing, or abstraction. It is a word that carries certain formal characteristics (*inflection) and occupies certain slots (*distribution).

A noun, then, is a word that can have <em>the</em> put in front of it, and can be made plural in ways characteristic of the language. A verb has inflectional terminations that indicate mood, tense, person, voice, and the like. An adjective is a word that can set directly ahead of a noun, on the other side of a verb that indicates linkage, or after a noun (objective complement) that itself receives the action of a certain kind of verb (active).

Part of speech is a more stable feature of inflected language like Greek and Latin because the alterations pretty well determine whether a word is a noun or adjective. In Latin there is <em>amicus </em>and<em> amica, t</em>wo nouns for male and female friend. There is a corresponding adjective <em>amicus, -a, -um,</em> which can carry ending sets for all three genders of the root. Greek behaves in the same way.

English, however, does not have much inflectional change. Consequently, part of speech is a more fluid matter that has to be determined on a case-by-case basis. The same spelling and sound may make an adjective, noun, or verb depending on where it is placed in the sentence. It is distribution—sentence placement—that most decisively determines part of speech. <em>Work</em> can be a verb in the sentence, “They all work together.” It is a noun in the sentence, “Work can be fun.” It is an adjective in the sentence, “He is really a work horse.”

In the last illustration, <em>work</em>, which is more usually a noun or a verb, has been inserted into an adjective slot, the place directly ahead of the noun <em>horse</em>. As a result, it is an adjective. Over time the two words <em>work</em> and <em>horse</em> can be hyphenated and later written solid; so we end up with a compound word like workhorse. Often it is necessary to consult a dictionary to determine whether standard usage still considers it two words, a hyphenated word, or one solid word. In spoken language, <em>work</em> and <em>horse</em> get two equal accents at the same pitch. With time, the pronunciation shifts to a heavy-light accent pattern accompanied by a drop in pitch. Ideally the change would be reflected in the written form, but that is often not—or not yet—the case.

In summation, part of speech cannot be indicated by dictionary entry, but by usage in a specific cases. A dictionary only indicates a word’s predominant usage as a part of speech. <em>Position </em>is historically a noun; but it is a verb if it is put in a verb slot and/or given verbal *formatives: “He positioned himself to receive another promotion.” <em>Friend</em> has historically been used as a verb, but here of late in computerese it has become a verb on Facebook entries: “He asked if he could friend me.” <em>Partner</em> has become a verb: “We are partnering with another agency to solve the crime.”

(in a set with *nouns, *pronouns, *adjectives, *verbs, *adverbs, *conjunctions, *prepositions, *interjections, *determiners, *expletives, *particles)

<strong>participial phrase, introductory</strong>: for clarity’s sake, an introductory participial phrase needs to modify the subject of the independent clause of the sentence; that arrangement make the subject of the sentence also the subject of the introductory participle. Otherwise, it is called a *“dangling participle” because it may not be immediately clear who the actor is in that participle: “<em>Leaving</em> home at fourteen, my <em>father</em> had to fend for himself.”

<strong>participles:</strong> Greek and Latin participles have tense, voice, number, gender, case, aspect, and *verbal adjuncts but not person. Hebrew participles have voice and gender as well as the aspects that derive from the various *stems, which all contain participles. English active participles end in –<em>ing</em> and passives that end with –<em>ed</em> (a plann<em>ed</em> outing). Since English has additional ways of forming the passive past participle (its third principal part), there are also passive participles ending in –<em>t</em> (<em>meant</em>), -<em>en</em> (<em>broken</em>), and -<em>n</em> (<em>sown</em>). Other verbs create the past participle with an infix change (sit > <em>sat</em>) or a combination of alterations (<em>flown</em>). In a verb’s *paradigm, participles are parallel to *moods and infinitives.

Adverbal participles can cover for time (as, while, when, after, during), means (by, with), manner (as, -ly), cause (because), condition (if), concession (although), attendant circumstance (co-ordinate circumstance), purpose, result. In Latin, the *gerundive usually carries the idea of necessity. In Greek the future participle most often indicates purpose. Adverbial participles equal *circumstantial participles.

Adjectival participles connect with nouns in the same way adjectives do: “he is a <em>working</em> man.” Adjectival participles equal *attributive participles.

In Greek, the distinction between adverbial (circumstantial) and adjectival usage generally corresponds respectively to anarthrous and articular usage of participles. One exception can be an participle that modifies an anarthrous noun. Sometimes even with anarthrous nouns, an articular participle occurs and so guarantees it adjectival intent.

[Latin also has gerundives.]

Participles can be called infinite in that they do not have person, but they differ from infinitives in having number-gender-case form in Greek and Latin and thereby attach to nouns. English participles differ from infinitives in form and usage. As the most basic form of the verb, an infinitive is not inflected except for tense, voice, and aspect and has the accompanying <em>to</em> in most usages. Infinitives fill noun, adjective, and adverb slots. Participles, on the other hand, attach only to nouns.

English active participles (-<em>ing</em> forms) have the same form as *gerunds but the latter have only active form and occupy only noun slots: “<em>Working</em> is fun” (S); the weather kept us from <em>working</em>” (OP); “Dad always emphasized <em>working</em>” (DO). Participle and infinitive usages differ as follows: “<em>Working</em> every day in the gym, he got in shape” (ptc); “By <em>working</em> every day in the gym, he got in shape” (ger). Adding the preposition <em>by </em>creates the noun slot occupied by <em>working</em>. Both <em>–ing</em> forms do need to connect with the main subject to show who is acting (clarity), but their syntactical placements differ (grammar).

Abstract verbal ideas in English can be covered either by gerunds or infinitives, but not by participles: “<em>Seeing</em> is <em>believing</em>” or (less usual) “<em>To see</em> is <em>to believe</em>.” Thus when rendering noun Greek infinitives, the translator can use a gerund in order to fit English idiom better. [  ] Similarly, Greek participles are not rendered with English gerunds: For example, Jn <strong><sup>13</sup></strong> says, “I am the voice [f] of <em>(one) crying</em> [m] in the wilderness” (anarthrous *substantive participle), not “I am the voice <em>of crying</em> in the wilderness” (whatever that would mean).

<strong>Are there a couple gerund-usage participles in NT?</strong>

(in a set with *finite verb, *infinitive, and *gerund)

<strong>particle</strong>: a short, uninflected form that is not a normal part of speech. Webster’s cites <em>to</em> as an example in English. See A. T. Robertson for list in Greek. In some usages of the word <em>particle</em>, it can refer to short words like articles, prepositions, conjunctions, or the additions to an English phrasal verb.

In Greek, a number of light, uninflected, relatively short words that nuance meaning in ways often untranslatable.

<strong>partitive</strong>: In Latin, the genitive case was used to indicate that the action in the verb affected only part of the item: WH305#15#4 <em>Medius</em> in Latin was a partative adjective despite sharing number, gender, case with the accompanying noun: <em>in medias res</em>, “in the middle of things.”

<strong>party line</strong>: the official, public position taken on some issue by a certain group, a position that members of the party are supposed to present and defend rather than a different opinion they may have personally. (in a set with *spin, *line)

<strong>passage</strong>: a short segment of a written work

<strong>passive verb</strong>: an action verb in the passive voice (in a set with active verb, middle-voice verb; *fientive verb; *stative verb; *copulative verb, *linking verb, *state-of-being verb, *to be verb)

<strong>passive voice</strong>: the form of verb that directs the action onto the subject rather than onto a direct object. “The article was written yesterday by the editor of the newspaper” <em> vs.</em> “The editor of the newspaper wrote the article yesterday.”

Passive voice exists because (1) sometimes the speaker does not know who did the action. At other times, (2) the speaker/writer may not want to make a point of who the actor is. It may not matter who did it, or the speaker does not want to make known who did it. (3) Sometimes the flow of thought ties in better with the new sentence if the object of the action is mentioned first and therefore made subject.

Teachers of public speaking and writing recommend using the passive voice as little as possible. Active voice seems to have more punch; so it creates a crisper expression.

(in a set with *active voice, *middle voice; *copulative verb, *linking verb, *state-of-being verb, *to be verb; cp. *fientive verb.)

<strong>past participle</strong>: the third principal part of an English verb, as in <em>take, took, </em><em><u>taken</u></em>. It is used in creating (1) all passive-voice forms: “am being taken, am taken, was being taken, was taken, will be taken, have been taken, had been taken, will have been taken”; (2) all actives in the perfect system: “have taken, had taken, will have taken”; along with the infinities and participles in those same sets.

<strong>past tense</strong>: action occurring prior to present time. In English that means the progressive past (<em>was working</em>, <em>used to work, would work, tried to work, kept working</em>, <em>began to work</em>), simple past (<em>worked, have worked</em>), past perfect (<em>had worked, had been working</em>)

<strong>Pater Noster</strong>: “The Our Father,” the Latin name for *The Lord’s Prayer (Mt <strong><sup>6</sup></strong>:9-13a; in the Latin *version lacking <sup>13</sup>b, “For thine . . . Amen.”); also called The *Model Prayer.

<strong>patois</strong>: a regional *dialect that has not been reduced to writing, not the standard form of the language. (in a set with *creole)

<strong>patronym/patronymic</strong>: a term that is derived from the name of one’s father or ancestor

<strong>pe</strong>: the seventeenth letter of the Hebrew alphabet. Also phe:  פ

<strong>pedagogue/pedagogical material</strong>: teaching material, *didactic material

<strong>pedagogy</strong>: the art of teaching

<strong>pedant/pedantic</strong>: an outmoded or unnecessarily formal way of saying something. An example is the use of “one” as an indefinite pronoun. It is almost always avoided in favor of using someone, a person, or *<em>you</em> advisedly: “<em>One</em> does not need to sound impressive when <em>one</em> is talking to a child.” “<em>You</em> don’t need to . . . .”

<strong>pejorative</strong>: having a negative connotation or implication, especially if aimed at a person

<strong>pen name</strong>: the *alias under which an author presents his or her work, as “Mark Twain” for Samuel Clemens. It has been done to avoid prejudice, as in the case of a female author who feared that her presentation would not be given proper credence under an obviously female name. In almost the opposite vein, someone well-known and liked might hide identity so the subject matter would stand on its own merits without interference from fame and love.

(in a set with *aka, *alias, *allonym, *pseudonym)

<strong>pendens</strong>: an unattached word or phrase at the beginning of a sentence that marks the topic of conversation before proceeding to specifics; a kind of double reference that brings emphasis to the doubled element. (in a set with *accusative pendens, *nominative pendens)

<strong>pentameter</strong>: a line of verse consisting of five metrical feet; same as English *iambic pentameter

(in a set with *trimeter, *tetrameter, *hexameter)

<strong>pentastich</strong>: a poem with five lines

<strong>penult</strong>: the next-to-last syllable (in a set with *ultima, *antepenult, *pre-antepenult)

<strong>pep talk</strong>: a speech given especially by a coach to a team to fire them up before the game

<strong>perfect tense</strong>: a verb form that indicates the fulfillment of an action prior to a point in time

(in a set with present perfect tense, *past perfect tense, *future perfect tense)

<strong>performative act</strong>: an act that says something; an act by which one makes a statement: burning the flag, burning the books about magic in Ephesus (Acts<strong> 19</strong>:20). Baptism and the Lord’s supper have that value as well. Differs from a *gesture by participating in the reality instead of signaling something beyond itself.

<strong>period</strong>: a dot on the line of writing used after a complete sentence, with *abbreviations, in web site addresses and e-mail addresses (*dot), or as a decimal in numbers (*point). An abbreviation at the end of a sentence uses one period for both the abbreviation and the sentence. Greek and Latin periods are the same as those in English, but their use does not include decimals and abbreviations.

(in a set with *dot, *point)

<strong>periodical</strong>: a *magazine or *journal that tends toward learned presentations. Libraries use the term to cover a fairly wide range of items that come out on a regular basis, all the way from newspapers to scholarly journals.

<strong>periphrasis</strong>: another word for *circumlocution

<strong>periphrastic</strong>: using two or more words for an idea rather than by just one; periphrastic verbs are construction that use *auxiliaries rather than inflected forms

<strong>permanent prohibition: </strong>formed in Hebrew by al + shortened imperfect (WG 114). (in a set with *immediate prohibition, or temporary prohibition)

<strong>permissive imperative</strong>: The idea of “permissive imperative” seems to misunderstand the “let” idea for translating third-person imperatives as “allow” in Greek, Latin, and Hebrew. “Let” doesn’t mean that in English idiom either.

<strong>persistent accent</strong>: accent tht stays on the same syllable (as counted from the front) throughout the inflection of the word. In Greek, accents on nouns and adjectives are generally persistent—except for –<em>ma</em> nouns of the third declension, where they are *recessive.

(in a set with *accent; *recessive accent)

<strong>person</strong>: the one acting in a verb. *First person is the one speaking (singular) or those standing with him (plural). *Second person is the one/ones spoken to—the one/ones addressed. *Third person is the one/ones spoken about. All nouns, therefore, are third-person references.

The use of third person for second person appears in scripture as a way of expressing respect, as when Moses approaches God, asking him to appoint a successor (Num <strong><sup>27</sup></strong>:15-17).

(in a set with *number, *tense, *mood, *voice, *aspect; *gender in Hebrew verbs)

<strong>personal letter</strong>: a letter sent to a person rather than to a business. In the New Testament, Paul sent such letters originally to Timothy (1 Tim <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1-2; 2 Tim <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1-2), Titus (Tit <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1-4), and Philemon (1). Similar are 2 John (1) and 3 John (1). Acts <strong><sup>23</sup></strong>:26-30 preserves a letter from Claudius Lysias, a chiliarch/tribune in Jerusalem, to Felix, the governor in Caesarea, explaining his reason for sending Paul down to him.

(in a set with *encyclical; *epistle)

<strong>personal pronoun</strong>: a word used in place of a noun without further implication and occurring only in places where a noun can occur. <em>Personal</em> pronoun is a technical expression in that it is not limited to persons, <em>per se</em>; beyond references to “persons” (<em>I, she, you</em>,<em> etc</em>.), it includes things: <em>it</em>. Thus, a “personal” pronoun is a word that stands “for” (<em>pro</em>-) a noun without added qualifications. As such, personal pronouns contrast with

*differential pronouns (“<em>Which</em> is easier?”),

*indefinite pronouns (“<em>any </em>can work here”; <em>“anyone</em> can do this”; “<em>anything</em> goes”; “<em>some</em> can do this”; “<em>someone</em> can do this”; “<em>something</em> just fell”; “a tall <em>one</em> is needed here”; “he would not do <em>a thing</em>”; “<em>a person </em>can change”; “<em>each one</em> teach one”; “<em>everything</em> is different now”; “<em>everyone</em> has to do it”). Several of these indefinite pronouns can also serve as adjectives: <em>any, some; each every. </em>

*indefinite relative pronouns (“I thank <em>whoever</em> did this”);

*intensive pronouns (“I <em>myself</em> did it” or “I did it <em>myself</em>”);

*interrogative pronouns (“<em>Who</em> did this?”);

*reciprocal pronouns (“They like <em>one another</em>);

*reflexive pronouns (“I shot <em>myself</em> in the foot”);

*relative pronouns (“The woman who did this”; “the tree <em>that</em> blew down”),

<strong>personification</strong>: a figure of speech that ascribes personal/human characteristics to impersonal/non-personal things.

(in a set with *anthropomorphism, *anthropopathic)

<strong>persistent accent</strong>: accenting that stays on the same *syllable (as counted from the front) during inflection as long rules of accent allow. (in a set with *recessive accent)

<strong>pesher</strong>:

<strong>petition</strong>: a request for official action signed by individuals. A circulated form on which signatures are gathered to request government officials to take some course of action.

<strong>petroglyph</strong>: a symbol or figure carved or otherwise inscribed on stone by prehistoric people

<strong>Phalaecean (meter Wh309) = hendecasyllabic</strong>

<strong>phatic</strong>: having to do with speech that communicates feelings and mood in order to establish comradery and sociability rather than to convey ideas.

<strong>phe/pe</strong>: the seventeenth letter of the Hebrew alphabet:פ   with ף  as its terminal form

<strong>phenomenological language</strong>: language based for the way something looks rather than for the way the speaker knows it is: “the sun rises in the east.” <em>Heartburn</em> is more the way it feels than something that has to do with the heart (pyrosis). In scripture, death is called sleep, not only as a *euphemism, but because a person who has died looks like someone who is asleep: they are not moving; the eyes are shut. For that reason, it is not wise to allegorize the comparison between death and sleep and conclude that in the intermediate state a person is not in a conscious condition.

<strong>phi</strong>: twenty-first letter of the Greek alphabet: φ/Φ.

<strong>philology</strong>: the study of language in contrast to the study of a language; a term formerly used for *linguistics

<strong>phone</strong>: a speech sound in and of itself without consideration of meaning connection or other linguistic relationship to phonemes and *allomorphs

<strong>phoneme</strong>: the smallest unit of sound that by alteration can change meaning: <em>d </em>and <em>t </em>in <em>dot</em> and <em>tot</em>.

(in a set with *allophone, *grapheme)

<strong>phonetic alphabet</strong>: a standard set of symbols that cover all the sounds of a language or of language in general.

<strong>phonetician</strong>: a specialist in phonetic notation

<strong>phonetic spelling</strong>: spelling words by the way they sound using a uniform value for letters and other symbols without regard to preserving derivational origins of the words; the kind of spelling given in dictionaries for the standard pronunciation of words

<strong>phonetics</strong>: the part of linguistics that deals with the way sounds are made, how they are combined, and how they are represented; the practice of spelling words by a standardized use of letters, diacritical marks, and symbols.

<strong>phonic</strong>: having to do with human speech sound

<strong>phonics</strong>: a method of teaching children how to read by showing them how letters and combinations of letters represent human speech sounds

<strong>phonology</strong>: the study of the sounds and combinations of sounds that make up a language

<strong>phrasal verb</strong>: a verb made up of more than one word where the added word is an adverb that follows the main verb; thus, not to be confused with *progressive verbs, whose added words precede the main verb: call out, calm down, come in, shut up.

(in a set with *merged verb, *fused verb)

<strong>phrase</strong>: set of words that does not contain a finite verb in contrast to *clause, which does have a finite verb. A phrase may serve as a *noun, *adjective, or *adverb.

(in a set with *prepositional phrase, *participial phrase, *infinitive phrase)

<strong>phrase book</strong>: a handbook of foreign expressions and their translations; organized per language and dealing with sets of words rather than individual terms. Perhaps an *idiom book.

(in a set with *dictionary, *lexicon)

<strong>phrase head</strong>: the main word to which the other words in a set connect: “that great big house”; “she very quickly went home”; “The man that I met on the street turned out to be a college professor.”

<strong>phrase prepositions</strong>: Prepositions in English sometimes are thought of as having more than one word: <em>because of, by means of, due to</em> (though not preferred since due’s adjectival force is lost), <em>for the sake of, in regards to, in the interest of, in accordance with, instead of, on account of, on behalf of, on the way to, owing to,</em>

<strong>phraseogram</strong>: a *shorthand symbol that * stenographers use to cover a set of words in contrast to a letter (usually for one sound) or an abbreviation (for one word)

(in a set with *letter, *abbreviation, *acronym)

<strong>phraseograph</strong>: a phrase represented by a *phraseogram

<strong>phraseology</strong>: the turn of expression a person may use that would not have to be exactly that way

<strong>phrasing</strong>: the way an expression is worded

<strong>pi</strong>: sixteenth letter of the Greek alphabet.

<strong>pictograph</strong>: a *hieroglyph; a picture that represents a word or idea in contrast to a letter, which represents a sound or syllable

<strong>pigeon language</strong>:

<strong>pitch</strong>: a presentation intended to get someone to accept or buy something. Used as a light, perhaps, pejorative term for such an instance.

<strong>plagiarize/plagiarism/plagiary</strong>:  to present someone else’s ideas as one’s own—especially written ideas. The act or practice of so doing.

<strong>play on words</strong>: (1) a comment that “plays” on alternate meanings a word may have. “You need to learn how to ‘handle’ people”; “people have been ‘handled’ too much already.” “I’m sorry.” “You’re sorry, all right.” (2) a case of taking advantage of alternate sounds between similar words: the use of the coined expression around Halloween: “spooktacular.”

<strong>pleonstic/pleonasm</strong>:  an expression that is “fuller” than it need be; a kind of hendiadys in some cases. It is a Greek phenomenon in the New Testament that was perhaps encouraged by the repetitive, or pleonastic, tendency of Hebrew idiom: cry out and say (Mt <strong><sup>12</sup></strong>:44); cry out saying (Mt <strong><sup>8</sup></strong>:29); ask and say (Jn <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:25); testified and said (Acts <strong><sup>13</sup></strong>:21); A. T. Robertson 1136

<strong>pleasantries</strong>: positive comments made between people, especially when they first meet on a given occasion. The comments are intended to indicate a positive attitude between people by avoiding serious, sad, or controversial matters: “It’s good to see you”; “How are you?” “How have you been doing?” “You’re looking good today”; <em>etc.</em> A person does not normally answer “How are you?” with comments about his recent setbacks even if both people know those setbacks have occurred. If real information is meant by what could be considered a pleasantry in other situations, the speaker’s voice modulation and accompanying kinesics will make that clear.

(Note *idiom)

<strong>plenary inspiration</strong>: fully inspired in contrast to a divine inspiration limited to “religious” matters but not including scientific, historical, geographical matters. With “infallibility,” as it is called, inspiration is pictured relative the purpose of revelation, which entails God’s word not returning to him void, but fulfilling the purpose for which it was given (Is <strong><sup>55</sup></strong>:10-11). If the purpose of revelation is thought of as limited to “religious concerns,” then scripture does not necessarily have to be accurate in other areas in order to meet the meet the criterion involved. <em>Plenary inspiration</em> has been used in contrast to that limitation.

(in a set with *dictation theory of inspiration, *dynamic theory of inspiration, *infallibility, *verbal inspiration; *inerrancy)

<strong>plene spelling</strong>: a lengthened form of a Hebrew word created by doubling the last consonant and inserting a *vowel letter between the duplicated letters. (in a set with *defective spelling)

<strong>pluperfect</strong>: another term for past perfect, a verb tense that indicates a completed action prior to a past point in time: “He had worked there for one month.”

<strong>plural, grammatical </strong>(<em> vs.</em> plural by sense; <strong>plural, poetic</strong> wh 209

<strong>plural</strong>: more than one. Although English uses the <em>s</em>/z sounds most frequently to indicate plural (<em>hat/hats, job/jobs</em>), it forms plurals in several other ways particularly because of its many derivatives from Latin, Greek, and Anglo-Saxon/Germanic background:

-s(z) (<em>work/work</em><em><u>s</u></em>),

-s (where <em>s</em> is voiced, <em>gram/gram</em><em><u>s</u></em>);

-<em>es</em> (<em>infix/infixes, </em>where it always has a z sound),

-<em>us/i</em> (<em>radius/radii</em>),

-<em>um/a</em> (<em>continuum/continua</em>, <em>curriculum/curricula, datum/data, microbiotum/microbiota, millennium/millennia, </em>(<em>asylum</em>, by meaning, cannot be plural)

-<em>ma/mata</em> (<em>schema/schemata, stigma/stigmata</em>),

-<em>is/ēs</em> (<em>basis/basēs,</em> where the <em>e</em> is always long in contrast to <em>base/basĕs</em>),

<em>#/-en</em> (<em>ox/oxen</em>),

-<em>a/ae</em> (<em>alga/algae, alumna/alumnae</em>),

-<em>os/oi</em> (<em>stichos/stichoi</em>),

-<em>on/a</em> (<em>protozoon/protozoa, logion/logia</em>),

-<em>nx/ngēs</em> (<em>phalanx/phalanges</em>),

-<em>ex/icēs</em> (<em>index/indices</em>),

-<em>ix/icēs</em> (<em>appendix/appendices</em>),

-<em>ax/acēs</em> (<em>thorax/thoracēs</em>).

There are also several words that form plurals by using *infixes: <em>foot/feet; goose/geese; man/men; mouse/mice; woman/women; this/these.  </em><strong>[[see *infixes]]</strong>

Some have no distinct plural form: <em>bison</em>, <em>craft </em>(boats), <em>deer</em>, <em>elk, fish</em>, <em>heathen, moose, sheep</em>.

With these belong all English adjectives used as *substantives, that is, adjectives used in noun slots. They do not add <em>s/z</em> to the root to form plurals, often have <em>the</em> ahead of them, and have plural verbs: <em>few, many, most, several, some,</em> <em>the dumb, the educated, the gifted, the halt, the ignorant, the lame, the maim, the poor, the weak</em>; i<em>n the singular, in the plural.</em>

Latin and Greek also use adjectives as substantives, but use them in both singular and plural. English translation often requires adding <em>ones, things, people. </em>Sometimes English happens to have a noun that can replace such substantives, as when <em>the foolish</em> is translated <em>fools.</em>

Not all English words ending in <em>s</em> are plurals: <em>aerobics, apologetics, athletics, </em><strong>[insert panel] </strong><em>calisthenics</em> (s /pl), <em>civics, cybernetics, dynamics, economics, esthetics, ethics, eugenics, euphemics, eurythmics, euthics, fiberoptics, genetics, geriatrics, graphics, gymnastics, hermeneutics, histrionics, homiletics, kinesics, linguistics, logistics, mathematics, (Popular) mechanics, metaphysics, mnemonics, morphemics, morphophonemics, numismatics, obstetrics, optics, pediatrics, phonics, phonemics, phonetics, physics, poetics, polemics, politics, pyrotechnics, Reaganomics, robotics, series, statics, statistics1, summons, tagmemics, theatrics, </em> Many–<em>ics</em> terms identify disciplines or areas of study.

For *agreement purposes, English, Latin, and Greek use singular verbs and modifiers with a *word used as word: “<em>This</em> <em><u>foolish ones</u></em> describe<em>s</em> the monkeys.” Cases of hendiadys also have singular verbs: “The God and Father of our Lord Jesus Christ <em>is</em>” (1 Th<strong> 1</strong>:3).

Hebrew masculine nouns (usually) end in –<em>im</em> while feminine plurals usually end in –<em>ot/-oth.</em> Consequently, words like the following that appear in older translations are plural: <em>Anakim</em> [not <em>Anakims</em>, KJV], <em>Anamim, cherubim, nephilim, seraphim, </em>

(in a set with *singular, *plural by sense, *poetic plural, *word used as a word)

<strong>pi</strong>:  the sixteenth letter of the Greek alphabet: π/Π.

<strong>plural by sense: </strong>English committee are, especially in British English

(in a set with *collective noun, *mass noun)

<strong>plural, poetic </strong>wh 209

<strong>plus sign</strong>: a symbol for addition (+)

<strong>poem</strong>: a highly structure piece traditionally with rhyme scheme and meter

<strong>poet</strong>: one who writes poetry

<strong>poesy</strong>: the art and practice of writing poetry

<strong>poetaster</strong>: someone who writer poor-quality poetry

<strong>poetic:</strong> having characteristics of poetry like *rhyme scheme and *meter; expressed in a picturesque way

<strong>poetics</strong>: a branch of literary criticism that deals with the characteristics of poetry

<strong>poetic license</strong>: departure from normal usage to meet the requirements of poetic form—rhyme scheme and meter—and to accommodate exaggeration for emphasis..

<strong>poetry</strong>: a division of literature; a form of writing that in English traditionally uses *rhyme scheme, *meter, and parallel lines. Latin and Greek poetry had meter and parallel lines. Hebrew poetry had *parallelism only and then a parallelism of ideas more than form. Thus, parallelism is the most basic feature of poetry, to which are added the other devices of form.

(in contrast to *prose, *free verse)

<strong>point</strong>: the term used for a *period when it serves the purpose of a decimal “point”

<strong>pointed text</strong>: a Semitic text with vowel “points” added to the original consonantal text

(in a set with *unpointed text)

<strong>poisoning the well</strong>: In debate, disproving an argument before the opponent has brought it into the discussion.

<strong>polemic/polemical</strong>: a confrontational, argumentive, defensive, apologetic presentation which may be so-called even when someone representing the opposing viewpoint is not present or is not involved in an interchange [<Gr <em>polemos</em>, “war”)

<strong>polemics</strong>: the art or practice of argumentative delivery

<strong>polite address</strong>: respectful approach as with the use of words like <em>sir </em>or <em>ma’am</em> during the conversation, the use of <em>Mr./Mrs./Miss</em> plus last name in contrast to using a person’s first name. Contrasts with *familiar address (<em>Tu</em> <em> vs.</em> <em>du</em> in GERMAN/French?)

(in a set with *honorific)

<strong>politically correct</strong>: speaking in a way that avoids any negative evaluative or prescriptive behavior. One aspect of this practice pertains to *inclusive language, the avoidance of masculine terminology in mixed, uncertain, and ambiguous circumstances. The situation involves finding substitute expressions for negative connotations in words like “retarded,” “mentally handicapped,” and the like. “Challenged,” for example, substitutes for teh negative element: “mentally challenged,” and so forth. The awkwardness of the phraseology has occasioned some interesting humor.

While the desire to avoid hurting people’s feelings is admirable, attempts to avoid terminologically anything that indicates failure, weakness, lower than norm, and the like, becomes unreal. It also seems to attribute meaning only to the use of vocabulary. The larger arena of discourse can relieve hurtful comments; and the overall attitude of a speaker is indicated by kinesics, pitch patterns, and other features of communication. In this attempt to solve the problem terminologically, there is a tendency to psychologize terms beyond what people normally mean by them. (in a set with *inclusive language)

<strong>polyglot</strong>: a book with more than one language or *version (*translation) in parallel columns, an arrangement used in modern times for several translations of the Bible put side by side for comparison purposes. (in a set with *version, *diglot, *Diatessaron)

<strong>polyphone</strong>: a written character that can stand for more than one sound in the language

<strong>polyphonic</strong>: having more than one phonetic value

<strong>polysemous</strong>: having many meanings. The meanings of a word like <em>on</em> are so numerous that it takes one whole column of small print in a standard dictionary just to summarize its uses.

<strong>polysyllable</strong>: a word having more than three syllables: cholangiopancreatography, laparohysterosalpingo-oophorectomy!

<strong>polysynthetic</strong>: pertaining to a language type with highly complex words created by affixes that express word relationships and meaning elements that in other languages are covered by separate words.

<strong>pony</strong>: a word-for-word translation of a text that makes it nearly possible to reconstruct the original text from the translation

<strong>possession</strong>: (1) a characteristic attached to a noun; (2) owning something

Grammatical p<em>ossession </em>and<em> possessive </em>does not mean ownership particularly. More broadly it means having some feature of identity connected to it: “the <em>table’s</em> leg was bent”; the leg <em>of the table</em> was bent.” The possessive case in the first instance and the <em>of</em> phrase in the second instance merely tie <em>leg</em> to <em>table</em>. How they relate has to be supplied by the listener on the basis of his own experience rather than the speaker’s indication. Different real connections exist in the following examples despite the fact that they use the same <em>of </em>expression:

love of a mother (<em>by </em>a mother; *subjective genitive in Greek and Latin);

love of a mother (love <em>for</em> a mother; *objective genitive);

the city of Cincinnati (equals; *appositive genitive);

house of sticks (made of; *genitive of material);

word of truth (description; *descriptive genitive);

distance of four miles (measure; genitive of measure);

English uses the<em>’s/s’ </em>or “of” expression to tie one noun to another. Though the two constructions are similar in meaning, they are not interchangeable ways of attaching a characteristic to a noun.  GET A RULE FOR DISTINCTION

In Greek and Latin, possession is expressed by verbs meaning “to have,” by the genitive case that ties one noun to another, and by the dative case in linking-verb sentences.

There is a difference in idiom between English and Latin, for example, in the use of possessives. English wants to specify the possessor even if it is clear from context who the possessor is: “I wanted to my old car.” Latin is satisfied simply to say, “I wanted to sell the old car.” Ex<em>. Ille poeta unus multa carmina de fratre beato scribebat</em> (“That one poet was writing many songs about the happy brother” [no word for <em>his</em>]).

(2) The verbs <em>have</em>, <em>owns</em>, <em>etc.</em>, covers the idea of owning something. Finally, there is the use of <em>to</em> after a linking verb: “There was a thrill to the experience.”

<strong>possessive adjective</strong>: adjectives that by their meaning (<em> vs.</em> genitive form as with nouns) indicate possession, and like all adjectives are tied to the accompanying noun by sharing number, gender, and case.

<strong>postfix</strong>: another term for suffix, an element added to the end of a word to add to or otherwise adjust its meaning.

<strong>posthumous</strong>: published after the death of the author

<strong>postpose</strong>: to place a word or set of words after other sentence parts in order to maintain better connection, say, between the verb and an element that would not otherwise fit in the sentence or would make its connection ambiguous: “He threw some hay to the cow on the other side of the fence,” which is slightly better than “He threw the cow over the fence some hay.”

<strong>postpositive</strong>: a structure in English, Latin, and Greek where certain words do not appear first in their clause, phrase, or construction. “I say, then, that you have done well.” Traditionally, <em>however</em> has been used in this way, although the practice is becoming less uniform.

In Greek, there is <em>gar, de, men, te,</em>

In Latin, <em>causa</em> is accompanied by a preceding genitive: <em>salutis communis causā</em>, for-the-sake-of common-safety. Other examples are <em>autem</em>, <em>igitur</em>, <em>enim</em>

(in a set with *appositive, *prepositive; *postpositive conjunction, *postpositive particle, *postpositive preposition, *postpositive verb)

<strong>postpositive conjunction/adverb</strong>: an adverb that does not come first in its clause; it usually appears second. Previously, the word <em>however</em> was not put first in its clause. <em>Then</em> and <em>therefore</em> often do not begin their sentences.

<strong>postpositive particle</strong>: a particle that does not appear first in its clause. Greek had <em>an</em> as part of the formula for the *consequent clause of a *contrary-to-fact *conditional sentence; <em>men</em> in the first half of a *balanced construction that had the effect of saying “on the one hand.” It also appeared in the consequent clause of a *vague future conditional sentence, and in the *potential optative construction.

<strong>postpositive preposition</strong>: a preposition that appears after its object. In English, this can take the form of a delayed preposition: “You are the one I am thinking of”; “I just have my brief case today; so I have a free hand to carry my coffee with.” In these examples there is also an omission of the relative pronoun that would have been used in the more pedantic layout: “the one of whom I am thinking.”

Latin regularly suffixes <em>cum</em> (with) to a personal-pronoun object: nobiscum, <em>etc.</em>

Gr ex.

<strong>postpositive verb</strong>: The Latin <em>inquit</em> is placed after one or more words of a *direct quotation.

<strong>postscript</strong>: an added note following the signature on a letter that comments on something perhaps aside from the main text; abbreviated PS, Latin for post scriptum.

(in a set with *salutation, *complimentary close, *signature)

<strong>postvocalic</strong>: a consonant sound that follows a vowel, a suffix that occurs only after vowels.

<strong>pot shot</strong>: a critical comment made without much thought

<strong>potential</strong>: a construction for what <em>may </em>or can happen.

<strong>potential optative</strong>: in Greek, a vague future conditional sentence without the if-clause: “[If he would/should cross the bridge,] he would break the treaty”: [<em>ei</em> + optative,] <em>an</em> + optative.

<strong>praenomen</strong>: the first name in the Latin three-name system (in a set with *nomen, *cognomen)

<strong>praise</strong>: (c)

<strong>praxis</strong>: the pattern or “practice” done in certain circumstances.

<strong>preach</strong>: to deliver a message in a religious setting, or service. Preaching tends to imply a greater stress on behavior response and affective dimension than *teach, which stresses content and understanding. Sometimes educators and preachers do not like to make the distinction just now offered since there is content in preaching and motivation in teaching. Maybe for them, the difference is more a matter of place and circumstance than anything else. Outside a religious context, preach has become a pejorative term for overly presenting an idea in a critical or extreme way. (in a set with *teach)

<strong>predicate</strong>: The second part of a clause from the verbs to the end of the clause. It includes the verb, direct object, and indirect object together with any modifiers of these elements. (in a set with subject)

<strong>predicate adjective</strong>: an adjective on the “other side” of a linking verb and modifying the subject of the sentence. So, it is not just an adjective in the predicate, but an adjective in the predicate of that modifies the subject of a linking=-verb sentence: “The tree was tall.” The “adjective” may be a prepositional phrase: “The tree was on the hill.” Acts<strong> 4</strong>:33

(in a set with *adjective, *attributive adjective, *compound adjective, *proper adjective, *substantive adjective)

<strong>predicate genitive</strong>:  In Latin, a genitive of possession used in the predicate. Latin construction WH322#67 332#69

<strong>predicate noun</strong>: a noun in the predicate of a linking-verb sentence that renames the subject. The two nouns involved have the same relationship as the two nouns in apposition and two nouns that are direct object plus objective complement.

<strong>predicate position</strong>: In Greek, an adjective that appears before a noun’s article or after the noun; it does not appear between the article and its noun. In possessive personal adjectives, English has a distinction akin to this *attributive-predicate distinction. <em>My, our, your, her, </em>and <em>their </em>stand immediately ahead of the noun they modify. <em>Mine, ours, yours, hers, theirs</em> are used only on the other side of linking verbs. There is no predicate-position form for <em>its.</em>

”<em>My</em> plate is on the table”; but “the plate on the table is <em>mine</em>.”

(in a set with *attributive position, *second attributive position)

<strong>preface</strong>: a section near the front of a work that may give historical background to the work at hand (in a set with *acknowledgements, *prologue, *introduction, *body of the work, *epilogue, *index, *table of contents)

<strong>prefix</strong>: an addition on the front of a word that adjusts the meaning of the root. As to meaning, these same elements may appear in altered form as suffixes or free-standing words; so a prefix is defined by position at a word-specific level.

If certain recurring elements appear only in first position, it may be practical to call them prefixes. In medical terminology, it is a matter of taste whether to call audio-, cranio-, <em>etc</em>., prefixes or common first roots of compounds. English draws on a plethora of Latin and Greek preposition, numbers, even roots to create lengthened forms. Consequently,, all the old Greek and Latin prepositions and prepositional prefixes along with numerical indicators are considered prefixes in this list of English derivatives. Initial morphemes like <em>audio</em>- [processes], <em>cephalo</em>- [body parts], <em>xantho</em>-, <em>erythro</em>- [colors], and the like have been considered first parts of compound words.

English prefixes: a-/an- (not, often without reference to rather than opposed to; cp. non-): amoral, asymetrical, atheism, aniconic, anion, anhydrous,

Greek prefixes: a(n)- (negation of the root) ; archi- (chief ___); dus-, ; heemi- (half); nee- () <strong>enter when get Greek font</strong>
