Latin prefixes:

(in a set with *affix, *suffix, infix, *epenthesis, *morpheme)

<strong>orthoepy</strong>: the study of the customary pronunciation of words, a term especially appropriate for the surprise pronunciations of words, say, with *silent letters, or variant values of the same letters. It is a relevant discipline since (1) oral language precedes written language, (2) language drifts over time so that its appropriate original spelling may no longer be used, (3 there is a tendency to assign quality of persons to certain ways of saying words.

(in a set with *pronunciation)

<strong>orthography</strong>: the study of spelling and the way letters are used to represent the oral expression of terms

<strong>prefixed preposition</strong>:  In Greek, Latin, and English, free-standing prepositions may be attached to the front of nouns, verbs, adjectives, and adverbs to adjust their meanings. (in a set with *prefixes, *suffixes)

<strong>prepositional prefix</strong>: in Greek and Latin, there are many compound words made by putting prepositions on the front of them. The phenomenon compares to English fused verbs. Often the cases that follow such compound verbs in Latin and Greek are the cases that would follow the prefixed preposition if it were free-standing. In these two languages there are three possibilities for verb and preposition relationship: (1) the plain verb with the preposition and its object separate. (2) the preposition prefixed and separately expressed (L. egredere ex urbe, “leave the city). (3) the preposition prefixed but not separately given.

<strong>preposition</strong>: a word that shows relationship between referents. In the classical languages, case forms were more basic than the prepositions used with them, being derived from earlier adverbs. Over time, Greek tended to use prepositions increasingly where case sufficed before. English has dropped case form almost completely in favor of prepositional constructions.

English prepositions include: before, in, of, on,

Greek prepositions include: aro, circum, epi, ek/ex, hypo,

Latin prepositions include: a/ab, in, sub, circa,

(in a set with *phrase prepositions, *prefixed prepositions [Greek, Latin, English, Hebrew], *prepositional phrase, *sequential preposition; *parts of speech; *nouns, *adjectives, *verbs, *adverbs, *conjunctions, *inseparable prepositions [Hebrew], *interjections, *particles, expletives;)

<strong>prepositional phrase</strong>: a *preposition plus its objects together with the modifiers of the preposition and its object(s).

One feature of usages bears noting. Sets of prepositional phrases in English, Greek, Latin, and Hebrew are put in series rather than in parallel: “to the praise of the glory of the grace of him” (translates a series of genitives; Eph<strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:6); “he ran to the top of the hill.” In each case, the next phrase modifies the object of the previous phrase. There is a breach of this principle in title of one religious organization: The Church of Jesus Christ of the Latter-Day Saints, where both phrases modify the same *phrase head.

(in a set with *phrase preposition)

<strong>prepositive</strong>: sequenced ahead of another word or prefixed to another word

(in a set with *postpositive, *appositive)

<strong>prequel</strong>: a previous episode or piece written after one already published and purports to create a history that leads up to the one already in existence.

(in a set with *episode; *sequel)

<strong>prescription: </strong>a medical doctor’s (usually written) order for drugs that are not available “over the counter”

<strong>prescriptive grammar</strong>: a grammar that tries to set norms for oral and written language usage in contrast to one that merely describes how people use the language. A prescriptive grammar describes contemporary consensus on what is considered proper, appropriate (*verisimilitude) for formal and informal situations, uneducated, regional, provincial, slang, and the like. It identifies conventions in punctuation so ideas may be best represented without ambiguity.

(in a set with *descriptive grammar)

<strong>present participle</strong>: An –<em>ing</em> form in English that is created from the first principal part of the verb and characterizes present active verbals in contrast to –<em>ed</em> (<em>work</em><em><u>ed</u></em>), -<em>en</em> (<em>beat</em><em><u>en</u></em>), -<em>n</em> (<em>draw</em><em><u>n</u></em>), and other forms that characterize *past participles (third principal part), which are passive voice.

“Present” participle means “present” relative to the main verb rather than present relative to the speaker of the sentence. In other words, a present participle is present in relative time rather than in absolute time. If the main verb is past tense, then a present participle (relative time) has a past-time reference (absolute time):

“Working all night, he is able to finish the job” (present-time action relative to the

speaker and the present-time main verb).

“Working all night, he finished the job” (past-time action relative to the speaker

and present time relative to the past-tense main verb).

In Greek, present participle is also the terminology used for active, middle, or passive participles, which are created from the first principal part. Again, tense in participles ais figured relative to the tense of the main verb rather than relative to the time of the speaker.

In Latin, participles in the present tense are active only, so that, as with English, “present” means contemporary with the main verb rather than contemporary with the speaker of the sentence.

(in a set with *relative time, *absolute time)

<strong>present perfect</strong>: a verb tense that refers to action completed by the present time: I have worked through the problem.” <em>Have/has</em> are the auxiliary verbs used to express the idea in English.

<strong>present tense</strong>: action going on at the same time as the speaker: <em>he works </em>(characteristic action); <em>he is working</em> (progressive action); <em>he does work</em> (emphatic present). Greek and Latin simply have present tense in that they cannot distinguish formally the difference between these aspects of the verb.

<strong>preterite tense/preterite?</strong>: past tense, especially the *simple past (*aorist tense in Greek, and one of the values of the *perfect tense in Latin)

<strong>prevaricate</strong>: to *lie, to deliberately give false information or at least evade the truth

<strong>preverb</strong>: a particle prefixed to the root of a verb, as the <em>for- </em>in <em>forget </em>(Wb ex)

<strong>priest</strong>: an *intercessor for men to God in contrast to *prophet, a *go-between from God to man

(in a set with *go-between, *intercessor, *intermediary, *prophet)

<strong>primary accent: </strong>on longer English words, one syllable may get the heaviest stress while another gets less stress, as in administration. (in a set with *secondary accent)

<strong>primary endings: </strong>personal endings used in the *primary tenses of Greek (present and future) and in the subjunctive mood.  (in a set with *secondary endings)

<strong>primary tense</strong>: the present and future tenses, the perfect and future perfect tenses, so called in Greek because they shared certain inflectional characteristics: personal ending sets and lack other features found only in *secondary tenses (like augment).  (in a set with *secondary tense)

<strong>primer</strong>: an elementary-level book that teaches children how to read; an entry-level text for teaching information on a certain subject.

<strong>primitive</strong>: the original form from which others developed or the one onto which inflectional adjustments are made.

<strong>principal parts: </strong>the basic forms of verbs from which its full conjugation derives. In English those are the present, past, past participle. English verbs most frequently have principal parts in <em>#, -ed, -ed</em> (<em>work, worked worked</em>). Another, albeit less frequent, format has <em>#, (infix),</em> -<em>en</em> (<em>ride, rode, ridden</em>). There are also *irregular verbs like <em>go/went/gone, hit/hit/hit, sit/sat/sat, </em>

In Greek they are the present (1ST), future (2nd), aorist (3rd), perfect active (4th), perfect middle (5th), and aorist passive (6th). From the first pp come all voices and moods of the present and imperfect tense. The second gives the base for future active and middle voice. The third give the base for aorist active and middle. Number four provides the base for perfect active, and the fifth supplies it for the perfect middle and passive. The last pp is the foundation for aorist and future passive.

Latin has present (1st), present infinitive (2nd), perfect (3rd), perfect participle (4th). The first two underlie all the forms in the non-perfect system, that is, the present, imperfect, and future. The last two give the base for all forms in the perfect system. In general, the perfect system of Latin covers the aorist and perfect tense of Greek. Latin principal parts parallel English principal parts if the first two items in the standard four-entry Latin list are combined—as they are really.

Hebrew studies do not speak of principal parts; instead they speak of stems, of which there are seven in a complete regular verb: *qal, *niphal, *piel, *pual, *hiphil, *hophal, and *hithpael.

<strong>printing</strong>: as form of handwriting that makes each letter separately from the others (in a set with *writing, *block lettering, *scribble)

<strong>privative</strong>: a prefix or suffix that negates the a root to which it is added: the alpha privative in Greek and its derivative forms (<em>a-theist</em>), <em>in-/un-, non-, less</em>.

<strong>proclamation</strong>: (1) a deliberate, public presentation of some message

(2) a public declaration about some rule of action; cp. *edict

<strong>proclitic</strong>: a word pronounced with the succeeding word; as such, it does not bear its own accent except at the end of a sentence, where there is nothing to pronounce it with.

In Greek, if a proclitic was followed by an *enclitic, enclitic rules take precedence; a proclitic can receive an accent from an enclitic but not vice versa. If two or more proclitics appear together, none of them has an accent. Greek proclitics are <em>eis, en, ek/ex </em>(preps.);<em> ho/hee/hoi/hai </em>(the nom. mas & fem sg art);<em> ou/ouk/ouch </em>(neg.);<em> ei, hoos </em>(conjs.).

(in a set with *enclitic)

<strong>proem</strong>: a preface or introduction

<strong>progressive verb</strong>: a verb form that looks at the action as in progress rather than as a fact or as characteristic, cyclical, emphatic, and other aspects of verb action: <em>I</em> <em>am working</em> <em> vs.</em> <em>I work</em>, <em>I do work hard.</em>

<strong>prohibition</strong>: a command not to do something

English normally uses don’t/do not + indicative verb to create a negative command. “Let him not (think)” or don’t let him (think)” are less usual formulas for third-person reference. Negative future indicatives make prohibitions as well: “You will not use the car tonight.” “You are not to” (go into their back yard).

Latin ordinarily uses non + imperative to cover prohibitions. Latin can also use nolo + a complementary infinitive. The negative future indicative can prohibit.

Hebrew never employs the imperative with a negative to mean prohibition. Instead, it uses al + shortened imperfect for *immediate prohibition and lo + imperfect for *permanent prohibition. (Weingreen, 114.)

In Greek, (a) me + present imperative third person indicates prohibition; rather infrequently (b) the aorist imperative serves this purpose in the New Testament; (c) me + aorist subjunctive: present imperative for prohibition forbids the continuation of some act (cessation); aorist subjunctive forbids starting to do it. (d) Ou plus future indicative can likewise be a prohibition. Both Greek and English may have been influenced in this regard by Hebrew practice. They may have picked up the “You shall not” way of speaking—as occurred notably in the ten commandments—because of the prominence of Hebrew idiom behind New Testament Greek and consequent literal translation of those constructions into English.

A refinement in Greek prohibitions may be that me + present imperative means to stop doing something <em> vs.</em> me + aorist imperative for saying not to start doing it. (Goethius, 269)

<strong>prohibitory subjunctive</strong> (in a set with *prohibition,* immediate prohibition, *permanent prohibition,

<strong>prolegomenon/prolegomena</strong>: a preliminary essay/essays that introduces a large, complex work by laying out the framework of discussion, presuppositions involved, and fundamental concepts involved in the main treatment

<strong>prolepsis/proleptic</strong>: a reference to something or some condition that has not yet been mentioned or has not yet come about: WB <em>They drained it </em><em><u>dry</u></em><em>. </em>using a pronoun before its noun has been entered in the presentation. In debate, disproving an argument before the opponent has brought it into the discussion: *poisoning the well.

(in a set with proleptic pronoun)

<strong>proleptic pronoun: </strong>a pronoun that refers to a noun not yet said

<strong>prolix</strong>: wordy, using more words than are necessary. Wordiness comes from repetition, including *redundancy, making an overly detailed presentation for the purposes at hand, including unnecessary accompanying information (“rabbit tracks”), and from using wordy constructions like *relative clauses instead of *adjectives or *participles.

<strong>prolog(ue)</strong>: an introductory chapter that sets the stage for the rest of the book, novel, or play by giving the historical conditions present at the time assumed by the main writing. Prologue and epilogue deal more with historical circumstances than with conceptual frameworks and ideologies as such; preface and introduction have a broader scope.

(in a set with *epilogue, *preface, *introduction)

<strong>prolusion</strong>

<strong>pronominal suffix</strong>: Hebrew has suffixes it can put on its verbs instead of having to use direct-object forms of pronouns

<strong>pronoun</strong>: a word that stands in the place of a noun. Pronouns have no specific meaning so that <em>it</em>, for example, can refer to a tree, an act, a rock, an abstraction, <em>etc.</em> They eliminate what would otherwise be repetitious use of nouns and so create greater variety of expression.

(in a set with *demonstrative pronouns, *indefinite pronoun, *indefinite relative pronoun, *intensive pronoun, *interrogative pronoun, *personal pronoun, *proleptic pronoun, *reciprocal pronoun, *reflexive pronoun, *relative pronoun)

Indefinite pronouns: somebody/someone/something/anyone/anybody/anything; “a person,” “they-ambiguous,”: “They want to build a crematorium near the high school!” “you advisedly”: “Later you add three teaspoons of butter to the mix.” Gr Lat Heb examples

indefinite relative pronouns: 	whoever; also the archaic whosoever.

Greek combines the relative <em>hos</em> + the indefinite <em>tis/ti</em> to form <em>ostis/o ti</em>.

Interrogative pronouns: <em>who/whose/whom? which? what?</em>

Personal pronouns: I, we; you; he, she, it, they

Reciprocal pronouns: “one another (Gr <em>alleloon</em>)

Relative pronouns get case from their function within their own clause; they get number and gender from the antecedent.

In Latin, there is a conjunctive use of relative pronouns Wh 308 306 qui = et hic

*proleptic pronoun: a pronoun that refers to an as-yet unmentioned noun. “<em>She</em> is a great woman, my mother.”

<strong>pronunciation</strong>/<strong>pronounce</strong>: <em>Pronunciation</em> emphasizes making the sound of the word while <em>saying</em> can include the word’s meaning. With its emphasis on sound, <em>pronounce</em> can contrast with spelling as in all cases of *silent letters: <em>often</em> with its silent <em>t</em>, <em>etc.</em> “It is spelled <em>o-f-t-e-n, </em>but it is pronounced<em> offen,</em>” spelled <em>soften</em> but pronounced soffen.


general notation: two of the same consonants are themselves pronounced the same as if there were only one. The difference is often that the preceding vowel is not long before a doubled consonant. The spelling practice was characteristic of Latin (and Greek?) words; so words derived from those languages retain that spelling and its corresponding pronunciation.


-<em>āte</em> is always the pronunciation of verbs (and their related –<em>ātion</em> nouns): <em>amputate/amputation, calibrate/calibration, correlate/correlation, eliminate/elimination, estimate/estimation, expectorate/expectoration, illuminate/illumination, illustrate, iterate, obfuscate/obfucation, obviate/obviation, orchestrate/orchestration, ruminate/rumination, truncate,</em> <strong>[in sert panel]</strong> (in a set with *homograph)

-āte/-ăte: With adjectives and nouns that do not have a related *homograph *verb, the –<em>ate</em> is word-specific as to whether –āte or –ăte: <em>magnāte</em> (n), <em>potentāte</em>, <em>triumvirăte</em>, <strong>[insert panel]</strong> (in a set with *homograph)

<em>c-</em> followed by <em>e, i,</em> or <em>y</em> has an <em>s</em> sound; followed by <em>a, o,</em> or <em>u</em>, and most consonants, it has a <em>k</em> sound: <em>coccus</em> but <em>cocci</em>,

-<em>cc</em>- words usually follow the rule above that <em>c</em> before <em>e, i, y = s,</em> while before most consonants and before the vowels <em>a, o, u</em> it equals <em>k</em>. So, double –<em>cc-</em> words have a <em>k</em> sound to end one syllable followed by an <em>s</em> sound to start the next, hence, -<em>ks</em>-: <strong>[insert panel] </strong><em>accent, access, accidence, accidents, cocci, coccyx/coccygeal, vaccine, </em>

<em>Ch</em> can have (1) a <em>k </em>sound<em>, </em>(2) a *plosive sound<em>, </em>(3) a<em> sh </em>sound, and (4) no sound (rarely)<em>. </em>In medical terminology, <em>ch</em> is always <em>k</em> because the <em>ch</em> comes from the Greek <em>chi</em>, a *fricative that becomes a *stop—<em><u>k</u></em>—in English derivatives: <em>achromophilic, chiropractor chromophilic, chromophobic, chromosome, </em>. (<strong>insert panel</strong>). The same is true of scientific terminology generally: xanthochoid, <strong>[insert panel] </strong>All biblical names in <em>ch</em> are pronounced <em>k</em> except <em>Rachel</em>. These names have the Hebrew <em>heth</em>, which has no equivalent sound or letter in Greek, Latin, or English. <strong>[insert panel]</strong> Other words, chiefly those of Greek descent, have <em>ch</em> as a <em>k</em> sound: <em>conch, monarch/y</em>, orchestra, <strong>[insert panel]</strong>

In initial, *medial, or *terminal position, <em>ch</em> usually has a *plosive sound, as it may elsewhere: [<strong>insert panel] </strong><em>arch, approach, attach</em>, <em>bachelor</em>, <em>belch, besmirch, birch, bunch</em>, <em>cha-cha</em>, <em>chafe, chaff, chain, chair, chalk, challenge, chamber, champion, chance, chancel, chancellor, chapel, chaplain, chapter, char, charcoal, chariot, charge</em>, <em>chaity</em>, <em>channel, chant, charm</em>, <em>chart, chase, chaste, chat, chatter, cheap, check, cheek, cheer, cheese, cheetah, cherry, chert, cherub, chess, chest, chew</em>, <em>chick/chicken</em>, <em>chickory</em>, <em>chief</em>, <em>child, chime, chimney, chimpanzee, chink, chip, chirp, chisel</em>, <em>chive, chock, choice/choose/chose/chosen,</em> <em>choke, chomp, chop</em>, <em>chore</em>, <em>chortle</em>, <em>chow, chubby, chuck</em>, <em>chug, chum, church, churl, churn, churr, coach, crunch, drench, hunch, lunch, lurch, march, mooch, much, munch, niche, orchard, ostrich, parch, paunch, ponch, punch, ranch, raunchy, reach, rich, roach, scorch, search, smirch,squinch, staunch, such, tench, torch, touch, trench, wench, winch, zilch</em>. Such words usually come from Alglo-Saxon heritage. This same sound is often spelled <em>–tch</em>; see under spelling <strong>[insert panel]</strong>

<em>Ch</em> can have a <em>sh</em> sound occasionally, as in <em>chauffeur</em>, <em>chagrin, chalet, champagne, chamois, chandelier, charade, charlatan, chauffeur, chauvinist, chef, chivalry, chute, crochet, echelon</em>. This value appears largely in words descending from or through French.

<em>Ch</em> is silent in <em>chthonic</em> because the initial double *fricative natural in Greek is not allowed in English.

-<em>cq-</em> equals plain <em>qu</em> because c ahead of consonants is <em>k</em>; so just like <em>qu</em>: <em>acquaint, acquiesce, acquire, acquit</em>,. <strong>also put under spelling</strong>

-<em>en</em>: more frequently pronounced like <em>in</em>, but often pronouced <em>ahn</em> in words and phrases from French influence: <em>en bloc, en brochette, en prise, en rapport, en suite, enceinté, enclave, encore, ennui, ensemble, entrée, entourage, </em><em><u>en</u></em><em>t</em><em><u>en</u></em><em>te, entr’acte, entrechat, entrecôte, entremets, entrepôt, entrepeneur, envelope1, envoy</em>

-ic adjectives receive accent on the next-to-last syllable.

-iac for one who does ___ carry accent on the third syllable back: <em>brainiac, demoniac, hemophiliac, maniac,	</em>

<em>	-g </em>followed by <em>a, o,</em> <em>u,</em> most consonants<em>, </em>and at the end of the word, has a “guh” sound:

<em>gab, garb; goon, got; gun, gut</em>

-<em>g-</em> followed by <em>e,</em> <em>i</em>, <em>y </em>usually has a j sound (but see <em>–ge</em> below): <strong>[insert panel]</strong>: <em>coccygéal</em>, <em>meningitis</em> (but <em>meningocele</em>);

but some other exceptions can be noted: <em>gib, gibbon, gid, giddy, gift, gig, giggle, gilbert, gild, gill, gilt, gimbal, gimlet, gimmal, gimmick, gimp, gingham, gird, girl, girn, girth, gittern, give. </em>Another set of words with <em>g</em> as <em>guh</em> is linger, longer, monger, stronger,

The <em>j</em> sound for –<em>g</em> applies also to words that end in those letters (except some –<em>ge</em> words given below under -<em>ge</em>): <em>age/agent, binge, cage/cager, carnage, charge, cringe, dirge, expunge, flange, gauge, hinge, huge, image/imager, large/larger, lunge, manage/manager, massage1, plunge/plunger, rage, range/ranger, sage, singe, stage, tinge, tonnage,urge, verge, visage, wage, </em>

To avoid the <em>j</em> sound, <em>-u-</em> is added after the <em>g</em>: <em>guest, guide,</em> <em>marguerite</em>, <strong>[insert panel]</strong> <em>catalog(ue1), demagogue/demagoguery, monologue, morgue, rogue, prologue, tongue, synagogue, vague, vogue,</em>

-<em>g</em>- can have a <em>zh</em> sound (like the <em>s</em> in <em>measure</em>) in a few words under French influence: <strong>[insert panel]</strong> <em>gite, lingerie, menagerie1, regime</em>

<em>	-ge </em>at the end of a word can have a <em>zh</em> as in<em> beige, collage, deluge1, entourage, garage1, luge, massage1, mirage, rouge, </em>

-<em>gh</em> can be silent, or carry the <em>f</em> sound: <em>cough, enough, laugh, rough, slough, tough,</em>.

–<em>gh is </em>Silent in <em>aught, blight, bough, bought, bright, brought, caught, delight, dough, drouight, fight, flight, fraught, haughty, high, height, light, might, night, nought, ought, plight, right, sigh, sight, sleigh, slight, sought, th(o)rough, though, thought, tight, through, tight, weigh/t, wright, wrought,  </em><strong>[insert panel]</strong>

<em>Gn</em>- on the front of words does not pronounce the <em>g</em>: <em>gnar, bnarly, gnash, gnat, gnathic, gnathite, gnaw, gnocchi, gnome, gnomic, gnomon, gnosis, gnu.</em>

<em>	H- </em>usually represents a *fricative in English (as in <em>hat, hot,</em> <em>etc.</em>), but in a few words it equals the Hebrew <em>aleph</em> and the Greek smooth breathing (’), a *glottal stop: <em>haute, heir, herb/al, herbage, honest/y, honor/able,</em> <em>hour.</em> British cockney typically substitutes glottal stop for *glottal fricative: <em>’orse</em> for <em>horse</em>, <em>etc.</em>

<em>	Kn</em>- on the front of words does not pronounce the <em>k: knack, knap, knar, knave, knavery, knawel, knead, knee, kneel, knell, knickers, knife, knight, knish, knit, knob, knock, knoll, knop, knot, knout, know, knuckle, knur, knurl.</em>


<em>	N</em> has (a) its regular “<em>ín</em>” sound everywhere except before *gutturals/*palatals/*velars. See <em>ng</em> below.

<em>N</em> has (b) an <em>ng </em>sound when it is followed by <em>c </em>(if = k), <em>k,</em> <em>g </em>(if = “guh”), <em>ch</em> (if = <em>k</em>): <strong>[insert panel]</strong> <em>an/ankle, ban/bank, can/canker, con/conch, Congo, dan/dank, gunk, kung fu, lawn/long/lung, man/mangle, ran/rank, rink, sin/sink/sinker, stink/stank/stunk, sun/sunk, tin/tinker, uncle, wrinkle, fungus</em> (but fun + gi, pl., because <em>g</em> before <em>e, i,</em> or <em>y</em> has a <em>j</em> sound; so the ŋ sound becomes plain <em>n</em>),

<em>N</em> has (c) <em>ny</em> sound with a tilde over it (words of Spanish origin): señor, señorita, señora.

<em>N</em> + <em>c</em> (if = <em>k</em>), <em>g, k, ch</em> (if = <em>k</em>) [that is *velars] has an <em>ng</em> sound: <em>rank</em> (<em>rangk  vs.</em> <em>ran</em>),


-<em>ng</em> at the end of a word has its typical sound as a *digraph (two letters for one sound; ŋ). When such words have added suffixes, the same sound usually remains: <strong>[insert panel]</strong> <em>bang/banged/banging, cling/clinged/clinger/clinging, hang/hanger, ring/ringer/ringing, sing/singer/singing, sting/stinger, thing/thingie, wring/wringer, </em>But note <em>long/longer</em>, <em>strong/stronger</em>

<em>	-ng</em> names of German descent can be unclear. Is <em>Hettinger</em> pronounced <em>Hettiŋer</em> or <em>Hettinjer</em>? Each family or person determines whether it has be “anglicized.”


<em>Rh</em>- and <em>rrh</em> words = <em>r: rhabdom, rhabdomancy, rhabdomyoma, rhapsody, rhatany, rhea, rhenium, rheology, rheometer, rheostat, rheotaxis, rhesus, rhetor, rheum, rheumatic, rheumatism, rhinal, rhinencephalon, rhinoceros, rhinology, </em><em><u>rh</u></em><em>ino</em><em><u>rrh</u></em><em>ea, rhizanthous, rhizobium, rhizocephalan, rhizogenic, rhizoid, rhizomer, rhizopod, rhizopus, rhodium, rhodochrosite, rhododendron, rhodolite, rhodopsin, rhodora, rhombencephalon, rhombic, rhombohedron, rhomboid, rhombus, rhonchus, rhubard, rhumb, rhyme, rhyolite, rhythm</em>;<em> -rrhea, -rrhagia, -rrhexis, -rrhaphy</em>;<em> catarrh, cirrhosis. </em>


Initial <em>bd-, gn-, kn- kt-, pn-, ps-, pt-,</em> <em>tl-,, </em>have the first consonant dropped in pronunciation because English speakers perceive the combination to be unnatural. The same goes for the *digraphs <em>ph</em> before <em>th</em> (<em>phthistic</em>) and <em>ch</em> before <em>th</em> (<em>chthonic</em>)..

<em>S</em> = <em>s, z, sh,</em> or <em>zh</em>

<strong>-</strong><em>S</em> <em> vs.</em> -<em>z</em> for plural sound, but always written <em>s</em>. The influence of a preceding voiced consonant (or a preceding vowel sound) carries on through the sibilant <em>s</em> to make a <em>z</em> sound: <em>spas, jobs, raids, bees, gags, blahs, skis, balls, rims, fins, twos, bars, revs</em>; also in plurals that add a syllable to the singular by adding a voiced <em>e</em> + <em>s </em>( <em>–es</em>): <em>bosses, bushes, prefixes,</em>

The pattern holds for third-person singular present-tense endings: <em>bids, chords, dubs, earns, fares, gores, has, lies, moos, nabs, pads, raids, sags, tabs, wads.</em>

The <em>s/z </em>variable marks the difference between noun and verb in words like <em>abuse, grease, house, lose(loose/loss), refuse, re-use, use</em>

<em>	S </em>as<em> sh </em>appears in<em> scena</em>

<em>	S </em>ahead of <em>u</em> can be<em> sh </em>in some words: <em>assure, censure, insure, sugar, sure,</em>

<em>S</em> ahead of <em>u</em> can have a <em>zh</em> sound: <em>measure</em>,

<em>sc</em>- ahead of <em>e</em> is plain <em>s: acquiesce, scenario, scend, scene, scenery, scenic, scenography, scent,scepter, septic,</em> <em>etc.</em> This is so because <em>c</em> ahead of <em>e</em>, <em>i</em>, or <em>y</em> has an <em>s</em> sound; so, two <em>s</em>’s in a row equal plain <em>s</em> (see below un –<em>ss</em>).

<em>sc</em>- ahead of <em>i</em> is plain <em>s</em>: <em>sciaenoid, sciatic, sciatica, science</em>, <em>scientiall, scientific, scientism, scientist, sci-fi, scilicet, scimitar, scincold, scintigram, scintigraph, scintilla, scintillate, scintillation, scintillator, scintiscan, sciolism, scion, scirrhus, scissile, scission, scissor, scissure, Scituate, </em>sciurid; <em>etc.</em> This is so because <em>c</em> ahead of <em>e</em>, <em>i</em>, or <em>y</em> has an <em>s</em> sound; so, two <em>s</em>’s in a row equal plain <em>s</em>.

sc- ahead of y is plain s: <em>Scylla</em> (and Charybdis), <em>scyphistoma, scyphozoan, scythe, Scythia, Scythian.  </em>But <em>Scyros </em>(<em>sk</em>-) as an alternate spelling of<em> Skiros.</em>

<em>Sch</em> has an <em>sk</em> sound on the front of most words<em>: schedule, schema, schematic, schematism, schematize, scheme, scherzando, scherzo, schizocarp, schizogenesis, schizogony, schizoid, schizont, schizophrene, schizophrenia, schizophrenic, schizothymia, schizy, scholar, scholarly, scholarship, scholastic, scholastic, scholasticism, scholiast, scholium; school, etc.; schooner, </em>

<em>Sch</em> has the <em>sh</em> sound in words with the <em>schisto</em>- prefix in <em>schistocyte, schistoglossia, schistorrchachis, shistothorax</em>, as well as in <strong>[insert panel]</strong> <em>scheelite, schefflera, schiller, schilling, schist, schistosome, schlemiel, schlep, schlieren, schlimazel, schlock, schmaltz, schmal;tzy, schmo, schmooze, schmuck, schnapper, schnitzel, schnopok, schnorrer, schnoz, schottische, schuss. </em>

<em>Sch</em> is plain <em>s</em> in <em>schism, schismatic, </em>

The <em>sh</em> sound can be covered also by –<em>ce</em>- (ocean), <em>ci</em>, -<em>si</em>, -<em>ti </em>(patience, vitiate); -s (sugar), -<em>ss</em>- (mission, passion),

<em>-sia/-tia/-cia</em> = shuh

-<em>si</em>- can be <em>zh</em>: <em>allusion</em>, <em>fusion</em>,

-ss is usually plain <em>s</em> (the two-consonant rule), especially on the ends of words: as in <em>access</em>, <em>across, actress, albatross, ass, assess, bass, bliss, boss, class, confess, crass, cross, cuss, dress, dross, ess, glass, gross, hiss, kiss, less, loss, mess, miss, mistriss, moss, toss, </em>and all words with the feminine agent ending –<em>ess</em> (<em>hostess, tigress,</em> <em>etc.</em>, see under <strong>suffix</strong>)

-<em>ss-</em> can be <em>sh: confession, mission</em>

-<em>ss-</em> can be <em>zh</em>: <em>fission</em>, <em>scissure</em>,

-ss- can be <em>z</em>: <em>scissors</em>

The <em>th</em> may be (a) a dental voiceless fricative: <em>both, catheter, doth, ether, loath, myth, panther, thin, thing, thingie, thither, thug, thong, thought, thumb, with.</em> All the words that derive from Greek <em>theta</em> have this same sound because theta was a voiceless sound: <em>anthropoid, arthropathy, atheroma, catheter, catholic, euthanize, orthodontist, therapeutic,</em>

<em>Th</em> may be (b) a dental voiced fricative (often spelled phonetically as <em>dh</em>): <em>another, bathe, bother, brother, dither, either, father, farther, farthing, further, gather, hither, lather, lithe, loathe, mother, mouth, neither, other, rather, seethe, smooth, southern, swathe, teethe, than, that, the, thee, then, thence, there, these, thine, this, those, thou, threw, thrice, thrift, thrill, throw, thud, thunder, together, tooth, thi</em><em><u>th</u></em><em>er, weather, whether, wither, writhe. </em>

Adding an <em>e</em> after a final <em>th</em> may changes <em>th</em> to <em>dh: bath/bathe, lath/lathe, loath/loathe, teeth/teethe,  </em>

The <em>th/dh</em> sound variant can mark the difference between noun and verb: <em>bath/bathe, mouth2,</em> or just between words: <em>lath/lathe, </em>

<em>Th</em> may have (c) a silent <em>h</em> as in <em>thyme.</em> More correctly, the <em>th</em> is a *digraph that represents a stop (plain<em> t</em>) rather than a fricative (<em>th</em>) in this case.

<em>x</em>: Words beginning with <em>x</em> are pronounced <em>z</em> because <em>ks </em>(<em>= x</em>) is not an acceptable combination of initial consonants in English; so the <em>k </em>part is dropped and the <em>s</em> is *voiced to a <em>z</em>. The least number of English words have <em>x</em> as their first letter: <strong>[insert panel] </strong><em>xanthan gum, xanthate, xanthene, xanthochoid, xanthoma, xanthophyll, xanthous, xebec, xenia, xenolith, xenoblast, xenocryst, xenogenesis, xenon, xenophobe, xeroderma, xerography, xerosis, xerophtahlmia, xerosis, xerothermic, xi1, xiphocostal, xiphoid, xylan, xylem, xylene, xylidinie, xylophagus, xylograph, xylophone, xylose.</em>

Unaccented vowels in English tend to drift toward *<em>shevah/schwa</em> (ǝ), a light vowel sound as in the word because, as examples show: <em>calendar</em>, <em>miner</em>, <em>fakir</em>, <em>minor</em>, <em>augur</em>, <em>satyr</em>.


Long <em>e</em> appears in words derived from Greek that have followed the <em>–ai-, -ae-, e</em> development: <em>palēography</em> (<em>pal</em><em><u>ai</u></em><em>os</em>), <em>pēdiatrician</em> (<em>p</em><em><u>ai</u></em><em>dion</em>), archēology (<em>arch</em><em><u>ai</u></em><em>os</em>), <em>encyclopēdia</em> (<em>p</em><em><u>ai</u></em><em>dion</em>)


Two vowels letters written together are often *diphthongs, that is, two letters for one sound (*monophthong) or a *vowel glide from one letter to the another, but not two syllables: <em>fail, fault, flay; feat, feign, feud/deuce, trey; mien; foal, foil, fount, boy; sue, s</em><em><u>ui</u></em><em>t/q</em><em><u>ui</u></em><em>t, quote; buy</em>.  Diphthongs can be two of the same letter: <em>feet</em> (ē), <em>fool</em> (ooh), <em>foot</em>. There are cases, however, where two same vowel letters are not a diphthong, but represent two syllables with two sounds, which will be different:

-<em>ii</em>- in <em>card</em><em><u>ii</u></em><em>ts, fasc</em><em><u>ii</u></em><em>tis, ang</em><em><u>ii</u></em><em>tis</em> ( ē + ī); <em>coiinine </em>a poison, oi + i); <em>skiing, taxiing</em> (<em>ē</em> + <em>ĭ</em>);

cooperate, oophorectomy (ō + ŏ, not the <em>oo</em> in <em>fool</em>).

-<em>uu</em>- in continuum, vacuum (ū + ŭ);

Double <em>ee</em> is always a diphthong (*monophthong) in ē. Some vowel combinations are rare or do not occur in English as diphthongs: -aa-, -ao- (but <em>gaol</em>, British for <em>jail</em>), -iy-, -<em>ue</em>- (a German spelling for rounded <em>u</em>), -<em>uo</em>-,

-<em>ae</em> occurs in limited circumstances: (1) on plurals of Latin singulars in –<em>a</em> (<em>amoeba</em>/<em>ae</em>), or (2) derivatives from Greek words in –<em>ai</em><strong>-</strong>, which over time become –<em>ae/-æ-</em> (<em>encyclopædia</em>) then –ē<strong>- </strong>(archeology, pediatrician), as illustrated in <em>αἰών/aeon/eon</em>.

When vowel sequences appear that could be diphthongs, but are not, spelling sometimes—but not always (<em>doable</em>)—uses a *hyphen between them or a *dieresis over the second vowel: co-operate, naïve.

Some possible vowel sequences either do not occur or are two syllables rather than diphthongs:

-<em>ia</em>- (<em>vial</em>),

-<em>io</em>- (<em>lion</em>),

-<em>iu</em>- (<strong>???</strong>)

-<em>eo</em>- is a mixed bag: <em>l</em><em><u>eo</u></em><em>pard</em> (diphthong, ě); but <em>l</em><em><u>eo</u></em><em>tard</em> (2 syl)<em>, leonine </em>(and other words built on Latin<em> leo/leonis</em>), <em>geode, paleography, phraseology</em>; <em>arch</em><em><u>eo</u></em><em>logy</em> has the sequence by virtue of –<em>ai</em> becoming –<em>ae-</em> becoming -<em>e</em>-. There is one syllable in the names <em>George</em> (German <em>Georg</em>, 2 syl) and <em>Leonard</em> (<em>Leonardo</em>, 2 syl)

-<em>oe</em>- does occur in (1) derivatives of some Greek words in –oi-, which over time went from <em>oi</em> to <em>oe</em> (as <em>œ</em>) to <em>ē</em>: poinee > (poenology) > pēnology. Since German uses <em>e</em> after vowels to show *rounding, some names and other words can crop up in English with the –<em>oe</em>- sequence: <strong>???</strong> . By chance, -<em>oe</em>- can fall in separate syllables: <em>orthoepy</em>.

-<em>ua</em>- (<em>dual</em>)

-<em>ue</em>- (<em>duel, suet</em>)

-ui- is a also mixed bag. There are two syllables in <em>ruin</em>, but a diphthong in <em>quit</em>, <em>suit</em>, as noted above.

A rule for pronouncing some vowel combinations has limited value: “When two vowels go walking, the first takes the lead”; that is, the first, pronounced long, is the sound of both. The pattern usually applies only for –<em>ai</em>- (<em>gait</em>), -<em>ea</em>- (<em>leaf</em>), (-<em>ee</em>-, <em>leek</em>), and <em>–oa-</em> (<em>boat</em>).


*Derivatives with odd consonant combinations on the front typically lose the first of those sounds<em>: bd</em>- (), <em>chth</em> (), <em>gn</em>- (<em>gnostic</em>), <em>kt</em>- (), <em>ll</em>- (<em>llama</em>), <em>mn</em>- (<em>mnemonic</em>), <em>pt</em>- (<em>ptyalism</em>), <em>wr</em>- (<em>wrong</em>). Other adjustments are also made: <em>x</em> becomes <em>z</em>; <em>rh-</em> and –<em>rrh/rrh-/-rrh- </em>become <em>r</em>.


Finally, some words are simply pronounced according to convention without adhering closely to what the spelling—often informed by derivational transliteration—would call for. <em>Ste</em><em><u>ph</u></em><em>en</em>, for example, is almost always pronounced <em>Ste</em><em><u>v</u></em><em>en.</em> Examples of extreme conventional pronunciation include <em>coxen</em> (for <em>coxswain</em>), <em>kernel</em> (for <em>colonel</em>), <em>fōx’il </em>(for <em>forecastle</em>), <em>gunnel</em> (for <em>gunwhale</em>), Wooster (for Worchester).


<strong>British pronunciation differs noticeably from American pronunciation</strong>. (1) Some words get different accenting or vowel length: labóratory, vĭtamin,

(2) Vowel sounds are significantly adjusted. The <em>a</em> as in <em>at </em>more often has an <em>ah</em> sound.

(3) Final <em>t</em>’s are often replaced with a *glottal stop—like the <em>h</em> in <em>honor.</em>

(4) Glottal stop and glottal fricative are reversed in certain speakers: ’orse for horse.


(in a set with *accent, *enunciation, *epenthesis, *pronunciation, *diction, *elocution, *orthoepy, *sandhi, *spelling))

<strong>pronunciation guide</strong>

<strong>proofreader</strong>: one who checks through the print copies of a forthcoming publication to catch any *typos that may have crept into the copy during typesetting and the like. (in a set with *galley proof, *galley sheets)

<strong>proper adjective</strong>:

<strong>proper diphthong</strong>: a diphthong in Greek that does not involve a long vowel followed by iota. When an iota comes into play after a long vowel, it ceases to affect pronunciation and is written under the other vowel. If the long vowel happens to get capitalized, the iota comes back up onto the line after the long vowel.

<strong>proper names</strong>: nouns used for identification more than description. Names typically originate from words that have meaning.

<strong>proper noun</strong>: a noun whose usage is not to indicate meaning, but identity. A proper name designates an individual within a class or group of individuals. English, Greek, and Latin convention calls for capitalizing such words while using *lower case on *common nouns.  John is my brother (proper noun, identity); John is my brother (common noun, meaning).

Hebrew did not have capital <em> vs.</em> lower-case letters so as to distinguish common nouns from proper nouns in writing.

Hebrew names in the Bible sometimes have derivations given with them. Moses was so named because he was “drawn from the water” (Ex <strong><sup>2</sup></strong>:10). The predecessor of the Messiah was to be named <em>John</em>, from the Greek <em>Iooannees</em>, from the Hebrew <em>Jonah</em>, presumably because John would be given the sign of the “dove” for identifying the Messiah (Jn<strong> 1</strong>:32-34).  Jacob became “Israel” because in his wrestling with the angel that night he “<em>strived with God</em>” (Gen <strong><sup>32</sup></strong>:24-29). In the same incident, Jacob named the place of the encounter “Peniel/Penuel” because there he saw “the <em>face of God</em>” (Gen <strong><sup>32</sup></strong>:30). Hannah named her son <em>Samuel</em> because she was “heard of God” (1 Sam <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:20).  Abram was re-named <em>Abraham (); </em>Sarai was re-named Sarah (). The name <em>Joshua</em> came into a Greek corrupted spelling as <em>Ieesouys</em>, and then into the Latin and English name <em>Jesus</em>, who was so named because he would “<em>save</em> his people from their sins” (Mt <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:21, 25; Lk <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:31; <strong><sup>2</sup></strong>:21; cp. Jn <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:29). The Messiah was to be called “Immanuel/Emmanuel,” <em>God </em>(is) <em>with-us</em>” (Is <strong><sup>7</sup></strong>:14; Mt <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:23). <em>Messiah</em> itself means “anointed one” (Dan <strong><sup>9</sup></strong>:25; Ps<strong> 2</strong>:2), whose Greek equivalent meaning is <em>Christos, </em>to Latin <em>Christus</em>, and then to English <em>Christ </em>(Jn<strong> 1</strong>:41; <strong><sup>4</sup></strong>:25). The Aramaic <em>Cephas</em> translated into the Greek <em>Petros</em> (Jn <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:42). <em>Rabbi </em>is <em>Teacher</em> (Jn<strong> 1</strong>:38).

*Nicknames, or *surnames, likewise have accompanying explanations as to why Joseph, for example, was called <em>Barnabas</em> (“Son of consolation/exhortation”). Even in these cases, once the origination of the name has taken place, the subsequent usages are not so much for description as for identity—who, more than what.


The older, more original Greek *uncial manuscripts of the New Testament were written in all capitals letters, which made capitalization a difference that could not be used to indicate proper <em> vs.</em> common nouns. This feature of the Greek text and its history of transmission raised questions for later copyists and for scholars who try to re-create a corrected New Testament text. That presents a question about the possible meanings of Phil <strong><sup>4</sup></strong>:3, “Yes, I beseech you, true <em>yokefellow</em>, help these women.” Is this a reference to Clement in<strong> 4</strong>:3, or is this term another man’s name: <em>Syzygos</em>?

(in a set with *common noun, *lower-case noun; *capitals, *small letters; *minuscules, *majuscules)

<strong>proper pronoun</strong>: a capitalized pronoun as in reference to deity. In English usage, capitalized pronouns in reference to deity should be limited to personal pronouns and not extended to reflexive, intensive, relative, indefinite pronouns, <em>etc.</em>

<strong>prophet</strong>: a spokesman for God to man in contrast to *priest, who intercedes for man to God. A prophet’s speaking for God includes the full range of what can comprise divine revelation, not just predictive prophecy. (in a set with *priest, *intercessor)

<strong>prophetic literature/prophecy</strong>

<strong>prophetic history</strong>: Old Testament historical literature is prophetic history inasmuch as it makes evaluative comments on whether the king or other leader whose life is being chronicled “kept *covenant.”

<strong>proposition</strong>: (1) a proposed idea; as a verb to proposition is (2) to seek sexual favors

<strong>proscribe/proscription</strong>: to denounce or condemn

<strong>prose</strong>: (1) a presentation in regular written form in contrast to poetry; its intention is more to inform and persuade than to beautify or extol.

(2) In a context where translation is involved, <em>prose</em> can be used to mean translating from a person’s native language into the one being studied, in contrast to translation from the foreign to the native language.

(in a set with *poetry, *free verse)

<strong>prose poem</strong>: a writing that by passes the rhyme scheme and meter found in traditional poetry and incorporates vivid imagery and concentrated expression WB

<strong>prosody</strong>: the study of metrical structure of verse WB

<strong>protasis</strong>: the if-clause of a *conditional sentence. Also called the *antecedent in logic. The protasis without a *consequent clause is used in Hebrews as a kind of *aposiopoesis for strong prohibition (  ).

(in a set with *apodosis) [<Gr. pro, before + teinein, to stretch].

<strong>prothesis</strong>: the addition of a meaningless phoneme or syllable to the front of a word as with the Spanish <em>e </em>on <em>español</em>. WB The opposite of *apheis, the loss of a sound on the front of a word with out changing its meaning.  They went <em>a-</em>caroling; the cattle were <em>a-</em>lowingan example???

<strong>proto-</strong>: a prefix denoting the earlier form of something—a language, letter form, and the like. <strong>proto-Masoretic</strong>: a form of the Hebrew text earlier than the standardized text produced by the Masoretes.

<strong>proto-semetic</strong>:

<strong>prototype</strong>: the qal stem in Hebrew is regarded as the prototype of the other stems in the verb system. It is the basic form.

<strong>protraction</strong>: the irregular lengthening of a short vowel

<strong>proverb</strong>:  (in a set with *adage, *maxim, *apothegm) (1)

<strong> pro-verb</strong>; the use of only part of a phrase verb. “Are you going to cut the grass.” “ I should.” “Did you get your homework done?” I did.” “I don’t want to.”

<strong>provincialism</strong>: a term or expression used in a limited geographical area, particularly an area off the main path of the political, governmental, or social order. Similar to *regionalism. (in a set with *archaism, *buzz word, *catch phrase, *colloquialism, *curse word, *formal term, *informal term, *oath, *obsolete term, *regionalism, *regionalism, *slang, *technical term, *vulgarism)

<strong>proviso clause</strong>: a dependent clause connected to the main clause with the idea ”as along as,” “provided that.” Latin uses a special construction for the concept: dummodo + subjunctive. 	Wheelock p 223)

<strong>proxy word</strong>: a term that replaces another that is not not acceptable in polite speach. These words are often *corruptions of vulgarisms like “freakin’” for __, “son of a gun” for __; of curse words like “dadgum” for __, “darn” for __, (in a set with *perversion, *corruption)

<strong>psycholinguistics</strong>: the study of psychological influences on the use and interpretation of human language

<strong>publication</strong>: broad term for what is put out for “public use”: *periodical, a *weekly, a *daily, a *monthly, an *annual, an *ephemeral paper, a *book.

<strong>pun</strong>: a *paronomasia; a *word play on different senses of the same word or on different words with similar sound. <strong>examples</strong>

<strong>punch line</strong>: a final comment that brings the preceding ones to sudden clarification; used mostly in presenting something humorous.

<strong>punctuation/punctuation marks</strong>:

Indicators beyond vocabulary that help reproduce oral language in written form. In general, punctuation marks deal with relationship between words in contrast to *diacritical marks, which deal with marks within a word. Punctuation marks often correspond to oral (1) pauses, (2) pitch patterns, or (3) emphases. At other times they indicate

(4) logical relationships not particularly marked in the spoken language.

(5) They show the relationship between words. The English *apostrophe <em>s</em>, <em>s</em> apostrophe, and plain <em>s</em> distinguish respectively singular possessor, plural possessor, and plain plural.

In many of these instances commas (6) simply help the eye of a reader. That is the reason a comma can be omitted between short co-ordinate clauses: “He hit the ball and John caught it.” That is also the reason introductory prepositional phrases do not always need to be separated from the main sentence:  “In the morning I’ll mow the grass” <strong>but “In Greek, commas look like they do in English.”???</strong>

Period: end of sentences, end of abbreviations, as a decimal point

Comma: separates (one comma before or after) or sets off (comma before and after)

Dieresis: a double horizontal dot above a second vowel to show that it does not form a diphthong with an immediately preceding vowel.

Semi-colon: used between independent clauses that are thought of as related though not joined by a coordinating conjunction: <em>and</em>, <em>but</em>, <em>or</em>, <em>nor</em>. It is used to separate two word groups that have a comma separation within one or both of them.

Colon is used after an independent clause to indicate a brief explanatory notation. It has the force of “that is,” “here’s an example,” “to put in a clearer way.” Colon is also used between chapter and verse in biblical citations.

Hyphen joins words into one word (<em>pick-up</em>) or into word parts that, taken, together modify the following term (a bigger-than-life hero). Hyphens break long words into syllables in order to divide them up into what will fit on a line of writing.

a dash separates words groups that intrude into the general flow of the main sentence.

An opening dash marks the beginning of the insertion; a closing dash marks the end of the insertion.

Ditto: a double vertical straight mark above the line of writing to indicate repetition of the item directly above it in a list. On typewriters the ditto was the same key as double quotation marks, which in turn were the same before and after the quotation; consequently, in typed material (but not necessarily in handwriting) dittos and double quote marks had the same form. With the advent of computer and “smart quotes,” the ditto can be supplied from a specialty list.

Em dash

En dash

Exclamation mark: indicates the presence of a significant degree of emotional

A quotation mark may be a single quotation mark or double quotation mark. Single quotation marks (‘ and ’) are used for a quotation within a quotation. “Smart quotes” (“ and ”;   ‘ and ’) are shaped in opposite curvatures at the front and the end of quotations. By convention, quotation marks follow periods and commas, precede colons and semi-colons; and follow or precede exclamation marks and question marks depending on whether the writer is quoting the question (after) or questioning the quote (before), quoting the exclamation (after) or exclaiming the quote (before).

Slash (diagonal) goes between switch-out words: and/or. A double slash isolates an infrequent usage like a phoneme<em>: f, ph, gh</em> are variant English spellings for the phoneme <em>/f/</em>.

Backslash

Brackets are used for insertions inside of quoted material.

Angle brackets ( < >) are used around e-mail addresses.

Parenthesis/parentheses

Apostrophe

asterisk

Matilda

With the advent of computers and their better range of available symbols, previous rules for some punctuation marks in typing can now be specialized. *Italics can now be used for word used as a word; *underlining, for emphasis; *bold face, for titles and headings (rather than underling), and *quotation marks for peculiar uses of words. (Italics and angle brackets were not generally available before except in type-set.)

See *apostrophe, *asterisk, *backslash, *bold (face), *brackets, *colon, *comma, *dash, *ditto, *ellipsis marks, *exclamation mark/point, *hyphen, *italics, *parentheses, *period, *question mark, quotation marks (single quotation marks, double quotation marks), *semi-colon, *slash, smart quotes, *underlining. Diacritical marks include *cidella, *dieresis, *tilde, *acute accent, grave accent, circumflex accent).

<strong>Some of these are diacritical marks</strong>

In New Testament Greek punctuation, there are periods and commas as in English. A raised period serves as the equivalent of colon or semi-colon. The question mark looks like an English semi-colon.

Hebrew punctuation: soph-pasook

The *uncial Greek manuscripts of the New Testament do not have spaces between words, no distinction by capitalization distinctions between *proper nouns and *common nouns, and no punctuation. Occasionally this latter feature of the uncial texts poses a question about sentence division. Should Jn <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:3 read, “Without him nothing was made that has been made. In him was life”? (United Bible Society text) or “Nothing was made without him. What has been made in him was life”? (Westcott & Hort text).

<strong>purist</strong>: someone who retains strict rules of word usage and grammatical rules no longer observed by the general populace or rules reserved for formal writing. Splitting infinitives, <em>e.g.</em>, may be a no-no; and certain sentence structures may be followed: “To whom do you want me to go?” <em> vs.</em> “Who do you want me to go to?”

<strong>purpose</strong>: the reason for doing something to bring about an effect. English can use infinitives to express purpose if the actor in the infinitive is the same as the subject of the leading: “He did it to impress his girlfriend.” After leading verbs , English can introduce a clause with “that,” “so,” “so that,” ”in order that”: “He worked two jobs that/in-order-that/so-that/so he could get married sooner.”

Latin ways of expressing purpose: acc. supine wh 331)

Gr purpose constructions: <em>hina/hoos/hopoos</em> + subj; infinitive (plain, with genitive article, with <em>hoos</em>, with hooste, with prepositions like <em>eis</em> and <em>pros</em>)

(in a set with *purpose clauses, *infinitives)

<strong>purpose clause</strong>: a subordinate clause that indicates the purpose for which the action in the main clause is done.

<strong>qere</strong>: what is to be “read” from the text of the Hebrew Old Testament in contrast to what is actually written. (in a set with *kethibh)

<strong>qoph</strong>: the nineteenth letter of the Hebrew alphabet. Also koph: כ

<strong>QR code</strong>: a square design that can be shot with a smart phone camera and can convey information visually in something of the same fashion as a bar code can indicate information in the field of marketing.(in a set with *bar code, *code, *language, *semaphore)

<strong>quadriliteral</strong>: having three letters

<strong>quadriliteral verb</strong>: a few Hebrew verbs have four-letter roots instead of three-letter ones.

(in a set with *triliteral root, *weak verbs, *doubly weak verbs)

<strong>quantitative verse</strong>:

<strong>quantity</strong>: the length or duration of time that it takes to pronounce a vowel; vowel length. Strictly speaking, what at first had to do with duration has bled off into systematic shifts between what are called long and short vowels even though their duration is not appreciably different. The shift from short <em>a</em> (ă) to long <em>a</em> (ā), short <em>e </em>(ě)<em> to long </em>e (ē), short <em>o</em> (ŏ) to long <em>o</em> (ō) are shifts between paired, or switch-out, sounds more than anything else.

To a lesser extent, the same goes for long and short <em>a, i,</em> and <em>u </em>in Greek and epsilon/eta and omicron/omega, although here duration is a more likely aspect of the difference.

<strong>question: </strong>a wording that asks for information.

English asks questions (1) about progressive action by reversing the auxiliary verb and the subject: “Are you working at home today?” <em> vs.</em> “You are working at home today.”

Questions (2) about characteristic action use the <em><u>do</u></em> auxiliary plus putting the subject after the auxiliary. “<em>Do</em> you work at home often?” In past tense, <em>did</em> has a *punctiliar value: “Did you work at home today?”

Questions can be asked by (3) intonation—raising pitch pattern at the end of the sentence without changing the word order from statement form: “You are working at home today?” This format is also used if the questioner is expressing amazement, but the intonation is more exaggerated at the end.

Questions can be asked (4) using *interrogative words: (a) pronouns, “Who is working at home today?” (b) adverbs, “When do you working at home?” “How do you get any work done at home?” “Where do you work at home?” (c) adjectives, “Which day do you work at home?”


In Hellenistic Greek, <em>ou(k)</em> + indicative implies that the speaker expects a positive response from the hearer, while <em>mee</em> + indicative implies he anticipates a negative response.

Latin uses for  anticipated negative response and   for anticipated positive response.

(in a set with *rhetorical question; *direct question, *indirect question; *informational question; *interrogative sentence)

<strong>question anticipating negative response</strong>: “You aren’t working at home today, are you?” <em> vs.</em> question anticipating positive response: “You are working at home today, aren’t you?”
