<strong>semiology</strong>: the study of sign language and communication made by signaling.

<strong>semiotic</strong>:  having to do with semantics

<strong>sentence: </strong>an expression deemed sufficient to express a thought that can stand by itself. (in a set with *phrase, *clause) (in a set with *complex sentence, *compound sentence, *compound-complex sentence, *elliptical sentence, *sentence fragment) (in a set with declarative sentence, exclamatory sentence, interrogative sentence)

<strong>sentence fragment</strong>: a part of a sentence considered insufficient to express a thought adequately, or to stand on its own.

<strong>separable suffix</strong>: a suffix that may also serve as a free-standing word<strong>. </strong>Latin cum could stand alone meaning “with,” but with personal pronouns it was attached as a suffix: nobiscum.

<strong>Septuagint</strong>: the standard Greek translation of the Hebrew Old Testament in use at the time of Christ. Its name comes from the tradition that “seventy” translators in Alexandria, Egypt, were involved in the effort (Gr. <em>septuagintos</em>, seventy; note The Letter of Aristeas). It dates from about 180 B. C. ???? Its renderings lie behind most New Testament quotations of the Old Testament instead of their being direct translations of the Hebrew. Often abbreviated LXX.

<strong>sequel</strong>: another writing, movie, or television show that piggybacks on a story already published or aired. A procedure often followed when an original piece achieves unexpected success.

(in a set with *episode, *prequel)

<strong>sequence of tenses</strong>: a tensing sequence that relates to the speaker’s viewpoint both the tense of the dependent and the independent verb forms of a sentence.  In English indirect discourse, for example, the verb in the indirect discourse part is also past if the main verb is past unless the dependent element expresses a timeless truth. Both verb are figured from the time standpoint of the speaker. <strong>Examples</strong> Greek and Latin, however, make indirect discourse verbs relative to the main verb rather than relative to the speaker of the whole sentence.

(in a set with *relative tense Wh 204, *absolute time)

<strong>sequential dative</strong>: the dative case instead of the accusative after Latin and Greek verbs that imply relationship: Latin had <em>credo</em> (trust),

Greek had <em>hepomai</em> (to follow),

<strong>sequential infinitive</strong>: the standard infinitive that by convention follows a certain construction.

<em>Persuade to do</em> is supposedly preferrable to <em>convince </em>(<em>__</em>) <em>to do</em>; there is <em>angry at, angry about, angry with</em>;

(in a set with *complementary infinitive)

<strong>sequential preposition</strong>: the standard preposition used after a certain term. We say, “allergic <em>to</em> hay,” not “allergic <em>with</em> hay.” <em>Enamored </em><em><u>of</u></em> sounds stilted; most people today say <em>enamored </em><em><u>with</u></em>. Is it <em>sign </em><em><u>of</u></em> or <em>sign </em><em><u>for</u></em>? Is it <em>convince someone </em><em><u>of</u></em> (with preposition) or <em>convince them to __</em> (infinitive)?

<strong>serial comma</strong>: a comma between the last two nouns of a series, a set that has three or more entries. Publishers differ as to whether their editors put a comma between the next-to-last and last noun in a series of three or more. An advantage of using the serial comma is that a distinction can then be made if the last items are thought of as a pair like “ham and eggs.” In such a case the comma would be omitted

Commas are used in a sequence of adjectives intended to be parallel rather than in series before a noun. The arrangement has the effect of connecting each adjective directly to the noun instead of piggy-backing each one on the rest as a set.

For this reason, commas are used when adjectives are not sequenced in the linguistically conventional pattern of the language. An English convention in serial modifiers is *determiner, size, color: “that big red barn”; but if the convention is departed from, a comma is required: “that red, big barn.”

Another application of commas in series is found when more than one long or hyphenated adjective ties to one noun: “my ever-ready, happy-go-lucky brother.” The comma helps the reader’s eye see how the relationship of words. Besides, the spoken equivalent requires a pause after the first hyphenation so the hearer can separate the first set and the second set as, in fact, two sets further tied to the *phrase head. The written comma represents the oral pause.

In a related situation, commas are used to avoid ambiguity: the advertisement goes “real, comfortable jeans” rather than “real comfortable jeans.” The latter format sounds like an every-day expression for “really comfortable jeans.”

<strong>serial modifiers</strong>: a series of adjective or adverbs connected with the same phrase head. If thought of as each one directly modifying the phrase head, commas are interposed. If thought of as each one tied to the remaining words as a group, no commas appear.

<strong><em>seriatim</em></strong>: in various places; used in citations to note that the point of interest shows up in several places throughout the work being cited.

<strong>serif</strong>: a short, thin, line optionally added to the ends of a stroke in forming letters like <em>A, E, F, G, H, I, K, L, M, N, P, R, S, T, V, W, Z.</em>

<strong>sermon</strong>: a presentation appropriate to a religious setting. In New Testament literature, it has been customary to call “sermon” The Sermon on the Mount (Mt <strong>5-7</strong>), The Sermon on the Plain (Lk <strong><sup>6</sup></strong>:17-49 ), Peter’s sermon at Pentecost (Acts <strong><sup>2</sup></strong>:14-40) and at the household of Cornelius (Acts <strong><sup>10</sup></strong>:34-43) as well as Paul’s sermon on Mars Hill (the Areopagus, Acts <strong><sup>17</sup></strong>:22-31). There is some overlap in custom of usage with *speech (<em>q.v.)</em> as types of *discourse.

(in a set with *speech; *discourse)

<strong>sermon, outreach</strong>: intended primarily for those not already members of the body of Christ

<strong>sermon series</strong>: a set of sermons delivered over several meeting times, usually related to one another in some way

<strong>sermonize</strong>: to prepare sermons; with a pejorative turn, to carry on orally about some matter

<strong>sestet</strong>: a set of six lines of poetry; the last six lines of a Petrarchan *sonnet.

<strong>sesquipedalian</strong>: a long word; given to the use of long words; ponderous because polysyllabic

<strong>set</strong>: a group of volumes that form the whole of perhaps an author’s complete works or several author’s books that belong together; The Ante-Nicene Fathers, <em>e.g.</em>

<strong>set expression</strong>: a monolithic phrase, thought of as an indivisible unit—as with titles: “I wanted to see the new version of <em>The King and I </em>[not The King and <em>Me</em>].” <em>Set expression</em> can apply to phrase idioms that could be regarded as hyphenatable expressions: “By tomorrow, I’ll be <em>good and </em>ready [adj].” “He was <em>the man of the hour</em> [noun].” “She went <em>over and above </em>the call of duty [phrase preposition].” “<em>By and </em>large, we have avoided that problem [adv].” “Well, <em>shut the front door </em>[interjection]!”

Biblical examples of set phraseology include (1) “God/___ do more to me/___ and more also” (Ruth <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:17; 1 Sam <strong><sup>3</sup></strong>:17; <strong><sup>20</sup></strong>:13; <strong><sup>25</sup></strong>:22; 2 Sam <strong><sup>3</sup></strong>:35);

(2) “What have I/___ to do with you/___” (Jud <strong><sup>11</sup></strong>:12; 2 Sam <strong><sup>16</sup></strong>:10; <strong><sup>19</sup></strong>:22; 1 Kg <strong><sup>17</sup></strong>:18; 2 Kg <strong><sup>3</sup></strong>:13; 2 Ch <strong><sup>35</sup></strong>:21, Jn <strong><sup>2</sup></strong>:4; Mk<strong> 1</strong>:24 = Lk <strong><sup>4</sup></strong>:34; Mt <strong><sup>8</sup></strong>:29 = Mk <strong><sup>5</sup></strong>:7 = Lk<strong> 8</strong>:28)

<strong>sheva/shewa/schwa</strong>: a lightly expressed vowel like the <em>e</em> in <em>b</em><em><u>e</u></em><em>cause</em>. It is a recurring phenomenon in Hebrew with unaccented syllables. Commonly written as an upside-down, lower-case <em>e</em> [ə].

<strong>shift</strong>: a systematic phonetic change such as the American replacement of <em>ah</em> with <em>ă</em> as in <em>hat</em>.

(in a set with *itization???)

<strong>shin</strong>: the twenty-first letter of the Hebrew alphabet. Also <em>sin</em>.

<strong>shoot the wad</strong>: to use up one’s material way before the time allotted for the presentation, much earlier than anticipated

<strong>short</strong>: the first part of a longer word used orally to save time or effort; in written form they are not normally followed by a period: <em>amp (amplifier), meds (medications), reps (repetitions in an exercise event), tat (tattoo), trig, uke (ukelele), ref (referee) , ump (umpire)	</em>

(in a set with *abbreviation, *acronym, *acrostic)

<strong>shorthand</strong>: a system of notation that enables a *stenographer to keep up with the pace of normal oral delivery and dictation.

<strong>sibilant</strong>: a hissing sound: <em>s, z, sh, zh</em> (voiced <em>sh</em>), <em>tz, dz,</em> retroflexed <em>s</em> (as in the Hebrew <em>samech</em> <em> vs.</em> <em>sin</em> or <em>shin</em>)

<strong>sibilate</strong>: to make a hissing sound

<strong><em>sic</em></strong>: a bracketed entry inside a quotation which indicates that the quotation is exact despite some error of content or form. A Latin word meaning <em>thus</em>.

<strong>sidebar</strong>: in a written presentation, supplementary or explanatory material that lies outside the general flow of the presentation, especially in computer-assisted deliveries

(in a set with *endnote, *footnote, *marginal note, *marginalia, *side note)

<strong>sidenote</strong>: a comment written in the margin of a text to given clarification to something in the text; also an aside in oral presentation

(in a set with *endnote, *footnote, *marginal note, *marginalia, *sidebar)

<strong>sigla</strong>:

<strong>sigma</strong>: eighteenth letter of the Greek alphabet: σ/Σ  with ς as its lower-case terminal form and C in the uncial manuscripts

<strong>sigmoid</strong>: shaped like the letter <em>s</em>.

<strong>sign language</strong>: a system of *gestures/signals with the fingers, hands, and arms used for communicating among the deaf.

(in a set with *alphabet, *dactylology, *manual alphabet, *signing)

<strong>sign manual</strong>: a handwritten signature by a king at the top of a royal decree

<strong>signalment</strong>: a detailed description of a person’s appearance

<strong>signage</strong>: the labels placed on a business or public building that identifies it or its function.

<strong>signal word</strong>: a term that appears, say, in the main clause of Latin sentences that helps distinguish a subsequent result clause (sic, qualis, quantum, talis, tantum) from a concessive clause (tamen)(wh ch 31, p 197), adversative clause, and the like.

<strong>signaling</strong>: communicating through visual, sound, electronic (including radar) means as with smoke signal, pyrotechnics (fire words in various colors), flag hoists (colored flags hung on a ship’s mast), radio, television, telephone, teletype.

<strong>signature</strong>: the author’//buyer’s handwritten name at the end of a letter or on an official document, used for guaranteeing authenticity.

(in a set with *inside address, *salutation, *complementary close, *postscript)

<strong>signing</strong>: using a system of *gestures/signals with the fingers, hands, and arms to communicate with the deaf.

(in a set with *alphabet, *dactylology, *manual alphabet, *signing)

<strong>sign-off</strong>: a stylized manner of closing a radio or television program

<strong>silent letter</strong>: a written letter that does not represent a sound spoken. In Greek, Latin, and Hebrew, there were no silent letters. English has more silent letters than most modern languages do. Several reasons contribute to this phenomenon: (1) a letter that retains an unused or unpronounceable derivational element. When the n/g/c endings on old Latin and Greek nouns were dropped, the word remaining letters sometimes created unpronounceable combinations: <em>paradigm-os</em>


-<em>gh</em> in many words this *digraph is silent: <em>aught, bough, bought</em>, <em>blight</em>, <em>bright, brought, caught, delight, dough, drought, fight, flight, fraught, haughty, height, high/higher, light, might/mighty, naughty, nigh, night, nought, ought,</em> <em>plight</em>, <em>right, sigh/sighing, slaughter, sleigh, slight, sought, th(o)rough, tight,</em> <em>weigh/weight,</em> <em>wright, wrought</em>,

((-<em>gh</em> can also be be <em>f</em>: <em>enough, rough, slough, tough,</em> ))

-<em>gm</em> at the end of a word has the <em>g</em> silent: <em>diaphragm</em> (but <em>diaphragmatic</em>), <em>paradigm</em> (but paradigmatic), <em>phlegm </em>(flem),

-<em>gn</em> at the end of words has the <em>g</em> silent: <strong>[insert panel]</strong> <em>align, assign, benign, deign, design </em>but <em>designation</em>)<em>, feign, foreign, malign (but malignant), reign, resign </em>(but <em>resignation</em>)<em>, sign </em>(but <em>signature</em>),

<em>	gn- </em>at the beginning of words keeps the <em>g</em> silent: <em>gnarl, gnash, gnat, gnathic, gnathite, gnaw, gneiss, gnocchi, gnome, gnomon, gnosis, gnu.</em>

-<em>h</em> in <em>wh</em> is often not pronounced by many American English speakers: <em>which</em> (= witch). But <em>whale, what, when. </em>Sometimes it is the <em>w </em>of <em>wh</em> that is dropped: <em>who, whom, whose</em>; <em>whole.</em>

<em>kn- </em>does not pronounce the <em>k</em> on the front of words:<em> knack, knap, knave, knee, kneel, knell, knife, knight, knit, knob, knock, knoll, knop, knot, know, knuckle, knur, knurl. </em>

<em>	-pn </em>in<em> pneumonia</em>,

-<em>ps</em> in <em><u>p</u></em><em>sycho</em>-,

-<em>pt</em> in <em><u>pt</u></em><em>yalism, </em>

<em>	-rh-</em> is pronounced as if it were simply <em>r</em>: <em>rhabdon, rhabdomancy, rhabdomyoma, rhabdovirus, Rhadamanthine, rhapsodic, rhapsodist, rhapsodize, rhapsody, rhatany, rhea, rhenium, rheology, rheometer, rheostt, rheotaxis, rhesus, rhetor, rhetoric, rhetorical, rhetorician, rheum, rheumatic, rheumatism, rheumatoid, rhinal, rhinencephalon, rhinestone, rhinoceros</em>, <em>rhinology, rhinopharyngitis, rhinoplasty, rhinoscopy, rhinovirus, rhizanthous, rhizobium, rhizocephalan, rhizogenic, rhizoid, rhizome, rhizopod, rhizopus, rhizosphere, rhirhizotomy, rho, rhodium, rhodochrosite, rhododendron, rhodolite, rhodorite, rhodopsin, rhodora, rhombencephalon, rhombic, rhombohedron, rhombold, rhombus, rhonchus, rhubarb, rhumb, rhyme, rhyolite, rhythm, rhythmic, rhythmist, </em>(The <em>rh </em>combination descends from Greek. The <em>rho </em>on the front of Greek words was aspirated—accompanied by an <em>h </em>sound.)

-<em>rrh</em>- is pronounced as if it were simply <em>r</em>: suffixes in <em>–r</em><em><u>rh</u></em><em>agia, –r</em><em><u>rh</u></em><em>aphy,-r</em><em><u>rh</u></em><em>ea, </em>-<em>r</em><em><u>rh</u></em><em>exis</em> ; <em>cirrhosis</em>, 	(The -<em>rrh </em>combination descends from Greek. The <em>rho </em>on the front of Greek words was aspirated—accompanied by an <em>h </em>sound. If an <em>ě</em> was prefixed to it for *augment purposes, the <em>rho </em>was first doubled. Derivatives into English have maintained that general pattern by doubling the <em>r </em>whenever anything is put ahead of <em>rh</em>. Consequently, English words have –<em>rrh </em>in the middle and at the end of all words derived from Greek)

<em>w-</em> is silent on the beginning of <em>wr-</em> words: <strong>[insert panel] </strong><em>wrach, wraith, wrangle, wrapper, wrasse, wrath/e, wreak, wrech,wreck </em>(<em>etc.</em>), <em>wren, wrest, wrestle, wretch, wriggle, wright, wring/wrang/wrung, wrinkle, wrist, writ/write/wrote/writing/written</em>, <em>wrong, wroth, wrought, wry.</em> It is silent also on the beginning of <em>who, whom, whose, whole.</em>

<em>	-w</em> is silent on the end of words like <em>bellow, blow, barrow, borrow, bow1, crow, farrow, flow, glow, grow, harrow, hollow, know, low, marrow, mellow, morrow, mow1, narrow, pillow, row1, sallow, sew, sorrow, sow1, sparrow, tallow, tomorrow, tow; caw, claw, craw, draw, flaw, gnaw, heehaw, in-law, jaw, law, maw, outlaw, slaw, straw, yaw; </em>But there is a <em>wuh</em> sound on the end of other words: <em> allow, bow1, brow, cow, how, mow1, now, ow!, plow, pow, prow, sow1, vow, wow.</em>

The acceptable sound combinations of modern English do not correspond with the pronunciation habits of ancient our Greek, Latin, and Anglo-Saxon heritage.

(2) a letter that is simply not pronounced in some forms of the root: <em>answer, bristle, condemn but </em>condemnation), <em>debt, deign, design </em>(but <em>designation</em>)<em>, dythyramb, ensign, exhume, hustle, of</em><em><u>t</u></em><em>en, mistletoe, mus</em><em><u>c</u></em><em>le </em>(but <em>muscular), reign </em>(but <em>regnal), resign </em>(but <em>resignation</em>)<em>, sa</em><em><u>l</u></em><em>ve </em>(but <em>salvation</em>)<em>, sign </em>(but <em>signature</em>)<em>, sof</em><em><u>t</u></em><em>en </em>(but <em>softer</em>). <em>su</em><em><u>b</u></em><em>tle</em>

Words with terminal <em>ld</em> sound sometimes lose the <em>l</em>: <em>could, should, would</em> (but <em>billed, bold, cold, culled, dulled, foaled, fold, fooled, guild, hold,hulled, knelled, lulled, mold, mould, mulled, polled, rolled, sold, told, </em>

Words with terminal -<em>mb</em> lose the <em>b</em>, especially if they are in the same syllable: <em>bomb, bomber </em>(but <em>bombastic</em>)<em>, climb, climber, comb </em>(but <em>combine</em>)<em>, crumb, crumbier </em>(but<em> crumble</em>)<em>, dumb, dumber, dumbest, iamb </em>(but <em>iambic</em>),<em> jamb </em>(<em>jamboree</em>)<em>, lamb, limb </em>(but <em>limber</em>)<em>, numb/number1</em>(but number1),<em> plumb, plumber, rhumb, thumb, tomb, womb. </em>

Words with -<em>lf</em>, -<em>lk, -lv- </em>often drop the <em>l </em>if they are in the same syllable and are preceded by <em>a </em>or <em>o</em>: <em>balk/balks, calf, calve/calves, caulk/caulks, caulker, folk/folks, half, halve/halves, polk/polks, salve/salves </em>(but salvation)<em>, talk/talks, talkathon, walk/walks, walkathon, yolk/yolks. </em>Most of these are <em>al </em>and<em> ol </em>words<em>. </em>With <em>e, i,</em> and <em>u</em>, however, the <em>l</em> is pronounced: <em>elk </em>(but <em>welkin, </em>)<em>; bilk, ilk, milk, silk</em>; or <em>bulk, hulk, sulk. </em>That is because <em>e, i, </em>and<em> u </em>are vowels with the tongue in high position, which is close to the position of the <em>l</em>; so, it is easier to get into the <em>lk</em> sequence. The <em>a</em> and <em>o </em>have the tongue lower; so it takes more movement to get to the <em>l</em> position.

Many words with a medial <em>st</em> sequence do not sound the <em>t: bustle, castle, christen, fasten, glisten, hasten, hustle, jostle, listen, mistletoe, moisten, nestle, rustle, whistle, wrestle,</em>

Many words with <em>sce/sci</em> sequence change the <em>c</em> to <em>s</em> before <em>e, i, </em>or<em> y </em>(by standard rule) and so lose the separate <em>k</em> sound: <em>ascent, discipline, scent, science, visceral, viscid, </em>

(3) a letter that has some other function in a word: the terminal <em>e</em> after a single consonant (or *digraph like bathe) lengthens the preceding vowel<em>: mat/mat</em><em><u>e</u></em><em>; met/met</em><em><u>e</u></em><em>; mit/mit</em><em><u>e</u></em><em>; mot/mot</em><em><u>e</u></em><em>; mut/mut</em><em><u>e</u></em> <strong>[insert panel</strong>.

(4) Custom simply generates a different sound in speaking while the spelling is retained: <em>colonel</em> as <em>kernel</em>; <em>coxswain </em>as <em>sox’n; forecastle </em>as <em>fox’l</em>;<em> gunwhale</em> as “gunnal”;

(4) Silent letters near the front or end of a word may come into play when pronounceable combinations are created by additions at, before, or after. The <em><u>pn</u></em><em>eo-</em> terms bring the <em>p</em> into play with <em>apnea, eupnea. </em>The silent <em><u>g</u></em> in <em>diaphragm</em> becomes pronounced in <em>diaphragmatic</em>.  The silent <em>n</em> in <em>damn</em> is pronounced in <em>damnation</em>.

(in a set with *pronunciation, *spelling)

<strong>simile</strong>:  a figure of speech that uses the words <em>like</em> or <em>as</em>: “He’s as slow as a sloth.” “He’s like a horse.” Removing <em>like </em>creates a *metaphor: “He’s a horse.”

(in a set with *figure of speech; *metaphor, *metonymy, *anthropomorphism, *anthropopathic, *hendiadys, *irony)

<strong>similitude</strong>: *archaism for *allegory or *simile

<strong>simple sentence</strong>: a sentence that does not have a dependent clause combined with a main clause. It expresses one complete thought.

(in a set with *complex sentence, *compound sentence, *compound-complex sentence)

<strong>simplex</strong>: a word without *affixes and is not part of a *compound word: <em>care  vs.</em> <em>uncaring, caregiver.</em>

<strong>sin</strong>: the twenty-first letter of the Hebrew alphabet. Also shin:שׂ   שׁ

<strong>single-space</strong>: (1) not having an empty line between lines of type; having type on every single line. (2) Within a line of typing, <em>single-space</em> means that only one blank space lies between one word and the next. A single space is used after a comma, semi-colon, quotation mark,

(in a set with *space, *double space, *triple space, *space-and-a-half)

<strong>singular</strong>: one rather than more than one. In Greek, Latin, and English, a *word used as a word has a singular verb and a singular modifier: “<em>This </em><em><u>foolish ones</u></em> describe<em>s</em> the monkeys.” *Hendiadys also takes a singular verb: “The God and Father of our Lord Jesus Christ <em>is</em>” (1 Th <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:3).

<strong>sirvente</strong>: a satirical form of lyric verse of the Provençal troubadours. WB

<strong>skit</strong>: a short, comic play designed to entertain

<strong>skywriting</strong>: the process of making cursive lettering in the sky by using an airplane to release a trail of visible vapor.

<strong>slam</strong>: a comment intended to criticize or hurt a person’s feelings, a “cut,” an *insult, a *slur. Slam differs from *innuendo and *insinuation by being overt and direct to the person instead of implied and thus inferred. A slam is “in your face.”

<strong>slander</strong>: to tell something that is not true in order to hurt a person’s reputation

<strong>slang</strong>: a made-up word used in light, every-day circumstances. Slang words may be made up completely “from scratch,” or they are based in—or perversions of—other words already within the language. Seldom do they derive from earlier languages since they come into the language from the general public.

(in a set with *archaism, *buzz word, *catch phrase, *colloquialism, *curse word, *formal term, *informal term, *oath, *obsolete term, *provincialism, *regionalism, *technical term, *vulgarism)

<strong>slash</strong>: a line rising to the right (/) used between entries for different parallel realities:  “I/We must take care of the problem.” Similar in value to <em>and/or</em>. The idea contrasts from appositional <em>or</em>, which gives alternate names for the same thing, another way of saying, “to put it another way.” Same as *diagonal.

<strong>	example </strong>(in a set with *backslash)

<strong>slogan</strong>: a principle or ideal prized by the entity that uses it; a *motto. <em>Ad astra per aspera</em> (“To the stars Through Difficulties”), motto for the state of Kansas; <em>to phoos tou kosmou</em> (The Light of the World), motto for Cincinnati Christian University. <em>Slogar</em> may connote a less formal situation as with a group of people; <em>motto</em> has a more formal ring to it.

<strong>slur</strong>: v. to speak indistinctly as when drunk or as a result of injury or disability. The indistinctness mainly comes from not pronouncing the consonants clearly.

n. a derogative comment aimed at a person; what is done when one “casts aspersions on” someone or, perhaps, something. (in a set with *enunciate, *pronounce)

<strong>small letters</strong>: another expression for *lower case, the smaller forms of letters in the alphabet; not capitals

(in a set with *higher case, *capitals; see also *common noun,*proper noun; *all caps; *majuscules, minuscules)

<strong>small talk</strong>: talk about relatively unimportant matters that takes place as a way to pass the time than to inform the person

<strong>smart quotes</strong>: quotation marks that are curled opposite ways—toward the writing quoted—on the front and end of a citation.

<strong>snide remark</strong>:

<strong>social media</strong>: the use of unofficial computer systems of communication electronically in contrast to public communication systems—the “media.” Examples include Facebook (formerly Myspace), e-mail, blog, twitter, flicker, pinterest.

<strong>Socratic method</strong>: an instructional pattern using a question-answer format in such a way as to draw ideas out of the student in contrast to telling the student those ideas. Associated with Socrates, It was built on the idea of anamnesis, which postulated that knowledge is inborn; consequently, the teaching task was to bring that knowledge to conscious mind. (in a set with *lecture, *memorization, *rote learning)

<strong>solecism: </strong>the literal translation of an idiom or structure in one language into another where it does not convey the same idea—or convey any sensible idea at all. An example is a combination of the Hebrew infinitive absolute plus cognate finite verb used to express intensity: “I have really wanted to eat this supper with you.” Luke 22:15 says, “<em>Epithumia epethumeesa</em>, dative noun plus cognate finite verb. In normal Greek such a construction does not appear, but Jewish authors writing in Greek sometimes used a dative-case noun plus a cognate finite verb to convey it, as Luke does in this quotation.

Another Greek option was to use a participle plus cognate finite verb for this idiom: “Peter says to Ananias, “Remaining, it remained under you authority” (<em>menon emenon</em>, Acts <strong><sup>5</sup></strong>:4). (cp. *idiom).

In Acts, Luke quotes the Septuagint rendering of Is <strong><sup>6</sup></strong>:9-10, which handles the idiom with both dative noun and cognate participle in <strong><sup>28</sup></strong>:26. Mt <strong><sup>13</sup></strong>:14-15 cites the same passage (= Mk<strong> 4</strong>:12; Lk <strong><sup>8</sup></strong>:10).

The Latin New Testament handles the Isaiah quotation with a supine plus cognate and a participle plus cognate verb in Mt <strong><sup>13</sup></strong>:14a. In Mk <strong><sup>4</sup></strong>:12 and Lk <strong><sup>8</sup></strong>:10, however, participles appear in reference to both the seeing and the hearing. Acts <strong><sup>28</sup></strong>:26 says, <em>aure audietis . . . videntes videbitis</em>, “With-the-ear you-will-hear . . . seeing you will see.” Thus, it is a dative-noun-plus-verb construction, but the abstract hearing reference (<em>akoee</em>) is replaced with a noun for the hearing organ.

<strong>solid</strong>: said of a two-part word that does not have a space or hyphen between the parts: stockbroker

<strong>soliloquy: </strong>in drama a character speaking his thoughts when he is presumably “alone” [Lat. solus, alone]. A kind of thinking out loud while alone and spoken to no one in particular.

(in a set with *monologue, *dialogue)

<strong>Solomon’s Prayer of Dedication</strong>: The prayer Solomon delivered before the people in Jerusalem at the dedication of First Temple (1 Kg <strong><sup>8</sup></strong>:23-53).

<strong>Song of Deborah</strong>: the example of ancient Hebrew poetry preserved in Jud <strong><sup>15</sup></strong>:2-321a, identified with Deborah, a judge of Israel during the period of the judges before the monarchy.

<strong>Song of Solomon</strong>: another name for Canticles, Canticle of Canticles, or Song of Songs in the Old Testament scriptures.

<strong>Song of Songs</strong>: another name for Song of Solomon, or Canticles, or Canticle of Canticles.

<strong>sonnet: </strong>a fourteen-line verse using various rhyme schemes

<strong>space</strong>: one stroke of a typewriter or computer on a line of type; a space between lines of type (in a set with *double-space, *single-space, *triple-space, *space-and-a-half)

<strong>space-and-a-half</strong>: having one and a half line empty between each line of type, a feature that was available on some typewriters and one normally available on computers now. (in a set with *space, *double-space, *triple-space, *space-and-a-half)

<strong>spam</strong>: unwanted e-mails, in the form of advertisement, dangerous or risky messages with attachments that contains viruses, unimportant newsy items, and the like.

<strong>speaking in tongues</strong>: a phenomenon that came in consequence of the descent of the Spirit on the day of Pentecost (Acts <strong><sup>2</sup></strong>) and at the house of Cornelius later (Acts<strong> 10</strong>). Paul talks to the Corinthians about appropriate use of speaking in tongues during public worship services (1 Cor <strong>12-14</strong>). (a set with *xenoglossia, *glossolalia, *ecstatic utterance, *tongue speaking)

<strong>special pleading</strong>: endeavoring to change another person’s mind or avoid unwanted personal consequences by appealing to less-than-satisfactory reasons.

<strong>speech</strong>: (1) oral communication as a kind of *discourse; (2) a particular oral presentation. In Acts, Luke weaves several *discourses into his narrative. They include Stephen’s defense before the Sanhedrin (<strong><sup>7</sup></strong>:2-53) as well as Paul’s defense before Ananias, the high priest (<strong><sup>23</sup></strong>:1-6), his defense before the Jerusalem mob (<strong><sup>22</sup></strong>:1-21), his defense before Felix, the governor (<strong><sup>24</sup></strong>:10-21), and his defense before Festus and Herod Agrippa, the king (<strong><sup>26</sup></strong>:2-29). Inasmuch as these discourses were not delivered in a purely religious setting, they are listed here as speeches more than *sermons (<strong>q.v</strong>.).

(in a set with *reading; *oration, *declamation; *sermon, *discourse)

<strong>spelling</strong>: how the letters used in a language are arranged to represent its words; *orthography. Spelling becomes an issue because (1) language drifts over time; so a spelling that made sense before may no longer correlates closely with the way people say the word; yet the spelling remains for historic purposes and so there is no problem reading earlier writings: <strong>[insert panel] </strong>the –<em>gh</em> on <em>cou</em><em><u>gh</u></em><em>, enou</em><em><u>gh</u></em><em>, lau</em><em><u>gh</u></em><em>, rough, rou</em><em><u>gh</u></em><em>age, tough, </em>is pronounced <em>f</em>. However, <em><u>gh</u></em> becomes silent in<em> aught, blight, bought, bri</em><em><u>gh</u></em><em>t, brought, cau</em><em><u>gh</u></em><em>t, delight, dou</em><em><u>gh</u></em><em>, drou</em><em><u>gh</u></em><em>t, fight, fli</em><em><u>gh</u></em><em>t, fraught, haughty, hei</em><em><u>gh</u></em><em>t, li</em><em><u>gh</u></em><em>t, mi</em><em><u>gh</u></em><em>t/mighty, naught, nigh, ni</em><em><u>gh</u></em><em>t, nought, ought, pli</em><em><u>gh</u></em><em>t, ri</em><em><u>gh</u></em><em>t, sigh, si</em><em><u>gh</u></em><em>t, sleigh, slight, sought, th(o)rough, ti</em><em><u>gh</u></em><em>t, wei</em><em><u>gh/</u></em><em>t, wri</em><em><u>gh</u></em><em>t, wrought</em>. So, the English spelling system is not as good a fit as it is in many languages.

For another thing, (2) English vocabulary contains significantly more terms than most languages do—perhaps half again as many in normal use as are used in even modern, developed nations. The more examples there are, the harder it is to maintain uniformity.

Furthermore, (3) English is a melting-pot language, being supplied with words derived not only from the classical languages of Latin and Greek, but also from the Anglo-Saxon/Germanic background. The language spelling system has retained the lettering of the feeder languages; so it remains static while the spoken form keeps drifting. This phenomenon creates a host of *“silent letters” in English spelling. There is silent g in sign, <em>paradigm</em>. <em>Pneumonia</em> does not pronounce the<em> p</em>. See *“silent letters” for further examples.

<em>Diphthong</em> says <em>diphthong, ophthalmologist </em>says <em>opthalmologist,</em> and <em>d</em><em><u>ip</u></em><em>htheria</em> says <em>diptheria</em>. English wants to create a *stop-*fricative set from a fricative-fricative combination whereas Greek wants to create a fricative-fricative set from a stop-fricative set.

Initial spelled <em>w</em> does not affect pronunciation in <em>wrap</em>, <em>wreck, wring,</em> <em>wretched</em>, <em>wrung</em>, <em>wrong</em>, <em>write, wrote, wrought</em>.

Final spelled <em>w</em> does not affect pronunciation of words like <em>blow/blew</em>, <em>flew</em>, <em>flow</em> , <em>grow</em>, <em>grown</em>, <em>know</em>, <em>low</em>, <em>mow1</em>, <em>row</em>1, <em>slow</em>, <em>sow1</em>, <em>tow</em>, The final <em>w</em> does affect pronunciation on <em>bow1</em>, <em>brown</em>, <em>cow</em>, <em>down</em>, <em>frown</em>, <em>how</em>, <em>now</em>, <em>mow</em>1, row1, <em>sow1</em>, <em>wow</em>. Sometimes particular words come down through French and other European languages, which further adjust the Latin originals.


As a result, (1) letters and combinations of letters do not always have the same value, as says the famous jingle about <em>i</em> before <em>e</em> except after <em>c</em> or sounding like <em>ā</em> in <em>n</em><em><u>ei</u></em><em>ghbor</em> or <em>w</em><em><u>ei</u></em><em>gh</em>. But note the <em><u>ee</u></em> sound for <em><u>ei</u></em> in <em>caff</em><em><u>ei</u></em><em>ne</em>, <em><u>ei</u></em><em>ther/n</em><em><u>ei</u></em><em>ther/l</em><em><u>ei</u></em><em>sure, prot</em><em><u>ei</u></em><em>n, s</em><em><u>ei</u></em><em>ze</em>; or the “ear” sound in <em>w</em><em><u>ei</u></em><em>rd</em> or the <em>i</em> sound in <em>aparth</em><em><u>ei</u></em><em>d, ch</em><em><u>ei</u></em><em>lostomatoplasty, f</em><em><u>ei</u></em><em>sty, h</em><em><u>ei</u></em><em>ght, l</em><em><u>ei</u></em><em>tmotif</em>; or the short i in <em>for</em><em><u>ei</u></em><em>gn</em>, <em>forf</em><em><u>ei</u></em><em>t, forfeiture, surfeit</em>. Sometimes the two vowels belong to different syllables: <em>alb</em><em><u>ei</u></em><em>t, acqu</em><em><u>ie</u></em><em>sce, cl</em><em><u>ie</u></em><em>nt, </em>(fiery), spontaneity<em>, var</em><em><u>ie</u></em><em>ty</em>; also note <em>seize</em> but <em>siege</em>!  As a Facebook comment said, “Use <em>i</em> before <em>e</em> except when you run a feisty heist in a weird beige foreign neighborhood.”

<em>C</em> before <em>e, i, </em>or <em>y</em> has an <em>s</em> sound; before <em>a, o, </em>or <em>u</em> it has a <em>k </em>sound. That feature of pronunciation has a spelling implication in certain circumstances: picnic is spelled <em>picni</em><em><u>ck</u></em><em>ing</em> to keep the final <em>c</em> from becoming an <em>s</em> sound. The same holds for the words <em>panic/panicking, </em>

<em>Ch</em> is <em>k</em> in <em><u>ch</u></em><em>olangiopancreatography </em><strong>[insert panel]</strong>, an explosive sound in <em><u>ch</u></em><em>unk </em><strong>[insert panel]</strong>, an <em>sh</em> in <em><u>ch</u></em><em>ute, douche </em><strong>[insert panel]</strong>, and no sound at all in <em><u>ch</u></em><em>thonic,schism</em>. <strong>[insert panel] </strong>(See under *spelling)

<em>G</em> can be <em>guh</em>, <em>j</em>, or <em>zh</em> (<em>barrage1, beige, corsage1, garage1, mirage1, triage</em>)

<em>S</em> can be voiceless: <em>base, case, douse, gas, goose, loose, mouse, moose, noose, pastor, sat, tonsil, us, vase1, </em>Initial single <em>s</em> is always an <em>s</em> sound.

<em>S</em> can be voiced into a <em>z</em> sound: <em>basil, cosy, does, ease, fuse,</em> <em>laser, miser, pose, toes, woes,</em>

<em>ss: </em>Double <em>ss</em> (almost) always guarantees an <em>s</em> sound: <em>abyss, bass1, bass1, boss, class, crass, cuss, dross, fuss, gross, hiss, hoss, kiss, loss, miss, pass, toss, </em>(But <em>fissure</em>)

<em>S</em> can have a <em>sh</em> sound: <em>insure, sugar</em>, reassure, sure,

<em>	S</em> can have a <em>zh</em> sound: <em>fusion, visual</em>

<em>	-ss- </em>can have a sh sound:<em> mission</em>

-ss<em>- can have a </em>zh<em> sound: fission</em>

<em>Th</em> can be voiceless: mouth (n.), <em>south</em>, <em>thud, with, zither.</em>

<em>Th</em> can be voiced (<em>dh</em>): <em>another, bother, brother, druther, either, father, farther, further, gather, hither, mother, mouth </em>(v.), <em>neither,</em> <em>rather, soothe, southern, that, the, thee, then, these, thine, thither, those, thou, though, thy, whither, </em>

(2) Furthermore, the same sound can be covered in several ways. Such a list includes all the *homophones in the English language, of which there are an amazing number! 	See under *homophones. Other words create related forms spelled with <em>t</em> or <em>d</em>, but both spellings are pronounced voiceless: <em>feel/felt, give/gift, keep/kept, kneel/knelt, leave/left</em>, <em>rive/rift, sleep/slept, weep/wept</em> but <em>limp/limped</em>, <em>nipped, ripped, sipped</em>, <em>stepped, stressed, work/worked</em>.

The *plosive sound <em>ch </em>can be found in (a) <em>attach, blanch,</em> <em><u>ch</u></em><em>unk, church, gaucho, much, munch, pinch,</em> <em>rachet, rich, </em>

<em>Ch </em>can also be spelled (b) <em>tch</em> in the medial and terminal position: <em>batch, bitch, botch, butcher, catch, clutch, ditch, fetch, glitch, hatch, hitch, hatch, itch, kvetch, latch, match, natch, notch, patch, scotch, scutch, stitch, stretch, satchel, switch, thatch, twitch, vetch, vetchling, watch, witch, wretch</em>,

That same sound can occur with (c) <em>t</em> before <em>u</em>: <strong>[insert panel]</strong> <em>actual, adventure, amateur, aperture, armature, cariacature, creature, culture, denture/s, departure, expenditure, firfeiture, fixture, forfeiture, fracture, functual, future, gesture/maturation, indentured, instinctual, intellectual, investiture, lecture, legislature, ligature, literature, mature, miniature, mixture, moisture, nature, nomenclature, nurture, overture, pasture, pasteurize, picture, posture, pasturage, picture, punctual, puncture, rapture, ritual, sculpture, stricture, tablature, tincture, venture, vesture, victuals, vulture, </em>

Portuguese uses (d) <em>–ti </em>for the <em>ch</em> sound: <em>Tolentino</em> is <em>Tolenchino</em>.


The <em>j </em>sound can be covered by (a) <em>j</em>, of course, but it can also be covered by (b) <em>g</em> before <em>e, i, or y</em>. Thirdly, it can be covered by (c) <em>dg</em>, as in words like <em>hedge</em>, <em>judge</em>, <em>judgment</em>, <em>ledge</em>,

(3) The same spelling can have the same sound but different meaning (*homonym). Several verbs have two tenses spelled the same way and pronounced the same way: <strong>[insert panel] </strong><em>beat</em>, <em>bid</em>, <em>burst</em>, <em>clad</em>, <em>cost</em>, <em>cut</em>, <em>hit</em>, <em>hurt</em>, <em>knit</em>, <em>let</em>, <em>live???</em>, <em>quit</em>, <em>rid</em>, <em>shed</em>, [shit], <em>spit</em>, <em>split</em>, <em>spread</em>, <em>wet.</em>

Same spelling and sound can cover both singular and plural: <em>bison</em>, <em>craft</em>, <em>deer</em>, <em>fish</em>, <em>fry</em>, <em>sen, sheep</em>,

That’s why there are spelling bees in English!


<strong>English spelling, however, does follow certain general patterns.</strong> The letter <em><u>g</u></em> has a <em><u>j</u></em> sound if followed by <em><u>e, i,</u></em> or <em><u>y</u></em>; and a <em><u>guh</u></em> sound (“hard <em><u>g</u></em>”) if followed by <em><u>a, o,</u></em> or <em><u>u</u></em>. Exceptions are <em>get</em>, <em>gig</em>. Even double <em>gg</em> can sometimes be <em>j</em>: <em>exaggerate</em>. A final <em>e</em> after final <em>g</em> makes the <em>g</em> have the <em>j</em> sound<em>: age, beige, cage, dirge, gauge, huge, lung/lunge, merge, page, rag/rage, rang/range, sag/sage, ting/tinge, urge, verge, wag/wage. </em>

The same pattern <em>g</em> + vowels applies to the two values for the letter <em>c</em>: before <em><u>e, i,</u></em> or <em><u>y</u></em>, the <em><u>c </u></em>has an <em><u>s</u></em> sound; before <em><u>a, o</u></em>, or <em><u>u</u></em>, it has a <em><u>k</u></em> sound<em>: coalesce, excrescence, exscind, obscene, obsolesce, oscine, oscillate, ecalescence??, piscine, prescience, pubescence, pubescent, quiescent, rescind, rubescent, scent, scepter, sciatic, science, scintillate, scion, scission, scissor, scissure, susceptible, tabescent, </em>

In keeping with the pattern for <em>c</em>, the final <em>e </em>that is normally dropped before suffixes beginning with a vowel.is kept after <em>g</em> without adding a syllable to keep the g spriant in words like <em>chang</em><em><u>e</u></em><em>able</em>, <em>displaceable, manag</em><em><u>e</u></em><em>able, noticeable, peaceable, practiceable, replaceable, traceable</em>. Similarly –<em>i- </em>keeps <em>g</em> as a <em>j </em>sound: <em>allegiance</em>. A <em>k</em> may be added to <em>arc</em> to make <em>arcking</em> so the <em>k</em> sound is for sure kept before –<em>ing</em>. Likewise, a <em>u</em> comes after <em>g</em> in <em>g</em><em><u>u</u></em><em>ide</em> to help show that the <em>g</em> is not pronounced <em><u>j</u></em> before the <em>e</em>, <em>i, </em>or <em>y</em>. Such words are not to be pronounced <em>gw</em>: <em>guenon, guerilla, guess, guest, guide</em>, <em>guideon</em>, <em>guild</em>, <em>guilder</em>, <em>guile,</em> <em>guillemont, guilloche, guillotine, guilt</em>, <em>guimpe</em>, <em>guinea, guipure, guise</em>, <em>guy</em>, <em>guitar</em>, <em>morg</em><em><u>u</u></em><em>e</em>, <em>roug</em><em><u>u</u></em><em>e</em>, <em>tongue</em>, <em>vaguely</em>. (But note <em>guard</em>, <em>etc.</em>). Some double <em>cc</em>’s are followed by <em>e, i,</em> or <em>y</em>, so that the first gets a <em>k</em> sound and the second gets an <em>s</em> sound: c<em>occi, coccyx, succeed, succession, succinct, </em>

(2) English frequently follows the Latin practice of doubling a consonant to indicate a short vowel before it: <strong>[inset panel]</strong> <em>biter/bitter, inflame/inflammation, miler/miller, miter/admitted, rifle/riffle; ripe/ripple, stared/starred.</em> That gives rise to the rule that a word ending in a vowel-consonant doubles the consonant before adding a vowel suffix. In most cases, not doubling the consonant first would make the preceding vowel long: <em>begin/beginning; bit/bitten, cap/capped, clap/clapping, dim/dimmed, emit/emitted, fat/fatten, hit/hitting, hot/hotter; knotted, man/manning, omit/omitting, pan/panning, rip/ripped, sap/sapped, tan/tanned, win/winning, write/written,. </em>

A patterned exception to the doubling rule arises in *polysyllables with accents on the *antepenult (the third syllable from the end). Since English pronunciation tends to weaken unaccented vowels to sheva, an antepenult accent weakens an ultima vowel. As a result, doubling the final consonant is not necessary for keeping the preceding vowel short: <strong>[insert panel] </strong><em>benefited</em> (rather than <em>benefitted</em>), <em>bracketed, buffeted, canceled, reveled, riveted,</em> <em>signaled, </em>

(3) A final <em><u>e</u></em> after a single consonant (including *digraphs) makes the preceding vowel long: <em>mat/mate, met/mete, mit/mite, mot/mote, mut/mute.</em> Even with two consonants , the <em>e</em> lengthens the preceding vowel in some cases: bathe/bath, <em>haste</em> (but <em>hast</em>), <em>lathe/lath, lithe,</em> <em>waste</em> (but <em>wast</em>, OE); yet <em>caste</em> and <em>cast</em> are *homophones, and <em>mouth</em> is a homograph pronounced <em>–th</em> for noun and <em>–dh</em> for verb<em>. </em>Exceptions are <em>above, dove, glove, have, live </em>(v)<em>, love, move, relive, shove, sieve, </em><strong>[insert panel]</strong>

(4) On vowel-consonant-<em><u>e </u></em>words where the accent is three syllables back, the vowel may be short (unless a secondary accent falls on the last syllable), but the spelling is the same: Here belong the -<em>ăte</em> words—which are not verbs: <em>passionate</em> <strong>[insert panel] </strong>	Words ending in –<em>ive</em>, regardless of part of speech, have short <em>i</em> unless accented on the *ultima: <strong>[insert panel] </strong><em>abrasive</em>, <em>associative</em>, <em>collective, contemplative, copulative, directive, durative, elative, exclusive, expansive, expensive, factitive, festive, illative, laxative, locomotive, massive, missive, native, olive, pensive, provocative, quantitative, regulative, relative, responsive, restive, restrictive,</em> <em>restorative, sensitive, stative, transitive, unitive, </em>(no –<em>ives</em> under <em>w</em>). Long because accented on the last syllable are words like <em>contrive</em>, <em>dive</em>, <em>five, hive, knives, live </em>(adj),<em> lives </em>(n),<em> strive, wives,</em>

(5) There is an affinity between related consonants like lip sounds, (labials: <em><u>p, b f, ph, v</u></em>), tooth sounds (dentals: <em><u>t, d, th1, th2</u></em>), sibilants (c1, s1, s2, s3, sh, sch, z1, z2), velars/palatals (c2, k, g1, g2). Related words (*cognates) and different forms of the same word commonly shift between these consonants. Lip-letter shifts include <em>f/v</em> changes: <em>grief/grieve, knife/knives, half/halves, life/lives1, safe/save, wife/wives, wolf/wolves,</em> Not all singular-plural shifts go <em>f/v</em>; sometimes there is no shift: <em>café/cafes, clef/clefs, safe/safes,</em> <em>etc.</em>

There is an <em>s/t</em> affinity between cognates because the <em>s</em> sound and <em>t</em> sound are pronounced near the same place in the mouth. As a result, -<em>sis</em> nouns have a corresponding adjective (and/or noun) in –<em><u>t</u></em><em>ic</em>: <em>diagnosis/diagnostic, prosthesis/prosthetic/prosthetist</em>; –ence/-ance nouns have corresponding adjectives in –<em>t</em>: <em>eminence/eminent, imminence/imminent, sequence/sequential, dominance/dominant, romance/romantic, </em>The <em>s/t</em> affinity shows up in Greek in the shift from –<em>tt-</em> to <em>–ss-</em> (<em>thalatta/thalassa</em>, “sea”) in its classical to Hellenistic stages.


Vowel shifts may be from short to long, but there is less pattern to vowel alterations in English. (6) Dictionary forms in –<em>en</em> and other vowel-consonant terminations often follow their Latin ancentors and alter that vowel ahead of additions: <em>abdomen > abdominal, nomen > nominal, semen > seminal; femur/femoral, </em>

(7) Consonant + <em><u>y</u></em> terminations usually change the <em><u>y</u></em> to <em><u>i</u></em> before additions are made—unless successive <em><u>i</u></em>’s would result: happy > happier, but rally > rallying <strong>[insert panel]</strong>

(8) <em><u>q</u></em> is always followed by <em><u>u</u></em>: quack,<em> etc</em>.


Since custom drives usage, words that were formerly hyphenated may now considered one word for writing purposes. Hyphenation, for example, may no longer be considered necessary: <em>cooperate</em>, formerly <em>co-operate</em> or <em>coöperate</em>. Writing conventions lag behind spoken realities. For instance, <em>rain check</em> has the same accent and pitch pattern as <em>homework, rainbow, railroad, etc</em>., but dictionaries still list it as two words. About all a writer can do is consult a dictionary to see how far convention has carried this particular word in the opinion of those charged with tracking such matters.


Latin derivatives with <em>ex</em> + roots beginning in <em>s</em> drop the <em>s</em> in English; the <em>x</em> (ks) is allowed to carry the <em>s </em>sound; that is, <em>ex</em> “absorbs” the initial <em>s</em> of root: <em>execrate</em> (< <em>ex</em> + <em>sacrare</em>); <em>execute</em> (< <em>ex</em> + <em>sequi</em>); <em>exile</em> (< <em>ex</em> + <em>silium</em>); <em>expatiate</em> (< <em>ex</em> + <em>spatiari</em>); <em>expect</em> (< <em>ex</em> + <em>spectare</em>); <em>expire</em> (< <em>ex</em> + <em>spiro</em>); <em>extant</em> (< <em>ex</em> + <em>stare</em>); <em>extinct</em> (< <em>ex</em> + <em>stinguere</em>); <em>extirpate</em> (< <em>ex</em> + <em>stirps</em>); <em>exude</em> (< <em>ex</em> + <em>sudare</em>). In Latin, most of the time the <em>s</em> was separately written in compounds with <em>ex</em>.


<strong>British spelling differs from American spelling at certain points.</strong> (a) When <em>–er </em>does not refer to agent (worker) or comparative degree (stronger), British spelling sometimes uses –<em>re</em>: centre, litre, metre, theatre where the <em>er </em>is not an agent or comparative ending; (but not in <em>cancer, liver, pilfer, river, roster; brother, father, mother, sister,</em>)

(b) Some words are changed somewhat: aluminium


(in a set with *homograph, homonym, *homophone, *phonetic spelling)

(See also *epenthesis, *pronunciation, *orthography, *silent letters, *transliteration)

<strong>spelling bee</strong>: a contest based on the ability to spell English words

<strong>sphenogram</strong>: a cuneiform character with its distinctive wedge-shaped form

<strong>spiel</strong>: German for ; a somewhat ridiculing way of referring toa sales *pitch, for example.
<strong>spin</strong>: a particular way of presenting something so as to take away some negative way of looking at it. (in a set with *line)

<strong>spiritualize</strong>: to give a figurative meaning to a literal text, one not particularly intended to have that meaning

<strong>splice</strong>: what serves to connect material already presented and the next item of interest in presentation.

<strong>split infinitive</strong>: in English grammar, an infinitive construction with an adverb inserted between the sign of the infinitive (“to”) and the infinitive itself: “to quickly go.” The practice is becoming increasingly acceptable and used more naturally than putting the adverb ahead of the <em>to</em>. To the modern ear, a split infinitive has much the same sound as “splitting” the parts of a progressive verb: “We are <em>quickly</em> running out of gas.” In the nature of things, there is no reason not to use the split format since English considers the infinitive sign a separate particle from the infinitive itself. In Latin or Greek the infinitive sign was always suffixed directly to the verb as part of the same word; so it could not so easily be pushed apart to make room for an insertion.

<strong>spontaneous delivery</strong>: a form of public speaking in which the speaker structures the wording as he goes in contrast to *manuscript delivery.

<strong>spoof</strong>: a light imitation intended for good-natured fun rather than ridicule

<strong>spriant letter</strong>: a letter that stands for a fricative sound: <em>phi, theta, chi</em> (Gr); the “<em>bgdkpt</em>” Hebrew letters without <em>dagesh lene</em>.

<strong>spurious</strong>: in ancient manuscripts, a passage not part of the author’s writing, an *interpolation by a later hand. <em>Spurious</em> has to do with authenticity of authorahip, not particularly with correctness of content.

<strong>spurious writing</strong>: a writing not produced by the one who claims to have written it. Same as *pseudepigrapha.

<strong>square script</strong>: the modern Hebrew characters, which have a generally square shape, in contrast to the ancient form of the letters found in most inscriptions. Also called Aramaic square script.

<strong>squinting modifier</strong>: a kind of *double meaning, a modifier that can reasonably connect with a preceding or succeeding word: “We made the trip long before we knew how expensive it would be.” “We do business right here at home.” NT ex.

<strong>stand for</strong>: an expression used for *abbreviations, especially *acronyms

(in a set with *abbreviation, *acronym, *acrostic)

<strong>standard English</strong>: the agreed-upon form of English that is used among educated speakers and writers especially in public and formal situations. It applies to structure and written conventions.

<strong>standardized text</strong>: a text like the Hebrew Old Testament that became deliberately fixed despite variant *readings that previously existed. (in a set with *Masoretic text)

<strong>stanza</strong> (= verse)

<strong>star</strong>: same as *asterisk; used more recently with cell phones, <em>etc.</em>, for *economy of expression.

<strong>statement of faith</strong>: a summary of important convictions held by a religious group but not considered authoritative for <em>fellowship purposes</em>; it may be required for <em>leadership purposes</em> within the group or, if held in greater importance, for <em>membership purposes</em>. The last <em>purposes</em> assumes the validity of denominationalism. A statement of faith is distinguished from a creed in that a creed is authoritative for fellowship, leadership, and membership purposes.

(in a set with *creed, *scripture, *by-laws)

<strong>state-of-being verb</strong>: a verb that indicates being rather than action; another term for *copulative verb, *equative verb, *linking verb, *stative verb, *to be verb; cp. *fientive verb.

(in a set with *active verb, *middle-voice verb, *passive verb)

<strong>stative perfect</strong>: a value of the perfect tense that stresses the resulting condition of the completed action: I have it done (<em> vs.</em> I have done it); I had it done (<em> vs.</em> I had done it); I will have it done (<em> vs.</em> I will have done it). (in a set with *perfect tense)

<strong>stative verb</strong>: (1) a verb that indicates a condition rather than an action; another term sometimes used for *copulative verb, *equative verb, *linking verb, *state-of-being verb, *to be verb; cp. *fientive verb.

(2) a verb construction that indicates the condition that results from a completed action; a possible value of perfect tenses in Greek, Latin, and even English: it <em>stands </em>confirmed; I have it done (<em> vs.</em> I have done it).

The difference between stative and plain passive can be illustrated as follows: “They were married last Friday; so they were married when they got here Tuesday.”

(in a set with *active verb, *middle-voice verb, *passive verb)

<strong>stem</strong>: the constant part of a word when ending are added to indicate tense, number, gender, and the like. (in a set with *combining form, *root, *base)

<strong>stemma</strong>: a scroll that preserves an important record, as list of manuscripts of an ancient literary piece or of a genealogy, or family tree, especially of an ancient Roman family.

<strong>stenographer</strong>: a person who takes *dictation, one of the roles fulfilled by a secretary

(in a set with *amanuensis, *scribe)

<strong>stenography</strong>: the art or process of doing a stenographer’s work

<strong><em>stet</em></strong>: do not delete; an editor’s note in contrast to *dele, “remove.” [<Latin: “let it stand”]

<strong>stich</strong>: a line of writing; same as <em>stichos</em>

<strong>stichoi</strong>: the lines of an ancient text; also <em>stiches</em>

<strong>stichometry</strong>: the division of a prose text into lines of standard length and reflecting the syntactical layout of the writing, a device used prior to the development of punctuation. The counting of lines was able to identify the “Epistle of Barnabas” as in fact a title for the Book of Hebrews in the New Testament. See Westcott and Hort appendix to the commentary on the Book of Hebrews

<strong>stichomythia</strong>: Alteration between speakers of lines of verse in drama WB

<strong>stigma</strong>: same as *digamma, a Greek symbol for six.

<strong>stilted</strong>: overly formal in speech

<strong>tongue twister</strong>:

<strong>stop</strong>: a sound made by “stopping” the air flow through the oral cavity at one of the *pronunciation points. The sound may be voiceless (<em>p, t, k, h</em>[onor]) or voiced (<em>b, d, g</em>). The unvoiced stop is also called a *tenuis.

(in a set with *fricative, *nasal, *plosive, *trill)

<strong>story</strong>: an account, but also a made-up series of events that is similar to real life as in an anecdote; a lie, as in “to tell a story.”

<strong>streamlining causal distinctions</strong>: In this manner of speaking, what is allowed by a person that person is said to do. What is done indirectly is worded the same way as if it were done directly.

<strong>street talk</strong>: usually a way of speaking that implies coarse expression, the use of vulgarisms, swearing, cursing. The type of language used in impolite company.

<strong>stress</strong>: the accent put on a syllable within a word or on a word with in a set of words accomplished by increased volume, raised pitch, pausing, or a combination of these ways of drawing attention to the point of emphasis. Whereas accent is a term mostly applied to syllables within a word, stress applies to words or phrases within a sentence. Stress, or emphasis, many times sets up a contrast with parallel alternatives to the term stressed. (in a set with *accent, *emphasis)

<strong>structural meaning</strong>: consists of elaborations of basic meaning by *formal changes/additions, relationship connections (*distribution/word order/position), and oral nuancing (*intonation). Contrasts with *lexical meaning

<strong>stutter</strong>: a speech impediment somewhat more common in men than in women. In it is a tendency to say repeatedly the beginnings of words and/or to “lock up” so that the word does not come out.

<strong>stylized expression</strong>: similar to *formulaic; an expression that always takes the same form

<strong>subject</strong>: The grammatical subject of a clause is the noun/pronoun that does the action of an *active verb or the noun/pronoun the receives the action of a *passive verb.

Linking, or equative or state-of-being, verbs also have subjects that are readily identifiable most of the time. In Greek, where word order is not as crucial, linking verb sentences with a *predicate noun raise the question as to which noun is subject. The basic idea is that, regardless of word order, the subject is the more narrow reference; it is the particular while the predicate noun is the class: “The Word was God” (Jn <strong><sup>1</sup></strong>:1). <em>The Word</em> is the subject while <em>God</em> is the class—deity. The issue is not so much which noun appears first—even in English: “An extreme form of escape is suicide” means the same as “An extreme form of escape suicide is” or “Suicide is an extreme form of escape.” Which is said first may be for emphasis or may be because it links up more easily with the preceding context to put the predicate nominative earlier rather than later in the sentence.

The principle of narrower reference underlies other rules for identifying subject <em> vs.</em> predicate nominative: a proper noun is subject over a common noun; an articular noun (identity) is subject <em> vs.</em> an anarthrous one (quality); a pronoun is subject <em> vs.</em> a noun.

<strong>subject complement</strong>: in English, Greek, and Latin, a complementing modifier—adjective or participle—placed after the verb and harking back to the subject: “He was released from jail free from all charges.” “This man does not cease speaking words against this holy place and the law” (Acts<strong> 6</strong>:13). (in a set with *objective complement; *complementary infinitive)

<strong>subjective genitive: </strong>In Greek and Latin, subjective genitive involves a noun tied to an action noun by the genitive case. The genitive-case noun is the subject of the action in the other noun: “the love of God,” meaning “the love by God,” that is, “the love that God has for me.” Subjective genitive contrasts with *objective genitive, where the genitive-case noun receives the action: “the love <em>for</em> God,” as in “the love that I have for God.” English can use an “of” construction for both ideas. Note the situation in Rom <strong><sup>8</sup></strong>:35, (37), 39.

(in a set with *objective genitive)

<strong>subjunctive mood</strong>: a verb mood/mode that is used when the action is somehow uncertain or contingent on another action. It “supposes” action. Because of its contingency element, most usages of the subjunctive mood are in dependent constructions: purpose clauses, future conditional clauses, <em>etc.</em>.

(in a set with *indicative, * imperative, *optative (Gr), *infinitive mood)

<strong>subordinate clause</strong>: Also called *dependent clause in contrast to *insubordinate clause/*dependent clause. A subordinate clause does not stand alone because it does not express a complete thought by itself.

<strong>subscribe/subscription</strong>: a commitment to purchase a periodical for a given length of time.

<strong>subscript</strong>: a symbol placed beneath the line of writing. In Hellenistic Greek, the iota was written as a subscript with a long *vowel to form an *improper diphthong. (in a set with *superscript, *exponent)

<strong>substantive</strong>: A form that can fill a noun slot: *nouns, *pronouns, substantive *adjectives, *gerunds, *infinitives. Any *word used as a word is a substantive regardless of its normal usage as verb, preposition, <em>etc.</em> As replacements for nouns, substantives appear as *subjects of the sentence, *direct objects, *indirect objects, objects of *prepositions, *vocative nouns in *direct address.

(In a set with *substantive adjective, *word used as a word; *gerund, *infinitive, *noun, *pronoun,)

<strong>substantive adjective</strong>: a word that is normally used as an *adjective but in a given instance has no accompanying *noun and fills a noun slot. English substantive adjectives do not use pluralizing *morphemes (even when plural in meaning), can take a plural verb, have <em>the </em>ahead of them, and can require plural subsequent pronouns: <em>the halt, the happy, the ignorant, the lonely, the maim, the meek, the poor </em>[in spirit]<em>, the pure </em>[in heart]<em>, the sad, the strong, the weak</em>, <em>etc.</em>; also <em>in the singular, in the plural,</em> <em>etc.</em>

Latin and Greek also have substantive adjectives both in the singular and the plural. In translation, it is often useful to add <em>one/ones, thing/things,</em> <em>people, men.</em> Occasionally English may have a noun that can cover the original substantive: <em>foolish-ones</em> becomes <em>fools</em>. A word like the Latin <em>amicus</em> (friend) may be separately listed in a lexicon as an adjective (friendly). It is a matter of judgment whether a word is predominantly an adjective used here in a noun slot, or whether it is so frequently used both ways that, regardless of original usage, it is now considered either part of speech depending on the case.

(in a set with *declension, *noun)

<strong>substory </strong>

<strong>subtext</strong>: the underlying personality of a character in a drama as implied by the text

<strong>subtitle</strong>: a secondary title that appears with the basic title and adds clarifying information to the main title.

<strong>subvocal</strong>: not audible as perhaps in lips movements

<strong>suffix</strong>: a word ending that usually is not also a free-standing form; also called a *postfix. Suffixes create new words by adding another element of meaning to an already existing base, for creating related words of a different part of speech. Suffixes are forms that appear only at the ends of words even though related forms with the same meaning may occur elsewhere in words: -<em>stomy</em> is a suffix for mouth or opening, and -<em>stomato</em>- can be a prefix or combining form that appears earlier in words.

As with ful words, suffixes (and prefixes) can be compounds. For example, -ectomy is a compound of –ec-, out, -tom-, cut, and –y, a nominalizing termination. Together they make a suffix for excision, which can be added onto a host of roots for body parts that can be removed: <em>gastrectomy, nephrectomy,</em> splenectomy, <em>etc.</em>

As with *prefixes, there is a question about drawing the line between suffixes and the final forms of morphemes in compound words. Terminal elements like <em>–algia, -odynia, -emia, -logy, -pathy, -cele, -scope</em> occur frequently, and so have been included here but in italics to distinguish them from endings that manipulate the internal meanings of roots (in contrast to adding meanings that are not the outgrowths of the preceding roots).  ???Likewise missing from this list are those inflectional terminations that change tense, person, and voice on verbs as well as number on nouns. Included, then, are additions that change the *part of speech and otherwise manipulate the meaning of words. <strong>further examples</strong>
