List of Greek suffixes: -<em>akis</em> (multiplicity), -<em>aridion</em> (a diminutive of the root to which it is attached), -<em>(a)rion</em> (diminutive perhaps with endearment), -<em>eia</em> (< -euoo v. > abstract n.), -<em>eion </em>(place), <em>-es-</em>[1st decl] (act ptc fem), -<em>eus</em> (agent), -<em>then</em> (directional toward the speaker), -<em>ia</em> (quality n. < adj), -<em>ianos</em> (n. a *patronymic for one who is a follower of, or related in some way to, the proper name to which the suffix is attached), -<em>ikos </em>(adj < n. -<em>inos</em> (adj < n.<em>-ion</em> (diminutive), -<em>ios</em> (adj. < n),  -<em>idzoo </em>(verb), -<em>is</em> (multiplicity), <em>-ma</em> (result), -<em>mos</em> (name of an action from -dzoo verbs), <em>-menos</em> (mid/pas ptc), -<em>monee </em>(> n. = -ness), <em>-nt</em>[3rd decl] (act ptc mas/neut), -<em>ou</em> (adverb of clace), <em>-s-(</em>fut), <em>-sis</em> (abstract action; n < v.), -<em>sunee</em> (qualaity n. < adj), <em>-tatos</em> (superlative degree with adj/adv), <em>-teros</em> (comparative degree on adj/adv), -<em>teer</em> (agent), <em>-teerion</em> (place of), <em>-tees</em> (agent < -idzoo v.), -<em>tees </em>(quality n. < adj), -<em>toor</em> (agent), -<em>t(e)os</em> (usually passive adj > v. implying capability, possibility, necessity), -<em>oo</em> (adverb of location relative to another point), -<em>oov</em> (place), -<em>oos </em>(<adj > adv.; gen neut pl with nu to sigma),

List of Latin suffixes: <em>alis/aris/anus/icus</em> (adj., not worthy to be believed), -<em>bilis/bile</em> (adj., able to be, able to, worthy to be), -<em>ce</em> (intensive enclitic), -<em>ianus</em> (n. a *patronymic for one who is a follower of, or related in some way to, that proper name), -<em>ia</em> (n., quality), -<em>ium</em> (n., action or result of action), -<em>ne</em> (on first word of a clause to indicate asking an informational question; -<em>osus</em> (adj., full of), -<em>or</em> (n., action or result of action), -<em>que</em>, meaning “and”; -<em>tas</em> (ta[ts], n., quality), -<em>tia</em> (quality), -<em>tio</em>[ns]/<em>sio</em>[ns] (n., action or result of action), <em>-tor/-sor/-trix</em> (n., agent), -<em>tudo</em>[ns] (n., quality), -<em>ve</em> (“or”;)

List of Hebrew suffixes

(in a set with *epenthesis, *prefix, * infixes, *morpheme, *postfix, *separable suffix, *inseparable suffixes)

<strong>superlative</strong>: the highest *degree of an *adjective or *adverb

<strong>superscript</strong>: a symbol written above the rest of the word or formula

(in a set with *subscript, *superscription)

<strong>superscription: </strong>what is “written above”; used in reference to the accusation on Jesus’ cross, the crime for which he was crucified: “King of the Jews” (Lk <strong><sup>23</sup></strong>:38).

(in a set with *subscript, *superscript)

<strong>supine</strong>: a type of defective verbal noun in Latin formed on the perfect passive participle in mainly the accusative an ablative cases.

<strong>suppletive verb</strong>: a verb that gets its principal parts from more than one root. English has <em>go-went-gone.</em>

Latin has <em>fero-tuli-latus</em>. Greek has <em>erchomai-eleusomai-eelthon; horaoo, opsomai, eidon, heoraka,hooramai, oophtheen; pheroo, oisoo, eenegka.</em>

(in a set with *composite verb, *defective verb, *deponent verb, *regular verb)

<strong>suppositive</strong>: a conjunction that introduces a supposition

<strong>surd</strong>: voiceless sound, one in which the vocal cords do not vibrate, as in a whisper or with <em>p,f, ph, t, th1, c/k, ch1, h1</em> (honor), <em>s, sh. </em>

(in a set with *voiceless, *voiced)

<strong>surname</strong>: (1) the “last” name, the *given name, the family name that identifies the persons as immediate relatives; (2)

(in a set with *given name, *maiden name, *nee, *nickname)

<strong>surrejoinder</strong>: a reply to a previous *rejoinder, as may take place ina formal discussion of a significant issue, perhaps over a series of issues in a scholarly journal

(in a set with *rejoinder)

<strong>survey</strong>: an overview of an area of information

<strong>suspended hyphen</strong>: a hyphen with a space after it as when there are two first parts to a hyphenated expression connected by a coordinate conjunction: <em>tall- or short-lettered signee; one- and two-digit numbers</em>

<strong>suspended rhyme</strong>: repeats only the final sound: hit, lot. (get other examples)

<strong>swear</strong>: (n a set with *curse/cuss, *oath)

<strong>sweet talk</strong>: to talk nicely and encouragingly to get someone to like you and get them to do something, used especially of a man to a woman or a young person to a grandmotherly type.

<strong>syllable</strong>: a unit of pronunciation within a word consisting of a vowel sound (a-/-au), or a vowel sound (vowel or diphthong) and a consonant (or consonant cluster: <em>as/oil/ask/ails</em>), or a consonant (or consonant cluster) and a vowel (or diphthong) and a consonant (or consonant cluster (<em>sap/gait/gaits/shaft</em>).

In Greek, syllables were considered long or short depending on whether they had a long or short vowel in them. In Latin, a long syllable is one with a long vowel or a syllable with more than one consonant after the vowel.

(in a set with *syllabification; *monosyllable, *disyllable, *trisyllable, *multisyllabic; *parisyllable)

<strong>syllabism</strong>: the use of characters to represent syllable rather than individual sounds in writing

<strong>syllabic augment</strong>: On the front of Greek verbs beginning with consonants, syllabic augment helped mark the *secondary tenses of the *indicative mood by adding an epsilon.  See also *temporal augment. (in a set with *augment, *temporal augment).

<strong>syllabification</strong>: dividing a written word into its pronounceable units so as to know where to break it at the end of a written line, for example. Greek and Latin syllables consist of a consonant or pronounceable consonant cluster followed by the vowels that appear before the next consonant. A series of vowels that do not form diphthongs are separate syllables. Two same consonants are divided, and identifiable prefixes and suffixes are disjoined from the root.

Beyond a few basic patterns, English syllabification is rather complex so as to necessitate looking up the word in a dictionary. As in Greek and Latin, English words are divided between two same consonants, after certain identifiable prefixes and before standard suffixes.

<strong>syllabus</strong>: a document in a college class that gives information about the class: purpose, assignments, grading scheme, dates of meeting, outline of the materials, <em>etc</em>.

<strong>syllepsis</strong>: a construction in which a word governs two or more words but agrees with only one in number, gender, and case or has a different meaning with one than with the other: (Webster) Over Thanksgiving he went to Arrow Head Stadium and caught a game and a cold. Can be applied to situations in inflected languages where one adjective modifies two nouns of different gender: <em>multi </em>[m] <em>viri</em> [m] <em>et feminae</em> [f], “many men and women” (Latin).

<strong>symbol</strong>: a character that represent and idea or sound

<strong>syncopate</strong>: to shorten a word by dropping out an element in the middle; a kind of *contraction

<strong>syncopated nouns</strong>: nouns that have a vowel in the nominative singular ultima and lose it elsewhere: <em>meet</em><em><u>ee</u></em><em>r/meetros, pat</em><em><u>ee</u></em><em>r/patros, thugat</em><em><u>ee</u></em><em>r/thugatros</em>,

<strong>syncope</strong>: the dropping out of a middle part of a word: <em>bos’n </em>(boatswain), <em>gunwale</em> (pronounced <em>gunnel</em>), <em>nor’easter</em>,

<strong>synonym</strong>: a word that has approximately the same meaning as another. Strictly speaking, no two words in a language are absolute synonyms. They may differ in their connotations—their “feel”—or in the subject matter where they are used. (in a set with *antonym)

<strong>synopsis</strong>: a summary. In instructional grammars, the synopsis of a verb is a representative form—like third person singular or first plural—from each place it occurs in the verb system.

(in a set with *conspectus, *model)

<strong>synoptic gospels</strong>: the first three gospels in contrast to John’s gospel. So-called because the three “see” the subject matter “together.”

<strong>syntax</strong>: the connection between parts of a sentence , parts of an expression (cp. *grammar)

<strong>synthesis</strong>/<strong>synthetic</strong>: the use of inflectional affixes to indicate syntactical relationships in contrst to using *distribution (placement in the sentence) for that purpose, as in English.

(in a set with *thesis and *antithesis; *distribution)

<strong>syntax</strong>: the relationship between words in a sentence. That relationship may be indicated by word order, as in English, by inflection of individual words, or by a combination of these possibilities. An example of the predominance of word order in English is the possibility of changing a word’s normal part of speech by changing its placement in the sentence. “Savvy employees position themselves for advancement in the company” (A -<em>tion</em> word, originating as a noun, is here used as a verb). “Those biology dissections <em>weird</em> me out” (adjective shift to verb).

<strong>syntopicon</strong>: a scholarly reference work, alphabetically arranged by topic through key words, that traces a concept through many authors over time. References into the original works give access to crucial passages in the discussion.  Note <em>The Great Ideas: A Syntopicon of Great Books of the Western World, </em>2 vols. Encyclopӕdia Britannica, Inc., 1952. As a work that deals with ideas, a syntopicon differs from a dictionary or lexicon, which deal with the meaning of words; and from an encyclopedia by being limited to concepts, ideas, beliefs in contrast to geography and history.

(in a set with *dictionary, *lexicon; *encyclopedia)

<strong>syzygy</strong>: the combining of two *feet into one metrical foot in classical prosody WB

<strong>table of contents</strong>: an entry near the front of a longer work that shows in sequence the chapters or parts of the contents that follow.

<strong>tag line</strong>: a *punch line, a final line in a piece that drives home the point of the piece; a slogan for a product or company that serves that same purpose: “and that’s the cold truth” (Alka Seltzer); “Let’s build something together’ (Lowe’s).

<strong>tagmeme: </strong>

<strong>take-off</strong>: Beginning with some already-known wording or idea, a new author elaborates or adapts it his own purposes

<strong>tale</strong> a story not to be taken too seriously; perhaps even a lie, as in “he is telling a tale.”

<strong>talk</strong>: loose, general word for communicate, verbalize. <em>Talk</em> can be used in a *pejorative way to refer to words spoken as disconnected from internal conviction: “That’s just talk.”

<strong>talkathon</strong>: a playful take-off on <em>marathon</em> applied to talking, hence, a long, drawn-out discussion or presentation, but not necessarily as a delay tactic (in a set with *filibuster)

<strong>talking point</strong>: items on a subject that are made part of a presentation or discussion. The implication can be that an over-all impression, or spin, can be created by the choice of elements included or omitted.

<strong>Talmud</strong>: Hebrew word for “instruction” technically applied to the collection of rabbinic writings so named and consisting of the Gemara () and Mishnah (), which then serves as the basis for much of traditional Jewish law.

<strong>targum</strong>: Hebrew word for “translated”; hence, a translation. It is applied to the Aramaic *translations/*paraphrases (and quasi-commentaries) of the Hebrew Old Testament text. They arose in the time when the masses (the <em>hoi polloi</em> [“the many], the <em>am ha’aretz</em>, [“the people of the land”]) did not readily understand classical Hebrew.

<strong>tattle</strong>: to “tell” on someone who has done something not otherwise known to the one/s responsible for the situation; implies relatively minor things that go on between young kids

<strong>tattle tail</strong>: one who tattles

<strong>tau</strong>: nineteenth letter of the Greek alphabet: τ/Τ.

<strong>taunt</strong> to jeer someone especially in order to get a response out of him

<strong>tautology</strong>: saying the same thing twice with different words: widow woman () [Gr ta + auta = tauta]

<strong>tautonym</strong>: the same name twice, a terminology in zoology where the genus and species name is the same

<strong>tav</strong>: the twenty-second letter of the Hebrew alphabet. Also taw: ת

<strong>taw</strong>: the twenty-second letter of the Hebrew alphabet. Also Tilgath-pilneser: ת

<strong>taxeme</strong>: a grammatical feature that does not carry meaning by itself; its contextual placement is what WB check out

<strong>taxonomy</strong>: a system of naming items in a group as exemplified in the scientific system of two-word designation for flora and fauna (plants and animals; living things).

<strong>teach</strong> (<em> vs.</em> preach)

<strong>technical term</strong>: a term that is more limited in its application, given its derivational meaning, than it is in general usage. Rain check is an expression that originated from outdoor settings—like baseball—where the ticket was not able to be used because of inclement weather. It is now applied to a wide range of situations where something is not able to be used or done, as in a rain check for some sale item the store has run out of. Organ crawl was first used for “crawling” around the pipes of a pipe organ to see how they were assembled, <em>etc.</em> Now it is used for organ inspections where no “crawling” needs to be involved.

(in a set with *archaism, *buzz word, *catch phrase, *colloquialism, *curse word, *formal term, *informal term, *oath, *obsolete, *provincialism, *regionalism, *slang, *vulgarism)

<strong>telecommunication</strong>: communication at a distance made possible by electronic devices—radio, television, computers, telegraph, telephone

<strong>teleconference</strong>: a conference between people separated from each other but united by electronic device in order to carry on business

<strong>telegram</strong>: a brief note sent by telegraph to inform someone

<strong>telegraph</strong>: a device for sending and receiving messages by electronic impulses

<strong>telegraphy</strong>: the process of using telegraph communication

<strong>telephone</strong>: an electronic device for sending sound messages by impulses through land lines instead of radio waves

<strong>temporal augment</strong>: The front of Greek verbs were “augmented” to help mark secondary tenses of the indicative mood. On verbs beginning with vowels, temporal augment basically lengthened the vowel, following standard patterns of lengthening: alpha to eta, omicron to omega, iota to long iota, and upsilon to long upsilon. Naturally, initial eta and omega words could not be lengthened further. Epsilon was lengthened either by going to eta or to an epsilon-iota diphthong. See also *syllabic augment. (in a set with *augment, *syllabic augment)

<strong>temporal clause</strong>: a type of dependent adverbial clause used to mean when, while, after, before, and the like. Latin formula: cum + indicative; Gr = hote <em>etc.</em>,

<strong>Ten Commandments, The</strong>: the listing of God’s commandment given through Moses as found in Ex <strong><sup>20</sup></strong>:1-17 and Deut <strong><sup>5</sup></strong>:1-21.

<strong>tendential imperfect</strong>: Among the uses of the imperfect tense in Greek is the tendency for an action to occur. That may be the point of the imperfect in Acts 5:13, “But of the rest, no one tended-to-be-bold enough to-join them.” The next verse talks about a multitude of believers—men and women—being added to the Lord.

<strong>tendential result: </strong>the result in Greek grammar that tended to come as a result of action in the main verb. (in a set with *actual result). In distinction from *actual result, it may be translated “so as to” rather than “so that.”

<strong>tense:</strong> (in a set with *person, *number, *mood, *voice, and *aspect as possible features of verb forms)

<strong>tense: </strong>Tenses may not be identified in one language <em> vs.</em> another one even though the same ideas can be expressed in either. Tenses are traditionally identified by form. Latin and Greek do not ordinarily identify progressive present (as English can) because there is no distinctive wording or verbal morpheme corresponding to that idea (Greek can construct a periphrastic present past and future by using a finite form of “to be” plus a participles). With the extensive use of *circumlocution and *auxiliary verbs, English can nuance progression (are/are/was/were + __ing), emphasis (do/does/did + __), ingression (began-to-). Other languages often depend on context or the hearer’s independent understanding of the subject to *infer these kinds of distinctions.

Future perfect

Futuristic present: In English, the present tense can be used in place of the future, especially in certain if-clauses: “ If you go downtown tomorrow, please pay the mortgage.”

<strong>tense formative</strong>: See *tense sign.

<strong>tense sign</strong>: some element that indicates present, past, future, <em>etc.</em>, times for the action on verbs. Also called *tense formative. English uses an auxiliary word prior to the verb itself: will (and shall) work. In Greek and Latin the tense signs appear between root and personal endings: <em>b</em> for future in Latin, <em>s</em> for future in Greek; -<em>ba</em>- for the imperfect in Latin; -(<em>k</em>)<em>a</em>- as the tense sign for perfect active in Greek. Greek—and Latin less uniformly—also uses *reduplication on the front of the word as part of the sign for the perfect active tense. Greek has (theta)-eta as the main tense sign for aorist passive and (theta)-eta-sigma for future passive.

In Latin, the sign of the perfect is often reduplication and/or vowel changes within the root as well as additions to the end of the root just before a distinct personal ending set. *Tense formatives for the perfect active include –v/i for most verbs in –<em>are</em>, -u/i, -s/i, loss of a root nasal(?)

<strong>term</strong>: a word

<strong>terminal form</strong>: the shape a letter takes at the end of a word in contrast to its form elsewhere: Greek uses a different form of <em>sigma</em> at the end of a word; Hebrew does the same thing with five of its letters: . A similar thing happens with English words that end in <em>y</em> when vowel suffixes are added. The <em>y</em> is often changed to <em>i </em>before the additions are made even though there is no change in sound: vary > varied.

<strong>terminology</strong>: the words chosen to express an idea, particularly for a given subject. Often the terminology for a subject has been lifted out of the general vocabulary and specialized to the needs of explanation for the subject at hand. <strong>time</strong>: time references can be (1) time when, references to time as a fact. It can be diagrammed as a point < . > : “She slept <em>that night</em>.” (2) *Time during, or time within which, indicates the time frame during which some act or event occurred. It can be diagrammed as ( . . . ): “She slept <em>during the day</em>.” (3) *Extent of time indicates how long some act or event lasted. It can be diagrammed as as an arrow : “She slept (for) one night.”

Time words are the singular and plural of words like <em>second, minute, hour, day, week, fortnight, month, year, decade, score, century, millennium; </em>dates like<em> ides</em>; <em>Monday</em>, <em>etc.</em>; <em>January</em>, <em>etc.</em>; general words like <em>time</em> (for a long time).

Time may be indicated by nouns, nouns in prepositional phrases, or adverbs (<em>later, right away, sometime, soon, today, tonight, tomorrow, yesterday</em>)

(in a set with *time during/time within which, *time when, *extent of time)

<strong>time during</strong>: In Greek, time during is covered by the genitive case on the time word without the need of a preposition. <em>Dia</em> + G is also used: Acts<strong> 5</strong>:19.

In Latin time during is covered by the ablative case: “he worked <em>during the night</em>.” Same as *time within which. Latin does not formally distinguish *time when from *time during, covering both by the ablative case on the time word.

(in a set with *time, *time when, *extent of time)

<strong>time when</strong>: In Greek, time when is covered by the dative case on the time word. In Latin, it is covered by the ablative case: “He works <em>at night</em>.” English can use <em>in, on,</em> or <em>at</em> ahead of the time indicator, but often there is no preceding preposition. With dates and times, <em>in, on, </em>or <em>at</em> normally occurs: “He came back from Georgia <em>on</em> Moνday/<em>on</em> the twelfth/<em>in</em> September.” “He went to word <em>at</em> noon.” But there is no preposition necessarily in “he came home (<em>on</em>) Monday afternoon.”

(in a set with *time, *time during/*time within which, *extent of time)

<strong>time within which</strong>: same as time during

<strong>tip</strong>: an every-day term for an addition either before or after the *root of a word; a *prefix or *suffix.

<strong>title: </strong>a statement of ownership, particularly for non-landed property like cars. The “label” put over a writing for identification purposes

(in a set with *writ, *deed) (in a set with *entitled, *heading)

<strong>tittle</strong>: a little horn (<em>kepaia</em>; diminutive of <em>keras</em>) used originally to indicate small, perhaps variable or optional, part of a letter like the horns (flourishes, *serifs) on Hebrew letters (Mt <strong><sup>5</sup></strong>:18 = Lk <strong><sup>16</sup></strong>:17). Broadened somewhat to include diacritical marks in the spelling of some words such as accent marks, or to indicate vowel marks, or to refer to the dot on an <em>i.</em>

(in a set with *jot; *serif)

<strong>transformational generative grammar</strong>: an analysis of grammar that highlights the patterns by which one grammatical structure can be changed into another as with statement into question, participial phrase into a *relative clause, *direct discourse into *indirect discourse, *direct question into *indirect question,

<strong>transitional form/expression</strong>: an expression set off in commas that connects what has been said with what will be said. Transitional forms differ from conjunctions in that they make conceptual connection more than grammatical connection: <em>accordingly, as a result, consequently, hence</em>;

<em>otherwise, so, then, therefore, thus; additionally, also, besides, furthermore, in addition, moreover, too, what is more; </em>

<em>	afterward, at first, at the same time, finally, first, first of all, for now, for the time being, in conclusion, in the first place, in time, in turn, later on, meanwhile, next, respectively, second, then, to begin with; </em>

<em>	admittedly, all the same, anyway, at any rate, be that as it may, even so, however, in any case, in any event, nevertheless, still, this fact notwithstanding; to be sure</em>

<em>	by the by, by the way, incidentally; </em>

<strong>transition</strong>: passing from one topic to another, from one part of presentation to another (*splice)

<strong>transitive verb</strong>: a verb that has a direct object in contrast to one that does not. While some words may always be used transitively, transitiveness has to be figured on a case-by-case basis. <em>Work </em>is typically in*intransitive: “He worked yesterday”; but it can be transitive: “The point guard worked the clock so the team would get the last shot of the half.” Consequently, a dictionary can give only a general verdict on whether a verb is transitive or intransitive.

Some words may change meaning when used one way or the other. “He dranks the water”; but “his wife does not know that he drinks on business trips.” As an intransitive verb, <em>drink </em>means drink alcohol.

(in a set with *intransitive verbs; *linking verbs/*equative verb/*to be verb/*copulative verb, *fientive verb)

<strong>translatable</strong>: ideally, able to be expressed in another language without loss or addition of *meaning/*nuance/*connotation; a feature of one language that must be figured on a per-language basis with each other language since something might be translatable into one language but not into another.

(in a set with *untranslatable)

<strong>translation</strong>: <em>Translation</em> means transferring the ideas from one language to another. Render is another word for <em>translate.</em> A translation can refer to the whole body of a work while <em>rendering</em> looks at specific texts, passages, <em>etc.</em>.

Since languages differ so much in how they operate, it is frequently difficult to get everything in the one transferred over into the other. An African language, for example, has a tag on its verbs that indicates whether an action is legitimate. Imagine the difficulty that factor presents if the other language has no information on that point.  Consequently, things are said to “be lost in translation.”

Language is a matter of forms (words, word parts, and word combinations) used to convey meanings (*referents). Since meaning and form are conventional (not inherently necessary) and since forms and referents are usually not one-to-one, no two languages are likely to choose the same forms for exactly the same meanings. The translator’s must match up as well as possible the overlapping circles that exist across the two languages between words and expressions that are closest to each other.

There is a two-step device that helps keep the meaning close to the original and yet allows it to be idiomatic. Step 1 parallels the wording of the new language as closely as possible to the original one. Step 2 rewords it in a way a speaker would say it. Ideally the shift from step 1 to step 2 does not add lose content—a task more difficult than it might seem.

Distinctions relevant to translation include the difference between translation and transliteration. <em>Translation</em> deals with ideas; <em>transliteration</em> deals with symbols. Transliteration writes the sounds of one language in the alphabet designed of another.

A distinction needs to be drawn between translation and truth. So to speak, translation deals not with truth but with translation; it simply represents in another language the original writer’s intended ideas. The translator does not have to believe what he is translating; he simply has to translate it. In a similar vein, it is not the translator’s prerogative to make the text say what he thinks is true or what he would have said. This fundamental character of the translator’s work has been seriously breached by inclusive-language translations of the Bible. In the interests of getting the Bible to say what feminist theoreticians want it to say, the text is not allowed, for instance, to say <em>He</em> in reference to God because that is not considered true. It is not the role of the biblical translator to “correct” the content to his own understanding. If in commentary, preaching, or teaching a “spokesperson” wants to make explanatory notations to the effect that male/female is not a variable applicable to God, that is another thing; but the text itself needs to be left alone.

There is also a consideration of translation and interpretation. Good translation depends on proper understanding of the author’s intended meaning. Indeed, the Greek word for <em>interpret</em> and <em>translate</em> is the same: (<em>di/meth</em>)<em>ermeeneuoo</em>. If, for some reason, the meaning is ambiguous or unknowable, re-expressing that meaning in the new language is unavoidably inadequate. About all a translator can do is to make a word-for-word parallel accompanied by an *asterisk and marginal note that explain the difficulty and the options. In translating ancient texts, scholars can be hampered by vocabulary not known from elsewhere (*<em>hapax legomena</em>) or by words used in technical or otherwise unique ways that cannot be deciphered with confidence. At a higher level, the ideas expressed by words may not be retrievable because they address items, customs, or beliefs lost in antiquity. Indeed, the original writer may say something that would have been unclear even to a fellow native speaker or something capable of more than one interpretation. In all these circumstances, the resultant translation should be as precise or unclear as the original itself.

Finally, a difference can be maintained between translation and *paraphrase. A paraphrase approaches the enterprise in a way that reflects how the “translator” would have worded the matter from scratch. Of course, there are degrees of “looseness.” The danger is that the farther the translator moves from conveying the ideas in the author’s original terms, the easier it is to bend the ideas—however unconsciously—in the direction of his own thinking. Correctness, however, is not achieved by maintaining word-for-word parallel with the original. *Connotation particularly is lost or gained in moving from formal equivalence to paraphrase.

<em>A</em> translation is also called a *version.

(in a set with *pony, *transliteration, *translation types, *version)

<strong>translation types </strong>

word-for-word

wooden

formal equivalence

“literal”

paraphrastic

*pony

loose

functional equivalent

<strong>transliteration</strong> means transferring the symbols/letters for one language into the letters of another—using the letters of one *alphabet to represent the sounds of a different alphabet.

Greek transliteration into English.

There is some difference between the way Greek letters are spelled phonetically in English letters and the way English *derivatives from Greek words are spelled. That is evident with <em>upsilon, </em>which has derivatives spelled with <em>y</em> instead of <em>u</em>: <em>psychee</em>, rather than <em>psuchee.</em> Derivatives often use <em>c</em> for <em>kappa</em> instead of <em>k</em>: <em>cacophony</em> (“bad sound”) from <em>kakos</em> + <em>phonee</em>. But note <em>leu</em><em><u>k</u></em><em>o-</em> for <em>leukos</em> (white). The diphthong <em>ai</em> has undergone a pattern of <em>ai</em> < <em>ae</em> < <em>e. </em>Some of today’s words represent one stage of that development: <em>arch</em><em><u>ae</u></em><em>ology</em> (<em>archaios</em>, “old”); others represent a further stage: <em>encyclop</em><em><u>e</u></em><em>dia</em>.

A similar pattern is observed with <em>oi</em> < <em>oe</em> < <em>e</em>.

Hebrew transliteration

Hebrew transliteration into Greek.

The transliteration problem shows itself in the Bible where Hebrew names have been transliterated into Greek and then transliterated from Greek into English. All three alphabets lack the ability to represent adequately the range of sounds in the other two languages. In the older translations like the King James, Hebrew names were standardized within the Old Testament, but in the New Testament those same names were transliterated into English as based on their Greek spellings instead of being matched up consistently with the original ones. For instance, Greek had no <em>h</em> sound except in initial position, hence: <em>Barachias </em>(<em>Berechiah</em>), <em>Elias (Elijah), Isaias</em> (<em>Isaiah</em>), <em>Jeremias</em> (<em>Jeremiah</em>), <em>Jonas</em> (<em>Jonah</em>), <em>Zacharias </em>(Zechariah), <em>etc.</em> Then there were <em>Osee </em>(<em>Hosea</em>)<em>, </em> Newer translations have been avoiding this unnecessary difference between Old Testament and New Testament spellings of the same name.

(in a set with *translation)

<strong>transpose</strong>: reverse the sequence of items or letters (*metathesis). In Hebrew’s hithpael stem, if the root begins with a sibilant after the <em>hith-</em> prefix, <em>tav</em> and sibilant reverse, or transpose, to avoid a perceived difficult combination of sounds. Akin to “besgetti” in a child’s mispronunciation of <em>spaghetti</em>. The dental-sibilant sequence causes “problems” in Greek and Latin as well. In a set with eliding the dental, dropping the sibilant, throwing away both the dental and sibilant, interfixing a vowel.

<strong>treatise</strong>: a writing that “treats” some subject, a serious written study

<strong>treaty</strong>: an agreement between political entities

<strong>tribute</strong>: a favorable public statement about a person’s character, achievements

<strong>trigraph</strong>: three letters that form one sound like <em>sch</em> in <em>schist, schnookl, schuss, </em>the German <em>schule, </em>or Hebrew <em>schwa</em>. (in a set with *digraph)

<strong>trilingual</strong>: in three languages or able to speak three languages. The *superscription on Jesus’ cross was trilingual (Jn <strong><sup>19</sup></strong>:20), reflecting the fact that Latin, Hebrew/Aramaic, and Greek were currently used in the land.

(in a set with *bilingual, *monolingual)

<strong>triliteral root</strong>: three-letter *root, a designation for the three-consonant form of most Hebrew verbs and many of its other words.

(in a set with *quadriliteral root, *weak verbs)

<strong>trill</strong>: a series of fast contacts between the tongue and a pronunciation point—glottal, velar, dental, labial

<strong>trilled </strong><strong><em>r</em></strong>: (in a set with *flapped <em>r</em>)

<strong>trilogy</strong>: a set of three related works (in a set with *tetralogy)

<strong>trimeter</strong>: a line of verse containing three metrical feet

(in a set with *tetrameter, *pentameter, *hexameter)

<strong>triolet</strong>: an eight-line stanza with rhyme scheme <em>abaaabab, </em>in which the last two lines duplicate the first two lines

<strong>triple-space</strong>: having two blank lines between lines of type; typed on every third line

<strong>triplet</strong> (in a set with *couplet)

<strong>triplicate</strong>: in three copies, an expression that was used when typewriters were in general use and the typist made two carbon copies at the same time as the original

(in a set with *duplicate)

<strong>Trishagion</strong>: Greek form; same meaning as the *Tersanctus  (Latin), the “three holy’s” that appear in Isaiah 6:3 and repeated again in Rev <strong><sup>4</sup></strong>:8 in address to God.

<strong>trisyllable/trisyllabic</strong>: a word having three syllable

<strong>trochee</strong>: a metrical foot with a long-short, stressed-unstressed sequence of syllables

(in a set with *anapest)

<strong>trope</strong>: a figure of speech

(in a set with *allegory, *euphemism, *hendiadys. *irony, *metaphor, *metonymy, *simile, *zeugma)

<strong>trove</strong>: a collection of valuable items in a certain category, as in a trove of epigraphic texts

<strong>truism</strong>: something patently true, obviously true; something anybody would know.

<strong>tsade</strong>: the eighteenth letter of the Hebrew alphabet: צ with ץ as its terminal form: Also

<em>sade/sadhe</em>.

<strong>tutor</strong>: an assistant that helps a student that is having difficulty in a specific course.

<strong>tutorial</strong>: a medium by which individualized instruction is given on some topic

<strong>typo</strong>: short for typographical error, a mistake made at a keyboard or type-set machine.

<strong>typology</strong>: the study of comparisons between figures of different times in history. In scriptural study, it is wise to identify entities as types of later figures only if scripture makes that comparison. Otherwise, there is no boundary for using something earlier as a kind of prediction of something later. (in a set with *interpretation; predictive prophecy)

<strong>unaccented</strong>: not having attention drawn to it by increased volume, raised pitch, or pausing

<strong>unaccented vowels</strong>: unaccented vowels in English tend toward sheva—like the e in because, a phenomenon illustrated by the words calendar, miner, fakir, minor, murmur, martyr.

<strong>umlaut</strong>: a horizontal double dot over a German vowel to indicate *rounded pronunciation; equal to an <em>e</em> written after a vowel. An umlaut looks like a *dieresis, another kind of *diacritical mark.

<strong>unattainable wish</strong>: a wish that refers to present or past time. Since the situation is already past or already in existence, it cannot be attained. In classical Greek the idea was covered by ei gar or either plus the imperfect or aorist indicative or by ophelon plus present or aorist infinitive for present or past unattainable wishes respectively.

<strong>uncials: </strong>“inch-high” letters (in a set with *minuscules: cp. *capitals):

<strong>underlining</strong>: putting a line under words to emphasize them or in using words as words. (in a set with *bold face print and *italics)

<strong>underlining, continuous</strong>: underlining that continues under the spaces between words and under punctuation that may be present

<strong>understatement</strong>: saying something at a lesser level than the situation really is. A teacher might say to someone who has a 99% average in the class, “I think you will pass okay.” Understatement is a manner of speaking sometimes associated with British custom; a device that can be used for humorous purposes. (in a set with *over statement, *exaggeration)

<strong>ungrammatical</strong>: understandable but not following the conventional rules of grammar: “A great deal of money don’t lead men to wisdom”; “him and me are best friends”; “between you and I”; “he be my help at work.” “now looking at the weather, there is a cold front coming in.”(in a set with *usage)

<strong>uninflected</strong>: not having more than one form to indicate variation in meaning and syntax. Some *parts of speech are not inflected in any language: adverbs, expletives, interjections, prepositions. Even in highly inflected languages there may be a few *indeclinable nouns like Latin <em>nihil, satis</em>, <em>etc.</em>

<strong>universal language</strong>: same as lingua franca, language used over a wide area where several more local languages are used

<strong>unpointed text: </strong>the Hebrew consonantal text without the *vowel points added later to facilitate pronunciation

(in a set with *pointed text)

<strong>unreadable</strong>: (1) illegible, unreadable because of the sloppiness of the writing; (2) unreadable because it is written in an unknown *cipher

<strong>untranslatable</strong>: not able to be translated very precisely or efficiently into another language. Sometimes *circumlocution can clarify the idea by departing from the strict correlation of wording between the two languages. In some cases, it may take an explanatory addition outside the flow of the text to make the idea clear—a footnote, endnote, sidenote, marginal note, bracketed noe within the text.

(in a set with *translatable)

<strong>unwritten</strong>: not written, a nevertheless known rule of operation, one that may not publicly be acknowledged or so customary that it need not be said: “it goes without saying.”

<strong>upper case</strong>: another term for *capital letters (of the alphabet) or higher case <em> vs.</em> *lower case (in a set with *lower case, *small letters; see also *common noun, *proper noun; *minuscules, *majuscules; )

<strong>upsilon</strong>: twentieth letter of the Greek alphabet: υ/Υ.

<strong>uptalk</strong>: the habit saying everything with the intonation of a question. According to RD, May, 2010, p. 131, <em>uptalk</em> was coined in 1993 by a professor at New York University.

<strong>utterance</strong>: vocal expression, statement

<strong>vade mecum</strong>: a handbook, guidebook, something continuously useful.; built on the word picture of something that goes everywhere with a person: Latin, <em>go with me</em>.

<strong>value</strong>: the sound quality of a letter of set of letters

<strong>valley talk</strong>:

<strong>variant reading</strong>: After the advent of the printing press and modern copying machines, duplication can be precise. In ancient manuscripts, which were copied by hand, however, there were many more opportunities for mistakes to enter into the reproduction process. Consequently, in New Testament and Old Testament as well as in copies of other ancient writings, certain places in the texts “vary” in the way they read from manuscript to manuscript. This fact gives rise to a discipline called *textual criticism, which endeavors to retrieve the original, correct reading wherever variants occur. It works out that no variant reading in the Bible introduces a difference in doctrinal content.

Differences arise (a) because the original different wordings of the same account in the *synoptic gospels have been regularized.  Perhaps the scribe, in an effort to be more efficient, relied on memory too much and ended up confusing the different wordings of the two accounts.

(b) Sometimes word order is changed.

(c)  <strong>put under textual criticism</strong>

<strong>[[list of major manuscript variants]]</strong>

(in a set with *textual criticism, *dittography, *haplography, *homoioteleuton, *metathesis)

<strong>vav</strong>: the sixth letter of the Hebrew alphabet. Also waw.

<strong>veiled</strong>: not quite clear reference—veiled reference; implied, not straightforward, as in a veiled threat.

<strong>velar: a </strong>sound made with the tongue against the soft-palate in the back of the mouth; previously called *palatal or even *guttural. (in a set with *labials, *dentals, *liquids, *glottals)

<strong>verbatim</strong>: a word-for-word restatement of someone’s comment. Same as <em>ad verbum</em>, Lat)

<strong>verb</strong>: a word with certain inflection features and distribution placements in a sentence that indicates what the subject is or is doing.  Popularly defined as a word that expresses action or state of being.

An action verb refers to action, movement, process in contrast to linking verbs, <em>etc.</em>, which indicate mere existence


Copulative verbs (= linking verbs, equative verbs, state-of-being verbs)

Equative verbs (= linking verbs, copulative verbs, state-of-being verbs)

Linking verbs (= copulative verbs, equative verbs, state-of-being verbs)

State-of-being verbs (= copulative verbs, equative verbs linking verbs)

Fientive verbs: verbs that equal an English linking verbs plus predicate adjective: “is-strong”

Finite verbs, which indicate person

Infinite verbals, which do not indicate person; i. e., infinitives, participles, and gerunds

Main verbs appear in independent clauses.

Dependent verbs appear in dependent clauses.

Primary verb

Auxiliary verb: English uses forms of the verbs to be (is, are, was, were), do (do, does), have (has, have); may/might, will/would, shall/should, can/could, tried to.

Regular verbs are those whose other principal parts are predictable by standard patterns starting with the first principal part.

Irregular verbs do not have predictable principal parts.

Suppletive verbs are irregular verbs whose principal parts come from completely different roots: cp. English go, went, gone; Gr phero, oiso, enenka; in NT lego; Lat fero, ferre, tuli, latus and its cognates adfero, aufero, confero, offero, refero. (= *hybrid verb wh 212)

Defective verbs are those that do not have all the normal principal parts.

Deponent verbs are those that lack one or more of possible voices in one or more of their principal parts. In Greek there are first deponents, which lack the active voice only; as well as second deponents, which lack both the active and middle voices. In the case of second deponents, there is no third principal part because that principal part supplies the base for active and middle voice in the aorist and future tenses; consequently, those types of verbs have only a sixth principal part for covering the aorist tense. (The third principal part provides the base for active- and middle-voice forms.)

*Transitive verbs have *direct objects that complete the meaning: “He cried a river.”

*Intransitive verbs do not have an object: “He cried.”

*Impersonal verb exists only in the third person singular and technically has a in (phrase) as its subject: Latin <em>licet</em>

A *merged verb (English) closely combines a basic verb and an adverb in that order, either immediately or after separation: “She probably wears out the poor man”/”She probably wears the poor man out.” “Please look up the number”/”Please look the number up.” Also called a *fused verb although the latter is technically limited to phrases that cannot also be separated. The structure is similar to the Greek and Latin practice of prefixing prepositions to verbs to nuance or stress their meanings.

<strong>verbal</strong>: a form derived from a *finite verb: *infinitive, *participle, and *gerund. In addition, Latin has *gerundive (future passive participle specifically) and *supine.

Classical Greek had verbals that derived from the sixth principal part of the verb (-t[e]os, -t[e]a, -t[e]on) and were used to express necessity.

<strong>verbal adjunct</strong>: anything that connects with a verb—*direct object, *indirect object, *adverb, *prepositional phrase. This feature of grammar applies as well to *verbals—*participles, *gerunds, *infinitives, *gerundives, *supines.

<strong>verbal inspiration</strong>: a view of inspiration that attempts to avoid the ambiguity in use of <em>inspiration</em> by itself as a description of scripture. Verbal means that its accuracy is not a general conceptual matter, but lies at the level of the words of revelation through God’s prophets.

(in a set with *dictation theory of inspiration, *dynamic inspiration, *infallibility, *plenary inspiration; *inerrancy)

<strong>verbal noun</strong>: a noun derived from a verb, a *gerund, an *infinitive used in a noun slot.

<strong>verbalism</strong>: an expression in words

<strong>verbalize</strong>: to put a concept into words; to express it in words.

<strong>verbiage</strong>: the way something is expressed in words; also wordiness, *verbosity

<strong>verify</strong>: a possibility in English that comes from its relatively sparse inflectional endings: to use a noun in a verb slot, to “ verb” your nouns. Many English words like <em>work</em> and <em>hammer</em> have been serving a dual role for a long time in the language already. “Verbing a noun” in effect, then, means doing it to nouns that have not yet been entered into that usage. “to transition into a new role”; “to <em>position</em> yourself for advancement in the company”; <strong>example</strong>

<strong>verbose</strong>: wordy, “full of words,” using many words to say something or talking for a long time about it; lacking economy of expression (in a set with *prolix)

<strong>verbosity</strong>: wordiness [Latin: <em>verb</em>-, word, -<em>ose</em>, full of, -<em>ity</em>, condition]

<strong>verisimilitude</strong>: writing in a way appropriate to the setting of what is written about.

An example in the New Testament may be Acts <strong>1-12</strong> <em> vs.</em> Acts <strong>13-28</strong>. More distinctively Greek idioms like *litotes appear in the last section, where the account chronicles the Gentile mission, while *Hebraisms are more prevalent in the first part, where the events take place in Palestine. Samuel Clemens [Mark Twain] draws attention to that feature of his writing in <em>Tom Sawyer</em>.

<strong>vernacular</strong>: the standard, every-day, local language of the common person. In more recent times, the Roman Catholic Church has adopted the practice of conducting services “in the vernacular” in contrast to using universal ecclesiastical Latin for that purpose, a practice in place for several hundred years.

<strong>verse</strong>: the shortest division of a biblical text, which occurs within a chapter (except, of course, with the four short New Testament books that do not have chapter divisions: Philemon, 1 John, 2 John, Jude)

In music lyrics, the verse is the introductory section that leads to the chorus, which may repeat after each new verse.

In poetry, a verse equals a set of lines.

(in a set with *chapter, *book, *part, *volume)

<strong>version</strong>: a *translation, especially a translation of the same text in comparison with other translations of the same writing. Outside a translation context, <em>version</em> can refer to a particular form of a writing or account among several forms of the item

<strong>videlicet</strong>: a marker for introducing a list [Latin: *crasis of <em>videre licet</em>, to see is permitted]

<strong>viewpoint</strong>: the one through whose eyes a story is told Agatha Christie’s *mystery that tells the story through the eyes of the guilty character.

(in a set with *anthropocentric viewpoint, *geocentric viewpoint, *omniscient viewpoint)

<strong>vignette</strong>: a short literary sketch; a scene excerpted from a movie

<strong>visible speech</strong>: a system used in teaching the deaf or hard-of-hearing to “read lips,” more exactly to see all the factors that go into oral expression

<strong>vitriol</strong>: bitter, caustic, hurtful criticism of someone

<strong>vivid narration</strong>: the use of the *historic present instead of the past tense (perfect in Latin or aorist in Greek) when giving an account of an occurrence in past time: “Yesterday, I am going downtown to pay my mortgage, and this guy stops me on the sidewalk and . . . “

<strong>vocabulary</strong>: (1) the words a language uses.

*Cognate words are those that share the same or similar base: work (noun), work (verb), workable/unworkable, workman, working (man), (public) works, workbrittle, workhorse, workflow, worker, workaholic, workout, workmanship, a work-up, and a number of other words.

The speaking vocabulary, listening vocabulary, and reading vocabulary are in ascending order of size according to a native speaker’s use of the language.

(2) the distinctive words associated with a particular discipline or field of study.

(in a set with *inflection, *syntax)

<strong>vocative case</strong>: case of direct address: “John, come here, please.”

In English, a vocative noun may be preceded by the particle <em>O</em>, a practice that has largely fallen out of usage. English has no distinctive case form, the usage being signaled by pause, *distribution, or pitch pattern to keep it separate from the nominative. Written English sets off the vocative with commas, reflecting the associated pitch pattern and slight pause in oral expression.

In Latin and Greek the case form for vocative is always the same as the nominative in the plural. In the singular, Greek <em>–os</em> nouns use <em>–e</em>, and in Latin the corresponding <em>–us</em> declension likewise uses <em>–e</em> to indicate vocative. Virtually everywhere else the nominative and vocative singular have the same form.  Latin –<em>ius</em> nouns use <em>–i</em> for vocative singular; for <em>meus</em> the vocative is <em>mi</em>; and for <em>deus</em> the vocative plural is <em>di</em>.

(in a set with *nominative, *genitive, *dative, *accusative, *ablative, *locative)

<strong>vociferate</strong>: to speak loudly, vehemently, especially as in protest

<strong>voice</strong>: Depending on the language, voice may be *active voice, *passive voice, *middle voice, or simply *stative or *linking. Active voice has the subject acting in the verb. Passive voice has the subject acted on by the verb. Middle voice has the subject acting on itself or on a direct object so as to affect itself. A *linking verb does not show action in the verb; it simply connects the subject with another noun or *descriptor. A *stative verb is one that emphasizes the resulting condition of the action: “I have it done,” one possible translation of the Greek and Latin perfect tense.

(in a set with *person, *number, *tense, *mood, *aspect)

<strong>voiced</strong>: having the vocal cords vibrating in the making of the sound (in a set with *voiceless)

<strong>voiceless</strong>: pertaining to a sound that does not have the vocal cords vibrating, as in a whisper or with consonants that do not require the use of the vocal cords. (in a set with *voiced; *surd)

<strong>volume</strong>: (1) degree of loudness. (2) a subdivision of a large work that has more than one book form, or codex, to contain the material.

(in a set with *verse, *chapter, *paragraph, *part, *book, *set, *reference)

<strong>word play</strong>: word usage that takes advantage of the multiple meanings of words; *paronomasia

(in a set with *groaner, *Tom Swifty)

<strong>vowel</strong>:

(in a set with *consonant, *liquid, *click, *rounded vowel)

<strong>vowel points</strong>: markings developed in the ??? century by the Masoretes?? for indicating the vowel sounds that went with the consonants in the original Hebrew Old Testament.

<strong>vowel glide:</strong> a slide from one vowel to another; a kind of *diphthong.

(in a set with *monophthong, *diphthong)

<strong>vowelize</strong>: another expression for adding vowel points as in Hebrew, whose basic alphabet is consonantal.

<strong>vulgarism</strong>: a term or expression with extremely negative connotation, meant in complete disrespect and not welcome in polite company. (in a set with *archaism, *buzz word, *catch phrase, *coarse talk, *curse word, *colloquialism, *formal term, *informal term, *oath, *obsolete term, *provincialisms, *regionalism, *slang, *technical term)

<strong>Vulgate</strong>: the standard Latin translation of the Bible used even today. It was finished by Jerome at the end of the <sup>300</sup>’s A. D. and lies behind modern vernacular Roman Catholic translations including the Confraternity Edition Bible in English. The Vulgate replaced the Old Latin translation of the Bible, being better suited to use by the masses. The term <em>vulgate</em> originally meant the language of the common people, a usage related to the word <em>vulgar</em>, which has picked up a negative connotation in modern English.

<strong>waw</strong>: the sixth letter of the Hebrew alphabet. Also <em>vav</em>.: ו

<strong>weak verb:</strong> A Hebrew verb that has a guttural as one of its root letters is called “weak verbs.” Also pe-nun verbs, verbs that have nun in their initial root position

(in a set with *triliteral root, *quadrןliteral root, *doubly weak verb)

<strong>weasel word</strong>: a word used in such a way that it is intentionally misleading so as to avoid taking a position or making a commitment

<strong>western text</strong> (in a set with *majority text, *Byzantine text, *neutral text)

<strong><em>will</em></strong>: (1) the main auxiliary verb for expressing the future with English verbs; (2) as a development off of futurity, <em>will</em> can indicate a person’s intentions; (3) a *testament by which one indicates his intentions for passing on property after death

<strong>wisdom literature</strong>: Old Testament examples are Job and Proverbs; in the New Testament, The Sermon on the Mount/Plain, James, and Jude are examples. Wisdom literature makes observations and gives advice more exactly on the basis of the nature of things—what works or does not work. Thus, it is *advice more than *commandment, which is based in the authority of the essential author. Wisdom literature speaks about significant generalities, whereas law and commandment speak in absolutes

<strong>witnessing</strong>: in modern religious usage, speaking to non-Christians about Christian belief and practice; testifying, evangelizing

<strong>witticism</strong>: a cute, clever, light statement; *<em>bon mot</em> wh 217

<strong>word</strong>: a free-standing unit of communication that is understood by fellow speakers. Saying that something is not a word is not strictly a cogent statement if it is meant by a speaker and understood by hearers. What the expression “is not a word” means is that it is not considered a “proper” word in educated society. Consequently, the following—among many others—are words: <em>ain’t, dark complected, hisself, irregardless, theirselves</em>; all words for animal sounds: cluck, <em>meow, peep, quack, </em>[see under *onomapoeia]

<strong>word building</strong>: the creation of *vocabulary in a language by combining *roots, *suffixes,* prefixes, *infixes (*morphemes).

Sequence of roots. In compound words with more than one root, all the parts usually come from the same language. If Latin or Greek is involved, all the parts of the word are likely to come from Greek or from Latin. So the word is <em>n</em><em><u>y</u></em><em>ctalopia</em>, not <em>n</em><em><u>oc</u></em><em>talopia</em> because the latter morpheme for <em>night </em>comes from Latin while the <em>al- </em>(blind) and <em>opia</em> come from Greek.

In long compound words such as those frequently found in medical terminology, the order of the roots follows the sequence of the process. In the digestive process, the esophagus precedes the stomach, which precedes the duodenum, <em>etc.</em> Consequently, the words will be <em>esophagogastric</em> and <em>gastroduodenoscopy</em>. If the <em>esophagogastric</em> addresses an acid reflux problem, however, it may be written <em>gastroesophageal</em>. In medical terminology the body part comes first, followed by the condition or procedure suffixed to the end.

Words ending in –<em>ma</em> add<em> t</em> before connecting vowel and any further additional elements. That happens because the Greek words from which –<em>ma</em> words come had a <em>t</em> that ended the root everywhere except in the nominative singular. The *zero ending in that place for third-declension neuter nouns meant that the dictionary form would have ended in t. Greek words could not end in <em>t</em>, however; so the letter <em>tau </em>was dropped unless inflection added a sound. Then it appeared as part of the word. That same pattern applies to English derivatives of all -<em>ma </em>words to yield forms like <strong>[insert panel] </strong><em>atheroma/atheromatai</em>;<em> derma, dermatitis, dermatology</em>; <em>lipoma/lipomata</em>; <em>sarcoma/sarcomata</em>;<em> soma/psychosomatic</em>; <em>stigma, stigmata, stigmatize</em>; <em>trauma/traumatology</em>;

<strong>word of mouth</strong>: the spread of information informally individual to individual without deliberate intent to *advertise and without the use of *mass media. Similar to social media in contrast to dispersal by official publication.

<strong>word order</strong>

In Latin the basic word order is subject, indirect object, direct object, adverbs, and verb; but the considerable deviation from this sequence provides variety and emphasis. Languages like Latin and Greek that have case endings do not need to maintain a particular sequence of sentence parts.  In *poetry the sequence is even more radical that in *prose.  If a word appears up front that by standard order more likely would to appear later in the sentence, the author probably intends to emphasize it. Breaks from standard order are noticeable and therefore stressed. *Interrogative words typically appear at the front of their clause, whether they are subjects or not.

English has set formats because there are no case indicators except with pronouns; so placement/distribution—word order—indicates the role a word has in the sentence. Flexibility in sequence is greatly reduced from what it can be in the classical languages.

Latin has a basic sentence format consisting of subject, indirect object, direct object, adverb, and verb. The standard position for an adjective is after the noun it modifies: <em>femina bona</em>. Demonstratives and numbers, however, usually precede the noun.

Some words do not appear first in their clause, words said to be postpositive. English used to observe the custom of not putting <em>however</em> at the front of a sentence. Latin has <em>autem</em>, <em>igitur</em>, <em>inquit</em>, Greek has <em>de, gar, te</em>,

There are *bound constructions even in highly inflected languages that do not have to depend on word order for *syntax. Latin and Greek prepositional phrases have a noun-object sequence (with the exception of one postpositive preposition in Greek, where its object appears before the preposition:  ). Latin departs from this uniformity when <em>cum</em> has an object modified by an adjective; in that case the adjective precedes the preposition (<em>magna cum laude</em>). When <em>cum</em> has a personal pronoun as its object, it becomes a *postpositive attached to the end of the pronoun: <em>mecum</em>. Some other Latin bound constructions have <em>causa </em>preceded by its genitive (<em>remissionis causa</em>, “for-the-sake of-forgiveness”).

Greek always has its articles before its nouns. With articular nouns, adjective always appear between article and noun (first attributive position) or after the repeated article (*second attributive position), or on the other side of a linking verb. Demonstratives, however, always appear in *predicate position, that is before the article or after the noun.

Hebrew typically puts the verb first followed by the subject and direct object.

German tends to puts its verb last.

(akin to *distribution, *postpositive)

<strong>word parts</strong>: same as *morpheme; the smallest unit of meaning in a word. Thus “word parts” include, *roots/*bases/*stems, *combining forms, *prefixes, *infixes, *suffixes (in a set with *affix)

<strong>word picture</strong>: the imagery in a word because of its origin by derivation or coinage; probably a better concept than the oft-used “literal.” The word picture is usually less than its applied meaning. <em>Homosexual</em>, for example, by word picture means “same sex”; but its applied meaning adds the element “attracted to” (the same sex). The word for <em>resurrection </em>in Greek is <em>anastasis</em>, which is comprised of <em>ana</em>, “again,” and <em>sta</em>-, “to stand”; its word picture, then, is a standing again, but it is particularized into standing again after being prone because of death.

Sometimes the word picture does not even involve the most crucial aspect of the *referent. <em>Epistaxis</em> is a word for “nosebleed,” but nothing in it says “nose.” <em>Atelectasis</em> is a word for “collapsed lung,” but nothing in it refers to “lung.”

<strong>word play</strong>: *pun, *paronomasia. An expression that takes advantage of a word’s variant meanings. The Golden Rule has been humorously restated to mean “they that have the gold have the rule”; golden can mean very good or made of gold and rule can mean principle of control.

<strong>word square</strong>:

<strong>word used as a word</strong>: a word not used so much for its meaning or its role in the sentence, but for its own identity. Such a word is always put in the nominative case in Latin, Greek, and English: “She said <em>he</em> four times in one sentence.” A word so used has singular number, taking a singular verb and singular modifiers: “<em>This </em><em><u>foolish one</u></em>s describe<em>s</em> the monkeys.”

<strong>wordal/wordle</strong>: a collage of words related to a common theme and put on a page or sheet without regard for order, symmetry, or maintaining horizontal direction.

<strong>work</strong>: a scholarly writing in some important field of study; it may refer to a multi-volume set and usually the result of one author.

(in a set with *monograph, *book, dictionary, *encyclopedia, *synopticon, *writing)

<strong>workbook</strong>: a book that accompanies a *textbook and provides exercises that reinforce the materials presented in the text.

<strong>writ</strong>: a legal document (in a set with *deed, *title) (in a set with *deed, *title)

<strong>writer</strong>: another term for author, even if that person dictated the text to a stenographer. The person who writes the words of a song in contrast to composer, who writer the music.

(in a set with *author, *lyric, *lyrics, *lyricist, *libretto)

<strong>writing</strong>: putting something down on paper in contrast to oral expression; *cursive in contrast to printing. Originally applied to handwritten works, but retained after the printing press as a designation for an author’s work however produced.

(in a set with *prose, *poetry; *block lettering, *scrawl, *scribble)

<strong>writings</strong>: the compositions by which an author has formally expressed himself on subjects of interest to him; implies a scholarly product

<strong>xenoglossia: </strong>speaking in an actual contemporary foreign language, especially by the enablement of the Holy Spirit in contrast to a foreign language learned by normal means

(in a set with *glossolalia, *ecstatic utterance, *speaking in tongues. *tongue speaking)

<strong>xi</strong>: fourteenth letter of the Greek alphabet: ξ/Ξ

<strong>xylograph</strong>: an engraving on wood

<strong>Yahveh/Yahweh</strong>: the approximate vocalization of the *tetragrammeton, the ineffable name of the Hebrew God

<strong>yellow journalism</strong>: uninhibited, careless journalism usually negative or defamatory

<strong>yod/yodh</strong>: the tenth letter of the Hebrew alphabet: י

<strong>“you” advised</strong>: the use of <em>you</em> as an *indefinite pronoun in place of <em>one, a person, someone</em>: “When <em>you</em> throw a curve ball, <em>you</em> put <em>your</em> fingers on the seams this way.”

<strong>zayin</strong>: the seventh letter of the Hebrew alphabet: ז

<strong>zed</strong>: the letter <em>z</em> in British *parlance.

<strong>zero ending</strong>: In an inflection set, the absence of an ending parallel to the others endings becomes significant. +Latin examples. It occurs in the neuter nominative singular of third-declension Latin nouns and in the nominative masculine singulars of –er nouns and adjectives in the second declension. Ex. Latin, <sup>2</sup>nd sg imperative active (da, give); nom mas sg of –er nouns and adjectives from the second declension (puer, pulcher). Zero endings also appear in second singular imperatives of third-declension active verbs (dic, duc, fer, fac (or are their vowels lengthened in these examples?)

+Hebrew examples: third sg mas perf verbs

<strong>zeta</strong>: the sixth letter of the Greek alphabet: ζ/Ζ

<strong>zeugma</strong>: the use of a word to mean two things at once. WH 289

(in a set with *paronomasia, *play on words)
